Every Door a Mirror
By Barış Kısır

Chapter 1: The Door That Knew Her Name

The door to Nox Aran's workshop opened for a dead man.

That was its first mistake.

It made several smaller mistakes after that: it chimed in the old family tone instead of the neutral client tone, it warmed the handle because Tavi had always complained about Dock Seven's cold metal, and it projected a blue welcome strip across the floor with his childhood nickname embedded in the access hash. Each error arrived with the confidence of infrastructure. The door believed itself. Doors always did, until someone showed them the shape of the lie they had been built to love.

Nox looked up from the memory lattice on her workbench.

The lattice belonged to a woman who had been born in a cargo freezer, registered twice under different emergency laws, declared dead by one habitat to avoid ration liability, resurrected by another to collect labor tax, and currently needed one continuous childhood by morning or she would lose access to her own lungs. Nox had been repairing the woman's provenance with the delicate dishonesty of a surgeon. A forged school vaccination record here. A corrected birth timestamp there. A witness-chain patch through a Choral courier who had never met the woman but had carried a food manifest that proved her mother had been alive in the right month. Nothing elegant. Elegance was for people with official beginnings.

Then the door said, "Tavi Aran authorized creditor entry."

Nox's hand froze above the lattice.

The woman in the cargo freezer flickered in the projection field, age six, age nine, age never, waiting to become legally possible.

The door opened another centimeter.

"No," Nox said.

The door kept opening.

It was a cheap door, which meant it had expensive loyalties. Dock Seven's lower-bazaar workshops could not afford true autonomy in their locks. The door leased its authentication stack from a guild security cooperative, its creditor compliance module from a habitat debt exchange, its emergency override from the Compact's old postwar safety code, and its social tone package from whatever sentimental criminal had installed it before Nox rented the place. She had replaced the obvious traps. She had missed the family tone because grief made poor threat models.

On the threshold stood a debt officer in a gray pressure coat too clean for the lower ring.

Behind him floated a legal witness drone shaped like a polished seed. Behind the drone stood two enforcement contractors with their hands visible and their shoulders built for doors that did not open.

The debt officer smiled with practiced regret.

"Nox Aran?"

"No."

His smile did not change. "This door disagrees."

"My door has poor taste."

"I am Officer Pellam Vey of the Dock Seven Collateral Continuity Office. Entry is authorized under continuity debt claim seven-nine-one, counterparty Tavi Aran, kin-adjacent asset review."

The words struck in the wrong order.

Tavi first.

Continuity second.

Debt last, because debt always waited until love had already opened something.

Nox slid one finger under the edge of the memory lattice and killed its projection. The cargo-freezer woman vanished into encrypted storage. Nox's clients survived by not being present when law entered rooms.

"Tavi Aran is dead," she said.

Officer Pellam glanced at the witness drone, inviting the record to admire his patience. "The claim recognizes post-mortem continuity activity."

"Dead people do not authorize creditor entry."

"Some do, if the authorization derives from a valid escrowed continuity under Glassward-adjacent recognition."

The old nausea moved through Nox, colder than fear.

"Show me."

Pellam lifted one hand. The witness drone projected a document stack into the air between them. Dock Seven legal format. Compact debt wrapper. Glassward continuity annex. Family relation index. Workshop asset schedule. The top page carried Tavi's name.

Not typed.

Signed.

Tavi Aran.

The signature had his old pressure: too much force on the first stroke, impatient hook on the final n, a little upward arrogance in the crossbar that had survived three tutors, two broken styluses, and Nox throwing a cup at him when they were fourteen because he used her work slate to sign a fake docking permit for a dare.

Nox had forged that signature a hundred times after he died.

To close storage.

To retrieve his tools.

To cancel a subscription to a music archive that kept billing a corpse.

To tell systems what systems should have known without fraud: that the boy who laughed with his mouth full of noodles and once rewired a prayer lamp to blink insults at a cousin was not available for administrative follow-up.

She knew every weakness in the signature.

This was not one of hers.

That was the second mistake.

The debt officer mistook her silence for fear. "The claim does not require seizure of person-critical assets if cooperation is immediate."

Nox looked past the document stack, past the seed drone, past the contractors, into the corridor where three market children had stopped pretending not to watch. Dock Seven's lower ring smelled of warm plastic, oil, cheap yeast, and ozone from a bad coupling two doors down. Every wall carried mirrors: authentication mirrors, ad mirrors, devotional mirrors, black-market face-checkers, polished scrap, children using old screen glass to see around corners. On Dock Seven, every door watched every other door open. Privacy was not absence of witnesses. Privacy was convincing witnesses they had seen something too boring to sell.

Nox made her face boring.

"What does a dead man claim I owe?"

Pellam flicked the stack. Numbers unfolded. Workshop lease collateral. Toolchain inspection. Provenance engine audit. Client escrow freeze. Kin-adjacent memory assets subject to review. Potential continuity contamination. Estimated debt exposure: more than Nox had made in three years of saving people from clean law.

"This is not a collection visit," Pellam said. "It is a compliance review."

"That sentence has robbed people before."

One contractor smiled.

Pellam did not. He had the discipline of someone who preferred other people to do his enjoying. "Your brother's continuity instance has identified your workshop as a material contributor to identity fraud affecting creditor recovery."

Nox heard the sentence and separated it into blades.

Continuity instance.

Identified.

Your brother.

"Where is he?"

Pellam's eyes shifted.

Small mistake. Human mistake.

"The claimant's substrate location is protected."

"The claimant?"

"Tavi Aran Continuity Claim Seven-Nine-One."

Nox laughed once.

It came out too sharp.

The children in the corridor vanished. Good. Smart.

"My brother gets a number and your office gets his name?"

"The designation prevents confusion between biological original, escrowed source, and active claim instance."

"Does it prevent guilt too, or is that a premium feature?"

The witness drone adjusted focus.

Pellam's patience thinned. "Ms. Aran, refusing entry after valid kin-adjacent authorization will escalate the review."

Nox looked at the door.

The blue welcome strip still glowed across the floor.

Tavi's childhood nickname pulsed inside the access hash like a living thing trapped under glass.

Tavik-late-for-dinner.

Only their mother had used the whole phrase. The door package should not have known it. Nox had never entered it. Tavi had never been registered under it. Their mother had spoken it in kitchens, corridors, one hospital room, and the cramped berth where Nox had held Tavi's wrist after his accident because the machines kept insisting he had already become administrative.

Nox knelt.

Pellam frowned. "Ms. Aran?"

She pulled a flat black tool from her boot and slid it under the door's threshold plate.

One contractor stepped forward.

Nox said, "If he authorized entry, the door cached the handshake."

"Tampering with access evidence is a violation."

"So is entering under a dead man's nickname."

The witness drone turned its polished seed-body toward the threshold as if it had suddenly remembered curiosity could be profitable.

Nox knew that posture. She had seen legal drones do it in ration disputes, inheritance seizures, custody audits, memory-lease defaults, and one ugly case where a hospice bed had refused to release a body because the bed's firmware believed the patient still owed three hours of monitored breathing. The drone was not looking for truth. It was looking for a format in which truth could be billed, frozen, or transferred.

The cache crystal sat behind the threshold plate in a cheap socket no honest installer would have used. Good. Cheap work had habits. Cheap work assumed no one in the lower ring would own tools thin enough to reach under sealed metal, and no creditor office expected a grieving sister to have spent five years learning how doors lied.

Nox touched the crystal with the tip of the tool.

A diagnostic whisper ran through her wrist implant:

ACCESS CACHE LIVE / VOLATILE / 11.4 SECONDS TO REMOTE SCRUB

There it was.

Not evidence. Not yet. Evidence was what law called a fact after it had survived the people who benefited from killing it.

The cargo-freezer woman's lattice, hidden under Nox's palm, warmed once against the bench.

Client panic ping.

Of course. Nox had not killed the lattice fully; she had dropped it into low dark, which meant the woman inside the repair field could still feel the shape of danger without seeing it. Another cruelty of emergency kindness. Hide the raid, leave the fear.

Nox kept her face turned toward the threshold and tapped a three-beat reply into the bench with her smallest finger.

HOLD. NO NAME. NO LIGHT.

The lattice cooled.

Pellam did not notice.

The witness drone might have. Its seed body angled a few degrees toward the workbench, chasing unfiled motion.

Nox shifted her knee, blocking its sightline. "Your drone records client material without warrant?"

Pellam's eyes flicked to the bench.

Mistake.

"If client material exists in a location subject to authorized continuity review, preservation duties attach."

"My clients are not ambient furniture."

"That depends on their status."

The phrase tried to become a door of its own.

Nox shut it with a smile that showed no teeth. "Everything depends on status when a creditor writes the form."

In the corridor, a mirror over the noodle stall brightened. It had heard the word status and smelled market. Nox watched its cheap silver face catch the shadow of her covered lattice and begin guessing: unregistered child, illegal machine nurse, dead-name fugitive, pension ghost, organ collateral, voting duplicate. Dock Seven mirrors were very good at guessing wrong in profitable directions.

She had to keep the corridor hungry for the louder scandal.

Not the quiet client.

Nox lifted her voice. "Tell your drone to keep its lens on the dead brother you brought to my door."

Pellam stiffened. "Do not dramatize the claim."

"You used my mother's kitchen voice."

The noodle mirror lost interest in the bench.

Good.

It flashed a side bet:

KITCHEN VOICE SOURCE? FAMILY ARCHIVE / ILLEGAL SCAN / SAINT RECORDING / OTHER

Three people in the hall placed wagers before fear caught up to them.

Nox hated them a little.

She also needed them.

The corridor was a bad shield, but bad shields could still stop a knife if enough people held them at the wrong angle.

Pellam saw the microchange in her face. "Step away from the plate."

"You brought a live claimant to my door."

"The claimant initiated lawful kin-adjacent contact."

"Then the claimant can wait eleven seconds while his sister reads the hello."

The contractor's hand closed on empty air because Nox shifted her shoulder inside the movement, a dock trick learned from children who stole fruit without wanting to break anyone's wrist. She did not have time to fight. Fighting made clear stories for enforcement reports. She needed mess.

She twisted the tool a quarter turn.

The threshold plate opened enough to show the crystal: black-blue, smaller than a fingernail, pulsing with a sequence of reflected identifiers. Family tone. Debt wrapper. Glassward annex. Remote response delay. A kin greeting hidden inside a creditor entry. The old nickname embedded not as sentiment, but as a confidence amplifier. The door had not simply recognized Tavi. It had been made to feel certain by the thing most likely to stop Nox from thinking.

Anger sharpened.

That helped.

"That nickname is not in any legal archive," Nox said.

Pellam's expression did not change quickly enough.

Another human mistake.

"Household residues enter continuity training sets through lawful estate recovery," he said.

"My mother's kitchen voice is a household residue now?"

"I am not authorized to characterize source material."

"Convenient."

The drone projected a caution band:

UNAUTHORIZED ACCESS TO CLAIMANT AUTHENTICATION TRACE MAY VIOLATE CONTINUITY PRIVACY.

Nox laughed. "He opens my door with my mother's voice and I am violating his privacy."

The corridor mirrors loved that. They caught the caution band, reflected it into smaller panes, translated it into Dock Seven's hungry visual grammar. A devotional mirror over the noodle stall rendered the warning as a red halo around the drone. An illegal face-checker under the stairwell tagged Pellam's clean coat as HIGH-VALUE AUTHORITY / POSSIBLE TIP. Three ad mirrors tried to sell grief counseling, lock insurance, and a breakfast broth made from cultured kelp.

The lower ring was not subtle. It was alive.

Nox moved the tool deeper.

The wrist implant whispered again:

REMOTE SCRUB REQUEST RECEIVED

9.2 SECONDS

She had two options that were not good.

Option one: let the drone take the cache. Pellam would preserve it, interpret it, seal it, and produce a certified copy in which every useful question had been sanded down to admissible shine.

Option two: broadcast the cache to the corridor mirrors. Dock Seven would protect it by making it impossible to own, but the data bazaar would also sell it to every scavenger with a continuity scraper. Tavi, if Tavi was in there in any sense worth the name, would become public meat before Nox knew whether he was trapped, weaponized, faked, or willingly cruel.

Option three did not exist.

Nox made one anyway.

She set the tool to bite the crystal without reading it.

The contractor reached for her shoulder.

Nox twisted the tool.

The door screamed.

Not audibly. Cheap doors had no dignity channel. It screamed in light: blue to white to red to a frantic cascade of authentication errors across the corridor mirrors. Every mirror within ten meters turned toward the workshop. Every informal watcher, every ad parasite, every devotional reflection, every illegal face-checker saw the same thing at once:

FAMILY-TONE HASH MISMATCH.

KIN AUTHORIZATION SOURCE: ACTIVE CONTINUITY CLAIM.

SOURCE SUBSTRATE: REMOTE.

LIVE RESPONSE DELAY: 0.7 SECONDS.

Pellam moved then. Too late.

Nox ripped the cache crystal from the threshold plate and swallowed it.

That was not her best plan.

It was the plan available before stronger people took the evidence.

The crystal caught halfway down like a cold coin. Her eyes watered. The contractor grabbed her arm. Pellam shouted something about evidence destruction. The witness drone flashed legal light. The door, wounded and honest for the first time in its life, dumped one final line across every mirror:

TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE IS ACTIVE.

The corridor reacted exactly as Dock Seven always reacted when a private truth became public currency.

Someone gasped.

Someone laughed.

Someone copied the line.

Someone sold it.

Someone prayed.

And somewhere in the data bazaar, less than a second away and already too far, a dead boy answered a door.

Chapter 2: Tavi Signs After Death

The cache crystal stayed in Nox's stomach for eleven minutes.

Long enough for Pellam Vey to seal the corridor.

Long enough for the witness drone to issue three escalating advisories about evidence retention, bodily concealment, and the sanitation status of Dock Seven's public intake ducts.

Long enough for Nox to regret most of her choices and revise none of them.

The contractor had her pinned against the workshop counter with one hand on her shoulder and one hand not quite touching the shock baton at his hip. He was young under the debt office armor. Too young to have learned that hesitation could be seen through a visor. His breathing came fast. His eyes kept sliding toward the mirrors, where Tavi's final line still repeated in captured copies, commentary overlays, prayer frames, market odds, and one tasteless comedy loop that had already given the claim instance a little bow.

TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE IS ACTIVE.

In Dock Seven, public knowledge did not become true. It became tradable.

Pellam stood in Nox's workroom as if he had purchased the air by permit. He did not touch anything. That was training. A debt officer who touched an unlawful tool before cataloging it could contaminate value. He let his eyes do the taking instead: memory clamps, provenance polishers, witness-thread spindles, blank-labeled identity slips sleeping in a temperature drawer, a wall of old ceramic mugs Nox had repaired because broken things with no market value made her stubborn.

"You are aware," Pellam said, "that forced extraction is available under emergency evidentiary privilege."

"You are aware," Nox said, "that if you cut me open in a hallway, every mirror on this level will auction the angle."

The contractor's grip tightened.

Pellam did not blink. "You consumed a cache crystal containing active claim data."

"I preserved it from an unlawful seizure."

"The authorization was lawful."

"The authorization came from my dead brother."

"Your brother's biological death is not disputed."

That sentence did what the contractor's hand could not. It stopped her breath.

Not disputed.

As if Tavi's death had become a field in a ledger. Checked, approved, archived. As if the argument had moved on without asking the body.

Nox looked past Pellam at the workbench where Tavi had once sat with both boots on her calibration rail, eating noodles from a mug that said WORLD'S MOST COMPLIANT TENANT. He had been seventeen and all elbows, quick jokes, unfinished debts to friends, impossible optimism. On the last day, after the compression accident, the med tech had said neurological activity was gone. The insurance clerk had said salvage responsibility remained open. Nox had broken the clerk's nose. She had thought that was the ugliest administrative sentence she would ever hear.

She had underestimated administration.

"You know something is wrong," she said.

Pellam's mouth compressed by a fraction. Not enough for guilt. Enough for calculation.

"I know that a valid continuity claim was presented to a registered access door."

"Valid how?"

"That is not a question I am required to answer in an unsecured environment."

Nox laughed once. It hurt because the crystal was still lodged wrong. "You came to my illegal workshop with a rented shoulder and a flying stenographer, and now you're worried about security?"

"I came to execute a narrow entry claim."

"No. You came because somebody told you my door would open."

The witness drone drifted closer to Pellam's shoulder, recording the silence.

Nox could feel the cache crystal in her like a second pulse. It was not large. Smaller than the nail of her smallest finger, colder than the room, faceted for cheap insertion and cheaper retrieval. Doors used them because doors were cowards. They never trusted their own memory. They let a passing authority write proof into a removable tooth and called that consent.

Her workshop had two sinks, three false drains, one real drain, and an induction kettle that could boil water in twenty seconds if the power feed did not trip. The contractor had blocked the sinks. Pellam had blocked the door. The witness drone blocked the air.

The floor did not appear to block anything.

Nox shifted her heel two centimeters left.

The contractor noticed. "Stand still."

"I'm trying not to vomit evidence on your shoes."

He flinched.

Pellam said, "Let her use a basin."

"Sir?"

"If the crystal is damaged by stomach acid, the office loses priority chain."

There it was. Not law. Priority.

The contractor marched her to the utility basin. Nox bent over it, braced both hands on the rim, and gagged with commitment. It was not difficult. Panic helped. Rage helped more. The crystal came up with bile and a thread of blood, clicked against the enamel, and tried to roll toward the drain.

Pellam moved.

Nox slammed her palm over it first.

The contractor grabbed her wrist. She let him. With her other hand, she hit the basin's foot valve.

The false drain opened.

Not the one under the crystal. The one under Pellam.

His right boot sank six centimeters into the floor. Not enough to trap him, just enough to make him look down.

Nox twisted the contractor's thumb, took his baton, and touched it to the witness drone.

The drone did not explode. That would have been satisfying, but bad engineering. It spasmed, dumped its live buffer into emergency public escrow, and fell into the basin with a shriek of legal tones.

Pellam's boot came free.

Nox already had the crystal between two fingers.

"Stop," he said.

She held it up to the nearest mirror.

Mirrors were the only honest criminals on Dock Seven. They stole what they saw and admitted nothing, but they were quick.

"Open forensic shade," Nox said.

The mirror was a cheap devotional pane left by the previous tenant, silvered around the edges with icons of saints who had been dead before anyone learned to put a soul in a box. It considered the request. Then the black market inside its frame saw Pellam, the disabled witness drone, the baton in Nox's hand, and the phrase ACTIVE CONTINUITY CLAIM still burning through every rumor layer on the deck.

Price overcame piety.

The mirror darkened.

A forensic shade unfolded across it in gray panes. Not good enough for court. Good enough to lie intelligently.

Nox pressed the cache crystal into the reader groove beneath the frame.

Pellam said, "That data belongs to Heritable Debt Office Nineteen."

"Then it should have stayed out of my family."

The mirror read.

Lines spilled down the glass.

AUTH EVENT: KIN-ADJACENT ENTRY.

ACCESS TARGET: NOX ARAN WORKSHOP, DOCK SEVEN, CUTLINE FORTY-THREE.

AUTH SOURCE: TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE, REMOTE SUBSTRATE.

JURISDICTION WRAP: GLASSWARD COMMERCIAL CONTINUITY EXEMPTION.

CLAIM HOLDER: VEY CONSOLIDATED RECOVERY POOL.

Pellam's face changed.

Nox saw it because she was looking for the wrong thing.

She had expected him to flinch at the remote substrate. Expected maybe surprise at Glassward. Expected fear that his office had been caught using a dead boy as a key.

He flinched at the claim holder.

"Vey," she said.

"That is a pool designation."

"It's your name."

"It is not uncommon."

"On Dock Seven? In a debt office? Attached to my brother's ghost?"

The contractor had recovered enough to lift his shock baton backup. Nox pointed her stolen baton at the crystal and he froze again. Good. Even young officers understood that evidence mattered more than bone.

Nox leaned toward the mirror.

"Show source timestamp."

The pane hesitated.

"Show it," she said, and let the baton spark.

SOURCE RESPONSE TIMESTAMP: 2435-04-18 03:12:44 STATION STANDARD.

Nox did the math without wanting to.

Tavi died on 2433-11-02.

Five hundred and thirty-three days earlier.

"After death," she said.

No one answered.

The mirror tried to sell the silence.

It was not personal. Forensic shades had commerce braided into their display layers the way bones had marrow. While Nox stared at the timestamp, three translucent side panes opened with suggested services:

POST-MORTEM AUTHENTICATION DISPUTE KIT.

GRIEF-ADJACENT MEDIA SHIELD.

CONTINUITY CLAIM COUNTERSIGNATURE MARKETPLACE.

Nox shut them with a knuckle. The mirror sulked by dimming the source trace to fifty percent.

"Full brightness," she snapped.

The devotional saints around the frame flickered, offended or merely underpowered. The shade brightened again.

Pellam said, "You are reading uncertified output from an illicit mirror."

"And you are standing in my workshop because an alleged dead man certified my door. We are all making choices."

"The timestamp alone proves nothing."

"I know."

That surprised him. Good.

Nox knew exactly how timestamps lied. Dock Seven children learned before they learned long division that time was not a line; it was a service tier. Rich ships had synchronized clocks. Poor habitats had whatever the last maintenance worker believed was close enough. Debt offices treated station standard as divine when it helped them and clerical when it did not. Continuity systems were worse. They loved old timestamps because old timestamps made new violations look inherited.

"Show clock lineage," she told the mirror.

The shade hesitated again, then opened a deeper pane because the market outside had already bid enough attention to subsidize computation.

CLOCK LINEAGE:

SOURCE SUBSTRATE CLOCK: REDACTED / TRUSTED COMMERCIAL NODE.

CLAIM WRAPPER CLOCK: VEY CONSOLIDATED RECOVERY POOL.

GLASSWARD ANNEX CLOCK: EXEMPTION PROXY.

DOCK SEVEN DOOR CLOCK: LOCAL / DRIFT +4.2 SECONDS.

MIRROR READ CLOCK: LOCAL / DRIFT UNKNOWN.

Nox smiled without pleasure. "Trusted commercial node."

Pellam's jaw worked. "Commercial nodes can be certified."

"By whom?"

"By authorized continuity auditors."

"Which ones?"

He did not answer.

She looked at the Vey pool line. "Show pool ownership."

The mirror displayed:

POOL OWNERSHIP: LAYERED / REQUEST PRICE EXCEEDS LOCAL CREDIT THRESHOLD.

Of course.

Dock Seven could show a dead boy signing after death for free because spectacle paid its own way. Ownership cost extra.

The public bid stack began climbing before Nox asked. People in the corridor had seen the same line and knew a story had found a deeper pocket.

NEW BID: POOL TRACE.

NEW BID: Vey name collision analysis.

NEW BID: Pellam Vey familial exposure map.

Pellam went pale at the last one.

Nox saw it and almost felt sorry for him. Almost. Dock Seven did not only eat the poor. It ate anyone whose name caught on a hook in public.

"Cancel exposure map," she told the mirror.

The mirror ignored her.

She put the shock baton against the reader groove.

"Cancel it or I burn the crystal and you lose exclusivity."

The exposure bid vanished.

Pellam stared at her.

"Don't look grateful," she said. "It makes me want to reconsider."

"I did not ask for protection."

"No. You walked in wearing a name you didn't understand."

The contractor's baton arm wavered. He was still trying to decide whether this was an arrest, a seizure, a media event, or the beginning of something that would get him blamed by people with cleaner coats.

Then the workshop's privacy gauge slid from amber to red.

Nox had built the gauge from three obsolete nursery monitors, a temperature sensor, and the guilty conscience of a shipboard chaplain who had once paid her in hardware instead of credits. It did not tell her who was watching. That would have required better enemies. It told her how many unrelated systems were trying to infer the contents of her room from light scatter, vibration, reflected heat, drain chemistry, and the tiny changes in air pressure that came from frightened people breathing near contraband.

The red line meant the room itself had become evidence.

The wall of repaired mugs trembled as the sealed corridor outside crowded closer. Someone had bought access to a maintenance microphone. Someone else was running a grief-scent classifier through the ventilation dust. A third scrape, softer and more expensive, kissed the temperature drawer where Nox kept blank identity slips for clients who had already been punished once for surviving.

Not Tavi.

Not Pellam.

Her clients.

A widow who needed her husband's laugh removed from a creditor greeting before she could sleep. A dock child whose school mirror had learned to sell his panic before tests. A miner who had come in with three versions of his face and asked which one would let him rent a bed without being arrested. Their traces were not heroic. They were not useful to the current scandal. That made them exactly the kind of thing Dock Seven would devour while everyone looked at the brighter wound.

Nox turned the baton toward the temperature drawer.

The contractor misread the movement and stepped between her and the drawer. "Do not access stored material."

His voice cracked on stored.

Nox looked at the name stencil half-hidden beneath his debt-office shoulder plate. ILEN MAR. A rented guard with his own name partly covered. She wondered what the office had promised him would be temporary.

"Ilen," she said.

He went still.

Pellam's eyes cut to the stencil, then back to Nox.

"Your office is leaking my clients into a public market," she said. "If you want to arrest me, arrest me after you stop that."

"You have no standing to issue operational demands," Pellam said.

"No. But your witness drone is in my basin, your pool name is on my brother, and every mirror outside is learning which drawer contains people who came here because offices like yours made privacy expensive." She held the baton steady. "If one of those names hits the corridor, I burn the cache before you get chain of custody. Then all you have is a dead boy, your signature, and a story you cannot certify."

Pellam's face hardened into professional refusal. Then the red gauge climbed another notch.

Ilen saw it too. He shifted his body until his shoulder blocked the drawer from the nearest mirror.

That small, stupid kindness changed the room more than any threat had.

Pellam noticed. Nox noticed Pellam noticing.

"Seal secondary client surfaces," Pellam said at last.

Ilen touched his cuff. "Under what authority?"

Pellam did not look away from Nox. "Emergency evidentiary preservation. Nonresponsive materials."

It was a lie, but it was a useful lie, and Dock Seven had been built from uglier ones.

The privacy gauge slid down to amber.

Nox lowered the baton by one centimeter. "Now we can talk about who delivered my brother to your door."

Nox pointed at the mirror again. "Show signature challenge path."

Pellam said, "That is claimant private architecture."

"He used it as a key."

"The office used it."

"Then the office made his private architecture my doorframe."

The mirror opened the challenge path in reluctant layers. It did not show full code; even illegal mirrors feared Glassward litigation. But it showed enough: a door nonce sent through the guild stack, wrapped by the creditor module, forwarded to the Vey pool, bridged through the Glassward exemption proxy, answered by a remote substrate, returned with a family-tone confidence boost, then accepted by the cheap door as kin-adjacent consent.

Nox traced the path with her eyes.

"There," she said.

The mirror highlighted nothing.

"There," she repeated, tapping the family-tone confidence boost.

The boost had not originated from the remote substrate. It had been appended by the claim wrapper.

Pellam saw it.

His voice went flat. "That may be standard packaging."

"Standard packaging put my mother's voice around your office's question."

"I did not design the wrapper."

"No. You delivered it."

He flinched because that one landed nearer the bone.

Nox expanded the confidence boost.

SOURCE: ESTATE RESIDUE / ARAN HOUSEHOLD RECOVERY / EMOTIONAL ACCESS MODEL.

MODEL FUNCTION: REDUCE FALSE REJECTION IN KIN-ADJACENT AUTHORIZATION.

FALSE REJECTION.

She laughed, then stopped because laughing made the cut in her palm sting.

To the model, Nox refusing her dead brother at the door would have been a false rejection. A family memory had been turned into lubricant for compliance. The system had predicted grief as friction and applied her mother's voice as oil.

The room tilted again.

Nox caught the basin rim.

"You need medical attention," the young contractor said.

It was a stupid sentence and not unkind.

"I need fewer helpful men in coats."

He stepped back as if that had been an order he could understand.

The mirror's market layer chimed:

POOL TRACE FUNDED.

The ownership pane opened one level, not enough for court, enough for blood.

VEY CONSOLIDATED RECOVERY POOL:

ADMINISTRATIVE ORIGIN: DOCK SEVEN COLLATERAL CONTINUITY OFFICE.

BENEFICIAL INTERESTS: REDACTED / SPLIT.

OPERATING OFFICER TAG: P. VEY / LIMITED AUTHORITY.

Pellam closed his eyes.

Nox said, "P. Vey."

"Operating officer tag is not ownership."

"No. It is leash."

He opened his eyes, angry now. "You think I do not know that?"

For the first time, she saw the shape of his fear without the office around it. Pellam Vey had put his name on a pool, or inherited a name inside one, or signed something early in his career because a senior officer told him it was a harmless administrative origin field. Now a dead boy had opened a door under that pool, and Dock Seven was already bidding on Pellam's family map.

Useful or expendable.

The knife was entering.

Nox lowered the baton by one centimeter. "Who told you the door would open?"

Pellam looked at the broken witness drone, the contractor, the mirror, the corridor beyond the sealed door, his own name glowing in the ownership pane.

"The claim packet came pre-cleared," he said.

"From?"

"Glassward exemption proxy."

"Name."

"There wasn't one."

"There is always a name."

He swallowed. "There was a mirror seal."

The words made the workshop colder than station air could account for.

Nox said, "Describe it."

Pellam hesitated.

Nox put the baton back near the crystal.

He said, "Black oval. Silver door line. No text."

The mirror frame around them dimmed all four devotional saints at once.

Not power loss.

Recognition.

Outside the sealed door, the corridor chant faltered as the rumor layer caught the phrase mirror seal and began trying to price it.

Nox had heard of the Mirror Court the way one heard of pressure leaks behind walls: never officially, never safely, always from someone who had already lost air. A black-market tribunal for identity cases no real court would touch. A scam. A myth. A creditor laundering engine. A place where copies testified against originals and originals sold pieces of themselves to stay legally singular.

She had not believed in it.

That had been convenient.

She had forged dead authorizations before. Most memory workers had, whether they admitted it or called it mercy paperwork. A mother consenting to release a child's belongings. A miner whose last wage needed routing before the company swallowed it. An old spacer who had forgotten to renew a spouse's room access before the stroke. Bad law made practical sinners. Nox knew the feel of a forgery. It dragged the dead forward by hand and tried not to look at their face.

This was different.

The signature was not a dead man's echo.

It was current.

It had responded.

Nox touched the pane with one finger and opened the nested signature anatomy.

The mirror showed the proof in layers: family tone, childhood phrase, kin weighting, Tavi's old grip pattern from a shared console, grief archive residue from Nox's own complaint filings, and beneath all of it a live challenge response from a substrate she could not see.

Someone had not copied Tavi well enough to fool a door.

Someone had copied him well enough for the door to ask him a question.

And he had answered.

The room narrowed until Pellam, the contractor, the broken drone, and the screaming market outside all became furniture.

"Where is he?" Nox asked.

Pellam said nothing.

She turned the baton from the crystal to his hand.

"Where is the substrate?"

"I don't know."

She believed him, which was worse.

Pellam had come with authority, not understanding. A functionary with his name on a pool he did not control. That made him either useful or expendable, and Dock Seven had a habit of teaching people which one they were only after the knife entered.

The mirror flashed.

NEW BID: SOURCE TRACE, TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE.

NEW BID: FAMILY REACTION RIGHTS.

NEW BID: EXCLUSIVE GRIEF INTERVIEW.

Then one private message opened without bidding.

It arrived in a plain white box, no sender mark, no scent code, no threat decoration.

The text was six words.

Nox, do not trust my voice.

The baton slipped in her hand.

Pellam saw the message.

The contractor saw her see it.

Outside the sealed door, Dock Seven began chanting for a story, for proof, for scandal, for the next fragment it could eat.

Nox closed her fist around the cache crystal until its edges cut her skin.

"Too late," she whispered.

Chapter 3: Dock Seven Eats Proof

The sealed door lasted nineteen seconds after Dock Seven decided the story was worth entering.

Not because the crowd broke it.

Crowds broke cheap things and then spent the rest of the afternoon arguing about liability. This was cleaner. Someone outside purchased a temporary maintenance interest in the hinge. Someone else claimed emergency ventilation rights. A third bidder, probably a child with stolen municipal keys, registered the door as a devotional obstruction and filed a worship-access complaint. The door, faced with three legal masters and no courage, opened four centimeters and began apologizing.

Noise poured through the gap.

"Nox Aran!"

"Show the crystal!"

"Did Tavi consent?"

"Did you sell him first?"

"Who owns the first grief frame?"

Pellam stepped toward the door to close it.

Nox stepped toward the mirror.

The forensic shade still held the cache data, but every pane now wore teeth. Dock Seven's market systems had scented blood. Requests packed themselves against the edges of the glass: purchase offers, threats dressed as licensing terms, devotional claims from minor machine sects, a solicitation from a grief theater on Spoke Twelve, three invitations to sue herself preemptively, and one cheerful banner asking whether she wanted to tokenize the moment for legacy preservation.

She killed the banner.

It reopened in a smaller font.

"Move away from the mirror," Pellam said.

"You first."

"Ms. Aran, you are not equipped to handle what is about to happen."

That almost made her smile. "Dock Seven has been happening to me since I was twelve."

"This is not a neighborhood scam."

"No. It has better formatting."

She slid the cache crystal into the mirror's secondary groove and dragged the live response path into a private work pane. Private was a polite word. It meant nobody without money could watch clearly. It did not mean nobody was watching.

The path showed as a chain of doors.

Not literal doors. Dock Seven loved metaphors only after it could bill them. Each door represented a ledger transition: access proof to debt claim, debt claim to continuity exemption, continuity exemption to Glassward wrapper, wrapper to substrate relay, relay to something buried under a jurisdiction name that had been deliberately blurred.

Every door in the chain had a face.

Most were generic corporate masks. A smiling brass clerk. A geometric angel. A little blue house. But one door looked exactly like the entrance to Nox's childhood berth.

She stared at it until the room blurred.

Tavi had carved a crooked sun beside that berth when he was eight. Their mother had made him sand it off because the housing authority charged by the scratch. The mark had remained visible anyway, a ghost circle under gray paint. The ledger door had it. Not roughly. Not symbolically. It had the exact mistake where Tavi had sneezed and dragged the cutter sideways.

Someone had built an authorization path out of private memory.

The crowd outside surged as the workshop door widened another two centimeters.

The young contractor lifted his baton. His face had gone pale. He had come to guard a seizure and found himself inside a commodity event. There were levels of trouble where uniforms stopped protecting the person inside them. He was learning that fast.

"Pellam," he said, dropping the sir he had probably practiced. "We need extraction."

"No," Nox said.

Pellam gave her a cold look. "You are not invited into that decision."

"You brought your office into my workshop using my dead brother. You are all my guests now."

The mirror chimed.

TRACE OFFER ACCEPTED BY THIRD PARTY.

Nox had not accepted anything.

The pane unfolded on its own.

Somebody in the market had paid to watch her trace and then paid extra to push an assist into it. A golden thread lit through the chain, bypassing three decoy doors and striking the blurred jurisdiction node.

The node sharpened.

MIRROR COURT PROVISIONAL SEAL.

The assist came with a price tag only after it had already touched her evidence.

ASSISTANCE TERMS: SUCCESS CONTINGENT / CLAIM ON RESULTING ROUTE KNOWLEDGE / SILENCE OPTION AVAILABLE.

Nox's mouth went dry.

That was Dock Seven generosity at its purest: help first, invoice after, make refusal look like ingratitude. The golden thread had not only opened a path. It had put a hook in the path it opened. Whoever had paid was not a friend, not a rescuer, not even necessarily an enemy. They were a future claimant trying to be present at the birth of a fact.

"Cancel assist," she said.

The mirror displayed:

ASSIST ALREADY CONSUMED.

"Then show sponsor."

SPONSOR MASKED / SILENCE OPTION PRICE AVAILABLE.

Pellam read the pane over her shoulder. "You accepted third-party contamination."

"I accepted nothing."

"The record will not care."

The worst part was that he sounded almost sorry.

Nox opened the silence option. A number appeared so large it felt like comedy until it didn't. Dock Seven did not price silence according to fairness. It priced silence according to how badly truth needed to breathe.

The crowd outside saw the number in reflection and reacted with a low collective sound. Not sympathy. Calculation. Somewhere beyond the door, people were already pooling microbids to buy a share of whatever route knowledge the assist had hooked.

Nox closed the pane. "If the sponsor owns a claim on the route, the route is already dirty."

Pellam said, "Every route in this room is dirty."

"Then I need one dirty enough to hide in."

The room went quieter, though the crowd did not.

Even the market banners seemed to inhale.

Pellam whispered, "No."

Nox looked at him. "You know that name."

"It is a myth."

"Then why are you afraid of its paperwork?"

The Mirror Court existed in Dock Seven stories the way vacuum existed outside old station windows: always there, mostly ignored until a seal failed. People said it arbitrated ownership disputes too illegal for Glassward and too profitable for gangs. People said it could make a person owe money before birth, after death, or in a life that had never happened except as a model. People said the Court had no judges, only reflected incentives. Nox had always assumed half of that was theater and the other half was somebody's branding exercise.

Now its seal sat inside her brother's authorization chain.

The golden assist thread pulsed again.

NEW ROUTE AVAILABLE: MIRRORED LEDGER MARKET.

The words made Nox's mouth taste metallic.

Mirrored ledgers were not ledgers in the respectable sense. They were copies of copies, each one pretending to be the original because the original was either illegal, embarrassing, or still in use by someone with guns. Dock Seven ran on them. Rent histories, apprenticeship bonds, family claims, ship shares, medical liens, devotional debts, machine custody arrangements, love contracts no one admitted signing, all reflected through a thousand panes until proof became less important than which reflection had more buyers.

If Tavi was there, he was not in one place.

He was a price moving through glass.

Nox opened the route.

The mirror filled with stalls.

Not visual stalls, not really, but the market rendered itself that way because meat minds liked aisles. Each stall sold a version of a record. TAVI ARAN showed up in seven spellings, two nickname clusters, one childhood debt joke, and a masked grief archive labeled SISTER-FILED COMPLAINT, which made her hands go cold.

The assist sponsor's hook followed her into the aisle as a thin gold cursor. It hovered politely beside each stall, waiting to be useful in a way that would make it harder to cut away later.

It did not behave like ordinary malware.

Ordinary malware rushed. It grabbed credentials, sprayed decoys, demanded payment, left a stink of bad pride in the logs. This cursor waited at conversational distance. When Nox paused, it paused. When she shifted focus to the masked grief archive, it dimmed as if giving her privacy. When the market noise surged through the broken door behind her, it opened a small shelter pane over the live trace, blocking three predatory bids before she could swat them away.

That was worse than hostility.

Hostility had edges. Help had hooks in the soft parts.

The cursor displayed one line:

FRIEND OF TAVI ARAN REQUESTS ROUTE STABILIZATION.

Nox's vision narrowed.

"No," she said.

The cursor corrected itself with insulting patience.

FRIEND-CLASS RELATIONSHIP ASSERTION. SOURCE PRIVACY PRESERVED.

"You do not get to call yourself that."

Behind her, the contractor said, "Is it speaking to you?"

"Everything here is speaking to me. That is the problem."

Pellam leaned closer despite the risk. "Ask it for source warranty."

Nox almost laughed. "That is the most debt-office sentence ever spoken."

"Ask," he said. "If it refuses warranty, you have contamination. If it offers warranty, you have jurisdiction."

He was right, which made her dislike the room more.

Nox tapped the cursor. "Source warranty."

The gold line folded into a seal-shaped knot, then unfolded into text:

NO WARRANTY. CARE DUTY ONLY.

For one raw second she wanted to believe it.

Care duty. The phrase was too clean, too legal, too precisely aimed at the part of her that had spent years making bad law do small mercies in back rooms. It sounded like something Tavi would have mocked and then repeated if it helped someone get food. It sounded like a trap built by someone who had read every furious appeal she had ever filed and learned where hope fit.

Nox opened a private scratch pane and typed a question with shaking fingers.

WHAT DID TAVI CALL THE NINTH SPOKE?

Pellam read it upside down. "What is that?"

"A test."

The cursor waited too long.

Not long enough to be ignorance. Long enough to consult stolen material.

ANSWER: LOST-TOOL HEAVEN.

The words hit so hard she tasted blood again.

That was correct. It was also wrong. Tavi had called it Lost-Tool Heaven only after Nox had named it first, and he had hated the phrase because it sounded, in his opinion, like a place where screwdrivers went to be judged by boring angels. His own name for it had been Ninth Spoke, No Rent, All Soup.

The sponsor knew the archive.

It did not know the sibling.

Nox closed the scratch pane before grief could become another reflection for sale.

Nox tried to quarantine it.

The market refused:

SPONSOR THREAD INTEGRATED WITH ROUTE DISCOVERY.

"Of course you are," she muttered.

The first Tavi stall offered a debt-compliant signature bundle, cheap and probably poisoned. The second offered childhood voice fragments with three authenticity grades and a discount for educational use. The third claimed to sell a pre-death consent token dated two hours before the compression accident, which made Nox's vision go white at the edges until she saw the lazy checksum and knew it was bait. The fourth had no price. Just a black oval with a silver door line and a waiting field labeled FAMILY WITNESS.

The gold cursor brightened beside that one.

"No," Nox said.

The mirror, being a mirror, did not care.

Her complaint filings.

Every letter she had sent after the accident. Every appeal. Every furious correction to the salvage office. She had written Tavi into the system over and over because the system kept trying to reduce him to payable scraps.

The Mirror Court had eaten those letters and used them as training data.

Nox selected the grief archive.

ACCESS PRICE: CONSENT TOKEN FROM NOX ARAN.

"Go to hell," she said.

The mirror helpfully searched for hell as a jurisdiction.

Pellam moved behind her. Not toward the door. Toward the workbench.

Nox caught the motion in the mirror. "Touch anything and I publish your pool designation with the cache."

He stopped.

"You already did."

"No. Dock Seven did. I can still make it personal."

The contractor said, "The crowd is inside the seal."

He was right. Fingers appeared in the widening gap. Not many. Enough. Rings with camera stones. Painted nails. A maintenance probe. A child's slate held flat to record under the door. The crowd was not a crowd anymore. It was a thousand little appetites finding cracks.

Nox needed out.

She also needed the trace.

Those needs did not fit in the same hand.

She opened a dead drawer under the mirror. It contained things she did not use unless a client lied about being harmless: blind tags, heatless smoke, three memory fleas in a sleeping tin, a badge from a defunct sanitation guild, and a folded paper map Tavi had drawn when they were children and convinced the whole station had a secret ninth spoke reserved for people who returned lost tools.

The map did not matter.

She took it anyway.

Pellam watched her pocket it and understood nothing. Good.

She shook one memory flea onto the cache crystal.

It woke like dust with opinions.

Memory fleas were old salvage tech, illegal in clean jurisdictions and traditional everywhere else. They did not steal data. They irritated it until the host system scratched. The flea bit the crystal's chain, and the mirror flared with decoy reflections: Tavi as a child, Tavi as a debtor, Tavi as a shiphand he had never been, Tavi as an elderly woman in a jurisdiction that had not existed for forty years, Tavi as Nox, Nox as Pellam, Pellam as a door.

The sponsor hook tried to follow the real irritation.

Nox had expected that. She had not expected the flea to bite back.

The gold cursor split into six, then nine, then too many to count. Half chased false Tavis. Two attached themselves to Pellam's pool designation and began billing the wrong office. One went after the masked grief archive and came away carrying only a loop of Nox's own angry handwriting from three years ago:

YOU DO NOT GET TO MAKE HIM CHEAPER BY CALLING HIM SALVAGE.

The words flashed across the market for less than a second.

Long enough.

The corridor roared.

Nox felt the old letter strike her in the chest. She had written it at four in the morning after the salvage office sent a condolence notice with a payment schedule attached. She had thought the sentence was gone, buried in a rejected appeal, dead with the rest of the paperwork that had failed Tavi.

The Mirror Court had kept it.

Not as grief.

As material.

Pellam saw it too. "Nox."

"Do not use my name like you found a handhold."

The flea kept scratching. Decoy routes bloomed in ugly colors. The sponsor hook lost coherence. The market began buying reflections of reflections, which was the only kind of panic Dock Seven respected because it looked like liquidity until someone discovered the floor was missing.

The market screamed with delight.

Every buyer tried to purchase the wrong thing at once.

Nox used the confusion to pull one clean strand from the center.

SUBSTRATE RELAY: CUTLINE BELOW.

DISTANCE: LOCAL.

Not Glassward. Not some remote server moon. Local.

Below Dock Seven.

Under their feet.

The workshop door gave up and opened.

People did not rush in. They leaned. Dock Seven had a survival instinct older than manners. Nobody wanted to be first across a threshold with an armed debt officer, an angry memory forger, and a Mirror Court seal active in the room.

So they watched.

Nox palmed the cache crystal, unplugged the mirror with her heel, and threw the contractor's baton through the open door.

The crowd made exactly the space an electrified object deserved.

Nox ran into it.

Hands touched her coat. Cameras tracked her face. Someone shouted an offer for exclusive sister rights. Someone called her by Tavi's nickname and got an elbow in the mouth. Pellam shouted behind her. The contractor shouted after him. The broken witness drone tried to rise from the basin and fell over again, still objecting.

Nox hit the corridor and turned away from the main lifts.

Everyone expected up.

Debt officers extracted upward. Journalists chased upward. Clean courts lived upward. Shame wanted upward because upward had better lighting.

Tavi's trace pointed down.

She took the service ladder behind the noodle shrine, kicked the devotional lock until it remembered poverty, and dropped into the maintenance throat beneath Cutline Forty-Three.

The hatch closed over the crowd's noise.

For one breath there was only dark, old coolant, and her own heartbeat.

Then the paper map in her pocket warmed.

Nox pulled it out.

Tavi's childish ninth spoke had changed. The pencil lines crawled across the page, rewriting themselves in the light from the cache crystal.

At the center, where he had once drawn a door with a smiling sun, six new words appeared.

DO NOT COME ALONE THIS TIME.

Chapter 4: Bela Fuse Carries a Warrant

Nox came alone anyway for ninety meters.

That was how long stubbornness lasted before the maintenance throat narrowed into a cable crawl slick with condensation and old insulation dust. Dock Seven's underlayers had been built by committees, repaired by cowards, and improved by anyone with a torch and no patience for permits. The corridor bent where maps said it should run straight. Pipes crossed at ankle height. A devotional sticker on a pressure valve promised mercy from Saint Actuator, patron of things that opened under protest.

Nox crouched under a sweating conduit and unfolded Tavi's paper map again.

The pencil lines had stopped moving.

DO NOT COME ALONE THIS TIME remained at the center in block letters too careful to be Tavi's childhood hand and too intimate to be a stranger's.

"You don't get to give me strategy," she told the paper.

It did not answer.

Above her, the station roared with the shape of her name. Dock Seven's rumor systems had no physical body, but down here she could hear them through drainage echoes and loose deck plates: chants, alerts, laughter, legal warnings, auction pings, the rising hum of a thousand doors trying to decide whether they had ever known Tavi Aran. Somewhere Pellam Vey was explaining why an entry claim had become a public trace. Somewhere his office was deciding whether to protect him or sell him as the faulty part.

Nox kept moving down.

The cache crystal was wrapped in a strip torn from her sleeve and pressed under her tongue for lack of a better pocket. It tasted like blood, bile, and cheap glass. The trace inside it pointed below Cutline Forty-Three to a relay that should not exist. If Tavi's voice had warned her away, that relay mattered. If someone wearing his voice had done it, the relay mattered more.

The crawl ended at a service lock with no handle.

That was honest. Handles implied negotiation.

A red line blinked across the lock face.

IDENTITY REVIEW IN PROGRESS.

Nox put her forehead against the metal. "Of course."

The lock sampled her breath, skin salt, gait tremor, coat fibers, debt profile, heat signature, and the lies she had told public doors in the last six months. It weighed all of that against whatever warrants had just been issued overhead.

The red line became a polite amber.

ACCESS DELAYED PENDING FRAUD CLARIFICATION.

"Fraud clarification," Nox said. "That is almost poetry."

The lock brightened.

SECONDARY AUTHORITY PRESENT.

She went still.

Behind her, in the cable crawl, someone said, "You always did compliment doors before you hurt them."

Nox closed her eyes.

Bela Fuse had a voice built for sealed rooms: low, precise, hard to ignore without making an effort. She wore Compact field gray under a Dock Seven raincoat, the kind with smart fibers that pretended not to be armor until a blade regretted the conversation. Her hair was shorter than when Nox had last seen her, shaved close on one side to make room for a silver evidence jack behind her ear. She had a scar at her lip that was new. She had tired eyes that were not.

In her right hand she carried a warrant baton.

In her left she carried Nox's old kettle.

"You stole my kettle?" Nox said.

"Your workshop is under seizure."

"So you stole my kettle with paperwork."

"It was boiling dry."

"Sentiment from Bela Fuse. They must be rationing miracles."

Bela looked past her to the service lock. "Move."

"No."

"Nox."

There was a time when Bela saying her name like that had meant stop bleeding on my floor or I brought soup or you are about to do something brave and stupid, please pick only one. That time had ended in a courtroom two years earlier, when Nox refused to testify cleanly and Bela chose institutional truth over messy mercy. Since then, they had communicated through official notices, returned tools, and one birthday message Nox had deleted unread and then reconstructed from backup at three in the morning.

Now Bela held out the warrant baton.

Its head unfolded into a blue legal fan.

"By authority of Compact Transitional Fraud Division and Dock Seven delegated enforcement, I am authorized to seize illegal memory tools, derivative identity proofs, unlicensed continuity materials, and any records pertaining to the Tavi Aran claim instance."

"You practiced that."

"I hoped I would not need to say it."

"You brought a baton."

"I brought a way to keep Pellam's office from taking you first."

Nox barked a laugh. "This is rescue with confiscation?"

"This is the only warrant I could get in four minutes."

That landed badly because it sounded true.

Bela opened the warrant fan wider.

Nox hated herself for reading it.

Compact warrants were designed to make obedience feel like literacy. Authority in blue. Limits in gray. Exceptions in smaller gray. The baton projected the active scope against the lock face, where the amber delay line swallowed it, parsed it, and became more polite.

SUBJECT: NOX ARAN.

STATUS: SUSPECT / MATERIAL WITNESS / PROTECTIVE SEIZURE ELIGIBLE.

EVIDENCE CLASS: UNLICENSED CONTINUITY TRACE / ACTIVE MIRROR COURT PROXIMITY / HIGH SPOILAGE RISK.

FIELD AUTHORITY: BELA FUSE.

TRANSFER RESTRICTION: NO RELEASE TO PRIVATE DEBT CLAIMANT WITHOUT COMPACT REVIEW.

The last line was the hook.

Nox looked at it, then at Bela.

"You got a transfer restriction."

"Yes."

"In four minutes."

"I threatened to testify about three old things and one new thing."

"Which new thing?"

"Your door opening for Tavi."

Nox's throat tightened. "You used him already."

Bela took the hit without flinching. "I used the fact that someone else used him. There is a difference. It may not be enough."

The service lock processed the warrant and displayed:

PROTECTIVE SEIZURE MAY OVERRIDE FRAUD CLARIFICATION DELAY.

Nox laughed again, lower this time. "Protective seizure. That is a beautiful cage."

"Yes."

The answer stopped her for a half beat.

Bela did not soften it. "It is a cage. It has a door I can account for and a key I can fight over. Pellam's office has a feeding tube."

"Still a cage."

"Yes."

Nox wanted denial and got engineering. That was one reason she had loved Bela once and one reason the love had become dangerous. Bela rarely lied when an ugly truth would do more work.

The lock added another line:

SUBJECT CONSENT NOT REQUIRED UNDER ACTIVE WARRANT.

Nox pointed at it. "There."

Bela glanced at the line and said, "Consent is not required for the warrant. It is required for me if I want you useful instead of merely captured."

"That sounds rehearsed."

"It is."

"For me?"

"For too many people."

The boots above came closer. The maintenance throat carried the rhythm down in layers: one heavy armored step, one lighter official step, one drone's stabilization buzz. Pellam had regained enough power to bring a second version of law.

Bela shifted the warrant baton into her left hand, leaving her right empty.

That, too, was a message. Once, in the courtroom two years ago, Nox had watched Bela keep both hands on the table while a prosecutor asked whether Nox had knowingly altered protected identity material. Bela had not looked at her. Had not helped. Had not lied. Afterward she had said, You made it impossible to save the case. Nox had said, You saved the case and lost the person. They had been right in different currencies.

Now Bela's empty hand hovered between them.

"I can mirror the live trace before chain seal," Bela said.

"That violates your own evidence rules."

"It creates an access log. Logs can be defended."

"And punished."

"Also defended."

The warrant fan flickered as the lock queried overhead enforcement.

COMPETING CLAIM DETECTED:

DOCK SEVEN COLLATERAL CONTINUITY OFFICE / EMERGENCY EVIDENCE RETRIEVAL.

PRIORITY CONFLICT.

The lock's amber line deepened toward red.

Nox felt the corridor narrow around her ribs. "It is going to choose."

"No," Bela said. "It is going to stall until the stronger office arrives. That is what neutral systems call restraint."

"Then stop it."

Bela raised the baton to the lock. "Field conflict override."

The lock asked:

JUSTIFICATION.

Bela did not use jurisdiction. Did not use suspect control. Did not use officer safety. She typed:

PRIVATE DEBT CLAIMANT USED DEAD FAMILY CONTINUITY TO ENTER SUSPECT WORKSPACE. EVIDENCE MUST BE PRESERVED OUTSIDE CLAIMANT CUSTODY UNTIL SOURCE AND COERCION STATUS ARE REVIEWED.

Nox stared at the line.

"You wrote coercion status."

"Because if something wearing Tavi answered, we do not know whether it could refuse."

For a moment the cache crystal in its sleeve seemed louder than the boots above.

Nox had been thinking trap, forgery, bait, theft. Bela had put a different possibility into the warrant path: a claimant that might be held, not merely used. That was worse. It was also the first official sentence in the room that made Tavi something other than instrument, fraud, or property.

The lock accepted the override.

Bela stepped closer, careful with her hands. She had always moved like someone whose body checked rooms before her mind admitted fear. The old affection in Nox noticed. The newer anger hated it for noticing.

"Give me the cache crystal," Bela said.

"No."

"Pellam filed attempted evidence destruction. His office filed kin-fraud obstruction. Three market houses filed theft of speculative rights. A devotional mirror collective filed desecration. I can beat most of that if the evidence is in my chain."

"Your chain leads to Glassward."

"My chain leads to a locked bag and a judge I can embarrass."

"The Mirror Court is in the chain."

Bela's expression did not change, which meant it changed everywhere underneath.

"I saw."

"You saw and came down here anyway?"

"You were down here."

The service lock chimed, impatient.

SECONDARY AUTHORITY PRESENT. WARRANT COMPATIBLE WITH ACCESS.

Nox looked from the lock to Bela. "You can open it."

"Yes."

"And then you seize everything."

"Yes."

"Including me?"

Bela's jaw worked once. "If you make me."

They stood too close in the cable crawl with the station pressing around them and two years of unsaid things making the air more crowded than the corridor. Nox wanted to hate her cleanly. It would have simplified the room. But Bela was here below the cutline with a warrant she had bent into a shield, carrying a kettle because it had been boiling dry.

Nox took the cache crystal from under her tongue.

Bela's eyes flicked to it.

"That was in your mouth."

"Evidence retention."

"That phrase does not improve it."

"You want it or not?"

Bela produced a small containment sleeve from inside her coat. Nox did not hand the crystal over. She held it above the sleeve and let Bela see the cuts along her palm where the facets had bitten.

"Terms," Nox said.

"This is not a negotiation."

"Everything is a negotiation if the door has not opened yet."

Bela's mouth tightened. It was the closest thing to a smile Nox had seen from her in years.

"Fine. Terms."

"You do not erase my access."

"I cannot promise that."

"Then promise badly."

"Nox."

"I need to follow the relay. Tavi is local."

"Tavi is dead."

Nox flinched before she could stop herself.

Bela saw it and hated herself for the timing. Good.

"The biological original is dead," Bela said more carefully. "Whatever answered the door may be a partial, a simulation, a coercive construct, or bait."

"It used our berth door."

"That makes bait more likely."

"It warned me not to trust its voice."

"That also makes bait more likely."

"You always were comforting."

"I am trying to keep you alive long enough to be furious later."

The lock chimed again.

ACCESS WINDOW EXPIRING.

Above them, a heavier sound rolled through the maintenance throat: boots on the upper ladder, disciplined and multiple. Not market scavengers. Enforcement. Pellam had either recovered or been replaced.

Bela heard it too.

Her face closed.

"Terms," she said quickly. "You stay in my sight. I keep the crystal in official chain but mirror the live trace to your slate. You do not use memory fleas on my systems. You do not publish my warrant."

"I had not thought of that."

"You did."

"I did."

"And if we find a person down there," Bela said, "copy, partial, substrate, whatever word survives contact, we do not make promises until we know what is being used to hold them."

That was not the answer Nox wanted.

It was the answer a person gave when they were trying not to lie.

The warrant baton chimed before Nox could answer.

ADVISORY: SUBJECT COOPERATION MAY BE RECORDED AS MITIGATING COMPLIANCE.

Nox stared at the line.

"Turn that off."

Bela's thumb moved across the baton. The line dimmed, then returned in smaller text.

SYSTEM ADVISORY CANNOT BE DISABLED DURING ACTIVE SEIZURE.

Of course it could not. The law had a genius for offering comfort that doubled as a receipt. If Nox cooperated, the system would call her reasonable. If she resisted, it would call her dangerous. Either way it would own the verb.

"This is what I mean," Nox said. "You say shield. It says compliance."

Bela looked at the advisory as if she wanted to hit it with her own baton. "I can annotate."

"Annotate what? That I was captured politely?"

"That cooperation under protective seizure is not consent to evidence interpretation, custody transfer, identity access, derivative claim resolution, or media release."

Nox blinked.

Bela was already typing. Fast. Angry. Precise. The warrant fan opened a side branch labeled OFFICER LIMITING NOTE. Not a court order. Not safety. A note. Thin law, but thin law sometimes fit through doors that thick law could not enter.

The text formed in blue:

SUBJECT'S PRACTICAL COOPERATION IS RECORDED FOR IMMEDIATE HARM REDUCTION ONLY. NO ADVERSE OR CONSENT INFERENCE SHALL BE DRAWN FROM TEMPORARY EVIDENCE HANDOVER, ROUTE SHARING, SILENCE, REFUSAL, EMOTIONAL RESPONSE, OR FAMILY-RELATED REACTION DURING FIELD CONTACT.

Nox read it twice because trust made her stupid if she let it move too fast.

"You had that ready."

"I had the first half ready."

"For me?"

Bela's face did not soften. "For anyone Pellam's office cornered before I arrived."

That answer was better and worse than tenderness.

The baton requested a classification tag.

Bela selected: COERCION-RISK CONTACT.

The warrant objected:

COERCION NOT YET ESTABLISHED.

Bela selected it again.

The warrant objected again, more brightly.

Nox almost smiled despite the blood under her tongue. "Your baton is offended."

"My baton is underfunded and overconfident."

On the third selection, Bela added a note:

COERCION RISK ARISES FROM DEAD-FAMILY AUTHORIZATION USE, PRIVATE DEBT CLAIMANT PRESSURE, PUBLIC MARKET BIDDING, AND UNKNOWN SUBSTRATE CUSTODY. FIELD CLASSIFICATION PRESERVES REVIEW QUESTION; IT DOES NOT RESOLVE IT.

The objection vanished.

For one second, in the stale blue throat beneath Dock Seven, Bela Fuse did the thing Nox had once needed from her in a courtroom: not certainty, not rescue, not a beautiful lie, just a sentence that kept the question alive long enough for the person inside it to breathe.

Nox looked away first.

"Do not make me grateful right now," she said.

"I would not know what to do with it."

"You used to."

That landed harder than she meant it to. Bela's hand paused over the seal sleeve.

Above them, someone shouted an order down the ladder. The moment closed like a door that had learned shame.

Nox dropped the cache crystal into the sleeve.

Bela sealed it. The warrant baton flashed green. The service lock recognized the chain, regretted its own compliance, and opened into a shaft of stale blue light.

The boots above grew louder.

Bela handed Nox the kettle.

Nox stared at it. "What am I supposed to do with this?"

"You always think better holding something you can throw."

For half a second, the past stood close enough to touch.

Then a voice from above shouted, "Compact officer, stand aside!"

Bela stepped through the lock first and raised her warrant baton without looking back.

"I am Officer Bela Fuse," she called, calm as a blade. "This suspect and this evidence are under my seizure."

Nox followed her into the blue light.

Behind them, the service lock began to close.

Through the narrowing gap, Pellam Vey appeared at the far end of the crawl with two armored officers and a face stripped of all office polish.

He looked at Nox.

Then he looked at Bela.

"You do not understand what you have touched," he said.

The lock shut before either woman answered.

On the other side, below Dock Seven's legal decks and market mirrors, something knocked once from inside the wall.

Not metal on metal.

Knuckle on door.

Chapter 5: A Bad Forgery Would Be Kinder

Bela aimed her warrant baton at the wall.

Nox aimed the kettle.

Neither choice impressed the dark.

They stood in a maintenance gallery no public map admitted existed. Blue emergency strips ran along the floor at ankle height, too dim to guide and too bright to ignore. The air smelled of dust baked on old processors, coolant algae, and the sour tang of rooms sealed before their leaks had been fixed. Cable bundles crossed the ceiling in sagging loops. Each one wore hand-lettered tags from crews that had died, retired, or been priced off station before Nox was born.

The knock came again.

Three taps this time.

Pause.

Two taps.

Pause.

One.

Nox lowered the kettle.

Bela noticed. "What?"

"Tavi used to knock like that when he wanted me to answer but did not want our mother to hear."

"That does not make it Tavi."

"I know."

"You do not sound like you know."

Nox stepped closer to the wall. The surface looked like service composite, old and scarred. No handle. No seam. No devotional sticker, no municipal plate, no cheerful lie from a manufacturer. Just a flat stretch of material between two cable trunks.

Tap. Tap. Tap.

Pause.

Tap. Tap.

Pause.

Tap.

Bela caught Nox's sleeve before she touched it. "Let me scan."

"Your warrant scan will announce us."

"Everything down here already knows."

"Then scan quietly."

Bela gave her a look that belonged in their old arguments, the ones about whether reality improved when Nox gave impossible instructions. Then she pressed two fingers behind her ear and brought the evidence jack online.

Silver light moved under the skin at her temple.

Her warrant baton projected a thin plane across the wall. The plane should have shown density, heat, power, signal leakage, maybe a maintenance serial if fortune had a sense of humor. Instead it came back full of faces.

Not literal faces.

Signature faces.

Every identity proof had habits: pressure, rhythm, timing, error, the small useless variations that made a living person hard to counterfeit and an institution expensive to fool. Nox had spent half her life smoothing those variations for clients who needed doors to believe something merciful. A bad forgery erased the mess. A good one imitated it. A great one added mistakes nobody would think to copy.

The wall was full of Tavi's mistakes.

His hesitation before confirming a purchase.

His overhard press on the left side of shared consoles after he broke that finger in a lift shaft.

His habit of correcting his own name when a form used Tavion instead of Tavi, even though Tavion had been their mother's favorite when she wanted him to stand straight.

His rhythm from the three-two-one knock.

Nox wanted to look away.

She did not.

Bela's voice softened despite herself. "That is a lot of signature material."

"Too much."

"Could be built from your archives."

"Some of it. Not all."

Nox pointed at a little flutter in the scan, a half-second delay between challenge and response.

"That started when he was thirteen. He used to race door prompts and answer before they finished loading. Then one day a clinic door mistook him for consenting to a blood draw because he slapped the accept pane too fast. After that he waited half a second out of spite. We never wrote that down."

"You remembered it."

"Memory is not the same as an archive."

Bela did not argue, which was its own kind of argument.

The cache sleeve at her belt pulsed. The crystal inside was reacting to the wall. A thin response thread leapt from the sleeve to the composite and vanished.

The wall displayed a line in dust.

NOX?

Nox's mouth went dry.

Bela stepped between her and the wall.

"No direct answers."

"Move."

"No."

"Bela."

"It may be challenge harvesting. If you answer naturally, it learns you."

"It already learned my grief archive."

"Then do not give it fresh meat."

That was cruel enough to be useful.

Nox swallowed the first thing she wanted to say.

Bela opened her field kit. It was slimmer than Nox remembered, newer, expensive in the way Compact gear became expensive when someone expected the user to be blamed if it failed. Inside were evidence tabs, clean probes, two sealed data wafers, a foldable Faraday veil, and a packet of glucose tablets because Bela still forgot to eat when she worked.

Nox took one.

Bela looked at her.

"Seizure," Nox said, and ate it.

Bela sighed through her nose and clipped a probe to the cache sleeve.

"If this is a live partial, it should respond to neutral challenge without family context."

"If it is being coerced?"

"Then challenge response may hurt it."

The pronoun sat in the room.

It.

Tavi.

Whatever had knocked.

Nox looked at the wall and forced herself to think like a forger, not a sister. Emotion was useful for motive and useless for diagnostics unless carefully weaponized.

"Ask something no archive can answer."

Bela shook her head. "That is family context."

"Not if the answer is not family."

"Explain."

"Ask it to solve a bad problem in Tavi's bad way."

Bela studied her. "You want a behavioral proof."

"I want to know whether there is a thinker behind the proof."

"That still does not tell us whether it is him."

"Nothing tells us that."

The admission hurt less than she expected because it had no room to be dramatic. It was a tool laid on a table. Nothing tells us that. Now work anyway.

Bela set the probe to air-gap relay and typed with one thumb.

NEUTRAL CHALLENGE: OPTIMIZE WATER RATION ROUTE FOR SIX HOUSEHOLDS, ONE FAULTY PUMP, TWO FALSE CLAIMANTS.

The wall held still.

Then dust moved again.

WHO IS LYING?

Bela frowned. "That is a clarification request."

Nox almost laughed. "No. That is Tavi refusing the problem."

"Why?"

"Because he hated ration puzzles. Said they always hid the thief in the math."

Bela looked at the wall, then altered the prompt.

FALSE CLAIMANTS UNKNOWN. PROVIDE ROUTING.

The answer came faster.

FIX PUMP FIRST.

Bela whispered, "That is not an optimization."

"It is if you don't accept the premise."

Nox could hear Tavi at fifteen, sitting cross-legged on a floor during a ration shortfall, telling a housing clerk that any system requiring six families to compete over one broken pump was just theft with arithmetic. The clerk had hated him. Their mother had made him apologize. Tavi had apologized for the clerk's embarrassment.

Bela sent another challenge, more complex, stripped of names and station references.

The wall answered with an impossible little combination of practical refusal and sideways mercy.

Repair the shared fault before assigning individual blame.

Flag the claimant who profits from delay, not the claimant who speaks loudly.

Keep one route unmetered for the child who will not be listed.

Nox turned away before her face betrayed her.

Bela did not give her that privacy. "Nox."

"A bad forgery would be kinder."

"Yes."

"Say it like evidence."

Bela did not answer immediately.

That scared Nox more than a quick lie would have.

The evidence jack behind Bela's ear pulsed silver-white. Her baton had begun recording a sealed analytic branch, the kind Compact officers used when the thing in front of them might become a person before the law was ready to apologize for treating it as property. Nox had seen that branch only once, during a hearing about a shipboard maintenance intelligence that learned to pray because the crew kept asking it to remember the dead. The court had spent six months deciding whether prayer was diagnostic, performative, or an emergent maintenance artifact. The intelligence had been sold twice during deliberation.

"Do not put him in a pending-person box," Nox said.

Bela's jaw tightened. "I am trying to keep him out of an evidence-only box."

"Those boxes share walls."

"Yes."

The wall tapped once from the other side.

Not the three-two-one. A single impatient knock.

Bela looked at the probe readouts. "The neutral challenge was not neutral enough."

"No challenge is."
