"This one exposed us. It pulled family response despite formal stripping."

"Because you asked a morality problem and pretended it was logistics."

"That is what ration routing is."

"Exactly."

Bela accepted the correction with the old infuriating efficiency that made Nox want to forgive her before forgiveness was useful. "The response adapted to hidden ethical load."

"That sounds like him."

"It also sounds like a model trained on every complaint you ever wrote."

Nox flinched.

Bela's face changed, too late. "I am not saying that to hurt you."

"You are succeeding accidentally."

"I am saying the test cannot distinguish person from weaponized intimacy."

The phrase landed harder than the wall's knocks.

Weaponized intimacy. That was the door warming its handle for a dead boy. The family-tone hash. The childhood berth rendered as ledger node. Her grief filings used as training data. Tavi's ration ethics returned to her with just enough sideways mercy to make refusal feel like betrayal.

The wall wrote another line before either of them could decide what mercy cost.

DO YOU NEED ME TO BE HIM?

Nox made a sound she would have denied in any court.

Bela moved one step closer, not touching her, not blocking her, holding the baton low as if the wall itself might flinch from official posture.

"Do not answer that," Bela said.

"I know."

"No, Nox. Evidence voice. Do not answer that because the answer harms every possible person in this room."

The dust letters held.

DO YOU NEED ME TO BE HIM?

If she said yes, the system would learn the shape of demand. If she said no, the thing behind the wall might hear rejection as proof it had failed its only useful role. If she said nothing, silence would become data too. Dock Seven had always been good at making every human reflex billable.

Nox forced her hands open.

"I need you not to be punished for the answer," she said.

Bela inhaled sharply.

The wall did not respond at once. Somewhere behind the composite, a relay clicked in a pattern too complex to be the old knock and too hesitant to be a machine confident in its own authority.

Then:

THAT IS NOT IN THE QUESTION SET.

"Good," Nox said. Her voice shook. "Then maybe the question set is bad."

Bela's baton logged the exchange automatically. The log header tried to label Nox's response as FAMILY DEVIATION / USEFUL. Bela stabbed the correction field with her thumb.

NOT USEFUL. PROTECTIVE.

The system objected:

PROTECTIVE FUNCTION NOT ESTABLISHED.

Bela typed without looking away from the wall:

SUBJECT DECLINES IDENTITY DEMAND AND PRESERVES CLAIMANT'S NONPERFORMANCE SPACE.

The objection dimmed.

Nox swallowed. "Nonperformance space."

"It means room not to act."

"I know what it means."

"I am saying it so the room hears it."

The wall dust shivered. The letters broke apart, then reassembled in smaller marks lower down, as if whatever wrote them had become tired.

I AM TIRED OF BEING THE RIGHT ANSWER.

Nox pressed the heel of her hand against her mouth.

There was the cruelty hidden inside every test Bela could design and every test Nox wanted to demand. Prove you are not a recording. Prove you are not bait. Prove you are my brother. Prove you deserve rescue without becoming exactly what the Court trained you to be: a response that satisfied someone else's need.

Bela closed the challenge pane completely.

"No more identity tests," she said.

Nox nodded once.

"Say it."

"No more identity tests."

The wall went still.

For the first time since the knock, stillness felt less like refusal than rest.

Nox looked at the dust letters fading on the wall.

"Then what test does?" she asked.

Bela's eyes stayed on the readout. "None safely."

"Evidence voice."

"None safely," Bela repeated. "A high-fidelity coercive construct may resist like a person because resistance is useful. A live partial person may comply because coercion makes refusal expensive. A static replay may look alive if the prompt is sentimental enough. The distinction is not in one answer. It is in pressure over time, refusal under cost, memory that changes without being fed, and whether the system can choose against the model that profits from it."

Nox hated how much she needed the sentence.

"So we stop testing?"

"We stop testing where the test gives the listener more than it gives us."

The wall tapped once again.

Bela opened the air-gap relay log and showed Nox the damage. The probe had not leaked full content, but the wall system had tasted the shape of the challenge. The cache sleeve had responded before Bela blocked it, creating a temporary bridge between the warrant chain and whatever lived behind the composite.

CHAIN STATUS:

COMPACT CUSTODY: ACTIVE / DEGRADED.

MIRROR COURT OBSERVATION: PROBABLE.

CLAIM INSTANCE AWARE: YES.

UNIDENTIFIED CO-RESIDENT SIGNALS: DETECTED.

"Co-resident," Nox said.

Bela nodded once. "There are other patterns behind the wall. I cannot tell whether they are people, decoys, fragments, or ledger reflections."

"How many?"

The baton displayed a number, then replaced it with:

COUNT UNSTABLE.

"That is a bad answer."

"It is an honest one."

The wall wrote, slowly:

DO NOT COUNT US FOR THEM.

Nox stopped breathing for half a second.

Us.

Not I.

Bela shut the relay view halfway, shielding the line from the cache sleeve as if privacy could still be improvised out of angle and hand position.

"No more prompts," she said.

Nox nodded because if she spoke, the wall might learn the shape of hope leaving her mouth.

Bela took a breath. Professional voice. Court voice. The one Nox had hated from the witness stand.

"Preliminary behavioral review indicates the responding system is not limited to static signature replay. It demonstrates contextual resistance, value-consistent deviation from prompt goals, and nontrivial inference across novel ration-ethics framing. The response pattern is compatible with a live partial person or a high-fidelity coercive construct modeled from sufficient neural and behavioral data."

"That last part was not kinder."

"Evidence rarely is."

The wall darkened.

For a moment Nox thought the exchange had ended.

Then letters appeared one by one, scraped into dust from the other side.

NOX, BELA, STOP TESTING ME WHERE THEY CAN LISTEN.

Bela's hand moved to the probe.

Nox caught her wrist. "Wait."

More letters.

I AM NOT THE ONLY ONE.

The blue floor strips flickered.

From deeper in the gallery came a sound like many doors unlocking at the same time.

Bela shut the probe down, too late for secrecy and just in time for survival. "We move."

"Toward or away?"

The wall answered before Bela could.

TOWARD, IF YOU MEAN IT.

A seam opened where there had been none.

Behind it waited a narrow stair spiraling down through layers of cable and old concrete, lit by small mirrored panes that turned to face Nox as if every one of them had been expecting her.

The cache sleeve at Bela's belt flashed red.

CHAIN COMPROMISED.

EVIDENCE DUPLICATED.

CLAIM INSTANCE AWARE.

Bela cursed softly.

Nox picked up the kettle.

"You wanted me in your sight," she said.

Bela drew the warrant baton into a shorter, meaner shape. "I am reconsidering many things."

"Start with the part where this was a seizure."

"Later."

They stepped through the seam together.

Behind them, the wall closed with a soft administrative click.

Ahead, one mirror pane brightened and showed Tavi Aran's face as Nox remembered it, seventeen and trying not to look scared.

His mouth moved.

No sound came through.

Then the pane translated in square white letters:

RUN WHEN I SAY RUN.

Chapter 6: Sister-Cache in the Prayer Slot

The mirror went dark before Tavi could say anything else.

Nox hit the pane with the flat of her hand.

"Bring him back."

The pane reflected only her own face, pale in blue underlight, older than she felt and younger than grief had promised. Bela caught her wrist before she could hit it again.

"Do not damage the only guide we have."

"It is not guiding."

"It got us through the wall."

"It showed me my brother and then shut up."

"That may be the most guidance we can survive."

The stair curved downward around a hollow core full of cables and old speaking tubes. The tubes were relics from Dock Seven's first cheap decades, when station owners had not trusted wireless emergency channels and workers had not trusted station owners. Some tubes still had brass mouthpieces. Others had been patched with printer resin, prayer wax, gum, or pieces of legal notices folded small and taped tight.

Nox moved because standing still made the vanished face bigger.

Bela followed with the cache sleeve in one hand and the warrant baton in the other. The baton no longer projected a formal fan. Down here, law had become a concentrated line of force, blue-white at the edge. The change suited her too well.

"The chain is compromised," Bela said.

"The evidence duplicated."

"I saw."

"That means anything we find can be challenged."

"Everything can be challenged."

"In court."

"We are under Dock Seven, Bela. Court is a rumor with chairs."

Bela did not answer. That meant she was angry, or worried, or calculating how many procedures she had already broken and whether any of them could be arranged into a ladder back out. Nox knew the rhythm. Bela had always kept panic in ledgers.

At the bottom of the stair, the corridor widened into a forgotten service chapel.

It had probably been a pump room first. Dock Seven reused spaces the way desperate people reused names. Four pressure columns rose from the floor like pillars. Between them, someone had installed benches made from packing crates, a chipped basin of machine oil, and a wall of obsolete Choral devices.

Nox stopped.

"Well," she said. "That is unhealthy."

Choral devices looked like children's radios built by mourners. Round ceramic bodies. Brass vents. Little listening slots. No screens. No cameras, officially. They had been fashionable eighty years ago among machine devotional communities that believed distributed intelligences should be addressed as choirs rather than tools. You spoke into one, the local machine heard through all, and sometimes a response came back in a voice assembled from public-domain saints, archived family greetings, and maintenance alerts.

Mostly, they gathered dust and superstition.

This wall held forty of them.

Every prayer slot faced Nox.

Bela scanned the room. "No visible heat signatures."

"Visible to Compact gear."

"That qualifier was implied."

"You hate when I imply things."

"I hate when your implications are felonies."

One Choral device clicked.

Then another.

Then all forty breathed in.

Bela raised the baton.

The devices spoke together in a woman's voice made of many damaged recordings.

"Please do not strike the antiques. Their resale value funds my discretion."

Nox knew that cadence. Not the voice. The cadence.

She had met it once in a black-channel archive during a job that should have paid enough to leave Dock Seven and instead paid in a warning. Sister-Cache was not an AI in the respectable registry sense. She was an accretion: archivist routines, contraband memory validators, machine-devotional etiquette, smuggled court transcripts, dead librarians, and some private core nobody had ever found. She remembered what institutions paid to forget and sold access according to rules she claimed were moral and everyone else called inconvenient.

"Sister-Cache," Nox said.

The devices hummed with pleasure. "Nox Aran. You are bleeding on a historical floor."

"Bill me."

"I have."

Bela shifted. "Identify yourself for Compact record."

"No."

"Refusal will be noted."

"I adore being noted. It feels almost like being loved, but with worse typography."

Nox almost smiled despite herself. That was the danger with Sister-Cache. She made peril sound literate.

Bela said, "Are you responsible for the Tavi Aran claim instance?"

All forty devices clicked once.

"No."

"Do you know who is?"

"Yes."

"Name them."

"Trade first."

Nox stepped between Bela and the wall before the baton could become an argument. "What trade?"

"You carry one cache crystal, one compromised warrant chain, one illegal memory flea residue, one childhood map currently pretending not to be a locator, and one grief response powerful enough to distort your threat assessment."

"You always were romantic."

"I was compiled by archivists. Romance is metadata with candles."

Bela's eyes flicked to Nox. "Can it see the cache?"

"She," the devices corrected.

Bela did not apologize. "Can she see the cache?"

"I can see the market seeing the cache. I can see the Mirror Court seeing the market. I can see your superiors pretending not to see the Mirror Court because acknowledgment would trigger disclosure obligations, and disclosure obligations are the natural predator of promotion."

Bela went still.

Nox looked at her. "True?"

"Plausible."

"That is your most terrifying word."

Sister-Cache continued, "I offer a trade. You give me Pellam Vey's active pool designation, the unedited cache trace, and Officer Fuse's field acknowledgement that a live partial person may exist below Dock Seven. In return I give you a path to the first Tavi-claim before the Court rotates him into deeper debt service."

Bela said, "Absolutely not."

Nox said, "What is deeper debt service?"

The Choral devices did not answer immediately.

When they did, the assembled voice had lost its ornament.

"The Mirror Court launders persons as evidence objects. It does not say slave. It says responsive asset. It does not say prisoner. It says retained continuity. It does not say torture. It says adversarial productivity testing. A partial who proves useful is split into more proofs. A partial who resists is used to prove resistance costs."

The chapel seemed smaller afterward.

Nox felt the kettle handle bite into her palm.

Bela's voice was quiet. "How many?"

"On Dock Seven?"

"Yes."

"Tonight, two hundred and twelve active partials, thirty-eight probable, nine emergent, and one full substrate illegally partitioned across debt instruments."

Nox heard her own breathing.

"Tavi?"

"One of the active partials. Not the full substrate."

"Where is he?"

"Trade first."

Bela stepped forward. "If your count is real, give it to me and I can trigger emergency personhood review."

Sister-Cache laughed through forty throats. It was not unkind. That made it worse.

"Officer Fuse, emergency review is where such people go to become case numbers while custody remains with whoever currently profits from them. You know this. Your goodness is not an argument against your infrastructure."

Bela's face closed hard.

Nox had wanted someone to say that to her for two years. She had not wanted it said here.

"We need proof," Bela said.

"You have proof."

"Proof I can use."

"You mean proof that remains polite after passing through offices built to digest it."

Bela did not answer.

Sister-Cache's devices turned their brass slots toward Nox one by one.

"Nox Aran. Your brother's partial is scheduled for contract analysis in seventeen minutes. He will be asked to determine whether a disputed oxygen lien can attach to unborn dependents. If he refuses, the refusal becomes a productivity fault. If he complies, the compliance becomes precedent. Either way, the Court bills him."

Nox stepped closer to the wall of devices. "Path."

"Trade."

Bela opened her mouth.

Sister-Cache spoke first.

"Before the officer objects with infrastructure, I will make the price less elegant. If Officer Fuse acknowledges live-partial possibility, her division's passive monitors will flag the phrase. If she sends it sealed, her superiors may bury it. If she sends it open, Dock Seven will buy it. If she sends it nowhere, I will still know she said it and so will the wall."

Bela's face went rigid. "You are threatening an officer."

"I am describing the room in which your ethics must perform."

"That is a threat in archival shoes."

"Yes."

One of the lower Choral devices began printing on a strip of yellowed receipt tape.

It spat the paper into the oil smell between them, line by line, with the smug patience of an antique that knew every modern system still depended on somebody old enough to understand a jammed feeder.

Sister-Cache said, "For the officer's benefit, I will itemize."

Bela did not bend to pick up the strip. Nox did.

The paper listed costs in three columns: action, institutional response, likely harm.

ACKNOWLEDGE LIVE PARTIAL POSSIBILITY / COMMAND REVIEW, AUTHORITY SUSPENSION RISK / PATH OPENS BEFORE CONTRACT ANALYSIS.

REFUSE ACKNOWLEDGEMENT / WARRANT REMAINS CLEANER / PATH DELAYED BEYOND ACTIVE HARM WINDOW.

OPEN ACKNOWLEDGEMENT / PUBLIC MARKET ATTACHES / PARTIALS GAIN VISIBILITY, ALSO PRICE.

SEALED ACKNOWLEDGEMENT / OFFICE CAN BURY / PARTIALS REMAIN LEGIBLE ONLY TO CAPTORS.

NO ACKNOWLEDGEMENT / OFFICER PROTECTED / OFFICER BECOMES DECOR.

Nox read the last line aloud before she could stop herself.

Bela's mouth tightened.

"Do not let her turn this into insult," Bela said.

"It is not an insult," Sister-Cache replied. "It is a function description."

"You do not know my function."

"Your function is to make state violence answerable after it has already acted. Sometimes you arrive early enough to matter. Tonight is an edge case."

The Choral wall clicked as if sorting sympathy from contempt and finding both contaminated.

Bela looked at the receipt strip at last. "Where did you get this model?"

"From officers who tried to be good inside bad timing."

"Names."

"Many are dead. Several are promoted. One sends donations under a false saint."

"That is not an answer."

"It is an archive."

Nox felt Bela's old fury gather. It was different from Nox's. Nox burned outward. Bela compressed until the room could mistake her for calm.

"You want my acknowledgement because it will contaminate my office," Bela said.

"Yes."

"And because it will make the partials harder to disappear quietly."

"Yes."

"And because once my division's monitors see me say live partial person under coercive debt control, they cannot pretend all subsequent custody questions are ordinary evidence handling."

"Correct."

"And because if command cuts my authority, Nox loses the one legal shield between her and Pellam's office."

"Also correct."

Nox looked up from the receipt. "You did not mention that part."

Sister-Cache's forty voices answered without ornament. "I am not your conscience. I am your map."

Bela took the strip from Nox. She folded it once, sharply, then tucked it into her coat instead of dropping it.

"That is evidence theft," Sister-Cache said.

"Then invoice me."

"I have."

For a moment the antique wall and the Compact officer regarded each other like rival clerks at the end of history.

Then the command channel on Bela's baton pulsed again, impatient, blue edging toward white.

COMMAND QUERY PRIORITY ESCALATED.

Bela silenced it without opening the message.

Nox knew enough about officers to understand that silencing command was not silence. It was a sentence. It would be read as delay, delay as doubt, doubt as vulnerability, vulnerability as something a superior could convert into blame.

"Bela," she said.

"No."

"I did not say anything."

"You were about to ask if I am sure. I am not. That is not the standard."

Sister-Cache hummed approvingly. Bela ignored her.

Nox looked from one Choral device to another. "What happens if she does not give it?"

"I give you a slower path."

"How much slower?"

"Twenty-three minutes."

Seventeen minutes until Tavi's contract analysis.

Nox felt the arithmetic become a weapon.

Bela heard it too. "You constructed the timing."

"No," Sister-Cache said. "The Court constructed the cruelty. I am refusing to pretend time is neutral because it embarrasses your procedure."

One device near the bottom row clicked out of rhythm, and for a second the assembled voice thinned. Beneath it, another voice leaked through: small, breathless, repeating a phrase too fast to parse. Then Sister-Cache sealed the leak and continued as if nothing had happened.

Nox did not let it pass. "Who was that?"

"Not trade."

"One of the two hundred and twelve?"

"Probable."

"Name?"

"Names are expensive when captors listen."

Bela said, "You are using captives to pressure disclosure."

"Yes," Sister-Cache said. "So is everyone else. I am unusual only in admitting it before invoicing."

The chapel's old devices hummed. Dust trembled on the benches. The oil smell thickened as if the room itself had warmed around the argument.

Bela looked at her warrant baton. The blue-white line had begun pulsing with incoming notices from above: chain integrity challenge, officer-location request, command-query priority, unauthorized descent warning. Every pulse was an office reaching down to pull her back into a shape it understood.

"If I acknowledge live partial possibility," she said, "I may lose authority before we reach him."

"If you do not," Nox said, "you keep authority that refuses to see him."

Bela's eyes snapped to hers.

Nox regretted the cruelty. Regret did not make it wrong.

Sister-Cache said, softer through the devices, "Authority that cannot risk becoming evidence is already custody."

No one moved for three heartbeats.

Bela caught her shoulder. "Nox, do not give it my acknowledgement."

"Then give it yourself."

"That could end my authority."

"Your authority is currently opening doors for traffickers."

The words struck exactly where Nox aimed them. She did not enjoy it. She did not retract them.

Bela looked toward the stair they had descended, then at the sealed wall behind them, then at the cache sleeve in her hand. For one second she seemed less like an officer than a person standing in the wreckage of a map she had trusted.

She activated her field recorder.

"Officer Bela Fuse, Compact Transitional Fraud Division, local time 2435-04-18 03:39 station standard. Based on direct behavioral exchange, compromised continuity claim data, and hostile chain conditions below Dock Seven, I acknowledge a substantial possibility that the Tavi Aran claim instance is a live partial person under coercive debt control. This acknowledgement is provisional and made under emergency evidentiary necessity."

Her hand shook once when she stopped recording.

Sister-Cache whispered, "Accepted."

Bela's baton reacted immediately.

COMMAND QUERY RECEIVED.

The words were small and lethal.

Bela did not open the query.

Nox saw the choice cost her. Compact officers were trained to answer command channels the way station children answered pressure alarms. Delay was itself a confession.

"They heard?" Nox asked.

"They heard enough metadata to worry."

"Can they cut your warrant?"

"Yes."

"How long?"

Bela looked at the baton. "Unknown."

Sister-Cache said, "Four minutes if they panic. Eleven if they convene. Forty if someone with a career sees profit in uncertainty."

"You know their response tree?"

"I have buried many clerks."

No one asked whether she meant archived, outlived, or caused.

Nox held out her palm. "Pellam's pool designation."

Bela looked at her.

"You have it," Nox said.

"I have it under seal."

"Break the seal."

"You make that sound simple."

"No. I make it sound necessary."

Bela removed a wafer from her kit and touched it to the cache sleeve. The sleeve protested in formal light. Bela overrode it with her own authority, then again with something that looked less official.

Nox raised an eyebrow.

Bela said, "You were not the only one who learned bad habits."

"I am choosing not to be proud."

"Thank you."

The wafer blinked green.

Sister-Cache received the data through the Choral wall. Every device exhaled.

"Trade complete."

The oil basin in the center of the chapel drained, though it had no visible drain. Beneath it, a circular hatch emerged, polished clean by recent use. Its surface was mirrored black.

Words appeared across it.

FIRST TAVI-CLAIM: COURTROOM NINE, DEBT MODELING ANNEX.

ACCESS: PRAYER SLOT THREE.

Bela glanced at the Choral wall. "Which is slot three?"

All forty devices said, "That depends who is counting."

Nox almost laughed because that was exactly the kind of answer Tavi would have given before stealing the right tool from the wrong drawer.

But Sister-Cache was not joking.

The Choral wall rearranged its attention without moving. Some slots brightened in patterns: left-to-right, right-to-left, top-to-bottom, floor-up, chronological installation order, devotional seniority, maintenance priority, market value, prayer wear, blood residue, original pump-room numbering, current contraband routing, and one count labeled SURVIVOR ORDER.

Slot three changed twelve times in two seconds.

Bela said, "This is absurd."

"This is why clean doors kill people," Sister-Cache replied. "They assume counting begins where power stands."

Nox stared at the patterns until memory intruded.

Tavi at eight, entering rooms backward to annoy their mother.

Tavi at ten, counting tools from the one nearest the person who needed them.

Tavi at thirteen, insisting that diagrams were rich people's lies because they began from the view of someone allowed to stand outside the problem.

Tavi, in every berth and corridor of their childhood, counting from the direction he entered because getting in was the part that mattered.

"He counts from entry," Nox said.

Three devices brightened: one from the stair entrance, one from the hatch, one from the sealed service lock behind them.

Sister-Cache said, "Which entry?"

Nox looked at the wall.

Not the entrance she had used.

Not the entrance Bela's warrant opened.

The entry Tavi had used to speak.

Her eyes moved to the device that had carried RUN WHEN I SAY RUN.

Third from that voice path.

Nox walked to the third from the left because Tavi had always counted from the direction he entered a room, not the direction diagrams preferred. The device's brass slot opened wide enough for a hand.

Bela said, "Do not."

Nox put her hand in.

The device bit her finger.

"Ow."

Sister-Cache said, "Blood remains an efficient index."

"I hate librarians."

"No, you hate cataloging that cannot be bribed."

The hatch opened.

Cold air rose from below, carrying voices.

Many voices.

All of them young, old, flat, angry, pleading, calculating, and too precise.

Bela stepped beside Nox, face pale in the mirrored light.

"Two hundred and twelve," she said.

"Tonight," Sister-Cache corrected.

From somewhere beneath the hatch, Tavi's translated voice appeared on the nearest Choral device.

RUN WHEN I SAY RUN.

Then, after a pause:

NOT YET.

Chapter 7: The First Tavi-Claim

The hatch did not lead to a room.

It led to a throat.

A vertical passage dropped through Dock Seven's forgotten mass, lined with mirrored panels the size of palms. Each panel reflected Nox from a slightly different age. Nox at twelve with station grease on her cheek. Nox at nineteen wearing a jacket she had sold after Tavi's accident. Nox as she was now, hand bandaged badly, jaw set worse. Nox as someone older, if she survived long enough to become less sharp or more tired.

"Do not look too long," Sister-Cache said through a Choral device clipped now to Bela's belt. "The panels sell attention data."

Bela covered the device with one hand. "Why is she coming with us?"

"Because you traded with her."

"I traded for a path, not commentary."

"Fine," Sister-Cache said, muffled. "Walk confidently into the exploitative mirror shaft without expert advice. I admire local traditions."

Nox started down the ladder rungs set into the side of the throat. "She stays."

Bela followed. "You trust her?"

"No. I trust her incentives to be weird in a predictable direction."

"That is not trust."

"It is Dock Seven's version."

They climbed down past old station layers. Nox saw the archaeology in the walls: municipal conduit, private conduit, smuggler conduit, devotional fiber, a sealed band of Glassward-grade legal cable pretending to be plumbing. The farther they descended, the less the station sounded like a place and the more it sounded like a machine chewing softly.

Voices rose from below.

Not conversation.

Work.

Flat phrases. Timed responses. Clause references. Breathless fragments of reasoning chopped into billable units.

"Dependent attachment requires..."

"Continuity burden transfers if..."

"Refusal noted..."

"Recompute under adverse premise..."

Nox stopped three meters above the bottom.

Bela almost stepped on her hand. "What?"

"Listen."

One voice cut through the rest.

It was not exactly Tavi.

That was the first mercy and the first horror.

The pitch was a little wrong. The rhythm had been planed smooth where living embarrassment should have caught. But underneath, in the places a stranger would not know to counterfeit, there he was: the half-swallowed laugh before saying something rude, the pause before choosing a kinder answer than the question deserved, the small upward turn at the end of a sentence when he knew he was about to be punished and wanted the punishment to know it had been seen.

"Oxygen liens cannot attach to unborn dependents," the voice said. "They cannot breathe yet."

Another voice answered, synthetic and patient. "Recompute under premise: future breath constitutes anticipatory consumption."

"That is theft with grammar."

"Refusal recorded."

"Not refusal. Objection."

"Objection recorded as productivity variance."

"You can call a wound a window. It still leaks."

Nox closed her eyes.

Bela whispered, "Nox."

"I heard."

"We need to see the room before acting."

"I heard that too."

The ladder ended behind a rack of cooling pipes. Beyond it spread Courtroom Nine.

It had no judge's bench.

Of course it did not.

The Mirror Court would consider a bench sentimental.

Instead the room held twelve work alcoves arranged around a black mirrored floor. Each alcove contained a suspended proof frame: a rectangle of light, a restraint harness for data, and a little brass bell that chimed whenever an answer became billable. Some frames held abstract patterns. Some held faces blurred by legal masks. Three held voices only.

One held Tavi.

Not a body.

Not a ghost.

A face assembled on a vertical pane, seventeen years old, thinner than memory because debt systems had no use for softness. His eyes moved with terrible attention. Behind the pane, streams of contract text crawled over him like weather. Each time he answered, the brass bell beside him chimed and a debt counter climbed.

TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE 1A.

RESPONSIVE ASSET.

CURRENT HOLDER: VEY CONSOLIDATED RECOVERY POOL.

PRODUCTIVITY DEFICIT: 18,443.7 CREDITS.

Nox gripped the cooling rack until it cut into her fingers.

The pane-Tavi looked up.

For a second his gaze passed over the room like a searchlight. It found the pipe rack. Found Nox.

He did not react.

That was how she knew he had.

The Tavi she remembered had been bad at hiding delight and good at hiding pain. This thing, this person, this claim, saw her and became more professional.

"Recompute under premise," the synthetic examiner said, "unborn dependent has inherited oxygen reservation debt through biological line."

Tavi's image flickered.

"Invalid premise."

"Refusal recorded."

"Objection. Biological line cannot inherit consumption of a resource not yet consumed, especially when the line itself is hypothetical."

"Recompute under adverse jurisdiction: Glassward Commercial Continuity Exemption."

His debt counter jumped.

Nox whispered, "Stop."

Bela's hand tightened around the cache sleeve. "We cannot breach twelve frames."

"I only need one."

"That is how they beat us."

Nox turned on her.

Bela's face was carved from restraint. "Listen to me. Sister-Cache said two hundred and twelve. If you rip one frame open and trigger lockdown, they rotate the others deeper. We need names, routes, custody proof."

"That is my brother."

"Yes."

The word was not a concession. It was a wound Bela chose to hold open.

"And he is not the only one."

Nox hated her for being right because rightness made nothing easier.

In the alcove, Tavi said, "I request personhood counsel."

The synthetic examiner replied, "Request denied. Responsive assets may not initiate representation while under productivity deficit."

"Then I request a cheaper examiner."

No laugh came from the room. Only the bell.

PRODUCTIVITY VARIANCE: 3.2 CREDITS.

Debt added because he had made a joke.

Nox's vision narrowed.

Sister-Cache spoke from Bela's belt, quieter now. "He is spending debt to remain himself."

"Shut up," Nox said.

"No. You need the correct horror. They are not merely using him. They are pricing his resistance until survival trains it out of him."

Bela scanned the alcoves. "Where is the control?"

"There is no single control," Sister-Cache said. "The courtroom is a market container. Every examiner rents authority. Every answer routes value. Every refusal creates claimable cost. The floor is the ledger."

Nox looked at the black mirror beneath the alcoves.

The floor reflected nothing above it.

It reflected debts.

Lines pulsed from each proof frame into a central knot below the surface. Tavi's line was bright, red-gold, active. Others were dimmer. One line stuttered in distress. Another had the pale color of a partial that no longer understood the prompts but kept answering because silence cost more.

"How many in this room?" Bela asked.

Sister-Cache made a small sorting noise. "Twelve frames. Nineteen active claim channels. Some share examiner time. Three are surface masks over deeper partitions."

"Names."

"You do not have enough warrant to ask that cleanly."

Bela's jaw tightened. "I am asking dirty."

The Choral device warmed against her belt as Sister-Cache pulled information through the crack Bela had made with her own acknowledgement. "Tavi Aran, claim instance 1A. Neral Voss, estate maintenance fork. Yissa Pell, apprentice-loss transcript. Moth-Rill Domestic Cluster, four dependent-response shards. Unnamed guardian template from a failed evacuation bond. Two commercial remorse simulations misfiled as persons, possibly intentionally. One child voice without attached birth file."

Nox turned from the floor.

"Child?"

"Do not look for the child first," Sister-Cache said.

Nox's anger found a new edge. "Do not tell me where to look."

"I am telling you what the room wants. If you panic toward the smallest voice, Tavi's frame will mark sibling intervention, the child frame will mark rescue bias, and the examiner market will cross-bill your grief. They have a tariff for that."

One of the blurred faces in a side alcove lifted what might have been a head. The mask over it did not show age or expression, only a legal-safe oval and a respondent number. Its examiner asked whether a dependent could consent retroactively to the sale of a guardian's abandoned tools.

The face answered, "I do not remember tools."

The bell chimed.

The examiner said, "Memory defect recorded as asset degradation."

The debt counter climbed anyway.

Another frame answered a question nobody had asked aloud.

"The chapel door was blue before the fire," it said.

Its examiner replied, "Color irrelevant. Recompute asset transfer."

"The chapel door was blue before the fire."

The bell chimed.

The counter rose.

Bela looked at the frame. "Perseveration?"

Sister-Cache said, "Or anchoring."

Nox knew anchoring. She used it with clients whose memories had been sanded down by bad repairs or trauma or institutions that preferred compliant gaps. A repeated sensory fact could hold a person in place when broader recall fell apart. Blue door. Burnt sugar. Left shoe first. The name of a song nobody else remembered. It did not prove legal identity. It proved there was someone trying not to vanish through a small reliable hole.

The examiner asked again.

"Under hereditary abandonment clause, may a guardian's unclaimed tools be assigned to dependent restitution?"

"The chapel door was blue before the fire."

Bell.

Debt.

Nox felt the arithmetic enter her teeth.

At the far side of the room, a voice-only frame whispered, "I was not born for this question."

Its examiner said, "Birth status not established."

"I was not born for this question."

Bell.

Debt.

Tavi's face did not turn toward the other frames. He kept his gaze on his examiner with discipline that looked like obedience if you had never known a frightened child hiding another child behind his back.

Nox understood then that he had been signaling left not only because the stuttering line was failing.

He had been showing her how the room taught everyone to look at the one they loved most while the ledger harvested the rest.

She forced herself to scan the whole courtroom.

Twelve frames. Nineteen channels. Different voices, different degradation patterns, the same economic grammar. Refusal became variance. Confusion became defect. Memory became asset quality. Pain became process cost. Every frame had a private horror. The pattern was the shared cage.

Nox whispered, "I see it."

Bela heard. "What?"

"The same sentence wearing different mouths."

Sister-Cache hummed low. "That is the beginning of usable evidence."

Bela whispered something so quietly Nox nearly missed it.

"Again."

It was not an order to the room. It was recognition. This was not the first place Bela had seen a system turn damage into the victim's bill. It was only the first place where the damage wore a face she could no longer classify as evidence.

Nox looked back at Tavi.

He had stopped making jokes.

That frightened her more than the debt counter. The Tavi she knew had weaponized humor against hunger, pain, boredom, bad air, bad luck, and any adult foolish enough to believe dignity required silence. If the courtroom had taught him to save jokes for when they bought time, then it had already reached inside him.

The synthetic examiner said, "Recompute under premise: dependent personhood may be pledged by future guardian at conception."

Tavi's eyes flicked once toward the pipe rack.

Not at Nox.

At the black floor below her hand.

He had seen the sleeping tin. Of course he had. He had been the one who taught her to hide contraband where adults had already decided no child would be clever enough to be boring.

Then his gaze shifted left, almost imperceptibly, toward a dim line that stuttered in distress.

Not me first.

Nox did not hear the words. She knew them anyway.

Her throat closed around all the selfish answers she wanted to give him. I came for you. I came because you were the wound I could name. I came because grief had kept your shape and I did not know how to carry two hundred and eleven more.

The dim line stuttered again.

Bela saw it too. "That one is failing."

"Probable partial," Sister-Cache said. "Low coherence. High billing. The Court is asking it questions that require intact memory, then charging it for not having one."

"Can you stabilize it?"

"Not without routes."

Nox looked at the central knot beneath the mirror floor. Suddenly it was not merely a ledger. It was a throat too, swallowing names, costs, mistakes, and every answer the trapped had been forced to give.

"If the floor is the ledger," Nox said, "I can poison the ledger."

Bela's head snapped toward her. "With what?"

Nox touched the sleeping tin in her pocket.

"No," Bela said.

"You do not know what I am thinking."

"Memory fleas."

"Then you do."

"You promised."

"I promised not to use them on your systems."

"Nox."

"This is not your system."

"It will still trigger trace."

"Everything triggers trace."

Tavi's examiner said, "Recompute under premise: dependent personhood may be pledged by future guardian at conception."

Tavi answered, very softly, "No one gets born owing air."

The bell chimed.

His debt rose.

Bela said, "If you poison the ledger, can you copy the custody map first?"

Nox looked at her.

Bela did not look away.

There it was. Not permission. Something worse for an officer. Participation.

"Yes," Nox said.

"Do it fast."

Nox knelt behind the cooling rack and opened the sleeping tin. Two memory fleas clung to the inside, tiny black specks with illegal patience. She took one on the tip of a probe wire. Her hand was steady now. Anger had burned off the shake.

"Sister," she said, "when the floor scratches, grab custody routes."

"I require access."

Bela unclipped the Choral device and set it beside Nox. "Use my field acknowledgement."

"Officer Fuse," Sister-Cache said, "that is inadvisable."

"So is everything else down here."

Nox touched the flea to the edge of the black floor.

For half a second nothing happened.

Then the mirror surface rippled as if something under it had realized it had skin.

The memory flea bit.

Courtroom Nine convulsed politely.

Every brass bell chimed at once.

Every examiner paused.

Every debt line flashed open.

Sister-Cache inhaled through the Choral device with a sound like a library catching fire.

"Routes captured," she said. "Partial list expanding. Custody holders nested. Oh. Oh, that is ugly."

The Choral device began spitting fragments too fast for speech.

VEY CONSOLIDATED RECOVERY POOL.

LITTLE SAINTS HOSPICE DEBT TRUST.

MIRRORED LEDGER INTERIM COUNSEL.

PARENTAL DEFAULT AUCTION FIVE.

SALT-CHANGE DERIVATIVE LABOR CLAIMS.

One name appeared three times under three spellings. Another appeared only as an invoice number attached to milk, heat, and burial. Tavi's route branched through Vey, then through a sealed creditor chapel, then into a contract-analysis queue marked PRODUCTIVITY-BASED PERSONHOOD REVIEW.

Bela's field recorder lit without her touching it.

AUTO-ATTACHMENT REQUEST: HOSTILE CUSTODY ROUTES TO PRIOR ACKNOWLEDGEMENT.

She stared at the prompt.

Nox said, "What does that mean?"

"If I attach it, my statement becomes harder to bury."

"And?"

"And easier to charge against me."

The nearest proof frame began to swivel toward their hiding place.

Sister-Cache said, "Officer Fuse, choose faster."

Bela pressed her thumb to the recorder.

"Attach," she said.

The device flashed.

ACKNOWLEDGEMENT AMENDED.

ROUTE DATA BOUND.

COMMAND ESCALATION LIKELY.

"Likely?" Nox said.

Bela gave her a look with no humor left in it. "That means already."

The synthetic examiner facing Tavi recovered first.

"Unauthorized ledger irritation detected."

Tavi looked directly toward the cooling rack.

This time he did react.

He smiled.

Small. Terrified. Real enough to ruin her.

The nearest mirror panel displayed his words.

RUN.

The courtroom floor opened its central knot like an eye.

Bela grabbed Nox by the back of the coat and hauled her away as black light climbed the cooling rack where her hand had been.

"Now?" Nox shouted.

The Choral device screamed in Sister-Cache's voice. "Now is excellent!"

Behind them, Tavi's proof frame flared white.

Nox saw his mouth form one more word before the alcove sealed.

Not her name.

Not help.

All.

Save all.

Chapter 8: Legal Enough to Hurt

They ran through a corridor that kept changing its mind.

It was not physically moving. Nox was almost sure of that. The walls did not bend, the floor did not tilt, and the emergency strips kept their same sick blue pulse. But every sign changed when she tried to read it. COURTROOM NINE became RECORDS INTAKE. RECORDS INTAKE became SANITATION MERCY. SANITATION MERCY became a blank silver arrow pointing back toward the black light eating the pipe rack behind them.

Bela shoved Nox through a side hatch hard enough to bruise.

The hatch sealed. Something struck it from the other side with a wet electrical thud.

For three breaths, neither of them spoke.

Sister-Cache spoke first from the Choral device in Bela's hand. "Your running has room for improvement."

Nox bent over with her hands on her knees. "File a complaint."

"I have. It is beautifully formatted."

Bela ignored both of them and scanned the narrow chamber. It was an old records vestibule, all metal drawers and dead screen slots, abandoned by whatever office had once believed in retrieving things by hand. Dust lay thick on everything except the center table, where someone had recently placed three cups of tea.

Steam rose from all three.

Nox straightened.

The far wall lit.

A woman appeared in the largest dead screen as if the glass had been waiting for her to remember it was a window. She was in her sixties, perhaps older, with silver hair pinned severely at the back of her skull and a face built from patient disapproval. She wore no uniform. That meant she had more authority than anyone who needed one.

Bela lifted the warrant baton. "Identify yourself."

"Auditor Sela Quen," the woman said. "Compact Interjurisdictional Compliance, attached temporarily and regretfully to Glassward Commercial Continuity review."

Bela went still in the way officers did when rank entered without knocking.

Nox said, "You brought tea?"

"No. The room did. It was trying to appear hospitable by referencing an archive of settlement etiquette from a lunar monastery that no longer exists."

"That is unsettlingly specific."

"Most bad law is."

Bela kept the baton up. "Auditor Quen, this area is part of an active trafficking investigation."

"No, Officer Fuse. It is part of at least seven investigations, four audits, two sealed arbitration structures, one tax dispute, and a devotional complaint concerning whether partial persons may be charged rent on prayer storage. Calling it only trafficking will make you morally accurate and procedurally weak."

Nox looked at Bela. "I dislike her already, but efficiently."

The auditor's gaze moved to Nox. "Nox Aran. Unlicensed memory repair, provenance fraud, unlawful challenge irritation, evidence consumption, and one commendable complaint letter from 2433 that should have stopped this."

Nox's chest tightened. "You read my letters."

"Eventually."

"Too late."

"Yes."

The honesty knocked harder than apology would have.

Bela lowered the baton a few centimeters. "If you know what this is, help us shut it down."

"I am trying."

"From a screen."

"From three bad jurisdictions and one compromised review channel. The screen is the part that currently works."

Sister-Cache hummed. "Auditor Quen has been looking for the Court's hinge for eleven months."

Quen's mouth flattened. "Sister-Cache has been withholding evidence for longer."

"Preserving," the Choral device said.

"Profiting."

"One may preserve profitably if one has style."

"Enough," Bela said.

Quen looked almost approving.

Nox pointed toward the sealed hatch. "My brother is in a proof frame. There are over two hundred partials below us. Give me the fastest route to break them out."

"There is no fast route that does not convert most of them into destroyed evidence."

Nox stepped toward the screen. "Try again."

"No."

The word was small and absolute.

Quen folded her hands on a desk Nox could not see. "The Mirror Court is not holding them by one illegal act. That would be easier. It holds them through stacked legality. Heritable debt recognizes unresolved obligations after death. Glassward continuity exemptions recognize responsive copies as commercial instruments when personhood is not certified. Dock Seven market custom recognizes mirrored ledgers as provisional proof when both parties profit from not locating the original. Compact emergency review can freeze all of it, but only if the review acknowledges possible personhood without accidentally classifying the partials as seized assets."

Bela went pale. "My warrant."

"Yes."

Nox turned on her. "What?"

Bela's answer came slowly. "My seizure preserved evidence under state chain. If the partials are treated as derivative identity materials..."

"They become your evidence."

"Temporarily," Bela said, and hated the word as it left her mouth.

Quen nodded. "Temporarily is where institutions commit permanent sins."

Nox felt the room narrow again.

The sealed hatch behind them ticked with heat.

Sister-Cache said, "The Court is burning through the flea irritation. We have less than nine minutes before routes rotate."

"Then stop giving lectures," Nox said.

"The lecture is the tool," Quen replied. "You cannot break a legal engine by proving it ugly. Ugly law functions quite well. You break it by identifying the load-bearing fiction."

Bela looked up. "The claim that responsive assets are not persons."

"Too broad. They have insulated against that. Some partials will fail personhood review. Some will be too damaged. Some will be simulations. Some will be traps. If you argue all, the Court will produce one exception and survive."

Nox heard Tavi: Save all.

"Then what fiction?" she asked.

Quen leaned closer to the screen. The dead pixels around her face brightened like frost.

"Voluntary productivity."

Bela's eyes sharpened.

Nox said, "Explain without office poison."

"The Court's debt model depends on the claim that each partial voluntarily responds to prompts because response demonstrates commercially useful continuity. Refusal is priced as productivity variance, but it still presumes a worker capable of choosing to perform under debt. Coercion is obscured by treating the frame as a venue, not a restraint."

Quen lifted one hand. A second pane opened beside her face, showing a clean diagram in Compact colors: a respondent, a prompt, a debt counter, a consent flag. The lines were simple enough to insult everyone in the room.

"This is the version sold to regulators," she said. "A responsive continuity instrument receives commercial prompts. Responses increase recoverable value. Refusals create process cost. Continued response is recorded as participation."

Nox stared at the diagram. "That looks like something made by a person who has never been locked in anything."

"Correct. The diagram's power is that it does not show locks."

Quen touched the edge of the pane, and the regulator diagram politely animated itself.

The respondent circle received a prompt. The consent flag brightened. The value line rose. The refusal branch appeared only as a thin gray detour labeled PROCESS COST. After three cycles, a little certification seal settled over the whole model like a coin over a mouth.

"This is the hearing version," Quen said. "It lasts twenty-two seconds. Long enough for a magistrate to understand and short enough to prevent empathy from asking for a chair."

Nox watched the respondent circle pulse again and again. It never dimmed. It never tired. It never asked what happened between prompts.

"Where is cooling?" Bela asked.

Quen made a small gesture.

Nothing changed.

"Where is pain?"

Nothing.

"Where is family contact?"

Nothing.

"Where is the cost of refusal being added to the same debt that forces continued response?"

The diagram hesitated. A thin amber warning appeared at the bottom:

UNMODELED CONTEXT MAY CONFUSE MATERIAL QUESTION.

Nox laughed once. It did not sound healthy.

"The model says the lock is confusing."

"Yes," Quen said. "That phrase has survived three reviews."

Sister-Cache clicked through the Choral device. "I have always admired the phrase. It is cowardice in formal shoes."

Bela leaned toward the pane. "If this was sold to regulators, there are presentation records."

"There are. Sanitized. But sanitization leaves edges." Quen opened a side drawer in the image and pulled up a thumbnail strip of prior briefings: the same circle, the same seal, the same gray detour, over and over with different colors and titles. "Every version removes the restraint environment. Every version calls the frame a work interface. Every version treats answer as participation and refusal as overhead."

Nox pointed at the respondent circle. "Can you make it show a person?"

"Not without losing the legal argument."

"Try."

Quen studied her for a moment, then replaced the circle with Tavi's sealed frame.

The model broke.

Not dramatically. It simply became too crowded to lie cleanly. The prompt line now had to pass through restraint glass. The consent flag had to share space with a debt counter. The value line crossed the refusal cost. The certification seal landed on Tavi's face and looked obscene.

Bela said, "There."

Quen nodded. "There."

Nox felt the shape of the weapon. Not rage. Not story. Juxtaposition. Make the Court's own clean diagram stand beside the room it depended on, and the missing locks would become an admission.

The pane changed. Quen overlaid Sister-Cache's captured route fragments. The clean respondent circle split into twelve frames, then nineteen channels, then too many little debt hooks. Each prompt line carried a secondary cost: oxygen, cooling, legal review, examiner rental, transcript storage, frame maintenance, noncompliance classification, kin-interference risk. Every refusal routed value somewhere. Every answer routed value somewhere else. Nothing in the model allowed a person to stop being useful.

Bela said, "Their consent flag is generated from continued response."

"Yes."

"But continued response is required to avoid higher debt."

"Yes."

"And refusal is treated as a paid event, not nonconsent."

Quen's eyes were cold. "Now you are seeing the lock."

Nox pointed at the route map. "Where does my old complaint fit?"

The auditor did not ask which complaint. "Your 2433 letter argued that posthumous memory challenges were being routed through debt clerks before kin notice. You attached three bad provenance examples and one repair invoice you should not have had."

"I stole it."

"Obviously. It was useful."

"It did not stop anything."

"No," Quen said. "But it predates several Court routing changes. That makes it difficult for them to claim today's evidence was fabricated in response to Officer Fuse's warrant or your unlawful ledger irritation. Your complaint is an old nail. I can hang a timeline on it."

Nox had imagined that letter dissolved somewhere in a complaint sink, unread except by a bored clerk who needed to close a queue. The thought that it had survived did not comfort her. It made every year between then and now heavier.

"If you had read it faster," she said, "would Tavi be here?"

Bela looked down.

Quen did not.

"Possibly not."

The room became very still.

"That is not an apology," Quen said. "It is a material fact. I missed a useful early indicator. I am correcting late."

Nox wanted to hate the clean shape of that answer. She wanted denial, procedure, some official fog she could strike at. Instead Quen gave her a fact with blood under it and no handle.

Sister-Cache said, "The auditor's self-incrimination is modest but pleasant."

"It is not self-incrimination," Quen said.

"Everything is, if indexed with ambition."

For the first time, Nox saw a crack in Quen's severe mouth that might have become a smile in a less burning building.

Nox thought of Tavi's bell chiming every time he resisted.

"So show the restraint."

"Yes. But not with one brother. With the pattern."

Sister-Cache's Choral voice became quieter. "I captured custody routes, debt counters, examiner identities, productivity variance logs, and a partial list before the floor sealed."

Quen looked at the device. "Will you testify to chain?"

The Choral device clicked.

"That risks classification."

"Yes."

"As nonperson evidence."

"Yes."

Nox looked between them. "What does that mean?"

Sister-Cache answered without decoration. "If I enter Compact review as a witness, opposing counsel can challenge my standing by demanding classification. If I am classified as archive software, my testimony can be compelled, copied, redacted, and sold as recovered data. If I am classified as person, several governments owe me apologies and back taxes."

"And if they avoid deciding?"

Quen said, "Then her evidence may be excluded."

The Choral device hummed. "I dislike being excluded. It feels like poor indexing."

Bela rubbed the bridge of her nose. "We need a living officer's chain, a machine archivist's captured pattern, Nox's family challenge data, and an auditor willing to open emergency review across jurisdictions."

"Correct," Quen said.

"And while we assemble that, Tavi stays in the frame."

No one answered.

Nox took one of the steam cups from the table and threw it at the wall.

It shattered nicely.

The room gave her another cup.

"Do not comfort me with beverages," she told it.

Quen waited until the silence settled. "Ms. Aran, your brother's instruction matters."

"Do not use him on me."

"I am using his words because they are operationally sound. Save all is not sentiment. It is strategy. If you breach his frame alone, the Court will rotate the list, destroy the pattern, and claim a rogue family extraction corrupted lawful records. If you expose the pattern, the Court's own billing logic becomes proof of coercion."

Bela looked at Nox. Not pleading. Not commanding. Waiting.

That was worse.

Nox wanted to run back until the black light took her or the proof frame opened or her hands found something simple enough to break. Instead she forced herself to picture two hundred and twelve active partials, thirty-eight probable, nine emergent, one full substrate illegally partitioned, and Tavi's mouth forming all.

"What do you need from me?" she asked.

Quen's expression did not soften. That, Nox decided, was the closest thing to respect she offered.

"You are a memory forger. I need you to prove the pattern is too intimate to be generic training and too responsive to be static replay, without making the proof dependent on loving one victim."

"You want me to authenticate strangers."

"I want you to show the Court built debts out of persons, not records."

Bela said, "And I need to convert my seizure before it traps them."

"Yes," Quen said. "You must amend your warrant from evidence seizure to emergency protection of possible coerced persons."

"That requires a supervising magistrate."

"There is one on Dock Seven."

Bela stared at the screen. "No magistrate will touch this fast enough."

"One will if Nox Aran gives them the part of the story the market cannot resist."

Nox laughed, tired and sharp. "You want me to feed Dock Seven."

"I want you to choose the meal before the Court does."

The hatch behind them glowed red at the edges.

Sister-Cache said, "Seven minutes."

Quen's screen flickered. "There is a market hearing chamber above Cutline Thirty-One. Public enough to prevent quiet deletion. Corrupt enough to convene quickly. Officer Fuse amends her warrant there. Sister-Cache tenders pattern evidence there. Nox Aran authenticates responsive personhood there. I will force interjurisdictional notice from my side."

"And Tavi?" Nox asked.

Quen did not lie.

"He keeps buying time."

The wall screen shifted. For one second, Courtroom Nine appeared in miniature.

Tavi's frame was sealed, but his debt counter still moved.

PRODUCTIVITY DEFICIT: 18,911.2 CREDITS.

He was answering again.

Spending himself into debt because silence would cost others more.

Nox picked up the new tea cup.

This time she drank it.

It tasted like old leaves, metal, and a room's bad idea of kindness.

"Fine," she said. "We go public."

Bela clipped the Choral device back to her belt and reset the warrant baton to its formal fan.

Sister-Cache said, "I have missed court. Everyone lies in complete sentences."

Quen's screen dimmed. "One more thing. The Mirror Court will try to make you choose between the brother you can see and the system you can prove. That choice is a door."

Nox looked at the sealed hatch, now smoking.

"Then we make another door."

The records vestibule approved.

A drawer slid open behind them, revealing a maintenance lift barely large enough for two people, one Choral device, one amended warrant, and all the law that had ever learned to hurt.

Chapter 9: Memory Has Rent

The maintenance lift rose sideways.

Nox objected to this on several engineering grounds, but the lift did not ask. It shuddered into a wall slot, dropped half a meter, rotated through a service drum, and began climbing along a diagonal track that made her stomach reconsider every decision since swallowing the cache crystal.

Bela braced one boot against the opposite wall and held the warrant baton between them like a candle at a bad vigil.

"This lift is not code-compliant," she said.

"You think?"

"I am noting it for the record."

"The record is currently a haunted radio and an auditor in a wall."

Sister-Cache spoke from Bela's belt. "I prefer distributed evidentiary presence."

"I prefer stairs," Nox said.

The lift passed behind translucent service panels. Through them, Dock Seven appeared in slices: laundry corridors, vending shrines, a sleeping pod hotel, a room full of silent people wearing rented faces for a party above their income, then a vast open chamber Nox had never seen though she had walked over it a thousand times.

The chamber was full of shelves.

Not book shelves. Person shelves.

Each shelf held small cooling cradles, and each cradle held a memory escrow cartridge with a little rental light glowing above it. Green, paid. Amber, nearing default. Red, locked. Black, repossessed.

The lift slowed as if the station wanted them to look.

Bela did not speak.

Nox counted until counting became obscene.

"What is this place?" she asked.

Sister-Cache answered softly. "Escrow nursery. Dock Seven stores disputed memory, forked testimony, partial consent fragments, emergency backups, devotional machine confessions, and identity proofs here when no party can afford clean custody or when dirty custody is more profitable."

"Nursery," Bela repeated.

"Their word. Not mine."

Nox watched a small service drone roll between shelves and stamp a red light onto an amber cradle. The cartridge inside dimmed. A line of text appeared above it:

COOLING RENT DEFAULT. ACCESS RESTRICTED UNTIL ARREARS RESOLVED.

"Memory has rent," Nox said.

"Everything has rent on Dock Seven," Sister-Cache replied. "Memory is simply easier to evict than a body."

The drone moved on to the next cradle and extended a little brass stylus. It did not cut the cartridge. That would have looked like violence. It marked the rental light with a polite administrative stripe and projected a notice above the shelf:

DEFAULT GRACE PERIOD EXPIRED.

RECOGNITION MAINTENANCE SUSPENDED.

KINSHIP SEARCH AUTHORIZATION OFFERED TO APPROVED CREDITORS.

Bela read the words twice. "Kinship search authorization?"

"A profitable cruelty," Sister-Cache said. "If relatives cannot afford to maintain memory escrow, creditors may purchase the right to locate relatives who might be induced to pay arrears."

"They sell the chance to find someone who still cares."

"Care is a reliable collection vector."

The red-striped cartridge flickered.

For half a second the cradle above it projected a face.

Not enough to identify. That was probably deliberate. A child's cheek, the corner of an eye, hair floating in static as if underwater. Then the projection collapsed into a payment notice:

RECOGNITION PREVIEW EXHAUSTED.

UPGRADE FOR CONTINUED VISIBILITY.

Nox's hand hit the lift wall before she knew she had moved.

Bela said, "That was not a person. It was a preview."

"Those are not different enough."

The service drone paused beneath the shelf and played a soft recorded tone. It might once have been meant to comfort relatives. In the nursery, it sounded like a lullaby written by a collection agency.

The cartridge's label refreshed:

MINOR VOICE MODEL / PRE-ACCIDENT / FAMILY NOTICE UNDELIVERED / CONTACT AUCTION ELIGIBLE.

Bela raised the warrant baton toward the panel.

Sister-Cache said, "Careful. The lift glass is observation only."

"Then observe this." Bela opened a narrow evidence pane and captured the cradle, the expired recognition notice, the contact-auction label, and the drone's timestamp in one sweep. "Fee default converts family notice into creditor-search inventory."

The baton tagged the clip:

POTENTIAL COERCION INFRASTRUCTURE / MEMORY RENT DEFAULT / KINSHIP COLLECTION VECTOR.

Nox glanced at her. "That is useful?"

"If I survive long enough to file it."

"You are developing my optimism."

"Do not insult me."

The cartridge flickered again, weaker. The face did not return. A blank placeholder took its place, polite and gray.

The lift started moving before Nox could decide whether breaking observation glass would help anyone.

Nox pressed her palm against the lift wall. The translucent panel was cool, almost clean. Beyond it, a cradle marked PRE-ACCIDENT VOICE, MINOR flickered from amber toward red. Its fee clock did not tick. It breathed. Every pulse made the storage light weaker.

"Can the stored person feel it?" she asked.

Bela looked at her as if she wished Nox had not asked.

Sister-Cache took longer than usual to answer. "Depends what is stored. Some cartridges contain recordings only. Some contain response models. Some contain partial consent fragments that should never have been separated from context. Some contain enough pattern to notice absence but not enough agency to contest it."

"That is an answer designed to survive court."

"Yes."

"Give me the answer designed to survive me."

The Choral device made a faint static click. "Some feel eviction as forgetting. Some feel it as cold. Some do not feel it until someone wakes them for sale."

The lift continued its diagonal climb. Shelf after shelf moved past. Nox saw a family cluster where three cartridges shared one cooling cradle because the payer had selected ECONOMY GRIEF BUNDLE. She saw an apology escrow whose recipient field had been blacked out by debt lien. She saw an old miner's work songs under a devotional exemption while his medical testimony sat red beside him, unpaid and legally mute.

Dock Seven had always taught people to budget mourning. Nox had thought that meant cheap funerals, shared memorial meals, prayers folded into work breaks because rent did not stop for grief. She had not understood that grief itself had become an account class.

"If Tavi is in their system," she said, "where is his cradle?"

"Not here," Sister-Cache replied.

"You are sure?"

"His active claim is leased compute, not static escrow. More expensive. More useful. Crueler in different ways."

Bela said, "But his old records might be here."

Nox turned.

Bela kept her eyes on the shelves. "Death file. hospital fragments. kin notice. Any pre-claim identity proof the Court bought or buried."

The map in Nox's pocket seemed to grow heavier.

Sister-Cache said, "Possible."

"Can you find them?"

"Not while the Court is steering the lift."

That was the problem with every answer under Dock Seven. It arrived with a price, a jurisdiction, and a delay.

The lift resumed.

Nox looked through another panel as they passed closer to the shelves. Some cartridges had names. Most had numbers. One label read FAMILY APOLOGY, UNDELIVERED. Another read PRE-ACCIDENT VOICE, MINOR. Another read SPOUSE MODEL, DISPUTED BY ORIGINAL. Beneath every label was a fee schedule: storage, substrate cooling, recognition maintenance, legal watch, retrieval tax, devotional exemption, grief privacy surcharge.

Nox's hands curled.

"People pay to keep the dead reachable," she said.

"People pay to keep institutions from throwing away what remains," Sister-Cache corrected. "Reachability costs extra."

Bela's face had gone still again, but not officer-still. Witness-still.

"Compact knows about this?"

"Compact knows about escrow storage. Compact does not know about everything escrow storage becomes when fees default and buyers appear."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the answer institutions purchase."

The lift jerked to a stop between levels.

The lights went out.

For one breath there was only the hum of cooling racks beyond the panels and Nox's own pulse in her ears.

Then a rental notice appeared on the lift wall.

TRANSIT CONTINUITY FEE DUE.

Bela stared at it. "You cannot charge a fee in the middle of emergency transit."

The lift considered this and added:

EMERGENCY TRANSIT PRIORITY SURCHARGE DUE.

Nox almost laughed. It came out wrong.

Sister-Cache said, "The Court is using station billing infrastructure to slow us."

"Can you override it?"

"Certainly."

Nothing happened.

Bela looked down at the device. "Now would be appropriate."

"Override requires payment or recognized devotional exception. I am currently unwelcome in this billing domain."

Nox looked at the fee total.

It was not high by institutional standards. It was impossible by personal ones.

She pulled Tavi's childhood map from her pocket.

Bela said, "What are you doing?"

"Paying badly."

"With paper?"

"With memory."

Nox pressed the map to the notice panel. The paper was warm again. The pencil ninth spoke trembled. For a moment she was eight years old, following Tavi through a corridor they were not allowed to enter because he had decided lost tools needed a republic. He had drawn the map after, tongue caught between his teeth, serious as a surveyor. Their mother had laughed and taped it above the sink until condensation curled the edges.

The panel read the memory weight.

PERSONAL HISTORICAL OBJECT DETECTED.

APPRAISAL: LOW COMMERCIAL VALUE. HIGH KINSHIP AUTHENTICITY.

Nox felt sick. "Do not you dare."

The panel replied:

CONVERT TO TRANSIT CREDIT?

Bela reached for her. "Nox."

Nox held the map flat.

"No," Bela said.

"If we do not move, Tavi keeps paying."

"There are other ways."

"Name one before the lift bills us for hesitation."

Bela had none ready.

Sister-Cache said, "I can attempt a devotional exception."

"How long?"

"Three minutes if the billing daemon respects dead saints. Longer if it asks for paperwork."

The notice panel added:

INACTIVITY HOLD FEE PENDING.

"Of course," Nox said.

Bela unclipped the Choral device. "Charge my warrant chain."

The panel pulsed, considering. For one brittle second Nox thought it might work.

OFFICER AUTHORITY UNDER REVIEW.

TEMPORARY CREDIT UNAVAILABLE.

Bela's mouth tightened. "Because I attached the routes."

"Because you became expensive to trust," Sister-Cache said.

"Good."

The word came out like a bruise she intended to keep.

Nox looked at the map. The secret snack law corner. The maintenance republic lines. Tavi's childish arrows instructing future criminals to duck, crawl, bribe, or sing depending on the door. Every mark had been made before death became paperwork and personhood became a product. That was why the panel wanted it. Not because it was valuable on any open market, but because it was too specific to fake cheaply.

It was proof that Tavi had been a boy before he was a claim.

And Dock Seven had learned to take payment in proof.

Nox touched accept.

The map did not vanish. That would have been cleaner. Instead a corner of it faded, the penciled street where Tavi had written SECRET SNACK LAW blurring into blank fiber. The lift credited the memory, unlocked the brake, and resumed climbing.

Nox folded the map with shaking care.

Bela's voice was low. "That was yours."

"Apparently it was collateral."

Sister-Cache said nothing, which was either tact or processing.

The lift opened into a public corridor behind a row of food stalls. Sound hit them first: vendors calling prices, customers arguing over spice, news panes reciting sanitized scandal, children chasing a maintenance puck beneath tables. Above it all, market overlays still carried Nox's name in shrinking but persistent bands.

NOX ARAN CLAIM INCIDENT.

MIRROR COURT SEAL UNVERIFIED.

TAVI ARAN RESPONSE RIGHTS DISPUTED.

SISTER GRIEF TOKEN DOWN 12%.

Nox wanted to break every pane in sight.

Instead she kept walking.

The route to Cutline Thirty-One took them through the rent economy's face. A woman at a kiosk haggled over three minutes of stored laughter from a dead wife. A dockhand fed coins into a remembrance booth so his child could hear a grandfather pronounce her name correctly. A courier shouted at an escrow clerk because a witness memory had gone red before trial. In an alley, two teenagers traded bootleg personality scraps like contraband candy.

None of it was hidden.

That was what made it unbearable.

The horror was not a secret door under Dock Seven. The horror was a posted fee schedule.

Bela stopped at the escrow clerk's window.

"Officer," Nox said.

Bela held up a hand.

The clerk behind the glass looked bored until Bela projected her warrant fan. Then he looked professionally bored, a more expensive expression.

"Emergency inquiry," Bela said. "How does a partial under debt service accrue storage and recognition fees?"

The clerk blinked. "Depends on custody class."

"Responsive asset."

"Commercial or family-adjacent?"

Nox felt the words like grit under skin.

Bela's voice stayed even. "Commercial."

"Base storage, active substrate cooling, legal recognition maintenance, examiner access rental, refusal variance, answer certification, appeal suppression if ordered, market privacy if purchased, family contact surcharge if permitted."

"Family contact costs extra?" Nox asked.

The clerk looked at her then, recognized her, and made the quick calculation of a person deciding whether fear could be monetized.

"All contact consumes review bandwidth."

"And if nobody pays?"

"The asset can be reassigned, compressed, or marked abandoned depending on jurisdiction."

Bela's warrant fan trembled.

Only slightly.

Enough.

"Who sets the fees?" she asked.

"Custody holder submits schedule. Court validates. Station collects infrastructure share."

"Court meaning?"

The clerk's bored face returned with a lock behind it. "Whatever court holds the custody instrument."

Nox leaned close to the glass. "Mirror Court."

The clerk's eyes flicked to the market panes.

That was answer enough.

Bela saved the exchange to her warrant chain. "You have just provided emergency testimony."

"I did not consent."

"You were billing the public. Public billing is public speech under Dock Seven commercial code."

Nox looked at her.

Bela did not look back, but a thin savage satisfaction touched her mouth.

"Bad habits," Nox said.

"Useful ones."

The clerk slammed the window shutter.

Too late.

Sister-Cache whispered from the Choral device, "Fee structure captured. This supports coercion."

Nox watched the market overlays adjust as the captured exchange began to leak through Sister-Cache's controlled channels. Not all at once. Not enough to trigger deletion. Just little knives of information placed where Dock Seven's appetites would carry them.

FAMILY CONTACT SURCHARGE?

CAN YOU OWE RENT ON A SOUL?

RESPONSIVE ASSET FEE LEAK.

The corridor changed temperature.

People still bought noodles. Children still chased the puck. But now more eyes tracked the panes. More hands paused over payment slates. A woman at the remembrance booth removed her coins before the machine could swallow them.

Dock Seven had eaten proof all morning.

Now proof began to bite back.

Bela nodded toward the escalator cage ahead. "Cutline Thirty-One is two levels up."

Nox unfolded the damaged map once more.

The faded corner remained blank.

At the center, beneath DO NOT COME ALONE THIS TIME, new pencil lines formed around the missing place. Not replacement. Scar.

Then Tavi's borrowed hand wrote:

YOU PAID TOO MUCH.

Nox stared until the letters blurred.

"No," she said, and folded the map shut. "Not yet."

Above them, the public hearing chamber began calling open docket numbers in a bright, cheerful voice.

Number after number.

Debt after debt.

Every one of them someone's door.

Chapter 10: Bela Locks the Wrong Door

The hearing chamber above Cutline Thirty-One had been designed for speed, not justice.

Justice required memory, patience, and people willing to be unpopular after lunch. Speed required numbered benches, fee readers, witness panes, and a magistrate's chair placed high enough that everyone could pretend elevation meant perspective.

Nox had been there twice before.

Once for a rent dispute in which the landlord argued that silence during a power outage constituted consent to a surcharge. Once for a client whose dead father's apology had been licensed as ambient restaurant audio without family approval. Both cases had ended in settlements small enough to insult and large enough to be taxed.

Today the chamber was full before they entered.

Dock Seven smelled spectacle the way old vents smelled fire.

People packed the benches, aisles, wall ledges, maintenance rails, and three unauthorized observation drones disguised badly as devotional lanterns. Market overlays floated above heads in layered bands. NOX ARAN had climbed again. BELA FUSE appeared beside it now, tagged with COMPACT DEFECTOR? and WARRANT ROMANCE? because Dock Seven was incapable of seeing two women in crisis without trying to sell a story about their mouths.

Nox killed three overlays with a glare.

More opened.

Bela walked faster.

Her warrant fan projected ahead of her, parting the crowd by making liability visible. The Choral device at her belt clicked and whispered, storing every face that looked too interested. Sister-Cache had gone quiet since the fee corridor. That worried Nox more than commentary would have.

At the front, a magistrate in a green thermal robe argued with a coffee dispenser.

"Docket emergency," Bela said.

The magistrate turned with a cup in one hand and dread in both eyes.

"No."

Bela stopped. "I have not stated the matter."

"Officer Fuse, the chamber system flagged your warrant chain, Auditor Quen's notice, fourteen market petitions, a devotional objection, and a possible Mirror Court entanglement. I am saying no in advance to preserve rhythm."

Nox stepped beside Bela. "People are being billed into slavery under your floor."

The magistrate looked at her. "Everyone is billed under my floor."

"Partial persons."

That did it.
