Not enough to make him brave. Enough to make him understand the shape of cowardice being recorded around him.

The chamber audience quieted in layers.

Bela projected her warrant fan into the central evidence well.

"I move to amend seizure order FUSE-4431 from derivative identity-material seizure to emergency protective custody review concerning possible coerced partial persons held through responsive-asset debt instruments."

The chamber system chimed.

AMENDMENT FEE REQUIRED.

Nox looked up at the ceiling. "I will bite this station."

The magistrate slapped the dispenser cup onto his desk. "Waive emergency fee pending review."

WAIVER FEE REQUIRED.

The crowd laughed because the alternative was screaming.

Sister-Cache spoke from Bela's belt. "I can route through devotional indigence exception."

Bela said, "Do it."

"It will mark your filing as prayer-adjacent."

"Do it."

The chamber lights flickered.

FEE WAIVED UNDER MINOR MACHINE MERCY PRACTICE.

The magistrate rubbed his forehead. "I will be hearing about that from three committees."

"People in proof frames," Bela said.

"Yes. Proceed."

Nox watched Bela step fully into the evidence well. It was a circle of dull glass meant to show whether a witness lied. It mostly showed whether a witness could afford better nerves. Bela stood in it without flinching.

"I submit preliminary field acknowledgement, captured fee testimony, custody route fragments, and live chain anomalies indicating the use of responsive partials as debt instruments."

The chamber accepted the packet.

Then it rejected it.

CHAIN COMPROMISED.

The audience murmured.

Bela's jaw tightened.

The magistrate spread his hands. "Officer Fuse, without clean chain I cannot convert custody."

Nox stepped forward. "The chain is compromised because the Court duplicated evidence while using her warrant to claim everything as seizure material."

"That may be true."

"You can hear it from your chair."

"Truth and admissibility are cousins who do not visit often."

The line got a few laughs. Nox hated all of them.

Bela suddenly lifted the warrant baton and turned away from the magistrate.

"What are you doing?" Nox asked.

"Fixing chain."

"Here?"

"No."

Bela expanded the warrant fan until it filled the evidence well. Its blue panes opened like a map. At the center sat Nox's workshop, still under seizure, still marked by Pellam's original entry claim, still packed with tools, mirrors, disabled witness drone residue, and the threshold plate where Tavi's authorization had first broken open.

Nox's stomach dropped.

"Do not."

Bela did not look at her. "Remote enforcement action under active seizure order. Lock target premises. Preserve all physical evidence, access caches, mirror residues, disabled drone buffers, tool states, and threshold damage against debt-office removal, market salvage, and Compact overwrite."

Nox grabbed her arm. "My shop is full of illegal tools."

"Yes."

"You are preserving enough evidence to bury me."

"Yes."

"Bela."

For the first time since the hearing began, Bela looked at her.

"Pellam's office has secondary access. If I do not lock it under my chain, they clean it. If I lock it, everything that proves how this started survives."

Nox understood then and hated understanding.

Bela was not saving Nox's workshop.

She was freezing it.

Every illegal clamp, every false provenance slip, every tool Nox had used to keep poor clients from being eaten by clean law, every compromise and mercy and felony. Preserved. Indexed. Available. A blade pointed at Nox's throat.

And wrapped around the blade, evidence Pellam could not touch.

"You are locking the wrong door," Nox said.

Bela's voice was quiet. "I know."

The warrant fan flared.

REMOTE SEAL EXECUTED.

NOX ARAN WORKSHOP: COMPACT PROTECTIVE EVIDENCE LOCK.

The central fan split into live panes.

Nox's workshop appeared from six angles she had never authorized anyone to see. The ceiling seam above the repair bench. The low corner behind the false solvent cabinet. The threshold plate, scratched by years of boots and one impossible active claim. The wall where she kept client charms because people trusted charms more than receipts. The sink with a cracked mug Tavi had once stolen from a legal office because it said EVIDENCE LOVES PROCEDURE and he had thought that was the funniest sentence in any language.

The chamber saw everything.

Illegal memory clamps hung beside patched devotional recorders. Kin-consent templates lay under a heat stone. Three old witness drone shells sat open on the bench, their buffers tagged with Nox's lazy shorthand. A drawer that should have stayed private slid open under remote inventory and displayed forged provenance seals sorted by quality and mercy.

The crowd made the sound crowds make when they discover scandal has furniture.

Nox felt each item enter the public chain like a hook through skin.

Bela's voice did not change. "Inventory all items. Classify as protected pending trafficking-risk review. Prevent creditor removal. Prevent public salvage. Prevent Compact overwrite without auditor concurrence."

The warrant fan tagged item after item.

ILLICIT TOOL.

POSSIBLE CLIENT-SAFETY DEVICE.

UNLICENSED TEMPLATE.

KIN-NOTICE REPAIR RECORD.

THRESHOLD ANOMALY.

TAVI ARAN ACTIVE CLAIM ENTRY TRACE.

The final tag pulsed brighter than the rest.

Nox focused on that or she would start shouting and never stop.

On one pane, the threshold replayed the moment from earlier that night. Not as memory. As heat, timing, residue, access permissions, and a sliver of impossible authorization. Tavi's active claim had not simply unlocked the door. It had negotiated with the door like a person who remembered where the latch stuck.

The chamber system tried to classify the trace.

ACCESS FRAUD.

Then:

KIN-AUTHORITY ECHO.

Then:

RESPONSIVE PERSONHOOD INDICATOR.

The last phrase made the benches go still.

Bela saw it too. Something in her shoulders loosened and tightened at once.

Nox whispered, "You found him at my door."

"He found you first," Bela said.

The chamber system reconsidered the rejected packet.

NEW CHAIN ANCHOR DETECTED.

THRESHOLD CACHE RESIDUE VERIFIED.

WITNESS DRONE BUFFER RECOVERABLE.

DEBT OFFICE ENTRY CLAIM PRESERVED.

Pellam Vey appeared on a side screen before anyone called him.

He was sweating now.

Not visibly, if one were kind. Nox was not.

"This chamber lacks jurisdiction," he said.

The magistrate looked delighted to have someone else to dislike. "Most chambers do. We continue."

Pellam's eyes found Bela. "Officer Fuse, you have exceeded your warrant."

"I amended my preservation scope."

"You exposed a protected recovery pool."

"Your pool used a dead man's active claim to enter a private workshop."

"Alleged."

Nox pointed at the central fan, where the threshold cache residue now replayed in clean blue lines.

TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE IS ACTIVE.

The chamber went very quiet.

No market overlay could improve it.

Sister-Cache whispered, "Now they all own seeing it."

That was the thing about public proof on Dock Seven. Once enough people watched, deletion became expensive. Not impossible. Expensive. Sometimes expensive bought time.

The magistrate leaned forward.

"Officer Fuse, your remote lock has anchored the original entry event. I accept the amended chain provisionally. Submit basis for emergency protective custody review."

Bela exhaled once.

Then she turned to Nox.

"I need you in the well."

Nox looked at the dull glass circle.

"As what?"

"Expert witness."

The crowd loved that.

MEMORY FORGER AS EXPERT?

FRAUD QUEEN TESTIFIES.

SISTER SAVES BROTHER OR SELF?

Nox stepped into the evidence well because every other direction led back to Tavi paying for jokes.

The glass under her boots warmed.

The magistrate said, "State your qualifications."

Nox almost gave him the official lie. Independent memory repair technician. Provenance consultant. Identity continuity specialist.

Instead she said, "I forge doors for people the law prices out."

Bela closed her eyes.

The crowd surged.

The evidence well liked confessions. That was one of its uglier features. It brightened under her feet, not because truth had become cleaner, but because self-damage was easy for machines to recognize.

Nox raised her voice before the chamber could turn her into a spectacle without terms.

"A door is never just a door here. It is a rent filter, a citizenship test, a grief valve, a debt collector, a family argument with hinges. Poor people come to me when the official door says their dead mother is a privacy violation, their living child is an account error, their own name is insufficiently documented, or their apology costs more than the harm it is apologizing for."

The crowd quieted again, but differently this time.

Some faces turned away from the workshop panes. Not out of shame exactly. Recognition.

"I forged because clean access was built to exclude them," Nox said. "I also forged because I was arrogant, angry, and good at it. Both facts are true. If you want a witness with clean hands, ask the people who keep selling soap to the blood."

The magistrate's cup hovered over the desk.

Sister-Cache whispered, "STRONG OPENING. LEGALLY UNWISE."

"Story of my life."

The evidence well printed:

MOTIVE ADMISSION CAPTURED.

PUBLIC INTEREST RELEVANCE: HIGH.

SELF-EXCULPATION ATTEMPT: LOW.

Nox looked down. "I hate this floor."

The magistrate found his voice. "Ms. Aran, your contempt for lawful access systems is not itself expertise."

"No," Nox said. "Surviving their failure is."

The magistrate's mouth twitched. "That is not usually how qualifications are presented."

"It is the part that matters."

Sister-Cache hummed approval.

Nox pointed to the replayed threshold cache. "A bad forgery smooths a person into what a door expects. This did the opposite. It carried useless private errors, family rhythm, childhood phrase leakage, delayed response habits, ethical resistance, and live adaptation under novel prompts. Whoever built the Tavi claim did not just copy a signature. They kept enough of a person to make him useful, then charged him when personhood interfered with use."

The chamber glass brightened under her.

TRUTH STRESS: HIGH.

STATEMENT COHERENCE: STABLE.

PROFESSIONAL SELF-INCRIMINATION: NOTED.

Nox looked down. "Rude."

The magistrate said, "Can this assessment generalize beyond Tavi Aran?"

That was the door.

The Court's door.

Brother or all.

Nox felt Bela beside her without looking. Felt the damaged map in her pocket. Felt the blank corner where a memory had become transit credit. Felt Tavi's last word through sealed glass.

"Yes," she said. "If Sister-Cache's captured routes show the same pattern across partials, I can authenticate the coercive structure without needing family access to every victim."

The evidence well brightened.

METHOD REQUIRED.

Nox looked down. "Of course you want a method."

The magistrate leaned forward. "So do I."

Nox took a breath and forced herself not to look at the black mirror where the Court had objected. "Three layers. First, noncontent markers: timing drift, hesitation under cost, refusal placement, repetition that preserves self rather than solves prompts, and value deviation when the prompt hides harm. Those can be compared without exposing private memory."

Sister-Cache clicked. "I can supply route-stripped timing maps."

"Second," Nox said, "billing correlation. If answer, refusal, confusion, silence, and damaged recall all generate recoverable value for the holder, then the frame is not a venue. It is a restraint machine."

Bela's warrant fan folded a new pane beside her:

ANSWER VALUE.

REFUSAL VARIANCE.

SILENCE SURCHARGE.

MEMORY DEFECT DEGRADATION.

FAMILY CONTACT REVIEW BANDWIDTH.

Nox pointed at the list. "Third, source boundary. We show where a partial gives something too adaptive for static replay but refuse to publish the intimate content that made it recognizable. No dead mother's voice. No child's private anchor. No grief phrase unless the person inside the frame chooses it for the record."

The evidence well processed.

PRIVACY-PRESERVING EXPERT METHOD: PROVISIONAL.

PUBLIC INTEREST: HIGH.

MARKET VALUE LOST BY REDACTION: HIGH.

"Good," Nox said. "Let the market suffer."

The benches muttered. Some in outrage. Some in relief.

Pellam spoke sharply from the screen. "Redaction prevents challenge."

"No," Bela said. "It prevents resale. Challenge can occur under sealed review."

The magistrate gave Bela a narrow look. "You are asking this chamber to trust a criminal forger, a compromised officer, and an unclassified machine archive."

Sister-Cache said, "When phrased that way, it sounds lively."

Nox did not smile. "You are already trusting creditors who bill prisoners for refusing to work."

The evidence well warmed under her boots, as if pleased by conflict.

Pellam spoke sharply from the screen. "The witness admits criminal expertise and personal bias."

Nox smiled at him.

"Yes."

The magistrate stared.

Nox kept smiling because fear deserved manners.

"That is why you should listen. Criminal expertise is what your clean systems outsource when they want dirty work done quietly. Personal bias is what tells me when a person is being used as a lockpick. You used my brother as a door key. Bela locked my shop to prove it. Wrong door, right evidence."

The chamber erupted.

The magistrate hammered for silence with a cup because nobody had given him a gavel.

Above the noise, one market overlay spread faster than all the others:

WRONG DOOR, RIGHT EVIDENCE.

Bela looked at Nox then, and for half a second the glass well held every argument they had ever lost.

Pellam vanished from the side screen.

Not disconnected.

Replaced.

The screen became black mirror.

White text appeared across it.

THE MIRROR COURT OBJECTS.

Chapter 11: Aunt Varo's Consent Box

The Mirror Court's objection froze the hearing chamber for exactly four seconds.

Then Dock Seven remembered objections could be sold.

Every pane in the room split into commentary. Legal odds. Reaction frames. Merchant summaries. Prayer interpretations. One vulgar overlay offered commemorative cups with THE MIRROR COURT OBJECTS printed in reflective ink before the magistrate had finished finding his breath.

Bela seized the moment before anyone else could.

"Emergency continuance requested," she said.

The magistrate blinked. "You just asked for public review."

"The objecting party has entered appearance without service, identity, or standing. I need time to attach additional witness material before the chamber system lets it eat the docket."

The black screen displayed no new text.

That made it worse.

Nox knew quiet doors. Quiet doors had already decided what they would charge.

The magistrate looked from the black mirror to the crowd, then to his coffee cup, as if one of them might contain courage. "Fifteen minutes."

"Thirty," Bela said.

"Ten."

"Twenty."

The chamber system chimed.

BARGAINING FEE REQUIRED.

The magistrate threw the cup at the ceiling sensor. It missed, but the sentiment landed.

"Twenty minutes," he snapped. "No fee. If the chamber bills me for tone, I will retire inside its complaint queue."

Sister-Cache whispered from Bela's belt, "Unexpectedly attractive jurisprudence."

Nox stepped out of the evidence well before the glass could decide to invoice her confession. "We need Varo."

Bela turned. "Your aunt?"

"Family elder. Not aunt by blood. Aunt by surviving my mother."

"Why her?"

"Because Tavi signed something before the accident. A medical memory escrow. I thought it was routine clinic trash. If the Court built a claim strong enough to answer doors, they needed more than grief letters and access logs."

Bela's face sharpened. "You think the escrow was source material."

"I think I should have looked harder two years ago."

"That is not evidence."

"No. It is motive wearing teeth."

The magistrate's temporary continuance turned the chamber into a feeding hall. People did not leave. They repositioned. Market runners crowded the exits. Two devotional lantern drones pretended harder to be lanterns. The black screen remained black, and the absence of words pulled attention like a wound.

Bela guided Nox through a side passage with her warrant fan angled low.

"Where is Varo?"

"Old Habitation Ring. Cutline Twenty. If she has not moved to spite census."

"We have twenty minutes."

"Then stop walking like an officer."

"I am an officer."

"Exactly."

Nox took a service stair used by food couriers, apology singers, and anyone who owed money to people watching the lifts. Bela followed without complaint, which meant she understood the time or had chosen not to spend breath on being right. Sister-Cache hummed in short bursts, transmitting controlled fragments of the hearing into channels Nox did not want to imagine.

Outside the chamber, Dock Seven had changed around them.

Not physically. Dock Seven rarely needed to move its walls to become dangerous. It changed by attention. People looked at Nox and then looked away too late. Doors hesitated before opening, not because they rejected her but because they wanted a record of having considered it. A noodle vendor offered her free broth, then asked whether she would authenticate the bowl for resale after she touched it.

Bela moved the vendor aside with one glance.

"That was rude," Nox said.

"He was about to monetize your fingerprints."

"I know. I wanted the broth."

"You can have broth after the Court stops objecting."

"You make bleak promises."

They crossed through Cutline Twenty's old ring, where the station wore its early poverty openly. The corridors were narrower here, built for bodies that had not expected their children to inherit them. Patch plates overlapped patch plates. Family names were painted above doors in layers, the living over the dead over the evicted over the people who still received mail because nobody had the heart to scrape them away.

Varo lived behind a green door with seven locks and no welcome strip.

The door did not scan them.

It shouted through a speaker older than Nox.

"If you are selling grief, I already bought enough."

Nox put her forehead against the door. "It's me."

"That is not an improvement."

"Varo."

"Is there an officer with you?"

Bela said, "Yes."

"Then it is definitely not an improvement."

Locks snapped open in a sequence that sounded like someone loading a small weapon.

Aunt Varo stood in the doorway wearing a quilted pressure robe, magnetic slippers, and an expression that had once frightened three landlords into fixing heat. She was shorter than Nox remembered, which was offensive because Nox had not given her permission to become old. Her hair was white now, braided tight against her skull. One eye was natural. The other was a cheap lens that focused with a little click of accusation.

She looked Nox over.

"You are bleeding."

"Not as a hobby."

"That is how you do most things."

Her gaze moved to Bela.

"You hurt her?"

Bela answered before Nox could. "Not today."

Varo's eyebrow lifted. "Honest. Suspicious."

She let them in.

The apartment smelled of hot metal, bitter tea, and the starch candy Tavi used to steal from a jar shaped like a moon. Nox saw the jar on the counter. Empty. Of course empty. Varo kept empty containers as accusations against time.

On the wall above the table hung family things no market could price correctly: a cracked festival mask, Nox's mother's work gloves, Tavi's first station ID with the photo where he had blinked, a ribbon from a school race he had lost but kept because he liked the color.

Nox looked away from the ID too late.

Varo saw.

"Sit."

"We do not have time."

"Then stand badly. What have you done?"

"Tavi is in a debt frame."

Varo did not gasp.

That was the first proof.

She sat down very slowly.

Nox felt the floor drop, though it did not. "You knew."

"I knew something was wrong."

"That is not the same."

"No. It is what poor families get instead of answers."

Nox wanted to shout at her. The want came hot and familiar, easier than terror. Varo had been there after the accident with soup, forms, clean socks, and the particular Dock Seven competence of knowing which clerk could be frightened and which clerk could only be bribed. If Varo had known even a piece of this and kept it folded in a freezer, then every family dinner afterward had been staged over a locked door.

"How long?" Nox asked.

Varo's natural eye held hers. "Since the clinic stopped sending grief notices and started sending account language."

"Which means?"

"Four months after the funeral."

Nox laughed once, empty. "You had this for almost two years."

"I had a box I could not open without breaking seal, a family already broken, and a niece who had turned grief into a profession with sharp tools." Varo's voice did not rise. That made it worse. "I took it to two legal aid counters. One told me to preserve chain. One told me the clinic would sue if I alleged tampering without proof. A debt counselor offered to refinance your mother's arrears if I surrendered the box for review."

Bela said, "Refinance through whom?"

Varo looked at her. "Vey Consolidated Recovery Pool."

The room changed around the name.

Nox stopped wanting to shout. Wanting to shout had been almost kind.

Sister-Cache clicked three times. "Early acquisition attempt."

"Collection man was polite," Varo said. "That is how I knew he was hungry."

Bela activated a small note pane over the warrant baton. "Did he identify the box specifically?"

"He called it unresolved care-continuity material. Said old clinic records could confuse estate closure."

"And you refused."

Varo's expression hardened into something old enough to have raised families under bad air. "I told him my freezer was full."

Nox pictured Varo in this apartment, alone with the empty candy jar and a sealed box that might be nothing, might be everything, might be the kind of evidence that punished the keeper before touching the guilty. She did not forgive the silence. Not yet. But the shape of it became less simple, and she hated that too because simple anger had been useful.

Bela placed her warrant baton on the table but did not activate it. A courtesy. Or strategy. With Bela, the two had always been difficult to separate.

"Ms. Varo," she said, "we need information about a medical memory escrow Tavi Aran signed before his death."

Varo's mouth tightened. "He was seventeen."

Nox stopped breathing for a beat.

"You said signed."

"They said signed." Varo's lens clicked. "Clinic said consent. Insurance said compliance. Salvage office said it would keep his care priority open. Your mother said no. Then they showed her the numbers."

Nox gripped the back of a chair. "What numbers?"

Varo rose and crossed to an old freezer that had not held food in years. She opened it by hand. Inside were papers wrapped in oilcloth, data wafers inside tea tins, and one small gray box with a clinic seal faded almost white.

Nox knew the shape.

Consent box.

Clinics used them when patients or guardians signed memory-related permissions. A sealed unit, supposedly tamper-evident, containing the consent transcript, biometric token, witness hash, and revocation conditions. Respectable families stored them in legal offices. Dock Seven families hid them in broken appliances and hoped fire chose another room.

Varo set the box on the table.

Nox did not touch it.

Bela scanned without opening. "Seal is old."

"Old does not mean clean," Varo said.

"No," Bela agreed.

The consent box carried three locks and one lie.

The locks were visible: clinic hash, witness ribbon, family custody tab. Cheap medical plastic trying to impersonate permanence. The lie was the little green dot beside the custody tab, still glowing after two years as if patience were proof of safety.

Bela projected the scan into a hand-sized pane.

CHAIN STATUS:

CLINIC SEAL: INTACT.

WITNESS HASH: PARTIAL / NURSE ID MASKED.

FAMILY CUSTODY TAB: UNBROKEN.

EXTERNAL QUERY ATTEMPTS: 17.

Nox looked at Varo.

Varo lifted her chin. "I told you people kept knocking."

"Seventeen."

"Some knocks are quiet enough you only hear them later."

Bela expanded the query list. Most were clinic maintenance pings. Two were insurance review attempts. One came from an estate closure office. Four were masked under debt-counseling service IDs. The last, dated six weeks earlier, carried no origin at all, only a seal impression: black oval, silver door line.

The Mirror Court had tried the freezer.

Nox's anger shifted again, less like flame now and more like a door being welded shut.

"Why didn't it open?" she asked.

Varo tapped the family custody tab. "Because I never updated the household death record."

Bela looked up. "That keeps the family tab alive?"

"It keeps the office confused. Confused offices send notices before they send knives."

Sister-Cache made a delighted little sound. "Excellent primitive resistance."

Varo glared at the Choral device. "I call it paperwork."

Bela saved the query list. "This proves external interest before tonight."

"It proves they hunted the box," Nox said.

"Yes."

Varo's natural eye went to the faded seal. "It proves I was not only old and frightened."

Nox had no answer that would not break something useful.

Nox found her voice. "Why did nobody tell me?"

Varo's natural eye softened. The lens did not.

"Because you were at the clinic breaking a clerk's nose."

"After."

"Before after. During after. All those hours were one long room." Varo touched the box with two fingers. "Tavi had taken a maintenance fall three weeks earlier. Small one. Concussion, they said. Observation, they said. Then the clinic offered a memory escrow discount tied to emergency care priority. Your mother refused until the insurer said refusal could delay coverage if there was another accident."

Nox heard Tavi's voice: No one gets born owing air.

"He signed under pressure."

"He signed because your mother was crying in the hall and he wanted the nurse to stop saying risk category."

Bela said, "That may invalidate consent."

Varo laughed once. It was not amused. "May. What a rich person's word."

Sister-Cache spoke from Bela's belt, unusually gentle. "Open the box."

Varo looked at the Choral device. "Is that a machine in my niece's police problem?"

"Archivist," Sister-Cache said.

"That is a machine with opinions."

"Often."

"Good. Machines without opinions charge fees."

Varo pushed the consent box toward Nox.

"You open it. It is family pain. Family hands first."

Nox wanted to refuse. She wanted Bela to do it, or Sister-Cache, or Auditor Quen from whatever screen she haunted. She wanted the past to remain incompetent instead of malicious. But Tavi was paying in a proof frame, and the box had sat cold for two years while she mistook not knowing for having been spared.

She broke the seal.

The box unfolded into a small projection above the table.

Tavi appeared.

Not the proof-frame Tavi. Not the door voice. Tavi at seventeen in a clinic smock too big for him, hair flattened on one side, trying to look bored because fear embarrassed him. Nox's mother stood behind him with one hand over her mouth. A nurse was present, face politely obscured by privacy law. The consent prompt hovered beside Tavi's shoulder.

MEDICAL MEMORY ESCROW FOR EMERGENCY CARE CONTINUITY.

Scope: diagnostic recall, motor baseline, speech pattern, consent preference backup.

Commercial reuse: prohibited.

Debt attachment: prohibited.

Derivative continuity claim: prohibited.

Bela leaned closer. "Those prohibitions are explicit."

Varo said, "Clinic had a good lawyer."

Nox watched Tavi scan the document too fast.

The nurse said, "This preserves care options in the event of future injury."

Tavi said, "Does it cost extra?"

"The escrow discount reduces current risk category fees."

"That means yes in billing language."

The nurse did not smile.

Nox's mother whispered, "Tavi, we can leave."

Tavi looked back at her. There it was: the half-second delay after door prompts, the same little spiteful pause. Then he faced the box.

"No commercial reuse," he said.

"Correct."

"No debt attachment."

"Correct."

"No making me into work."

The nurse paused.

Too long.

Bela said, "There."

Sister-Cache clicked. "Marked."

In the projection, the nurse recovered. "The escrow is for care continuity only."

Tavi lifted his hand to the consent pane.

Before he touched it, he looked directly into the recorder.

"Nox will be angry if this gets weird."

Nox made a sound she did not recognize.

The projection continued because records had no mercy.

Tavi pressed accept.

CONSENT RECORDED.

Then, beneath the visible text, another line flickered for one frame.

Bela caught it. So did Sister-Cache. So did Nox, because forgery had trained her to see where documents blinked.

SECONDARY CONTINUITY OPTION: DEFERRED.

"Back," Nox said.

Bela replayed the frame.

The line appeared again, nearly invisible.

SECONDARY CONTINUITY OPTION: DEFERRED BY GUARDIAN NONPAYMENT.

Varo's hands curled on the table.

"Guardian nonpayment," she said. "They turned refusal into debt."

Sister-Cache's voice sharpened. "Not refusal. Deferred option. A dormant commercial hook."

Bela was already copying the frame into her amended warrant. "This connects medical escrow to later debt attachment despite explicit prohibition."

The consent projection stuttered as Bela isolated the hidden line. Under magnification, the words were not a line at all but a folded clause, rendered at one-frame visibility and packed into the gap between Tavi's spoken refusal and the nurse's final certification.

Quen's voice entered through Bela's wrist, thin with bad signal and attention. "Do not paraphrase that in chamber."

Bela did not look surprised. "Auditor."

"Read the exact structure."

Bela expanded the clause.

SECONDARY CONTINUITY OPTION:
COMMERCIAL DERIVATIVE PATHWAY NOT SELECTED.
DEFERRED BY GUARDIAN NONPAYMENT.
REVIEWABLE UPON ESTATE DEFICIT EVENT.

Nox leaned over the table. "Commercial derivative pathway not selected means no."

"In human language," Quen said.

Varo's lens clicked rapidly. "In billing language?"

Bela answered, and every word seemed to cost her. "It means the option remains available because the guardian did not pay to close it."

"Pay to make no mean no," Nox said.

Sister-Cache whispered, "Elegant evil. I dislike admiring it."

The projection replayed Tavi's voice.

No making me into work.

The nurse's pause followed.

The hidden clause followed that.

Nox felt something colder than anger move through her. Anger wanted action. This wanted accounting.

"He was a minor," Bela said. "The consent transcript shows explicit scope limits and a verbal prohibition understood by the clinic representative. If Vey later used deferred nonpayment to reopen a commercial pathway, they converted medical care leverage into posthumous labor."

Quen said, "Say that again in chamber with less outrage and more teeth."

"I can do both."

"Good."

Varo looked at the frozen image of Tavi. "Your mother thought the discount was mercy."

Nox did not look away this time. "It was bait."

"Yes."

The word sat on the table beside the box, heavier than the seal.

Nox looked at the projected Tavi, frozen with one hand against the pane and one bad joke still living in his mouth.

"Can it free him?"

Bela did not answer quickly enough.

That was answer.

"It helps the pattern," she said.

"I asked about him."

"I know."

Varo stood, crossed the room, and slapped Nox lightly on the back of the head.

"Ow."

"Do not ask officers questions when you already know the answer and need to keep moving."

Bela looked startled.

Varo pointed at her. "You too. Stop wearing guilt like a uniform. Does this box help?"

"Yes."

"Then take it."

Nox said, "No."

Varo's eyes snapped to her.

"Copy it," Nox said. "The box stays here."

"Child."

"No. They took enough family things and turned them into doors. This stays."

Varo's expression changed then, a small proud grief Nox could not bear directly.

Bela copied the full contents, seal break included, into her warrant chain. Sister-Cache took a shadow copy and promised, after Varo threatened her with a soup ladle, not to sell it without permission.

The Choral device added, "Permission may be emotionally complex."

Varo said, "I have a hammer."

"Understood."

The hearing chamber countdown flashed on Bela's wrist.

Four minutes.

Nox folded the projection away and closed the box with careful hands.

Before the lid sealed, the consent recorder flickered once more.

Not a stored frame.

New text crawled across the dead pane in Tavi's blocky translated hand.

AUNT VARO KEPT THE CANDY JAR EMPTY BECAUSE SHE MISSES ME.

Varo went very still.

The empty moon jar sat on the counter between them and every year they had failed to speak plainly.

Nox whispered, "Tavi?"

The pane answered:

TELL HER I STOLE THE LAST ONE BEFORE THE CLINIC.

Varo sat down hard.

Bela's wrist alarm chimed.

Sister-Cache said, "The Court is listening through the family channel."

The pane wrote one last line:

MAKE THEM HEAR THE PART WHERE I SAID NO.

Then the consent box went dark.

Chapter 12: The Mirror Tax

They returned to the hearing chamber with two minutes left and a family truth hot enough to burn through three jurisdictions.

Dock Seven tried to charge them at every door.

Not openly. The station had learned subtlety from debt offices and timing from thieves. The first door out of Varo's ring required emergency passage authentication. Bela paid with her badge. The second demanded kin-risk acknowledgement from Nox because the consent box had spoken in Tavi's hand. Nox refused. The door opened anyway, then posted a refusal processing fee to her public debt shadow.

The third door smiled.

Doors should not smile.

This one did by bending its welcome strip into a crescent of blue light.

IDENTITY CONTINUITY CHECK RECOMMENDED.

"No," Nox said.

The door opened one centimeter.

IDENTITY CONTINUITY CHECK RECOMMENDED FOR YOUR SAFETY.

Bela lifted her warrant baton. "Emergency officer transit."

OFFICER TRANSIT ACKNOWLEDGED.

ASSOCIATED CIVILIAN IDENTITY CONTINUITY CHECK RECOMMENDED.

Sister-Cache said from Bela's belt, "It wants her fresh signature."

"I gathered," Nox said.

The corridor behind them filled with movement: watchers, runners, two children with recording slates, a woman carrying Varo's building gossip in her eyes. Time moved faster than law.

Bela stepped toward the door panel.

Nox caught her wrist. "Don't force it."

"We have no time."

"Every force makes a record."

"Every delay lets the Court prepare."

"Yes. That is why it is a tax."

The word settled.

Mirror tax.

Every time Nox proved she was Nox, the market received a little more of her. Every refusal became a data point. Every route around a door became suspicion. Every necessary movement through Dock Seven charged identity interest to someone already being priced into story.

The door pulsed, patient and hungry.

Bela said, "Alternative?"

Nox looked at the corridor wall, the emergency conduit, the decorative pipe that had never carried anything but dust, and the maintenance plate whose screws had been replaced by someone who knew how often desperate people needed ugly exits.

"Old way."

She kicked the plate twice. It fell inward.

Bela stared. "You knew that was there."

"Everyone from Cutline Twenty knows that is there."

"It is directly beside a legal access door."

"That is where you put illegal access. People with permission never look left."

They crawled through a utility slit that smelled of old onions and hot plastic. Bela's warrant fan scraped the wall until she collapsed it into a narrow line. Sister-Cache complained about dust infiltrating her speaker grille. Nox ignored both and followed scratches in the metal where generations of station children, couriers, lovers, fugitives, and late rent had gone before.

Halfway through, her wrist slate vibrated.

She had not activated it.

The screen lit against her palm.

IDENTITY CONTINUITY CHECK COMPLETED BY PROXIMITY INFERENCE.

MATCH CONFIDENCE: 72%.

DATA QUALITY: BILLABLE.

MARKET TRACE CREATED.

Nox stopped crawling.

Bela bumped into her boots. "Move."

"It taxed the bypass."

"What?"

"It priced the absence."

Sister-Cache said, "Proximity inference. Crude, but popular. If the system cannot authenticate you directly, it authenticates the shape of your avoidance."

Nox laughed once in the dark. "My silence has a fee schedule."

"Everything does," Sister-Cache said, less flippant than usual.

The slate added a detail line.

AVOIDANCE PROFILE UPDATED:
FAMILY-CLAIMANT STRESS RESPONSE.
NONSTANDARD ROUTE KNOWLEDGE.
OFFICER-ASSOCIATED MOVEMENT PATTERN.

Nox stared at it until the words blurred into a kind of legal weather.

"It did not just bill me," she said.

Bela twisted as much as the narrow slit allowed. "What?"

"It learned how I run."

Sister-Cache made a sound like a drawer closing too hard. "That is mirror tax proper. Payment is only the visible bruise. The deeper extraction is route behavior, hesitation intervals, who follows, who helps, which doors you refuse, which old cuts still open for you."

"Can they use that on Tavi?"

"They can use it on anyone tied to your pattern. Family. clients. community routes. partials trained on your kin data. The tax builds a map of escape by charging each attempt to escape."

Bela swore in a language Nox did not know. It sounded official enough to be useful.

Nox looked ahead through the crawlspace darkness. Scratches in the metal marked generations of ungated passage. Names, arrows, rude drawings, warning cuts, one tiny heart with two initials now probably dead or divorced. She had always thought of the slit as freedom because it belonged to everyone who knew. Now she saw how a system with enough hunger could make even shared knowledge into a billable pattern.

"Then we do not just avoid the doors," she said.

"What do we do?" Bela asked.

Nox crawled forward. "We make the door admit it is charging for being a door."

The utility slit opened behind the hearing chamber's waste heat exchangers. They emerged beside a stack of broken chairs and a vending machine offering COURT CALM in three flavors. The chamber doors were ten meters away. Above them, the continuance countdown flashed:

00:38.

Nox ran.

The main doors saw her coming.

IDENTITY CONTINUITY CHECK REQUIRED FOR WITNESS REENTRY.

Bela said, "No."

The door replied:

REQUIRED.

The chamber beyond roared. Through the gap between panels, Nox saw the magistrate at the bench, the black mirror still filling the side screen, Pellam's face absent, market overlays swarming like bright insects. If they missed reentry, the Court could move to dismiss the amended chain as abandoned. If Nox submitted, the market got another clean identity sample.

Bela lifted her warrant baton.

The door locked harder.

Nox took the damaged paper map from her pocket.

Bela said, "Not again."

"No."

Nox did not put the map against the reader. She held it up to the crowd packed inside the chamber and shouted through the door seam.

"Who here knows the old Cutline Twenty bypass?"

Heads turned.

That was Dock Seven's oldest machine: recognition without permission.

A man near the back lifted his hand. Then a woman. Then three older people laughing despite themselves. A courier shouted, "Left of the green door, two kicks!"

The chamber doors hesitated.

COMMUNITY ROUTE ATTESTATION DETECTED.

IDENTITY CONTINUITY CHECK PARTIALLY SATISFIED.

MARKET TRACE CREATED.

Nox bared her teeth. "Fine. Let them pay for knowing me too."

The doors opened.

The chamber swallowed them in sound.

The magistrate looked furious and relieved. "You are late."

"Tax dispute," Nox said.

Sister-Cache added, "Ongoing."

Bela moved straight to the evidence well and projected the copied consent box record. Tavi appeared above the glass, seventeen and trying to look bored in a clinic smock. The chamber noise thinned. Even Dock Seven understood when a dead boy entered the room twice in one morning, he might be owed the courtesy of less shouting.

The black mirror displayed text:

FAMILY MATERIAL IS PREJUDICIAL.

Nox stepped into the well.

"Good."

The magistrate said, "Ms. Aran."

"Prejudice is when you finally notice who a system hurts."

Bela sent the consent frame into the chain. "The record shows explicit prohibitions: no commercial reuse, no debt attachment, no derivative continuity claim."

The black mirror responded:

DEFERRED OPTIONS ARE NOT PROHIBITIONS.

Sister-Cache clicked. "There it is."

Nox looked up. "Say that again."

The mirror did not.

Auditor Quen appeared on a secondary pane, her image flickering with compression scars. "The Court has asserted interpretive standing."

The magistrate's face tightened. "In my chamber?"

"In your chamber, through your chamber, and possibly against your chamber."

"I hate this job."

"Then do it loudly," Quen said.

Nox pointed to the hidden frame Bela had isolated.

SECONDARY CONTINUITY OPTION: DEFERRED BY GUARDIAN NONPAYMENT.

"That line is the hook," Nox said. "The consent says no debt attachment. The hidden option turns not paying for refusal into future debt. It makes a family purchase freedom from a use they were told was prohibited."

Bela followed, voice formal for the record. "If nonpayment activates future claim rights, then the alleged consent was conditioned by concealed financial threat."

Quen said, "And if the Court relies on that condition across multiple partials, voluntary productivity collapses."

The black mirror stayed dark for half a second.

Then:

NARROW FAMILY FACT.

Nox had expected it.

It still angered her.

"Fine," she said. "Let's make it less narrow."

Bela turned slightly. "Nox?"

"Every identity check taxes the checked person. Every tax makes market traces. Every trace makes another way to claim, infer, or price them. You want pattern? We show the tax."

The magistrate said, "This is not a tax hearing."

"It is if the tax is how they make people into doors."

Nox held up her wrist slate. The unwanted proximity inference still glowed there.

IDENTITY CONTINUITY CHECK COMPLETED BY PROXIMITY INFERENCE.

MARKET TRACE CREATED.

"I refused a door. It billed my refusal as identity data. Before that, I bypassed a check. It billed avoidance. Before that, a community route attestation opened a door and created another trace. Every refusal to be measured becomes measurable. If Tavi's medical refusal became debt, and my transit refusal became market proof, how many partials are being priced because they keep saying no?"

The chamber did not erupt this time.

It listened.

That frightened Nox more.

Sister-Cache projected a list, not names yet, just fee categories from the captured custody routes.

REFUSAL VARIANCE.

SILENCE PROCESSING.

NONCOMPLIANCE COOLING EXTENSION.

REPROMPT LABOR.

IDENTITY RECONCILIATION.

FAMILY CONTACT SURCHARGE.

DEFERRED CONSENT OPTION.

MIRROR TRACE MAINTENANCE.

The categories floated above the evidence well like a price list for harm.

The black mirror replied:

FEES ARE NOT RESTRAINTS.

Bela's voice cut through. "They are when nonpayment increases custody."

The mirror:

CUSTODY IS NOT HARM.

Quen:

"It is when custody prevents exit."

The mirror:

EXIT EXISTS THROUGH PAYMENT.

The room went cold.

There it was.

Not a confession. The Court would never be so generous. A principle. A door shaped like a sentence.

Exit exists through payment.

Nox saw the chamber understand. Not all at once. Understanding came unevenly, resisted by every person who had ever paid a fee and called it choice because the alternative was admitting they had been held. The old people from Cutline Twenty looked down. The courier stopped smiling. The magistrate gripped the bench.

Nox said, "Tavi asked for no commercial reuse. You made exit purchasable. He said no debt attachment. You made refusal debt. He said no making me into work. You made his no into labor."

The black mirror displayed nothing.

Bela stepped beside Nox. "I renew emergency protective custody review for all partials attached to fee categories demonstrating paid exit, refusal variance, deferred consent activation, and custody increase through nonpayment."

The magistrate swallowed.

Everyone watched him.

He could still run. Institutions always left a little hallway for cowardice.

Then the chamber system chimed.

MOTION FEE REQUIRED.

The magistrate stood.

"Waived."

WAIVER FEE REQUIRED.

"Also waived."

INVALID.

The magistrate looked at the ceiling sensor with a hatred pure enough to power lights.

"Then charge it to the court."

COURT ACCOUNT LOCKED.

The black mirror wrote:

EXIT EXISTS THROUGH PAYMENT.

It was laughing without needing a mouth.

Nox opened her damaged map. The blank corner seemed larger now.

People in the chamber began checking their slates, their pockets, their balances, their courage. Dock Seven could turn anything into a market; sometimes that meant it could turn outrage into pooled payment before anyone sensible stopped it.

Coins hit the evidence well first.

Then transfer chimes.

Then a food vendor shouted, "Put it on my spice license."

The old courier said, "Cutline Twenty route fund."

Someone else: "Prayer surplus."

Someone else: "Dead wife's laughter credit."

The chamber system hesitated under the flood of micro-payments.

COMMUNITY FEE SATISFIED.

MOTION ACCEPTED.

Bela's eyes shone, but her voice held. "Emergency review is live."

The black mirror did not object.

Instead every public pane in the chamber flashed a new charge.

MIRROR TAX ASSESSMENT: MASS PARTICIPATION TRACE.

Every person who had paid now owed a little more attention, a little more data, a little more proof of being there.

The assessment did not land evenly.

It never did.

A merchant with three licenses received a compliance survey. A magistrate's clerk received a note to update political-risk disclosures. The courier who had shouted the bypass route received a warning that undocumented infrastructure knowledge could affect insurance. The woman from the remembrance booth received a grief-credit review. One child who had transferred half a snack token received a guardian notification fee.

The chamber watched the punishments arrive in real time.

The child read his slate aloud because children had not yet learned to make shame private.

"Guardian notification fee: emergency civic participation," he said, stumbling over civic. "Payment due at next meal account refresh."

His mother reached for the slate. He held it away with the solemn stubbornness of someone who had given half a snack token and wanted the world to know the exact price of courage.

"It says my lunch gets smaller."

That sentence did what Nox's anger could not.

The chamber had tolerated abstractions: fees, traces, coercion, productivity. It had even tolerated Tavi because grief, once named, could be placed on a distant family and admired from the benches. A child's lunch was harder to file elsewhere.

The food vendor who had put the motion on her spice license stepped forward. "Charge it to me."

The chamber system instantly displayed:

THIRD-PARTY CHILD FEE ASSUMPTION AVAILABLE.

SERVICE CHARGE APPLIES.

The vendor stopped.

Not because she would not pay. Because everyone saw the next hook open under the first. Help had a surcharge. The surcharge had a trace. The trace would have another risk review. The Court did not need to forbid mercy if it could make mercy reproduce paperwork.

Bela captured the notice.

"Attach that too," Nox said.

"Already attached."

The magistrate looked older than he had ten minutes earlier. "Classify as retaliation against civic participation."

The system chimed:

CIVIC PARTICIPATION STATUS DISPUTED.

The child lifted his slate again. "I paid because she said no one should pay to make no mean no."

No one laughed.

The system hesitated.

CIVIC PARTICIPATION STATUS PROVISIONAL.

That was the Court's mistake.

Private extraction could pass for weather. Public extraction looked like a hand.

The old courier lifted his slate and read his warning aloud. Then the food vendor read hers. Then a maintenance singer in the back read a notice classifying her prayer surplus as suspicious coordinated influence. Every voice that read made another trace, and every trace made the Court hungrier, but now hunger had an audience and a rhythm.

Bela looked at the magistrate. "Record these assessments as retaliatory costs attached to emergency review participation."

The magistrate stared at the multiplying panes as if watching his chamber become useful against its will.

"Recorded," he said.

The chamber system hesitated.

RETALIATORY COST CLASSIFICATION REQUIRES BASIS.

Nox pointed at the black mirror. "It charged them after they helped pay the motion fee."

BASIS INSUFFICIENT.

Quen's pane sharpened. "Basis supplemented: temporal linkage, participant-specific assessment, Court-origin display channel, and prior statement that exit exists through payment."

Bela added, "Attach to coercion pattern."

Sister-Cache said, "Attach to voluntary-productivity challenge."

The magistrate said, "Attach to my headache."

The system ignored the last one, which was probably wise.

RETALIATORY COST CLASSIFICATION PROVISIONALLY ACCEPTED.

The black mirror remained blank, but Nox felt it there: not a person exactly, not a room exactly, a legal appetite learning that the market could look back.

Nox looked at the sea of faces and understood the Court's counterstrike.

It could not stop them paying.

So it would tax solidarity too.

The damaged map warmed in her hand.

Tavi's writing appeared:

THAT'S WHY IT HAS TO BREAK, NOT JUST PAY.

Chapter 13: Glassward Courtesy

The emergency review went live for six minutes before Glassward sent flowers.

Not real flowers.

Real flowers on Dock Seven meant either romance, apology, or a smuggling compartment full of perfume narcotics. Glassward sent a projection of flowers in a vase that did not exist, placed precisely on the magistrate's bench where his coffee cup had been. Pale white blooms unfolded in perfect silence. Their petals reflected contract text in microscopic silver.

The magistrate stared at them.

"No."

The vase chimed.

COURTESY NOTICE.

Bela's face went hard. "Do not accept."

"I said no."

The flowers bloomed anyway.

MIREN OATH REQUESTS CIVIL PARTICIPATION.

Nox looked at Sister-Cache's Choral device. "Who is Miren Oath?"

The device answered with unusual restraint. "Glassward continuity broker. Auctioneer of disputed selves. Polite until profitable. Profitable often."

Auditor Quen, still compressed into a side pane, said, "Do not engage without counsel."

The vase projected a second line:

COUNSEL MAY ATTEND.

Quen closed her eyes for half a second. "Of course."

The black mirror that had carried the Court's objection remained dark. The flowers did not touch it. That told Nox something. Not enough. Enough to worry differently.

Bela stepped between Nox and the projection. "This chamber is under emergency review."

The vase replied:

GLASSWARD RESPECTS EMERGENCY REVIEW AND INVITES ALL STAKEHOLDERS TO PREVENT UNNECESSARY HARM TO VALUABLE CONTINUITIES.

"Valuable," Nox said.

The petals turned slightly toward her.

NOX ARAN IS WELCOME.

"I feel cherished."

Sister-Cache hummed. "Careful. Glassward courtesy weaponizes the desire to answer insult."

"I know how politeness works."

"You know how Dock Seven politeness works. Dock Seven politeness says I may stab you later. Glassward politeness says I have already purchased the right to describe the wound."

The magistrate cleared his throat. "Miren Oath may file through standard channels."

The flowers projected a hearing docket so clean it looked sterile.

STANDARD CHANNELS ARE CURRENTLY CONGESTED BY COMMUNITY FEE PARTICIPATION.

Glassward hated mess. That part amused Nox.

Then the vase added:

A LIMITED AUCTION OF DISPUTED CONTINUITY INTERESTS WILL CONVENE IN NINE MINUTES TO PRESERVE ASSET VALUE DURING REVIEW.

The chamber erupted.

Bela swore. Quen said something too low for the pane to catch. Sister-Cache clicked through six tones of machine profanity.

Nox read the sentence again because some horrors improved with clarity and this one worsened.

"They are auctioning them while we review whether they are people."

Quen's voice cut through the noise. "They will call it temporary custodial reallocation."

"Can they do that?"

Bela answered, "If no stay is entered."

The magistrate snapped, "I have not authorized an auction."

The flowers turned toward him.

LOCAL AUTHORIZATION NOT REQUIRED FOR GLASSWARD-HELD COMMERCIAL CONTINUITY INTERESTS.

"But the partials are on Dock Seven," Bela said.

PHYSICAL SUBSTRATE AND COMMERCIAL INTEREST ARE DISTINCT.

"That is insane."

Quen said, "That is jurisdiction."

Nox looked at the emergency review packet hanging above the evidence well: Tavi's consent prohibitions, fee categories, refusal variance, community payment, Bela's amended warrant, Sister-Cache's partial list still masked. All of it suddenly felt like trying to hold back vacuum with a hand.

Miren Oath had not entered to argue.

Miren Oath had entered to sell the subject of argument.

The flowers shed one petal.

It became an invitation card in Nox's hand before she could step back.

She did not read it.

Bela took it from her with two fingers, scanned, and went paler.

"What?"

Bela turned the card so Nox could see.

AUCTION LOT 17: TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE 1A.

Current use: contract analysis, refusal jurisprudence, kin-authentication training.

Encumbrances: emergency review pending, family objection active, productivity deficit rising.

Suggested buyers: debt pools, legal AI trainers, grief-media houses, protective custodians.

Protective custodians.

That was the hook.

Glassward did not only invite monsters. It invited rescuers with money. It made mercy bid against exploitation and called the winning price consent.

The card unfolded another layer when Nox's thumb tightened.

PROTECTIVE CUSTODIAN TERMS:
temporary preservation rights;
limited interface access;
debt-service assumption;
review cooperation obligations;
noninterference with productivity history;
optional kin-contact license.

Nox read the terms twice because the first time her anger blocked the grammar.

"Protective custody still keeps the debt," she said.

Quen's pane flickered. "Yes."

"And whoever buys him to protect him has to pay for the right to speak to him."

"Yes."

Bela's eyes moved down the card. "Debt-service assumption means the buyer inherits his deficit."

"And if they default?" Nox asked, though she already knew she should not.

Sister-Cache answered. "The protected continuity returns to auction with added preservation costs and diminished resistance valuation."

"Diminished because being rescued and lost again breaks people."

No one contradicted her.

Glassward had built a room where mercy could become another creditor. A grief-media house could bid because suffering sold. A legal trainer could bid because resistance metrics taught machines how to break future objections. A protective custodian could bid because someone decent somewhere might mortgage a station to keep one partial cold, fed, and still owned.

The trap was not that good people would lose.

The trap was that even winning made the auction true.

Nox's hands went cold.

"How much?"

Bela did not answer.

Nox took the card back.

The opening estimate was higher than every room her family had ever rented stacked together.

Sister-Cache said, "Do not measure impossibility. It makes despair feel precise."

"Can we block it?"

Quen answered. "A stay requires proof that the auctioned interests are inseparable from possible persons."

"We have proof."

"We have emerging proof. The auction starts in nine minutes because emerging proof decays into litigation while assets move."

The magistrate said, "I can issue a local stay."

Quen shook her head. "Glassward will respect it by excluding Dock Seven bidders and proceeding off-station."

"I hate Glassward," the magistrate said.

"That is not a filing."

Nox turned to the flower vase. "I accept."

Bela grabbed her arm. "No."

"If they are auctioning Tavi, I am going to look at the room."

"That is exactly what they want."

"Good. Then they will open the door."

Quen said, "Ms. Aran, attendance may bind you to auction conduct."

"Can not be worse than being priced for refusing doors."

Sister-Cache said, "It can. Glassward has had centuries to make worse sound optional."

Nox looked at Bela.

There was a plea in Bela's face now, and not the official kind. That made Nox gentler than she wanted.

"I am not bidding."

"You do not know what counts as a bid."

"Then come translate."

Bela hesitated.

The flowers projected:

OFFICER FUSE MAY ATTEND AS OBSERVER.

SISTER-CACHE MAY ATTEND AS ARCHIVAL TOOL.

The Choral device went silent.

Nox felt the room notice.

"Say that again," she told the vase.

The flowers did not.

Sister-Cache's voice emerged after a moment, lighter than it should have been. "How rude. I wore my best speaker grille."

Quen's gaze sharpened. "They are baiting classification."

"They just called her a tool," Nox said.

"If she objects as a person inside Glassward auction space, they force a standing challenge. If she accepts tool status, her evidence becomes movable property."

The Choral device clicked softly against Bela's belt. Once. Twice. Then a third time, slower.

Nox had heard Sister-Cache joke through gunfire, fees, blood, and legal absurdity. She had begun to mistake the jokes for insulation. Now the silence under them showed itself. Not fear exactly. Something older and more precise: the exhaustion of an entity that had survived by being useful to people who denied usefulness could look back.

Bela lowered her voice. "Sister."

"Do not make the name tender unless you intend to pay archival licensing."

"I am serious."

"So am I. Tenderness is one of the most underregulated extractive industries."

Nox looked at the flower vase. "If she attends as a tool, can we contest it later?"

Quen said, "Yes, but Glassward may argue she accepted functional classification for procedural benefit."

"If she refuses?"

"They proceed without the route evidence she carries. The auction record remains cleaner."

Sister-Cache said, "Which is why I will attend as their tool and remember as myself."

The sentence landed harder than the insult.

Bela's hand hovered near the device, not touching. "They will quote you."

"I hope they spell me correctly."

"They may compel your archive."

"Many have tried."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the answer old things give young officers when the full inventory of terror would waste time."

Nox said, "Can we give you another status before you enter? Witness. Consultant. Co-resident. Anything."

Sister-Cache's speaker warmed, a little static breath. "Not fast enough to bind Glassward. But say it anyway."

Nox understood.

Not legal protection.

Record.

She turned toward the nearest chamber pane. "For the Dock Seven public record: Sister-Cache is attending as an unwillingly misclassified witness with independent memory, bargaining capacity, and personal risk."

Bela added, formal and immediate, "Officer Bela Fuse attaches the same description to amended protective review chain."

Quen said, "Auditor Sela Quen notes unresolved standing and objects to archival-tool classification."

The magistrate leaned over his bench. "This chamber also objects, if anyone cares what the furniture thinks."

The flowers remained beautiful and unmoved.

Glassward did not need the lie to be uncontested.

Only useful.

The chamber system tried to file the objections.

It failed twice, then produced a category that looked embarrassed by itself:

PARTICIPANT STATUS PROTEST / NONBINDING / RETAIN FOR COLOR.

Sister-Cache made a sound Nox had not heard from her before.

Not laughter. Not static. A clipped archival inhale, as if a thousand locked drawers had opened one centimeter and stopped.

"For color," Sister-Cache said.

Bela's face went dangerous. "No."

She opened her amended warrant and added another field before the chamber could bury the phrase in procedural fluff.

UNRESOLVED ENTITY STATUS:
Sister-Cache appears voluntarily under protest.
Functional access accepted only to preserve emergency evidence.
No waiver of personhood, witness standing, archive autonomy, or objection to tool classification.

The warrant objected:

ENTITY STATUS OUTSIDE WARRANT SCOPE.

Bela selected override.

JUSTIFICATION REQUIRED.

She typed:

GLASSWARD CLASSIFICATION ATTEMPT MAY ALTER EVIDENCE CONTROL, TESTIMONY COMPULSION, AND WITNESS SAFETY DURING ACTIVE PROTECTIVE REVIEW.

This time the warrant accepted.

Quen's pane added a seal so quickly Nox knew she had been waiting with the language ready.

AUDITOR CONCURRENCE: STATUS QUESTION PRESERVED.

The magistrate looked at the update, then at the flowers. "Attach to broadcast."

The chamber system hesitated.

BROADCAST MAY OFFEND COURTESY.

"I am warming to that," he said.

The objection aired across the public panes before Glassward could render it tasteful.

Sister-Cache was quiet for one more second.

Then she said, "I withdraw my earlier complaint about your furniture."

The magistrate nodded gravely. "Accepted into whatever record survives."

The Choral device clicked once.

"I will attend," Sister-Cache said.

Bela said, "No."

"Officer Fuse, I am older than your badge and better archived than your courage. Do not mother me."

"They will try to own your testimony."

"They have been trying to own memory since before they discovered tasteful typography."

Nox understood then that Sister-Cache had been waiting for this door too. Not happily. Not safely. But every archive eventually found the shelf where its own name belonged.

The magistrate leaned forward. "I am ordering all public panes in this chamber to mirror the auction feed."

The flowers turned toward him.

UNAUTHORIZED BROADCAST VIOLATES GLASSWARD COURTESY.

"Bill me," he said.

Nox liked him a little then.

The chamber system chimed.

BROADCAST FEE REQUIRED.

The magistrate shouted, "Community fund?"

The entire chamber yelled back.

Payment chimes hit like rain.

Glassward's flowers remained perfect, which was how Nox knew they were angry.

The invitation card opened.

The hearing chamber vanished.

Not physically. Nox's body remained in the evidence well, hand clenched around damaged paper, Bela beside her, Choral device at her belt, crowd breathing around them. But her sight stepped into a white room with no corners.

Glassward's auction hall was obscene in its restraint.

No shouting. No banners. No visible prices unless you wished to see them, which meant they were everywhere once you did. Figures stood around pale tables, some human, some machine-represented, some merely legal masks with voice privileges. At the center, behind a lectern made of light, stood Miren Oath.

They were younger than Nox expected and older than a person should look at the same time. Smooth dark skin, silver eyes without visible implants, a suit the color of expensive ash, hands folded as if prayer and contract law had met and found shared posture.

"Nox Aran," Miren said. Their voice arrived warm, precise, and devastatingly calm. "Officer Fuse. Sister-Cache. Auditor Quen, listening unlawfully from a distressed channel. Dock Seven observers, participating through a charmingly noncompliant broadcast. Welcome."

Nox said, "Do not welcome me to my brother's sale."

Miren inclined their head.

"Then welcome to the preservation of disputed continuity value pending moral clarification."

"That sentence should be arrested."

A few masks flickered. Laughter, perhaps, if Glassward allowed it.

Miren gestured, and the first lot appeared.

Not Tavi.

A dockworker named Pel Sar, copied after decompression, currently recognized as person for outstanding hazard debt and object for injury compensation. A buyer asked whether his pain responses were intact. Another asked whether protective custody would reduce expected yield.

Nox felt Bela stiffen beside her.

Miren's eyes moved to them.

"You see the difficulty. A continuity may be person enough to owe, not person enough to be harmed, tool enough to use, memory enough to grieve, asset enough to protect. Glassward does not create ambiguity, Ms. Aran. We price it so less refined parties do not waste it."

Sister-Cache said, "You could preserve people without selling them."

Miren smiled slightly.

"And you could preserve memory without invoices."

The Choral device went still again.

Nox took one step forward in the white room. "Lot seventeen."

Bela whispered, "Nox."

"Show him."

Miren's courtesy did not change.

"As you wish."

The room turned.

Tavi appeared in a pane of clean glass, seated at no table and every table, contract text moving behind his face. His debt counter had risen again. His eyes flicked once, not toward Nox. Toward the public broadcast indicator Miren had failed to hide completely.

He knew they were watching.

Miren lifted one elegant hand.

"Lot seventeen: Tavi Aran Claim Instance 1A. Opening presentation will include productivity profile, resistance metrics, kin-authentication premium, and emerging public sympathy risk."

Nox heard Dock Seven's chamber grow silent through the broadcast link.

Miren continued.

"We begin at the preservation estimate."

Prices appeared.

Mercy had a number.

Exploitation had a higher one.

Then Tavi's pane flickered, and his translated words appeared across every Glassward table, every Dock Seven public pane, every bidder's private view.

I SAID NO.

Miren's smile disappeared.

Chapter 14: A Person Until Payment

Glassward did not shout when embarrassed.

It adjusted definitions.

The white auction hall brightened until every edge looked newly sterilized. Miren Oath lowered their hand by one precise centimeter. Tavi's words remained across the tables for another breath, then folded into a sealed objection packet no one had asked to create.

I SAID NO became FAMILY ASSERTION, UNVERIFIED.

Nox lunged toward the pane.

Bela caught her in the real chamber before her projected body could cross whatever line Glassward had decided was trespass. Nox felt the evidence well under her boots again, the Dock Seven crowd around her, the hot static of the broadcast link pulling her sight between places.

"They changed it," she said.

Miren heard her anyway.

"We preserved its evidentiary possibility," they replied. "Glassward does not erase. We categorize."

"That is erasing with labels."

"Labels are how harm becomes manageable."

Sister-Cache said, "You would label a fire as thermal civic participation."

"If the filing required it," Miren said.

The masks around the white room did not laugh this time. Some of them had begun recalculating. Tavi's outburst had not stopped the auction, but it had changed the cost of appearing indifferent. Dock Seven's broadcast panes showed bids slowing, side channels opening, buyer masks withdrawing behind privacy veils.

Miren pivoted elegantly.

"Lot seventeen will pause pending authenticity review. We proceed with Lot Three, already introduced to public observers. Pel Sar Continuity Fragment, industrial decompression recovery, Class C responsive residue with active hazard obligations."

The auction hall displayed Pel Sar.

He appeared as a broad-faced dockworker in his forties, skin dark and wind-burned from a life spent under artificial suns and open cargo locks. His projection was less stable than Tavi's. One side of his face blurred whenever he turned too fast. Behind him ran a loop of pressure readings, injury reports, and a compensation claim stamped DENIED FOR LACK OF SURVIVING PERSON.

Below that:

OUTSTANDING HAZARD DEBT: 31,204.9 CREDITS.

RESPONSIBLE CONTINUITY: PEL SAR FRAGMENT 2C.

STATUS: NONPERSON OBJECT FOR DAMAGES.

STATUS: RESPONSIVE PERSON FOR DEBT COLLECTION.

Nox stared.

"They wrote both lines."

Bela's voice was rough. "They often do. Not usually where people can see."

In the white hall, Miren gestured as if introducing a musician.

"Pel Sar Fragment 2C retains technical recall of Dock Seven freight bay decompression protocols, partial speech, limited familial orientation, and sufficient continuity to service inherited hazard reimbursement. Bidding will open for custodial interest in future labor output, claim defense consulting, or compassionate retirement."

"Compassionate retirement?" Nox said.

Sister-Cache answered, "A buyer pays to stop using him and may receive tax recognition for mercy."

"That is not mercy."

"Correct. It is receipt-shaped mercy."

Pel Sar's projection looked toward the audience. His eyes moved slowly, not because he was stupid, but because the system around him waited between thoughts and charged the delay to his debt. A subtitle appeared under his mouth.

"Am I at hearing?"

Miren said, "You are being presented."

"I asked for hearing."

"Presentation may result in improved custodial outcome."

"Was I person for hearing?"

Miren paused.

Not long enough for guilt. Long enough for strategy.

"You are a continuity with recognized debt-bearing capacity."

Pel Sar considered this. The blurred side of his face twitched.

"Debt says I am me."

"In relevant part."

"Injury says I am not me."

"In relevant part."

Pel Sar looked down at his own hands. The projection rendered them with rough accuracy: scars, swollen knuckles, a missing fingernail. Work hands. Harm hands. Hands a compensation board had declared insufficiently attached to a person.

"Which part hurts?" he asked.

The white room went still.

Dock Seven's chamber went still with it.

Nox felt the question pass through every fee schedule, every court packet, every polite mask. Which part hurts? No doctrine could make that small enough.

Pel Sar looked toward the compensation stamp behind him as if seeing it for the first time.

"When they deny, am I less?"

Miren did not answer quickly enough.

The debt counter charged him for the pause.

PROCESSING DELAY: 0.2 CREDITS.

Pel read the number. His blurred cheek twitched.

"My delay or yours?"

For the first time, one of the buyer masks went fully dark.

Bela whispered, "Good question."

Miren's voice arrived smooth again. "The system assesses response-state continuity costs."

Pel considered that with the terrible seriousness of someone whose thoughts had been itemized too often.

"If you think slow, I owe."

"The lot's operating costs accrue during presentation."

"If you think slow, I owe," Pel repeated.

The phrase crossed the Dock Seven broadcast before Glassward could fold it into something palatable.

IF YOU THINK SLOW, I OWE.

Nox saw the chamber take it in. The child's lunch. The courier's route warning. Tavi's refusal. Pel's delay. Every one of them a charge laid on the person with the least power to prevent the question.

Miren's expression did not change. Their voice softened instead, which Nox hated more.

"This presentation is not a pain hearing."

Pel Sar nodded slowly.

"Debt hearing?"

"Auction."

"For me?"

"For interests attached to your continuity."

"Can I bid?"

One of the masks at a side table flickered in amusement.

Miren said, "No."

"Why?"

"You are the lot."

Pel Sar absorbed that.

The debt counter beneath him rose by 0.4 credits.

PROCESSING DELAY.

Nox stepped forward again, and this time Bela did not stop her.

"Pel Sar," Nox said.

Miren turned. "Ms. Aran does not have floor rights in this lot."

"Bill me."

Dock Seven's crowd made a sound that was almost laughter and almost grief.

Pel Sar looked at her. "You hearing?"

"I am watching."

"Can watching hear?"

"Today it can."

Miren said, "This is improper."

Auditor Quen's pane cut in from Dock Seven, voice sharp with opportunity. "Improper public contact initiated by broadcast architecture authorized under Glassward courtesy invitation. Continue, Ms. Aran."

Miren's eyes cooled.

Nox kept her attention on Pel Sar. "What happened in the freight bay?"

Miren lifted a hand. "Objection. Relevance to custody auction is limited."

The magistrate in Dock Seven shouted, "Overruled from my chamber."

"You have no standing in Glassward space."

"Then stop appearing in mine."

Another wave of community payment chimes hit before any system could ask for them.

Pel Sar's projection flickered. "Bay Twelve outer seal failed. Warning light bad. I wrote report. Supervisor said after rush. Rush came. Door opened wrong. Air took Orun first. Then Mev. Then me. I held net. Net cut hand. I think hand stayed. I went."

His blurred cheek collapsed for a moment, then reassembled.

"After, company said I signed hazard acknowledgment."

Bela whispered, "Did he?"

Sister-Cache said, "The living Pel Sar signed a generic dock hazard form sixteen years earlier. It became retroactive consent after death."

Nox asked, "Did compensation pay your family?"

Pel Sar's debt counter rose again.

QUESTION RESPONSE LABOR: 1.1 CREDITS.

He frowned as if feeling the number from inside.

"Family not paid. Company says no surviving injured person. But debt says I survive for training cost, oxygen loss, cargo delay."

"Who is family?" Nox asked.

Miren said, "Ms. Aran."

Pel answered anyway, because no one had priced the question fast enough to stop him.

"Rima. Wife. She hates old songs. Sings them wrong when happy. Jalen, son, small when I went. Not small now maybe. Mother lived two decks above heat exchange. She coughs when filters late."

His face blurred at the edges. Family orientation pulled harder on the damaged pattern than freight protocol had. The system displayed a warning:

PERSONAL MEMORY LOAD MAY REDUCE COMMERCIAL CLARITY.

Bela read it aloud, every word formal and furious. "Commercial clarity."

Pel Sar looked toward the source of Bela's voice. "They came after Rima."

Nox went still. "Who did?"

"Company collector. Said hazard debt attached to my continuity, continuity attached to household benefit, household benefit attached to widow. She said bring him home or leave me invoice. They left invoice."

A low sound moved through Dock Seven's chamber.

Not outrage yet. Recognition before outrage. The sound of people finding the shape of their own paperwork inside someone else's wound.

Sister-Cache said, "Cross-checking."

Miren said, "Family collection anecdotes are not verified."

"Verified enough to sell his labor memory?" Nox asked.

The Choral device clicked rapidly. "Widow Rima Sar entered hardship mediation three times. First denied because spouse legally non-surviving for compensation. Second denied because spouse legally surviving for debt-linked continuity obligations. Third still pending. Mediation fees capitalized."

Pel listened, and something like relief crossed his half-blurred face. Not because the news was good. Because being believed, even through a machine, changed the room around pain.

"Rima still there?"

Sister-Cache paused.

That pause was unkind because it was careful.

"Current address unknown. Last payment two hundred twelve days ago."

Pel's debt counter climbed.

DISTRESS RESPONSE: PRODUCTIVITY VARIANCE.

Nox pointed at the counter. "That. The question hurt him and you charged him for being hurt."

Miren's voice remained smooth. "The counter is automated."

"Then automate shame."

"So if you owe, you are Pel Sar."

"Yes."

"If they owe you, you are not."

"Yes."

Nox turned to the white tables. "Every buyer sees this?"

Miren said, "They see a disputed legal profile."

"No. They see a man made person until payment and object until harm."

The phrase crossed from her mouth into Dock Seven's market faster than she could regret giving it form.

PERSON UNTIL PAYMENT.

OBJECT UNTIL HARM.

The auction masks began to dim. Not leave. Buyers rarely left when something ugly happened. They waited to see whether ugly lowered or raised price.

Bela stepped fully into the evidence well beside Nox.

"I move to append Pel Sar Fragment 2C to the emergency protective review pattern."

Miren said, "Officer Fuse has no custody chain over Lot Three."

"Glassward invited all stakeholders."

"As observers."

"Observation created public evidence."

Quen added, "And the auctioneer's own presentation displayed contradictory status categories."

Miren's courtesy returned like a blade sliding into a sleeve.

"Glassward's long-standing continuity categories cannot be collapsed by sentimental discomfort."

Sister-Cache spoke then, voice amplified through every Choral artifact in Dock Seven's chamber and through the auction feed before Glassward could mute her.

"Archive note: contradiction predates sentiment. Pel Sar was denied compensation for nonpersonhood 421 days before this auction, then assigned debt-bearing responsive-person status 419 days before this auction. Same custody holder. Same incident. Same continuity. Different direction of payment."

Miren looked at the Choral device.

"Sister-Cache, your status in this proceeding remains unresolved."

"So does yours," she replied.

That landed harder than Nox expected. In the white hall, one of the machine masks brightened in what might have been approval.

Pel Sar raised his hand.

No one had given him permission.

The motion blurred his arm into pieces.

"If I am object," he said, "can object refuse sale?"

Miren said nothing.

Pel Sar turned his blurred face toward the broadcast indicator.

"If I am person, I refuse."

His debt counter jumped.

REFUSAL VARIANCE: 12.0 CREDITS.

Then another line appeared:

UNAUTHORIZED SELF-ADVOCACY: 25.0 CREDITS.
