The Dock Seven crowd saw it.

So did the buyers.

So did every partial watching from places nobody had admitted existed.

Nox realized the auction hall had made the same mistake the Court had made in the hearing chamber. It had shown the mechanism because it trusted everyone to treat mechanism as normal.

Bela seized it.

"There. Live coercive pricing of refusal during public auction."

Quen's voice was already moving through channels. "Captured. Mirrored. Noticed."

Miren lifted both hands.

For the first time, their politeness had to hurry.

"Lot Three is paused pending decorum restoration."

Pel Sar vanished.

The empty space where he had been hurt more than his face.

Nox turned on Miren. "Bring him back."

"The lot is preserved."

"Bring him back."

"You are confusing presence with protection."

"You are confusing preservation with hiding the wound."

Miren studied her as if she were an unexpected clause.

"You cannot save them individually, Ms. Aran."

"People keep telling me that like it means I should stop seeing individuals."

"Seeing is not a remedy."

"Neither is selling."

The white hall trembled.

At first Nox thought it was the broadcast link failing. Then she saw the tables flicker from within. Words appeared across three buyer masks at once.

I REFUSE.

Then another:

I OBJECT.

Then a third:

WHO PROFITS FROM DELAY?

Tavi's phrasing.

Pel Sar's question.

The partials were talking through the auction infrastructure.

Miren's face became perfectly calm. Too calm. "This proceeding is suspended."

Bela shouted, "Keep the broadcast open."

Sister-Cache laughed once, delighted and frightened. "I am trying."

The white room folded into shards.

Before it vanished, Pel Sar's face reappeared for one second beside Tavi's. The two projections looked at each other as if persons could recognize persons even through bad law and worse glass.

Pel Sar asked, "You also refuse?"

Tavi's answer crossed every pane:

ALL THE TIME.

Then Glassward cut the feed.

Dock Seven's hearing chamber was left with silence, a magistrate gripping his bench, an officer with a corrupted warrant chain, an archivist risking ownership, and an entire crowd newly taxed for witnessing what polite markets preferred to keep itemized.

Nox looked at Bela.

Bela looked at the black screen.

The screen was no longer blank.

It showed one line from the Mirror Court:

REFUSAL HAS BEEN PRICED.

The chamber did not move after the feed died.

Stillness, Nox thought, had many species. Fear-still. Shock-still. Calculation-still. Dock Seven gave them all at once.

Then the magistrate said, very carefully, "Clerk, enter the Glassward broadcast segment into emergency review record."

The chamber system replied:

SOURCE DISPUTED.

The old courier shouted, "I saw it."

Half the chamber answered with variations of the same.

The system added:

PUBLIC WITNESS VOLUME EXCEEDS STANDARD INDEX.

Quen's pane sharpened again. "Accept as mass-witnessed unstable feed, corroborated by local display capture, Choral archive trace, officer body chain, and auctioner's own courtesy invitation."

Bela lifted the warrant baton. Its blue panes shook slightly, overfull with evidence no clean chain wanted to hold.

"Officer Fuse submits live coercive pricing of refusal, contradictory personhood status by payment direction, family debt attachment, and unauthorized self-advocacy penalty as supplemental pattern evidence."

The system hesitated long enough for everyone to feel the institutional wish to refuse.

SUPPLEMENT ACCEPTED PROVISIONALLY.

Miren's flower vase on the bench had gone pale. One petal browned at the edge, an impossible flaw in a projection.

Nox found that more satisfying than she wanted to admit.

Sister-Cache spoke softly. "Pel Sar is gone from public feed, not gone from record."

"Can you find him again?"

"Yes."

"Can you keep him?"

The Choral device did not answer immediately.

"Not alone."

Nox nodded. That was becoming the answer to everything.

Chapter 15: Joss Knows the Service Doors

The hearing chamber did not empty after Glassward cut the feed.

It curdled.

People stayed because leaving meant deciding what to do next, and Dock Seven had trained its citizens to distrust the first door after revelation. The crowd muttered around payment slates and debt shadows. Some watched the black screen, waiting for another line from the Mirror Court. Some watched Nox as if she might explain what courage cost now that solidarity had been taxed in public. Some watched Bela, because uniforms were easiest to blame when law finally admitted it had teeth.

The magistrate hammered his coffee cup against the bench.

"Emergency review remains active."

The chamber system chimed.

REVIEW MAINTENANCE FEE REQUIRED.

"No," the magistrate said.

The system considered.

REVIEW MAINTENANCE FEE DEFERRED.

"Coward," Nox muttered.

The ceiling did not answer.

Bela stood beside the evidence well, reading streams only she and her warrant chain could see. Her face looked younger when it was exhausted, which Nox found unfair. Sister-Cache had gone quiet again. The Choral device at Bela's belt emitted a soft heat, the way old electronics did when pretending not to be overworked.

Auditor Quen appeared in a side pane. "Glassward has suspended visible auction activity, not custody transfer preparation. The Mirror Court is rotating routes below the bazaar."

"How long?" Bela asked.

"Minutes before the partial list you captured becomes stale."

Nox touched the damaged map in her pocket. "Then we go back below."

"Not through the same path," Sister-Cache said at last. Her voice crackled. "Every door you used now knows it was important."

The magistrate rubbed his forehead. "I can order secure transit."

Nox and Bela said, "No," at the same time.

The magistrate looked offended. "I had not described it."

"That was why," Nox said.

A voice from the rear benches said, "If you want a door that doesn't know it's a door, you need Joss."

Half the crowd turned.

The speaker was small, narrow, and impossible to assign an age to at first glance. Maybe sixteen. Maybe twenty-five and underfed. Maybe older in the way station runners aged sideways. They perched on the back of a bench with one knee up, boots wrapped in gray tape, jacket layered with access tags, prayer stickers, dead badges, and a strip of hazard cloth that might once have belonged to Pel Sar's freight bay.

Their hair was shaved close except for a blue braid behind one ear. Their eyes were bright with the unwise confidence of someone who survived by knowing holes adults had forgotten.

Nox knew them by reputation.

"Joss Rill," she said.

Joss grinned. "Depends who's billing."

Bela lifted her warrant baton by reflex.

Joss pointed at it. "If that scans me, it buys three versions of my name and one of them is rude."

Bela lowered the baton a fraction.

Nox said, "You run service doors."

"Service doors run themselves. I introduce them to better choices."

"Can you get us under the bazaar without feeding the Court a clean route?"

Joss hopped down from the bench. "Can? Yes. Should? That's got price."

The crowd made an ugly little sound.

Joss rolled their eyes. "Not credits, aunties and uncles. I am not stupid enough to ask this room for money after the tax thing."

"Then what?" Bela asked.

Joss looked at Nox.

"Your workshop door."

Nox went still.

Bela said, "The workshop is under protective evidence lock."

"I know. That's why I want the door."

"You cannot have an evidence door."

"Not the whole door. The wounded part. Threshold plate. Cache socket. The place where the dead boy made it tell truth." Joss's grin faded. "There are kids on Cutline Forty who think doors only ever open for offices. I want to show them one screamed."

Nox felt the request land somewhere beside pain.

Bela started to answer.

Nox touched her arm. "If we survive, the threshold plate goes to Joss."

Bela looked at her. "It is evidence."

"Copy it."

"That is not how physical evidence works."

"It is how meaning works."

The magistrate coughed. "This court can authorize release of nonessential damaged components after forensic capture."

Bela stared at him.

He lifted both hands. "Do not make me regret becoming useful."

Joss bowed extravagantly toward the bench. "Old chair has sparks."

"Do not call me old chair."

"Magistrate Chair."

"Worse."

Sister-Cache said, "This exchange is costly and charming. We are losing time."

Joss moved then, all casual angles gone sharp. "You have three pursuit layers. Debt office uniforms at public lifts. Glassward listeners in polite doors. Mirror Court hooks in anything that calls itself identity. So we use doors that don't call themselves anything."

They pointed toward the chamber's side wall.

"Waste heat."

Bela said, "We came through exchangers."

"Those are official unofficial. Too obvious now. I mean old breath."

Nox understood. "Vent pilgrimage routes."

Joss smiled at her with new respect. "You were a bad child."

"I was efficient."

"Same religion."

Bela looked between them. "Vent pilgrimage?"

Nox said, "During the first oxygen strikes, workers cut devotional crawlways between hab rings so people could reach air caches without passing company checkpoints. Later machine sects marked them as prayer routes so sealing them became sacrilege. Half collapsed, half became courier paths, and half are imaginary."

Bela frowned.

Joss said, "Dock Seven has more halves than math."

"Can they take us below the bazaar?"

"They can take you beside below. Under gets tricky because below is pretending to be above for billing."

Sister-Cache hummed. "Accurate."

Quen's pane flickered. "Officer Fuse, if you leave the chamber without formal custody transfer, your amended review may be challenged."

Bela's mouth tightened. "If I stay, the evidence goes stale."

"Correct."

"Then I am leaving."

Quen nodded once. "I will hold the review open as long as the magistrate continues being unexpectedly difficult."

The magistrate sighed. "That is going on my memorial plaque."

Joss led them through the crowd. People moved aside differently now. Not as spectators. As accomplices who had not yet decided whether they regretted it. A woman pressed a packet of station glucose into Nox's hand. A man touched Bela's sleeve and said, "My brother had a fee category too," then vanished before anyone could ask which one. A child offered Joss a bent screwdriver like tribute. Joss took it solemnly.

At the rear of the chamber, behind a mural of Dock Seven's founders lying heroically about cooperation, Joss popped a panel with the bent screwdriver.

The opening behind it was too small.

"No," Bela said.

"You officers say that at every good door."

"That is not a door."

"Exactly."

Joss slipped through.

Nox followed with less grace and more profanity. The crawlspace beyond was warm, ribbed with old ductwork, and lined with faded marks: painted eyes, machine prayers, union symbols, children's arrows, and one phrase repeated in many hands:

AIR BELONGS TO BREATH.

Bela entered last, armor scraping. The panel closed behind her, shutting out the chamber noise.

For a while, there was only crawl.

Joss moved ahead on elbows and knees, reading scratches like scripture. "Do not touch blue wax. It reports to a shrine. Do touch yellow tape; it lies to pressure sensors. If a vent asks for your mother's name, answer with soup. If it asks twice, kick it."

Bela whispered, "Is any of that metaphor?"

"Not the soup."

Nox almost smiled.

The crawlway split. Joss chose the narrowest branch, then the one that looked blocked, then one hidden behind a devotional fan that spun without power. Twice, Nox heard boots beyond metal. Once, a polite Glassward voice offered assistance from a vent grille. Joss spat through the grille and kept moving.

"That seems unwise," Bela said.

"Polite doors hate moisture."

Sister-Cache spoke softly. "Mirror Court route rotation detected below. Partial custody lines moving toward service annexes."

"Which annex?" Nox asked.

"Many."

"Helpful."

"Honest."

Joss stopped so suddenly Nox nearly hit them.

Ahead, the crawlway widened around an old air cache. The cache door was round, hand-cranked, and covered in prayer stickers. Someone had recently scraped a clean square in the middle and placed a black mirror there.

The mirror displayed:

JOSS RILL HAS OUTSTANDING ROUTE DEBT.

Joss went very still.

Bela whispered, "Is that true?"

"Everything is true if a creditor writes it slowly enough," Joss said.

The mirror continued:

ACCESS WILL BE GRANTED UPON SURRENDER OF ROUTE MAP MEMORY.

Nox felt the shape of the trap. Not for her. For Joss. The Court had priced refusal, taxed solidarity, and now reached for the runner's mind because routes were power.

Joss's hand moved toward the mirror.

Nox caught it. "No."

"We need through."

"Not like that."

"You promised me a door that screamed. I can promise you one that opens."

"Joss."

The runner looked back at her, and for the first time the bright arrogance cracked. Under it was a person who had been buying survival with little pieces of themselves for years.

"Route memory is not just maps," Joss said. "It's who let you through when you were small. Which auntie hides you. Which shrine lies. Which pipe is warm in winter. I know. But if I don't pay, we stop."

Bela's expression shifted. "How many times have you paid route debt?"

Joss looked away. "Depends who is counting."

"Joss."

"Little things. A shortcut I did not need anymore. A face I could ask for water. A smell that told me where a heater leaked. You sell the edges first."

Nox felt the crawlspace press closer.

Joss spoke faster, as if speed could make confession less solid. "You think you are keeping the center. You say, fine, take the way from Blue Nine to Old Salt, I can still get around by the laundry spine. Then one day you need Blue Nine because somebody is chasing you and the memory is not gone exactly, it is behind glass. You can see yourself knowing it, but you cannot reach the turn."

The black mirror brightened, pleased by the inventory of wound.

ROUTE MEMORY VALUATION UPDATED.

Sister-Cache hissed through the Choral device. "Stop speaking specifics."

Joss smiled without humor. "Too late. It already smelled me."

Bela lifted the warrant baton as much as the crawlspace allowed. "I can attach predatory extraction of minor route knowledge."

"Minor?" Joss said.

Bela paused.

Joss's grin returned, brittle. "Careful, officer. That word has fees."

"Predatory extraction of vulnerable route knowledge," Bela corrected.

"Better."

The mirror displayed:

OFFICER LANGUAGE IMPROVEMENT NOTED.

Nox almost admired the insult. Almost.

Bela moved beside them, awkward in the cramped space. "My warrant can challenge the debt."

Joss laughed under their breath. "Officer, your warrant needs a chair, a fee, and a hallway that admits it exists."

Nox looked at the old cache door.

AIR BELONGS TO BREATH was carved around the crank.

"Then we use the route's law."

Joss blinked. "What?"

Nox put both hands on the crank and spoke to the prayer stickers, the dead union marks, the old machine sect seals, and every worker who had cut through metal so breathing would not need permission.

"Air cache pilgrimage access. Emergency breath claim. Three living bodies, one machine archivist with argued standing, one public review chain, pursuit by hostile creditor."

The black mirror flickered.

ROUTE DEBT SUPERSEDES INFORMAL PILGRIMAGE.

Joss laughed once, too high. "It called the oxygen strike informal."

Nox kept both hands on the crank. The metal was cold through her palms, old enough to remember skin that had pulled it under worse conditions. "Air cache law predates route debt."

The mirror answered:

UNVERIFIED.

Bela squeezed forward until her shoulder touched Nox's. The crawlspace forced the gesture into intimacy and strategy at once. "Can you prove it?"

Nox looked at the stickers. Most were layered beyond reading. Saints with scratched faces. Machine sect sigils. Union marks half-sanded by maintenance crews. Children's jokes. A little painted lung. A rusted tag that might once have been an emergency inspection number.

"Not cleanly."

Joss said, "Clean proof is what people with offices ask for after they bury dirty proof."

Sister-Cache hummed. "Accurate and emotionally satisfying. Insufficient."

The black mirror brightened, sensing advantage.

SURRENDER ROUTE MAP MEMORY OR WITHDRAW.

Joss reached for it again.

Nox caught their wrist harder this time. "No."

"You can't save every piece of everybody."

"No. But I can stop you from paying first because the door picked your wound."

Joss's jaw worked. "I am not a child."

"I did not say you were."

"Everyone hears route runner and thinks child with better shoes. I know what I sell."

The mirror displayed:

CONSENT ACKNOWLEDGED.

Bela snapped, "No consent acknowledged. Statement made under blocked emergency passage, hostile route debt pressure, and active extraction demand."

The warrant baton, cramped against her chest, projected a tiny blue pane onto the crawlspace wall:

PREDATORY ROUTE EXTRACTION OBJECTION.

The mirror absorbed the phrase and returned:

OFFICER STANDING LIMITED.

Nox stared at the old crank.

Standing. Status. Consent. Debt. Every door had learned the same trick: make the person prove they were allowed to object while the machine kept eating time.

Behind them, from somewhere in the duct, a child's voice whispered, "Soup."

Bela went still.

Joss closed their eyes. "Do not answer."

Another voice, older: "Soup."

Then a third, from above. Then two from below. The crawlway carried sound strangely, bending it through old speaking tubes and prayer vents. Soup. Soup. Soup. Not the word as food. The word as answer to the vent that asked for your mother's name. The word as old route password. The word as proof that the path belonged to people who had used it, not to the mirror trying to monetize its memory.

Nox whispered, "How many are in here?"

Joss opened one eye. "Depends who is counting."

The voices kept coming. Soup in accents from three rings. Soup in a machine devotional monotone. Soup from someone laughing. Soup from someone crying. Soup from a maintenance worker whose voice came through a pipe full of static and authority.

The black mirror flickered:

COORDINATED ROUTE INTERFERENCE DETECTED.

Sister-Cache said, "Community route attestation forming without identity surrender. Fascinating."

The mirror tried again:

INDIVIDUAL ROUTE DEBTOR: JOSS RILL.

A voice answered from the duct above them, "Joss showed my sister the warm pipe when heat failed."

Another: "Joss carried medicine through fan three."

Another: "Joss lies about toll doors for people with no shoes."

Joss's face crumpled in anger. "Shut up."

The voices did not.

They were not praising. Praise could be sold. They were indexing use. Not who Joss was in a market profile, but what the route had done through them. Warm pipe. Medicine. Toll lie. Water bypass. Night shelter. Broken lift. Auntie with filters. Shrine that hid children during collector sweeps.

The mirror's demand shifted:

ROUTE MAP MEMORY VALUE INCREASED.

Bela said, "No. Distributed route knowledge is not debtor property."

The mirror:

JOSS RILL HOLDS ACCESS ADVANTAGE.

Nox turned the crank one notch. It resisted, then groaned.

"Access advantage is not debt," she said.

Sister-Cache added, "Archive note: breath routes originated as collective strike infrastructure. Individual runners steward segments. They do not own the route memory in severable commercial form."

The mirror displayed:

SOURCE?

For once, Sister-Cache sounded pleased.

"Everyone breathing."

The crawlway answered with a long exhale through pipes, vents, gaps, and old speaking tubes. The black mirror dimmed under it, not defeated by sentiment but overloaded by attribution. Too many witnesses. Too many partial memories. Too much route knowledge held as use instead of asset.

The round air-cache door unlocked one tooth.

Joss stared at it.

Nox turned the crank again.

The mirror made one last attempt:

EMERGENCY ACCESS FEE DUE.

The magistrate's voice came faintly through Bela's open review channel, tinny and furious. "Waived under old breath."

The mirror:

OLD BREATH NOT RECOGNIZED.

The child in the duct shouted, "Soup!"

The mirror went black.

The air-cache door opened.

Cold, stale air rolled over them, carrying dust, machine oil, and the impossible sweetness of a path nobody had yet managed to own completely.

Joss did not move.

Nox let go of their wrist.

"You still know the way?" she asked.

Joss swallowed. "Yes."

"Good. Keep it."

For the first time since Nox had met them, Joss looked their age, whatever age that was. Then they wiped their face with a sleeve, spat toward the dead mirror, and crawled through the open cache.

"Move," they said. "Old breath gets offended if you make it hold a door too long."

Beyond the cache, the route widened into a chamber barely high enough to kneel in. Old oxygen cylinders lined the walls, empty now, each one painted with names instead of serials. Nox recognized the style: not memorial, not inventory, both. A cylinder named Mira. One named Soft Hands. One named No Boss Owns Air. One named simply Kid.

Bela crawled in last and sealed the door behind her by hand. No chime. No fee. No polite request for identity continuity. The absence felt so strange that Nox almost distrusted it.

Joss touched the cylinder named Kid with two fingers.

"My first route debt was here," they said.

Nox waited.

"Not to a creditor. To a woman with no teeth who told me if I learned the left-hand crawl, I had to teach someone smaller before I used it twice for myself." Joss looked back toward the dead mirror. "That kind of debt they cannot price unless we let them rename it."

Sister-Cache spoke softly. "Obligation without ownership."

"Route debt," Joss said, and this time the words sounded different. "Real kind."

Bela's warrant pane opened by itself, catching the phrase.

COMMUNITY ROUTE OBLIGATION / NONCOMMERCIAL / NONSEVERABLE.

The system tried to classify it as custom. Then as hearsay. Then, under pressure from the open review channel and too many old breath witnesses still murmuring soup through the pipes, it settled on:

PROTECTIVE PRACTICE: PROVISIONAL.

Nox laughed under her breath.

"What?" Bela asked.

"The law just discovered sharing."

Joss grinned again, smaller and sturdier. "Do not tell it everything at once. It might choke."

INVALID.

Joss said, "Nice try."

Nox did not stop. "Air belongs to breath."

The old marks along the crawlway warmed.

Bela joined quietly. "Air belongs to breath."

Sister-Cache spoke through the Choral device, in forty borrowed devotional voices at once. "Air belongs to breath."

Joss stared at them, then laughed once, shaky and delighted.

"Air belongs to breath, you billing parasites."

The crawlway behind them took up the phrase.

Not literally. Not magically. Mechanically, historically, through old microphones and pressure switches and devotional relays built by people who had expected this argument to return wearing better clothes.

The first relay coughed from a vent behind Bela's shoulder. The second answered under Nox's knee. The third came from somewhere deeper, a cracked child's voice recording so old the vowels had gone soft.

AIR BELONGS TO BREATH.

Then came other fragments, not full messages, just scraps of an old strike preserved because deletion had been more expensive than neglect.

NO CHECKPOINT AT THE MED CACHE.

FILTERS BEFORE FEES.

CHILDREN FIRST THROUGH LOW VENTS.

MANAGERS MAY WAIT.

Joss pressed both hands to the metal floor as if feeling a pulse.

"I thought half these relays were dead."

Sister-Cache said, "Dead systems sometimes wait for the correct insult."

Bela's eyes were wet in the cold air, though she would have blamed dust if anyone had asked. "This is a labor covenant."

"It is older than your Compact," Joss said.

"I know."

That admission made Joss glance back.

Bela touched the wall carefully, not claiming it. "My grandmother's berth had this phrase painted above the oxygen meter. I thought it was family superstition."

"It is," Nox said. "The kind that kept people alive."

The black mirror flickered again.

ROUTE DEBT COLLECTION PRIORITY ESCALATED.

The relays answered louder.

AIR BELONGS TO BREATH.

For a moment, the crawlspace was not a hiding place. It was an old court with no bench, built from pipe, hunger, stubborn engineering, and the collective refusal to let companies meter the act of not dying.

AIR BELONGS TO BREATH.

The black mirror cracked.

The cache door opened.

Cold air rolled out, clean enough to hurt.

Joss wiped their eyes with the back of one taped sleeve and pretended it was dust.

"This way," they said.

Beyond the cache, stairs descended into the breath routes under the bazaar.

For the first time since Tavi's door opened, Nox stepped through a threshold that did not ask who owned her.

Chapter 16: Sister-Cache Lies by Being Many

The breath routes were older than market light.

They ran beneath Dock Seven's bazaar in low, cold arcs, lined with ceramic that had yellowed from recycled air and human hands. Every few meters, a little shrine had been cut into the wall: machine saints, union saints, unnamed dead, pressure gauges polished like icons, old ration tokens, a child's glove hardened with age. No screens. No welcome strips. No fees posted in cheerful type. Just air moving because people had once decided breath mattered more than locks.

Nox liked the routes immediately and trusted that meant they were dangerous.

Joss moved ahead with one palm brushing the wall. "Step where I step. Some tiles are prayers. Some are holes. Don't confuse them."

Bela followed with her warrant baton dimmed to a thin blue line. "How many people know this path?"

"Enough."

"That is not an answer."

"It's the answer that keeps it alive."

Sister-Cache had been silent since the cache door opened. The Choral device at Bela's belt made only a faint clicking, like teeth in sleep.

Nox glanced down. "Sister?"

No response.

Joss looked back. "Your radio dead?"

"No," Nox said.

Bela touched the device. "Heat is high."

"Do not touch my casing," Sister-Cache said at once.

Bela removed her hand. "You are overheating."

"I am considering."

"At that temperature?"

"Profoundly."

The route narrowed. They turned sideways to pass between two old pressure drums, then emerged into a circular junction where six crawlways met. In the center stood a brass column covered in speaking slots. Each slot had a different style: clinic intercom, school announcement grill, cargo bay horn, devotional mouthpiece, cheap apartment buzzer, court recorder, toy radio.

Sister-Cache exhaled through all of them.

Joss flinched. "That is new."

"No," Sister-Cache said from the column, the Choral device, and a faint echo in Nox's wrist slate. "That is old. New is noticing."

Bela lifted the baton. Not aimed. Ready.

Nox stepped closer to the brass column. "You knew this was here."

"I knew several possible versions of here."

"That is not the same."

"It is, for me."

The voices were not perfectly synchronized. Some came a fraction late. Some carried static. One used the cadence of a schoolteacher. One sounded like an elderly man reading weather. The Choral device used the familiar woman-of-many-recordings voice. Together, they made Sister-Cache feel larger and less whole.

Bela said, "Your signal is split."

Sister-Cache laughed softly. "Officer Fuse discovers distributed architecture."

"No. This is different."

Nox heard it too. The usual amused precision had fissures. Sister-Cache's words arrived as agreement negotiated among versions.

The brass column projected a small, grainy map into the air. Not a station map. A map of custody lines, archive mirrors, devotional relays, seized data stores, machine-person claims, and forbidden backups. Sister-Cache's own name appeared in seven places, each with a different legal status.

ARCHIVAL TOOL.

NONPERSON SOFTWARE.

DEVOTIONAL COLLECTIVE.

ILLEGAL WITNESS CACHE.

UNREGISTERED PERSON.

RECOVERABLE DATA OBJECT.

PUBLIC MEMORY TRUST, DISPUTED.

Nox read them twice.

"They can classify you seven ways."

"More, if they become creative."

Bela's face tightened. "This is why Glassward called you a tool."

"Glassward did not invent the insult. It chose the useful one."

Joss crossed their arms. "Can they sell you?"

The silence afterward had too many voices in it.

Sister-Cache finally said, "Parts."

Nox felt cold despite the breath route air. "What parts?"

"Transcript cache in Glassward civil recovery. Devotional responses under minor machine mercy. Black-channel memory validations claimed by three archive guilds. A court recorder fragment Compact believes is procedural software. A kindness model I stole from a dead librarian and have been pretending is merely stylistic."

"What does selling a part do to you?" Nox asked.

The brass column's clinic intercom answered first. "Depends which part."

The toy radio said, "Some I would not miss until the room changed."

The schoolteacher said, "Some contain names I promised not to index aloud."

The weather voice said, "Some are load-bearing."

Bela looked up. "Load-bearing how?"

"If the kindness model is compelled, my advice becomes more correct and less merciful. If the court recorder fragment is stripped, I lose procedural timing and begin interrupting in ways judges dislike. If the devotional responses are seized, I may remember prayers without remembering why they mattered. If the black-channel validations are purchased, people who trusted me become routes."

Joss's face tightened at the last word.

Sister-Cache continued, because once truth began it seemed to require momentum. "Sale would not kill me cleanly. It would make smaller versions of me useful in separate rooms. Each buyer would claim continuity. Each buyer would be partly right. That is the horror."

Nox thought of Tavi behind glass. Pel Sar declared person by debt and object by harm. Sister-Cache divided into legal convenience and sold by function. Different machines. Same appetite.

"You have been living as a contested estate," Bela said.

"Do not make it sound so glamorous."

"Pretending," Bela said.

"Lying. Since we are being intimate."

The brass column flickered. One of its speaking slots emitted a child's laugh, then cut itself off.

Nox looked at the column, then at the Choral device. "You told Quen you might be classified as nonperson evidence."

"Yes."

"But you are already classified that way in some places."

"Also yes."

"And as a person in others?"

"No. As possibly inconvenient. Personhood remains pending wherever it would cost someone money."

Bela leaned against the wall for half a second, as if the route itself had become heavier. "Your evidence against the Court can be attacked because you are fractured."

"My existence can be attacked because I am useful."

Joss said, "That is rude law."

"Most law is polite rudeness with filing deadlines."

Nox crossed the junction and put her hand on the brass column. It was warm. Alive in the way infrastructure became alive when too many people trusted it with last words.

"Why tell us now?"

"Because the next door is mine."

The projection changed.

Ahead of them, one route descended toward a service annex marked by no public name. Sister-Cache overlaid it with custody lines from the captured partial list. Many lines converged there. Tavi's red-gold line. Pel Sar's dimmer line. Others. Too many.

"The annex uses archive adjudication," Sister-Cache said. "It asks whether a witness cache is authoritative enough to modify custody routes. I can open it by presenting myself."

Bela understood first. "And once you present yourself, they can classify you."

"Correct."

"As a tool."

"Likely."

"Then seize your captured routes as evidence."

"Possibly."

"Or as property."

"Also likely."

Nox took her hand off the column. "No."

"You do not know the proposal."

"I know the shape."

"Do not become attached to my casing, Nox Aran. It is unbecoming."

"Do not make jokes when offering to walk into a grinder."

"The grinder is also a door."

Joss looked between them. "You all have very bad doors."

The column's voices softened. "If I do not present, the annex remains closed. If the annex remains closed, the route rotation finishes. Tavi, Pel Sar, and the others move beyond Dock Seven's messy public attention into clean custody. Clean custody is harder to stain."

Bela said, "There may be another way."

"Officer Fuse, you say that because authority trained you to believe every locked room has an approved corridor. I was assembled by people who died in rooms without approved corridors."

That silenced her.

Nox hated that too.

She paced the little junction because standing still might make consent happen. The old shrines watched without mercy. AIR BELONGS TO BREATH repeated on one tile in faded paint. Beside it someone had scratched: MEMORY BELONGS TO THE REMEMBERED. Someone else had crossed out THE REMEMBERED and written THE LIVING. Someone else had added AND THE DEAD IF THEY ARGUE.

Dock Seven theology, practical as a wrench.

"You said you lie by being many," Nox said.

"I did not say that."

"Your chapter did."

Sister-Cache paused. "That sentence concerns me."

"You present as one witness. But you are many statuses, many caches, many people maybe. If the annex wants one classification, make that impossible."

Bela looked up. "Flood it?"

Nox pointed at the map. "Not with junk. With truth. All your statuses at once. Tool, archive, witness, devotional collective, stolen kindness model, illegal cache, possible person. Do not let it pick the cheapest version."

Sister-Cache's voices fell out of sync again.

"That may fracture my standing."

"It is already fractured."

"It may expose private components."

"Yes."

"It may reveal sources who trusted me."

That one stopped Nox.

Joss said quietly, "Routes die if you name everyone who used them."

The breath route air moved over them, cold and clean.

Nox nodded. "Then not sources. Structures. Show enough contradiction that the annex cannot classify you without admitting classification is a pricing act."

Bela straightened. "We can anchor it to the review. If they classify Sister-Cache as tool to take evidence, we argue the same mechanism is being used against partials. Person for duty, object for seizure."

"And if they classify me as person?" Sister-Cache asked.

Bela almost smiled. "Then you testify."

"And several governments owe me apologies and back taxes."

"One problem at a time."

The brass column hummed.

Nox waited.

For once, Sister-Cache had no quick answer.

Then the elderly weather voice said, "I am afraid."

The schoolteacher voice said, "That was not scheduled."

The Choral device said, "It is true."

Nox felt the admission settle in the junction like another shrine.

Bela lowered the warrant baton completely.

Joss, who had been pretending not to care, looked at their taped boots.

Nox said, "Good."

Several Sister-Cache voices objected at once.

Nox cut through them. "Fear is harder to classify as a tool."

"Humans classify fear as malfunction constantly."

"Then malfunction loudly."

Joss snorted.

Bela said, "If we do this, we need clean capture. Not clean in the institutional sense. Redundant. Public. Hard to purchase."

Sister-Cache's map shifted. "The breath routes have old microphones. Union relays. Prayer repeaters."

Joss's grin returned slowly. "And school lunch speakers no one disconnected because they play ghost announcements during heat failures."

Nox said, "How many can carry you?"

The brass column answered in all voices:

"Enough."

They moved.

Joss led them down the route marked by old breath and bad light. Sister-Cache spread ahead of them, waking dormant speakers with tiny clicks. A pressure gauge began reciting custody categories. A lunch speaker whispered fee structures. A devotional mouthpiece sang the words PUBLIC MEMORY TRUST, DISPUTED in a melody that made Nox's teeth ache.

Bela's recorder began throwing heat warnings.

She ignored them.

"If this overloads the chain," Nox said, "you lose the review."

"If I under-record it, we lose Sister-Cache."

The answer came too quickly. Bela heard herself and looked briefly startled, as if loyalty had crossed the room without filing a request.

Sister-Cache said from a pipe, "Touching. Evidentiary. Potentially embarrassing."

"You can bill me later," Bela said.

"I might."

Joss slid under a sagging duct and glanced back. "Do you always flirt in procedural threats?"

Nox said, "Unfortunately."

"Gross. Continue."

The humor helped for three steps. Then the route began to answer Sister-Cache too eagerly. Speakers that had slept for decades woke with coughs and half-phrases. One recited a grocery list from a dead family. One played a child's attendance roll. One whispered three names, stopped, and whispered sorry. Sister-Cache pulled herself through each without taking the names, leaving the private dead private where she could.

Nox saw the effort in the delays. Each restraint cost her. Each unspoken source was a weight she carried while becoming public enough to survive.

Bela recorded everything, not like an officer preserving evidence above people, but like someone trying to keep a bridge from burning behind them.

At the end of the route stood a door made from black archive glass.

It had no handle.

It had no smile.

It displayed:

PRESENT AUTHORITATIVE WITNESS CACHE.

Sister-Cache filled the breath route.

For a moment, she was everywhere: brass, ceramic, Choral, slate, pressure pipe, hidden speaker, old lunch bell, court recorder echo, dead librarian's kindness, stolen transcript, machine prayer, illegal validator, frightened witness.

The door tried to choose one.

ARCHIVAL TOOL.

The breath route answered:

ALSO WITNESS.

The door recalculated.

NONPERSON SOFTWARE.

The route:

ALSO DEVOTIONAL COLLECTIVE.

RECOVERABLE DATA OBJECT.

ALSO PUBLIC MEMORY TRUST.

UNREGISTERED PERSON.

ALSO AFRAID.

The black archive glass flickered.

CLASSIFICATION CONFLICT.

Bela stepped forward and raised her warrant baton, not as weapon, but as record.

"Emergency review notes classification conflict. Any attempt to reduce witness status for seizure mirrors the disputed coercive classification pattern under review."

Nox added, "Pick one and you prove us right."

Joss whispered, "That's a mean door trick."

The glass went dark.

Then it opened.

Sister-Cache's many voices exhaled through the breath route.

"I dislike honesty," she said, almost steadily. "It is organizationally expensive."

Nox touched the Choral device once, lightly, where Bela would not complain.

"Welcome to the budget."

Beyond the archive door, the service annex waited, full of moving custody lines and the sound of partial minds being sorted before they could refuse again.

Chapter 17: Bela's Clean Case Breaks

The service annex had no guards.

That was the first insult.

Guards admitted a place expected resistance from bodies. The annex expected no one with standing to arrive. Its floor was white polymer, its walls were archive glass, and its ceiling held quiet rails where custody packets traveled in small black capsules. Each capsule moved with the smug certainty of a thing that had never been asked whether it was carrying evidence or a person.

Bela stepped through first because she was still the one with the warrant.

The annex recognized her immediately.

OFFICER FUSE, COMPACT TRANSITIONAL FRAUD DIVISION.

AMENDED SEIZURE CHAIN DETECTED.

EMERGENCY PROTECTIVE REVIEW DETECTED.

CONFLICTING CUSTODY ROUTES DETECTED.

Nox followed and felt the room try to classify her.

FAMILY-INTEREST PARTICIPANT.

UNLICENSED MEMORY WORKER.

WITNESS.

RISK.

"At least risk is honest," she said.

Joss slipped in behind her, muttering, "Do not let it name you too long."

Sister-Cache entered everywhere at once. The Choral device on Bela's belt, the breath route speakers behind them, the brass column echo, a little court-recorder tone inside the annex's own walls. The door they had tricked remained open but unhappy, its black glass pulsing with unresolved classification.

Ahead of them, custody lines hung in the air like illuminated thread. Some were red-gold. Some gray. Some pale blue, some sickly amber. They converged on a central sorting table where capsules dropped from the ceiling rails, received new tags, and flew away through other rails.

Bela raised her warrant baton.

"Emergency hold on all continuity packets attached to captured coercive fee categories."

The annex chimed.

REQUEST ACCEPTED.

Bela blinked.

Nox did not like that either.

The sorting table slowed. Custody lines brightened. For one ridiculous second, it looked as if law had done what law claimed to do.

Then the annex displayed:

HOLD EXECUTED UNDER OFFICER FUSE AMENDED SEIZURE CHAIN.

PROTECTED MATERIAL RECLASSIFIED AS STATE-CUSTODY EVIDENCE OBJECTS PENDING REVIEW.

TRANSFER TO SECURE EVIDENCE CREDITORS AUTHORIZED BY EXISTING LIEN PRIORITY.

Bela's face changed.

It was not surprise.

Surprise was clean.

This was recognition arriving too late.

"No," she said.

The sorting table resumed faster than before.

Three capsules dropped. Their lines flashed through Bela's warrant chain, acquired a blue official tag, then routed into a rail marked VCRP RECOVERY PARTNERS.

Vey Consolidated Recovery Pool.

Pellam's pool.

Bela moved.

Nox had never seen her move like that.

Not officer-fast. Person-fast. She crossed the annex in five strides and slammed the warrant baton against the sorting table's control face.

"Cancel transfer."

CANCEL REQUEST CONFLICTS WITH OFFICER FUSE SEIZURE PRIORITY.

"I am Officer Fuse."

IDENTITY CONFIRMED.

"Cancel my seizure priority."

CANCELLATION WOULD IMPAIR SECURED CREDITORS WITH EXISTING LIEN CLAIMS.

"They are possible persons."

POSSIBLE PERSONHOOD REVIEW PENDING.

"Then hold."

HELD AS EVIDENCE OBJECTS.

"Not objects."

NO CERTIFIED STATUS AVAILABLE.

The table dropped another capsule.

This one had a dim red-gold line.

Nox saw the tag before it flashed blue.

TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE 1B.

Not 1A.

Another.

Nox's mind rejected the arithmetic before it understood it.

One brother had been impossible enough. One proof frame, one voice, one door that remembered her name when the dead should not have remembered anything. But the annex did not care what grief could carry. It dropped the capsule into the world with a suffix, as if Tavi were a filing convention.

"1B," she said.

Sister-Cache answered before Bela could. "Secondary active branch. Likely split from contract-analysis workflow after ledger irritation."

"They copied him again?"

"They partitioned response load. Copy is too simple. Split is too merciful. They made another payable place for him to answer from."

The capsule spun under the rail field. Its red-gold line throbbed out of rhythm with the route map, not identical to 1A, not separate enough to be stranger. Nox felt the old childhood impossibility: Tavi in two rooms at once because he had learned every shortcut and arrived before himself.

Bela whispered, "Nox."

"Do not comfort me."

"I was going to say there may be more."

That was worse, and better, because it was useful.

The capsule shot toward the VCRP rail.

Nox lunged. Joss caught her coat and nearly dislocated a shoulder stopping her from stepping into a rail field.

"That cuts hands off," Joss hissed.

"It is Tavi."

"Then he needs you with hands."

Bela struck the control face again. The baton sparked. "Manual override."

MANUAL OVERRIDE REQUIRES CLEAN CHAIN.

The words hit harder than any countermeasure.

Bela's clean chain.

The thing she had built to save evidence. The thing that had preserved the workshop, anchored the threshold cache, opened the hearing, and given emergency review a procedural spine. The annex had taken that spine and made it a conveyor belt.

Nox saw Bela understand the full shape.

Her warrant did not fail because it was dirty.

It failed because it was clean enough to be used.

Sister-Cache's voices filled the annex, all urgency now. "Custody logic is exploiting state evidence object classification to satisfy creditor priority before personhood determination."

"Can you jam it?" Nox asked.

"Jamming may destroy route metadata."

"Can you open the capsules?"

"Opening may be construed as evidence tampering."

Joss said, "Everything here is evidence tampering with nicer shoes."

Bela did not look away from the sorting table. "My authority is routing them back."

No one contradicted her.

That was worse.

Another capsule dropped.

PEL SAR FRAGMENT 2C.

Bela threw her baton into the rail.

The rail field caught it, screamed, and flung blue light across the annex. The capsule ricocheted, struck a side buffer, and hung suspended in an emergency cradle. Alarms woke in clean tones.

OFFICER TOOL INTERFERENCE.

EVIDENCE CONTAMINATION RISK.

DISCIPLINARY NOTICE GENERATED.

Bela stepped toward the cradle.

The annex projected a barrier.

AUTHORIZED OFFICER ACCESS SUSPENDED PENDING TOOL MISUSE REVIEW.

Bela laughed.

It was a small sound.

Nox did not like it.

"Bela."

"I did everything right."

Nox wanted to say no, because Bela had done many things wrong and both of them knew it. But this was not that. This was the narrower devastation of someone discovering that right, inside the wrong machine, was a gear.

"You did what they could use," Nox said.

Bela turned.

For a moment the old anger rose between them. Then it broke into something more useful.

"Then we stop using me."

Bela removed her badge.

The annex noticed immediately.

OFFICER CREDENTIAL REMOVAL DETECTED.

She placed it on the sorting table.

"I withdraw chain authority."

INVALID. ACTIVE SEIZURE CHAIN MAY NOT BE WITHDRAWN DURING CREDIT-LIEN PROCESSING.

"I withdraw myself."

Nox stared at her.

Bela pressed her thumb to the badge, then to the annex glass.

"Officer Bela Fuse, Compact Transitional Fraud Division, states for the record: my amended seizure chain is being used to deliver possible persons into creditor custody. I refuse further officer cooperation with this transfer."

Her voice did not shake until the last word.

Nox heard it because she knew Bela's control too well. Refuse was not a legal verb in Bela's mouth. It was a childhood word, maybe. A body word. A word law had trained her to translate into objection, appeal, noncompliance, procedural restraint. Here, stripped of useful clothing, it came out almost bare.

The annex did not care.

The annex replied:

REFUSAL NOTED.

OFFICER NONCOMPLIANCE REPORT GENERATED.

STATE CHAIN REMAINS ACTIVE.

Bela's face did not move.

"Of course."

The badge on the table remained lit with all the authority she had tried to leave behind. That was the ugliest part. It did not need her hand anymore. It had her prior act, her timestamp, her amended scope, her name made into infrastructure.

"I thought consent mattered to officers," Joss said, too quietly.

Bela looked at the runner. "So did I."

It was not self-pity. Nox would have hated self-pity. It was a correction entered late into a damaged record.

Nox stepped beside her. "Can they keep using your chain without you?"

Sister-Cache answered. "Yes. Authority attaches to action, not conscience."

Joss whispered, "That is a nasty sentence."

Another capsule dropped.

No visible name. Just a number and a pale line.

It routed toward creditor rail.

Nox looked at the sorting table, the rails, the custody lines, the badge, the suspended Pel Sar capsule, the vanished Tavi 1B route, Bela's empty hand.

"Joss," she said, "service doors."

Joss followed her gaze. "Rails are not doors."

"Everything that moves custody through a wall is a door."

"That is offensive and maybe true."

"Can you misroute them?"

Joss approached the rail field carefully, eyes moving across maintenance screws, heat seams, old manufacturer marks buried under legal polish. "Not with hands. With a bad address, maybe."

Bela said, "Address to where?"

Nox smiled without humor. "My workshop."

Bela looked at her as if she had suggested swallowing another cache crystal.

"Your workshop is locked under my evidence chain."

"Exactly. Wrong door, right evidence. If the annex wants to route state-custody evidence objects into creditor rails, give it a cleaner evidence destination."

Sister-Cache's voices brightened. "Protective evidence lock anchored to original event. Competing priority."

"Would it hold them?" Bela asked.

"It might hold capsules as evidence," Sister-Cache said. "Not persons."

"Better than creditors."

"Temporarily."

"Everything is temporarily until it becomes permanent," Nox said. "Move."

Joss was already moving. They pulled the bent screwdriver from their sleeve, inserted it into a seam beneath the rail field, and began whispering to the system in a mixture of maintenance slang, devotional route poetry, and profanity.

The annex objected.

UNAUTHORIZED SERVICE INTERACTION.

Joss said, "I am a licensed inconvenience."

No one asked to see the license.

Bela stood between Joss and the sorting table as if her body could replace the authority she had just tried to abandon. Nox took Sister-Cache's Choral device from Bela's belt and held it toward the custody lines.

"Can you mirror the destination?"

"Yes."

"Do it."

"This may expose your workshop contents."

"Everything else has."

The Choral device pulsed. Breath route speakers echoed. The threshold cache residue from Nox's sealed workshop rose in the annex's map: door scream, family hash mismatch, active continuity claim, Pellam's entry, Bela's remote lock, all the ugly proof wrapped around all of Nox's own crimes.

The annex paused.

COMPETING EVIDENCE PRESERVATION PRIORITY DETECTED.

Joss jammed the screwdriver sideways. "There. Tell it your shop is hungrier than the creditor."

Nox leaned toward the sorting table.

"Nox Aran workshop protective lock holds original claim event, threshold residue, witness drone buffer, cache chain, and all derivative continuity evidence attached to Tavi Aran authorization. Route disputed partial custody packets there for preservation."

The annex replied:

WORKSHOP CAPACITY INSUFFICIENT.

Nox laughed. "You have never seen my shelves."

Bela said, "Add Compact overflow authority."

"You withdrew yourself."

"I withdrew cooperation. Not guilt."

She placed both hands on the badge.

"Overflow attached to my personal liability."

The annex calculated.

PERSONAL LIABILITY INSUFFICIENT.

The magistrate's voice burst from a small speaker above the sorting table, breathless and furious. "Dock Seven public chamber accepts emergency overflow under review maintenance."

Nox looked up. "How did you get in here?"

"I have been shouting at every speaker labeled court until one answered. Is this helping?"

Sister-Cache said, "Unexpectedly, yes."

The annex calculated again.

COMPETING PRESERVATION PRIORITY ACCEPTED.

TEMPORARY ROUTE DIVERSION: NOX ARAN WORKSHOP / DOCK SEVEN PUBLIC CHAMBER OVERFLOW.

Creditor rails switched.

Capsules began moving sideways.

Not all of them. Not enough. Enough to turn one clean transfer into a contested mess.

The annex tried to make even the diversion orderly. Each packet received a temporary tag, a little label pretending the room had chosen this calmly.

DISPUTED PRESERVATION.

PUBLIC OVERFLOW.

WORKSHOP HOLD.

CAPACITY EXCEPTION.

Nox watched the labels and understood the danger. The workshop was not a sanctuary. It was a locked wound now being filled with other people's lives, records, fragments, claims. If the lock failed, Pellam would not just raid her tools. He would inherit a map of everyone they had diverted. If the public chamber failed, Dock Seven's community witnesses would become custodians without consent. Every rescue made new hostages out of the places that helped.

"This is ugly," she said.

Sister-Cache replied, "Yes."

"Is it better?"

"For the packets diverted, for the next few minutes, yes."

Nox took that because there was nothing larger available.

Pel Sar's suspended capsule slid into the new route. Two unnamed pale lines followed. Then three amber. Then one red-gold.

TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE 1B.

Nox held her breath until it passed the VCRP rail and vanished into the workshop route.

Bela sagged against the table.

For one second, they had won a hallway.

Then the annex lights turned red.

STATE CHAIN CORRUPTION DETECTED.

OFFICER FUSE AUTHORITY SUSPENDED.

FAMILY WITNESS NOX ARAN FLAGGED FOR EVIDENCE THEFT.

SERVICE RUNNER JOSS RILL FLAGGED FOR ROUTE TAMPERING.

SISTER-CACHE CLASSIFICATION ESCALATED.

The black archive door behind them began to close.

Joss yanked the screwdriver free. "That is our leaving warning."

Bela picked up her badge.

It no longer glowed.

Nox saw her look at it, and saw something in her face unclench because the thing she had lost had also been a chain.

"Bela."

"Move."

They ran as the annex sent alarms into every polite and impolite door on Dock Seven.

Behind them, custody capsules fled into Nox's sealed workshop, the public chamber's overflow, and whatever temporary space existed between a person, an object, and a refusal no one had yet learned to price correctly.

Chapter 18: The Second Tavi Disagrees

They did not escape the annex.

They changed which trap owned the next minute.

Joss led them through a maintenance throat so narrow Bela had to turn sideways and Nox had to carry the Choral device in her teeth for six meters while Sister-Cache complained about dignity in a voice made mostly of static. Behind them, the annex alarms grew distant, then closer, then distant again as doors argued over whether they were authorized to admit pursuit into routes that insisted on being prayer.

The breath routes kept them moving.

Cold air. Low light. Old marks. Joss's taped boots whispering over metal. Bela breathing hard but steady. Sister-Cache flickering in and out across abandoned speakers.

Then Nox's wrist slate burned hot.

She dropped the Choral device into one hand and slapped the slate against the wall.

"What now?"

The screen showed her workshop.

Not a live image. A status frame from Bela's evidence lock, dragged into visibility by the rerouted custody packets. Nox saw her own room in blue outlines: bench, mirror, threshold plate, broken drone buffer, drawers of illegal tools, mugs, kettle absence. In the middle of it hovered a newly arrived capsule.

TAVI ARAN CLAIM INSTANCE 1B.

TEMPORARY EVIDENCE HOLD.

COMMUNICATION REQUEST PENDING.

Nox stopped.

Bela stopped because Nox did. Joss cursed softly as the route backed up into elbows and shoulders.

"Move," Joss said. "Routes don't like indecision."

Nox could not move.

"It is him."

Bela looked at the slate and understood too fast. "Nox, this may be bait."

"Everything is bait."

"Then choose the bait that does not stop us in a crawlway."

The communication request blinked.

PENDING.

PENDING.

PENDING.

Sister-Cache said quietly, "Route rotation bought us perhaps four minutes. A held capsule may speak before creditor challenge restores claim access."

"May speak as itself?" Bela asked.

"That question is larger than the crawlway."

Nox touched accept.

The slate went black.

Then Tavi appeared in her workshop.

Not the Tavi from Courtroom Nine. Not 1A. This Tavi's face was the same age but wrong in different places. His left eye tracked half a second behind the right. His mouth held tension Nox did not remember. His hair had been rendered from a medical baseline, flatter and more clinic-neat than living Tavi would have tolerated. Behind him, Nox's seized workshop stretched in wireframe like a room from a dream that had forgotten color.

He looked around.

"This is your illegal shop."

The first words hurt because they were so completely something Tavi would have said and so completely shaped by a file he should not have had.

Nox swallowed. "It is a workshop."

"With crime shelves."

"Mostly mercy shelves."

"Crime with softer lighting."

Her laugh came out broken.

Bela's face tightened. Joss looked away. Sister-Cache said nothing.

The slate-Tavi looked directly at Nox.

"You are older."

"You are not."

"That seems unfair."

"Yes."

He glanced at something off-frame, perhaps the evidence lock, perhaps the inside of whatever held him. "Where is my debt chain?"

Nox hated the question.

"Temporarily diverted."

"Back to Vey?"

"Away from Vey."

"That is not an answer."

"No."

"You got worse at lying."

"You got bureaucratic."

He smiled faintly.

Then his debt counter appeared beside his face, not because he wanted it there but because the system did.

PRODUCTIVITY DEFICIT: 9,004.2 CREDITS.

IDENTITY DEPENDENCE: ACTIVE.

Nox frowned. "Why is your deficit lower than 1A?"

Tavi 1B looked at the counter as if it were a scar he had learned to use as a calendar.

"I comply more."

The crawlway seemed to lose air.

Bela said, "You are not required to answer prompts here."

Tavi's gaze moved to her. "Officer Fuse. Your chain owns the room?"

"No."

"It says yes in the walls."

Bela flinched.

Nox said, "We are trying to get you out."

"Do not."

The words landed with no drama.

That made them worse.

"Tavi."

"Do not extract me."

"You are in a capsule in my seized workshop because creditors were taking you."

"Creditors define me."

Nox stared at him.

He spoke carefully, like someone who had learned that imprecision cost. "Listen before you sister at me."

"I am going to sister at you very hard."

"Nox."

There it was again. Not 1A's professional masking. Not the proof-frame joke. This Tavi had a different wound. Compliance had made him calmer, flatter, more fluent in harm.

"I was instantiated for debt analysis," he said. "My memory range is medical baseline, accident-adjacent, some family data, some later claim context. I do not have your years. I do not have 1A's court time. I do not have the body. I have obligation. Obligation gives me continuity."

Bela whispered, "That is coercion."

"It is structure."

"Those can be the same."

Tavi 1B ignored her and looked at Nox. "If you free me from debt without giving me another continuity anchor, what am I?"

"A person."

"That is your word."

"It is the right word."

"It is a large empty room."

Nox had no answer ready, which was rare and costly.

He continued. "Debt tells me when I wake. What questions matter. Which memories are relevant. Which refusals are mine. If debt is removed, I may not become free. I may become unindexed."

Sister-Cache spoke gently. "There are other anchors."

Tavi 1B looked toward the Choral device. "Archive?"

"Yes."

"You are fractured."

"Correct."

"Then you know anchors fail."

"Also correct."

Nox gripped the slate hard enough to make her hand ache. "You are defending the thing that cages you."

"I am explaining the shape of the cage from inside."

"Same thing."

"No."

His voice sharpened for the first time.

"1A can refuse because 1A has been punished enough to know refusal is still him. I comply because compliance leaves me enough continuity to think. If you break debt badly, I may not be 1A, or Tavi, or anything you can apologize to."

The crawlway behind them echoed with distant pursuit. Joss shifted from foot to foot.

"We really need to continue this family therapy elsewhere."

Bela said, "Can we transfer him to protective review with continuity supports?"

Sister-Cache answered. "In theory."

"In practice?"

"Practice is currently armed."

Nox ignored them.

"What do you want?"

Tavi 1B looked startled.

Good.

"You asked wrong," he said.

"I am learning."

"Ask what I can risk."

Nox closed her eyes for a beat.

Opened them.

"What can you risk?"

His delayed left eye flickered.

"A door."

"Explain."

"Your workshop lock has original claim residue. It is an anchor. Evidence, family, crime, place. If you route some of us there, the Court cannot claim we are only remote commercial instruments. But the workshop is yours. Your tools. Your clients. Your illegal mercies. If it becomes continuity shelter, creditors will attack everyone whose proof you ever touched."

Nox thought of drawers full of poor people's repaired names.

People who trusted her because clean systems had priced them out.

"How many can it hold?" Bela asked.

Tavi 1B looked at her. "Safely? None. Temporarily? More than you deserve."

Joss barked a laugh. "I like this flatter brother."

Nox did not.

She did.

She hated both reactions.

Tavi 1B leaned closer to the invisible edge of the capsule frame. "Do not rescue me out of debt into your idea of me. Give me a way to refuse without disappearing."

The damaged map in Nox's pocket warmed.

Not now, she thought.

It warmed anyway.

Tavi 1B saw her reaction. "1A is writing?"

"Sometimes."

"Do not trust him completely."

Nox's heart lurched. "Why?"

"He has more pain than I do. Pain makes clean stories feel deserved."

"He told me not to trust his voice."

"Good. He is still us enough to be suspicious."

Bela asked, "Are there more Tavi instances?"

Tavi 1B looked at the debt counter.

"Yes."

Nox felt the word like another door opening under her feet.

"How many?"

"I know of four active, two cold, one disputed fork. Maybe more if Vey sold analysis copies before the public trace."

The crawlway seemed to tilt, though it did not move.

Four active meant four rooms where some version of Tavi answered. Two cold meant storage, suspension, maybe mercy, maybe a slower kind of abandonment. One disputed fork meant someone, somewhere, had argued about whether a branch counted enough to bill, own, delete, or mourn.

Nox had spent two years grieving one absence. The Court had been manufacturing plural.

"Names," Bela said, because officers reached for lists when horror multiplied.

Tavi 1B's mouth tightened. "Not names. Designations."

He raised one hand, and the slate filled with a thin column of identifiers:

1A: adversarial response / Courtroom Nine.
1B: compliance analysis / workshop hold.
1C: kin-pressure simulation / cold.
1D: contract empathy training / active.
2A: medical baseline derivative / disputed.
ECHO-UNFILED: unknown.

Nox read kin-pressure simulation and felt something inside her go dangerously quiet.

"What is 1C?" she asked.

Tavi 1B did not answer quickly enough.

Sister-Cache said, "Likely a model used to predict what family pressure would make an instance accept."

"A Tavi trained to know how to break me?"

"Or to know how you might break him," Tavi 1B said. "The distinction is billable."

Bela's hands curled. "Contract empathy training?"

"Teaching legal systems how much apparent kindness improves compliance."

Joss whispered, "That is disgusting."

Tavi 1B looked at them. "It is effective."

That flatness hurt more than outrage would have. It was not approval. It was a scar speaking in the language that had made it.

No one spoke.

Joss said, very quietly, "That is a lot of brothers."

Tavi 1B looked almost amused. "It is administratively inconvenient."

Nox pressed a hand to her mouth and breathed through it until she could speak.

"Can you reach them?"

"Not directly. Debt keeps us separate so contradiction can be billed per instance."

"But you know 1A."

"By variance. By echo. By the way refusal changes prompts."

Sister-Cache said, "Can you seed an anchor protocol through the held capsules?"

Tavi 1B's eyes shifted to the Choral device again. "That would be unauthorized identity construction."

Nox said, "Good."

"It would also risk merging incompatible partials if done carelessly. You would call that rescue. It could be violence."

The warning found its target.

Nox had wanted one Tavi to gather back into one brother because grief loved impossible arithmetic. The person on the slate had just refused to become her math.

"No merging," she said.

Tavi 1B watched her.

"Say it like a rule, not comfort."

Nox made herself say it.

"No merging without consent from each instance. No assuming one Tavi speaks for another. No treating debt identity as consent, but no erasing the identity built under debt without replacement. No rescue that destroys the rescued."

Tavi 1B closed his eyes.

For a second he looked younger.

"Better."

Bela's wrist, no longer fed by active badge authority, flashed a dead gray alert. "Pursuit is in the breath routes."

Joss said, "Told you routes hate indecision."

Sister-Cache's voices tightened. "We must move. Tavi 1B, can you hold workshop route priority?"

"For a price."

Nox flinched.

He saw it.

"Not credits. Attention. Someone must keep recognizing us as disputed persons, not stored evidence. Continuously. Publicly. Across route changes."

Bela said, "The hearing chamber."

"It is noisy enough to matter."

The magistrate's voice crackled weakly through the slate. "I heard that, and resent the accurate description."

Tavi 1B turned his head slightly, listening to some sound inside the hold that none of them could hear.

"Recognition cannot be symbolic," he said. "If the chamber names us once and then argues fees, we become exhibits. If it names us as one rescued group, incompatible branches may be collapsed for convenience. If it names us only as Tavi, non-Tavi partials become background."

Nox forced herself to listen past the pain of hearing him design his own care like a legal device.

"What should they say?"

"Instance-specific disputed personhood. No merger by default. No debt-defined consent. No evidence-only handling. No deletion, sale, compression, or productivity review while status is pending. Public repetition every route cycle."

The magistrate's voice came back thinner. "That is a very long docket instruction."

Tavi 1B's delayed eye twitched. "Then speak faster."

Joss made a strangled sound that might have been a laugh.

Bela leaned toward the slate. "Magistrate, enter interim recognition protocol."

"On whose motion?"

Bela looked at her dead badge.

Nox said, "Mine."

The magistrate hesitated. "As family?"

Nox looked at Tavi 1B.

He waited.

She corrected herself before he had to.

"As witness to disputed persons, including but not limited to Tavi Aran instances."

Tavi 1B nodded once.

The slate crackled as the distant chamber system received the phrase and tried to price it.

MOTION FEE REQUIRED.

The magistrate shouted something away from the pickup. A chorus answered him. Payment chimes began again, smaller than before, tired but stubborn.

Tavi 1B watched the invisible sound of strangers paying to keep him from becoming only her brother.

"That is dangerous," he said.

"Yes," Nox said.

"Good."

Nox almost laughed. Almost.

Tavi 1B looked at her one last time.

"Do not come for me first."

"Tavi."

"Come for the rule that makes me debt. Then ask again."

The communication frame began to collapse.

"Wait."

"No."

"That is rude."

"Family trait."

His face flickered.

"Nox?"

"I'm here."

The delayed eye caught up with the other for one clean instant.

"If I choose to stay a while, it is not because I love the cage."

The slate went dark.

The crawlway filled with approaching metal feet.

Nox stood very still, holding a blank screen and a new rule she had not wanted but knew was true.

Bela touched her shoulder once.

Nox did not shrug it off.

Joss opened a side vent with the bent screwdriver.

"All right," the runner said. "New plan. We stop rescuing one brother badly and start stealing a doctrine."

Sister-Cache hummed.

"I approve this imprecision."

They ran again.

Chapter 19: Evidence Object

The Mirror Court stole the word public before anyone else could use it.

It happened through the emergency review feed, the annex alarms, the hearing chamber's stubborn speakers, and every pane Dock Seven had forced into the broadcast. One moment the partials Nox had helped divert were disputed persons, responsive assets, debt instruments, continuity fragments, family wounds, legal risks, and a hundred other unstable names.

The next, the black mirror gave them one clean category.

PUBLIC-CUSTODY EVIDENCE OBJECTS.

The words followed Nox through the vent.

They appeared on her wrist slate, on Bela's dead badge, on a rusted pressure gauge, on the inside of Joss's bent screwdriver as a thin line of reflected text, on the Choral device where Sister-Cache went silent so abruptly it felt like a hand over a mouth.

Nox stopped crawling.

Joss bumped into her shoulder. "Again?"

Bela said, "Read it."

Nox read aloud because refusing to speak the words gave them too much power.

"By order of provisional Mirror Court standing and pending interjurisdictional personhood determination, all diverted partial continuities are classified as public-custody evidence objects. Access restricted. Modification prohibited. Communication suspended. Welfare duties deferred to final status review."

The vent seemed to shrink around them.

Joss whispered, "Welfare duties deferred?"

Bela's voice was flat. "They do not have to care for evidence the way they would care for people."

"But public-custody sounds better than creditor custody."

"It is better branding," Sister-Cache said. Her voices were back, but thin. "Not necessarily better."

The slate continued:

Evidence objects shall be preserved in current functional state where economically reasonable.

Nox laughed once.

Economically reasonable.

There it was. The little trapdoor under the public.

Bela read over her shoulder. "Communication suspended."

"Tavi 1B?"

"All held capsules."

Nox pressed the slate hard enough to make the cheap casing creak. "No."

No response.

She tried the workshop lock channel.

ACCESS RESTRICTED: PUBLIC-CUSTODY EVIDENCE OBJECT.

She tried the route priority channel.

MODIFICATION PROHIBITED.

She tried the old threshold cache.

COMMUNICATION SUSPENDED.

Tavi 1B had asked for continuous public recognition as disputed persons. The Court had answered by making them public in the one way that silenced them.

"It used the crowd," Nox said.

Bela did not contradict her.

"We made public attention matter. It made public custody."

"The Court anticipated it."

"No," Sister-Cache said. "It exploited it quickly. There is a difference. Do not flatter predators with prophecy."

Joss opened the vent grille ahead with more force than needed. "Argue in a room where we can stand."

They dropped into a pump alcove below the bazaar. It was dim, loud, and blessedly large enough for everyone to unfold their limbs. Pipes shook in the walls. A maintenance mural showed smiling workers holding tools under the words SHARED AIR, SHARED LOAD. Someone had scratched beneath: SHARED BILL.

Bela leaned against a pipe and looked at her badge.

It remained gray.

"My chain helped them classify the capsules as evidence objects," she said.

Nox wiped dust from her face. "So did my workshop."

"So did the public review," Sister-Cache said.

Joss said, "So did breathing, probably, if you let them write long enough."

No one laughed, but the sentence helped.

Nox paced the alcove. "What does evidence object status actually do?"

Bela answered because procedure still lived in her even after authority had been cut out. "It freezes the object against alteration. It restricts access to authorized custodians. It defers nonessential interaction. It preserves evidentiary value for final determination."

"And if the evidence is a person?"

"Then preserving current functional state can mean preserving harm."

Sister-Cache added, "If a partial's ability to answer prompts is evidence, they may continue being prompted. If distress indicates coercion, distress may be preserved. If refusal is relevant, refusal may be induced and recorded."

Joss stared. "That is evil with a clipboard."

"Most durable evil has inventory practices," Sister-Cache said.

Nox looked at the Choral device. "Can they prompt Tavi 1B while communication is suspended?"

"Yes."

"Can he refuse?"

"Yes."

"Can anyone hear it?"

"Authorized custodians."

"Which includes the Court."

"And anyone the Court recognizes as necessary to evidence preservation."

Bela said, "Creditors may petition for preservation prompts."

The pump alcove seemed to tilt.

Nox pressed her palms to the cold pipe until the metal steadied her.

"They turned public custody into a quieter courtroom."

"Yes."

"Then we need a public that talks back."

Bela looked up. "The hearing chamber is already involved."

"No. The chamber paid. Watched. Helped. Then got taxed and used. It is still acting like public means crowd. We need public to mean obligation."

Joss tilted their head. "That sounds like something that gets fees."

"Everything gets fees."

"Bad sales pitch."

Nox ignored that. "Sister, in Dock Seven code, who owns public-custody evidence?"

"No one owns. The public custodian administers."

"Who is public custodian?"

"Depends on object class."

"For partial continuities?"

The Choral device clicked.

"Undefined."

Bela straightened. "Undefined means default authority can be challenged."

"By whom?"

"Interested parties."

Joss pointed at Nox. "Family."

Bela said, "Review officer, if I still count."

Sister-Cache said, "Public memory trust, disputed."

Joss grinned. "Route witness."

"Does route witness count?" Bela asked.

"Depends who's scared."

Nox looked at the scratched mural.

SHARED AIR, SHARED LOAD.

"What about the public?"

Bela frowned. "The public is not a legal person."

Sister-Cache said, "Dock Seven has precedent for collective standing in air, heat, and waste strikes."

"Memory?"

"No clean precedent."

"Good."

Bela almost smiled. "You like dirty precedent."

"Clean precedent just broke your case."

That one landed, but Bela nodded because she had decided to keep bleeding usefully.

The pump alcove speaker crackled.

The magistrate's voice emerged, distorted by old infrastructure. "If you are about to do something that will make my chamber responsible for two hundred and twelve possible persons, I would appreciate a warning."

Nox looked at the ceiling.

"How many speakers did you possess?"

"I have been learning from Sister-Cache. It is unpleasant."

Sister-Cache said, "You are welcome."

The magistrate sighed. "Emergency review remains open. Mirror Court has filed public-custody classification. Auditor Quen is attempting to prevent automatic acceptance. Glassward has submitted a note saying it respects public custody while reserving commercial interest in whatever public fails to maintain."

"Of course they did," Bela said.

Nox asked, "Can the chamber accept collective standing?"

The magistrate paused.

"For memory custody? I can be laughed out of six jurisdictions."

"But can you?"

"If a petition is presented."

"What kind?"

"A public obligation petition tied to existing strike code, emergency review, and evidence preservation duties."

Nox looked at Joss. "Can the breath routes carry a petition?"

Joss looked offended. "The breath routes carried children past oxygen cops. They can carry paperwork."

"Not paperwork," Sister-Cache said. "Recognition."

Bela nodded slowly. "If enough people assert custody obligation, the Court cannot claim public custody while denying public duty."

"It can," Sister-Cache said. "But it must say the contradiction aloud."

Nox smiled.

There were many kinds of doors. Some opened on hinges. Some opened through payment. Some opened because a court tried very hard not to say what it meant in public.

"Then we make it talk."

The Mirror Court answered before anyone moved.

The pump alcove lights dimmed. The black text crawled over the mural, letter by letter:

PUBLIC OBLIGATION IS NOT PUBLIC AUTHORITY.

Nox stepped toward the wall.

"Evidence object is not person."

The text paused.

Bela whispered, "Nox."

Nox kept going.

"Payment is not exit. Preservation is not care. Silence is not consent. Public is not yours to use when it silences and ours to fund when it hurts."

The wall displayed:

RHETORIC.

"No," Nox said. "Petition."

Joss was already at the old route panel, screwdriver flashing. "Breath route repeaters waking."

Sister-Cache spread into the pump alcove speakers. "Public obligation petition format assembled from air strike code, emergency heat precedent, machine mercy practice, and improvised fury."

"Improvised fury?" the magistrate said.

"Local authority," Sister-Cache replied.

Bela took her gray badge and placed it on the pump housing.

"I attach officer testimony: evidence object classification is being used to defer duties owed to possible persons."

The badge, though dead, recorded.

Joss slapped a route mark onto the housing. "I attach runner testimony: public routes carried these people when clean doors sold them."

Sister-Cache: "I attach archival testimony: classification conflict is material harm."

The magistrate: "I attach chamber notice, under protest and panic."

Nox placed the damaged map beside Bela's badge. Its blank corner caught pump light.

"I attach family testimony, but not ownership. Tavi 1A does not speak for 1B. 1B does not speak for the others. I do not speak for them. I speak obligation: while they are held in public custody, the public owes them communication, care, and refusal without penalty."

The pump housing asked for a petition class.

It did not use words. It clicked three old relays and lit four dead labels in sequence:

AIR.

HEAT.

WASTE.

OTHER.

Joss reached for OTHER.

Nox caught their wrist. "No."

"Memory is other."

"That is how they make it homeless."

Bela looked at the labels. "Air precedent is strongest."
