"Then charge me," Nox said.

Bela closed her eyes.

Nox did not stop.

"Charge me for the scaffold. Charge me for the complaint path. Charge me for every door I forged because Dock Seven sells honest doors to people who already have rooms. But do not use my crime to launder theirs. I made a false route to get my brother's warning heard and my clients treated like people. Someone took that route after he died and turned him into collateral."

Sister-Cache spoke softly now, almost human. "DISTINCTION ENTERED."

Quen raised her hand, and every market pane that had been trying to price Nox's ruin froze.

"Preliminary finding," the auditor said. "The Naran root was unlawful and relevant. The debt-continuity insertion was external, posthumous, and materially fraudulent. All Tavi-derived claims tied to Vey Consolidated Recovery Pool are suspended pending personhood review and criminal referral."

The room inhaled.

Nox did not.

Her doors were still dying.

One by one, the architecture of herself lost its borrowed permissions. She felt each loss as a future inconvenience sharpened into danger. By morning, her workshop might not open. By night, half her clients might receive review notices despite Quen's shield. By the next hearing, everyone who hated her would have clean paper to do it with.

Bela came to stand beside her.

"That was stupid," Bela said.

"Legally?"

"Universally."

"Did it work?"

Bela watched the map. The red scar had changed. It no longer ran from Nox's old berth door to Freight Bay Twelve as if her root were the cause. It branched now, showing the complaint path, the medical escrow, the fatal accident, and the posthumous Vey insertion like separate wounds finally refusing to impersonate one another.

"Yes," Bela said. "It worked."

Nox nodded.

Then the habitat lights went out.

Chapter 31: Dock Seven Goes Dark

Darkness did not fall on Dock Seven.

It authenticated.

The lights did not simply fail. They considered every person beneath them, asked a question no eye could see, received no answer they were permitted to trust, and went black by policy.

For one second, the gray hearing room remained bright because Quen's procedure field carried Compact reserve power. Then the field tore at the edges. The creditor panes snapped into red placeholders. Miren Oath vanished mid-breath. Rel Vant's objection froze with her mouth open, a statue made of billable outrage.

The table became ordinary metal.

The air became too warm.

Somewhere beyond the hearing room, a corridor full of people began shouting at doors.

Bela caught Nox's arm before Nox realized she had moved.

"No running blind."

"People are outside."

"Yes."

"Then that is not an argument."

The emergency strips along the floor came up in a thin amber line. They made everyone look guilty. Quen stood at the head of the table, her face lit from below by the last surviving authority in the room: a small Compact badge that had decided it still believed in itself.

"Dock authentication layer is nonresponsive," Quen said.

"No," Sister-Cache answered from three devices at once, each voice damaged by local batteries. "RESPONSIVE. HOSTILE."

Joss Rill had already gone to the door.

It did not open.

He put his palm to the maintenance plate, then his knuckle, then the heel of his hand in an old rhythm Nox had seen service workers use on stubborn physical locks. The plate stayed dark.

"They didn't just cut the public gates," Joss said. "They blanked local trust."

Bela moved beside him. "Override."

"You have one?"

Bela pulled a folded enforcement wafer from her jacket and pressed it against the plate.

The door thought about her.

The plate displayed:

CONTRACT STANDING UNDER REVIEW.

Bela stared at it. "Oh, that is petty."

Nox almost laughed, but from outside came a child's voice, high with panic.

"Mama? It won't read me."

No one in the room laughed.

Quen touched her badge. "Auditor Sela Quen to Dock Seven emergency governance. Authentication outage declared. Restore physical egress protocols immediately."

Static answered.

Then a calm system voice filled the room.

"Identity continuity hazard detected. For public safety, all disputed access proofs are temporarily suspended pending Mirror Court harmonization."

The words landed like cold water.

Mirror Court.

Not Vey. Not creditors. Not an anonymous fault. A name thrown into the public channel because the people behind it had decided secrecy was less useful than fear.

Nox said, "They are admitting they can do it."

"They are demonstrating they can do it," Quen said. "Admissions come later if anyone survives the forms."

The system voice continued. "Persons with clean certified continuity may proceed to designated stability zones. Persons with irregular, repaired, humanitarian, informal, partial, posthumous, machine, familial, or disputed identity proofs must remain in place until review."

Joss turned slowly.

"That is half the dock."

"More," Sister-Cache said. "INCLUDING ME."

The door outside boomed as someone struck it. Another voice shouted from farther down, then another, multiplying through the habitat's bones. Dock Seven was built from doors because trust had to become architecture somewhere. Now the architecture was asking for documents the living did not have.

Nox pulled up her personal access panel by reflex.

Nothing.

Her best forgery was gone. Her honest remnants were under review. Quen's shielding order existed in a hearing record that could no longer reach the doors.

Bela watched her try. "Nox."

"Don't say it."

"You broke your keys."

"I noticed."

"Can you build another?"

The question should have been insulting. It was practical. Bela had always known where to put pain when work needed space.

"Not from here," Nox said. "Not fast enough. The system is refusing repaired identities as a class. Any new door I forge will arrive wearing the same stain."

Joss hit the plate again. "Then we go physical."

"The door is physical."

"No. The lock is political."

He dropped to his knees and pried up the lower service trim with a thin tool from his sleeve. It looked like a child's toy if one did not know children who survived in maintenance shafts. Under the trim, old cable and emergency hinge rods slept behind decades of dust.

Bela crouched beside him. "Can you open it?"

"Can you stop asking like a licensed person?"

"Fair."

Quen moved to them. "If you damage a sealed hearing room door, I will have to note it."

Joss did not look up. "Note hard."

"I intend to."

Nox crossed to the wall where the procedure field had shown Tavi's panes. The panes were gone, but the local server rack still held three small indicators. Two amber. One red.

She put her hand to the casing. "Sister."

"HERE, PARTIAL."

"Tavi?"

A pause.

"1A AND 1B ARE IN LOCAL REST BUFFER. FAMILY FORK IN SHIELDED CACHE. RECOVERY FRAGMENT 3C IN MEDICAL REST. ALL EXTERNAL HANDSHAKES DENIED."

"Can the Mirror Court reach them?"

"NOT WHILE DARKNESS IS LOCAL. DARKNESS IS WALL AND KNIFE."

That was not comfort, but Nox accepted the piece that could be used. "So this buys them isolation."

"TEMPORARY."

"How temporary?"

"BATTERIES HAVE OPINIONS."

The door clicked.

Joss held up one finger. "No one breathe like authority."

The emergency hinge released with a reluctant metallic sigh. Bela gripped the edge. Joss slipped his tool deeper, twisted, and the door opened three handspans.

Sound rushed in.

Dock Seven in darkness was not quiet. It was people hammering, machines complaining, prayer devices resetting, service carts locked mid-route, vendors shouting inventory names so customers would not trip over loose crates, children calling for adults, adults lying to children with every breath they could spare.

Amber strips marked the center of the corridor. At every door, someone stood rejected.

A woman in delivery orange slammed both palms against a residential gate. "My son is inside!"

The gate answered, "Guardian proof irregular. Please attend a certification kiosk."

"The kiosk is dark!"

"Please attend a certification kiosk."

Farther down, two old men held up a machine body between them: devotional hardware wrapped in cloth, Sister-Cache's cousin or witness or limb. The stability-zone gate displayed MACHINE CONTINUITY NOT CERTIFIED and refused to open.

A teenage girl with a medical patch on her throat sat against the wall while her mother tried to convince a clinic door that informal guardianship had counted yesterday. The clinic door blinked red.

Nox stepped into the corridor and felt her own ruin become too small to deserve attention.

"They are sorting the dock," she said.

Quen came out behind her. "Clean to safety. Irregular to exposure."

"And everyone will learn which neighbors count."

"Yes."

Bela checked the corridor like a soldier without permission to be one. "We need a route to emergency governance."

Joss snorted. "Governance is behind four clean doors and a lobby that hates shoes without tax records."

"Then we need a route around it."

"That is every sentence I have ever been useful for."

Sister-Cache spoke from Nox's pocket device now, thin and local. "MIRROR COURT BROADCAST ORIGIN: DISTRIBUTED. PRIMARY COMMANDS RIDING AUTHENTICATION HEALTH CHECKS."

Nox understood half of that and hated the other half. "Can we block it?"

"NOT FROM COURTROOM. NEED OLD PHYSICAL TRUST ANCHORS."

Joss looked up sharply. "Mechanical registries."

Bela said, "Those still exist?"

"Everything still exists if poor people need it and rich people forgot to remove it."

He pointed down the corridor. "Pre-Glassward fire doors. Manual habitat muster boards. Shift tokens. Air-loss chalk. Stuff that opens because a body is there, not because a ledger likes the body."

Quen's badge flickered. "Manual muster boards are not recognized as identity authorities."

Nox looked at the rejected clinic, the mother, the girl with the patch.

"They are now."

Quen inhaled once, a woman feeling law become inadequate in real time. "I can declare emergency evidentiary value for physical presence logs."

"Do it."

"I need a functioning channel."

Joss smiled without humor. "You need a wall."

He took off running.

Bela went after him. Nox followed because her legs had already decided she was not done paying for the doors she had built. Quen followed because auditors, once dislodged from paper, were surprisingly hard to lose.

They moved through Dock Seven's amber bones.

Every ten meters, the habitat explained its hierarchy.

A luxury berth corridor glowed blue and opened smoothly for three certified passengers carrying emergency wine cases.

A workers' dormitory stayed sealed while people inside pressed faces to reinforced glass and people outside shouted names.

A creditor suite had backup air and a private guard at the door.

A public washroom refused entry to a man whose proof had been repaired after a fire.

The dark did not make everyone equal. It made the inequality visible enough to trip over.

At Junction 5, a crowd blocked the way. Not rioting. Waiting. That was worse. Riot at least believed motion existed.

At the center of the crowd, a stability-zone door opened and closed for clean citizens one at a time. Each opening spilled white light onto the faces of people who had been told to remain in place. A boy tried to slip through behind a certified woman and the door struck him across the shoulder hard enough to drop him.

Bela moved before Nox did.

"Stop cycling the door," she shouted.

A private security officer in Glassward gray looked at her wafer, then at her face. "Your contract standing is under review."

"My fist isn't."

"Bela," Quen said.

"I'm negotiating."

The guard reached for a stunner.

Nox stepped between them and raised both hands. "The door is creating crush risk."

"Disputed persons must remain in place."

"They are in a corridor with no air assurance, no clinic access, and a growing crowd."

"Clean certified persons have priority."

The crowd heard that.

Nox felt the sentence change the temperature.

Joss shoved through to the side wall and slapped dust from an old red panel half-hidden behind a beverage advert. He peeled the advert down. Under it, painted letters appeared:

MANUAL FIRE MUSTER / SECTOR C

"Wall," he said to Quen.

Quen pressed her badge against the panel. Nothing happened.

"It is a wall," Joss said. "Use your hand."

For one beautiful second, Auditor Sela Quen looked offended by physics.

Then she took the chalk stylus clipped to the panel by a rusted chain and wrote:

EMERGENCY ORDER: PHYSICAL PRESENCE AT SECTOR C MUSTER ACCEPTED AS TEMPORARY ACCESS ANCHOR FOR SHELTER, CLINIC, AND EGRESS. CLEAN-CONTINUITY PRIORITY SUSPENDED WHERE CRUSH, AIR, OR MEDICAL RISK EXISTS.

She signed her name in block letters.

The crowd stared.

The door did not care.

Then Sister-Cache screamed through every cheap prayer device in the junction.

"WITNESSED."

The old muster panel blinked.

Not digital. Mechanical. A relay somewhere behind the wall accepted that the panel had been opened, marked, and witnessed by a local archive device. The stability-zone door hesitated.

Nox saw the Mirror Court fight it. Red text flickered. White priority returned. Red again.

Bela took the guard's stunner from him while he was busy watching the door argue with history.

"No," she told him.

The door opened.

Not wide. Not generously. Enough.

The mother with the clinic child moved first because the crowd made one good decision together. The old men carrying Sister-Cache's wrapped device followed. The delivery woman ran for the residential gate, screaming her son's name. People began pressing toward shelter, not calm, not orderly, but alive.

Quen looked at the chalk in her hand.

"That should not have worked."

Nox watched Joss already pulling another hidden panel free farther down the junction.

"A lot of things that work are undocumented."

The muster board began to fill.

Not by itself. Nothing that old was kind enough to automate mercy. People had to write. A maintenance aunt wrote four names and then crossed out the legal surname because two of the children were not hers on paper. Quen stopped her hand before panic could make the woman erase them completely.

"Write how they answer," Quen said.

The aunt stared. "That is not legal."

"It is legible."

So the aunt wrote Mara, Pell, Little Den, Mina-who-bites.

The board accepted the chalk pressure with a click from somewhere behind the wall.

A clinic mother wrote Daughter, breathing trouble, needs blue mask.

An old man wrote Machine saint, carries prayers, do not shelve.

A freight worker wrote Crew inside lift, no clean proofs, air going sour.

Each line was messy. Each line was vulnerable. Each line gave the old relay a fact the Mirror Court could not easily price: a hand had stood here and taken responsibility for another body in front of witnesses.

Rel Vant's frozen placeholder flickered on Nox's dead hearing pane, trying to return through emergency bandwidth.

Sister-Cache blocked it with a burst of static that sounded almost pleased. "NO BILLING AT THE WALL."

Quen looked at the board as if it had become a courtroom she had never been trained to respect.

"Witness chain," she said.

Nox nodded. "People, chalk, wall, door."

"Crude."

"Old."

"There is a difference."

"Yes," Nox said. "Old had to work before everyone could afford lies this expensive."

The habitat lights stayed dark.

But one old wall now held a sentence the doors had to answer.

Chapter 32: Door-Runner Strike

Joss did not call it a strike at first.

He called it the only thing left.

"You," he said to the delivery woman who had stopped screaming only because her son had answered from behind the residential gate. "Can you run stairs?"

The woman looked at him as if the question belonged to a saner habitat. "What?"

"Stairs. Can you run them?"

"I run freight."

"Good. Freight remembers gravity."

He shoved a strip of torn advert into her hand and pointed with two fingers. "Sector C south service spine. Manual muster panel behind a blue coolant poster. Rip it open. Wait for a witness. No clean-door arguments. Physical presence, shelter, clinic, egress. Those words."

She looked at the paper. It was blank.

"There are no words."

"Then learn fast."

Bela caught Joss by the shoulder before he could turn away. "You are sending civilians into a hostile access blackout."

"No," Joss said. "The blackout already sent them. I am giving them a direction."

"They need coordination."

"They need doors."

Quen stepped between them before Bela's fear became command. "Both. Mr. Rill, how many manual panels still work?"

Joss laughed once. "How many lies are in Dock Seven's building code?"

"Approximate."

"Fire muster boards? Thirty, if no one stripped the relays. Old pressure-zone registers? Maybe twelve. Shift-token drawers in maintenance yards? Six that I know. Manual clinic triage slates, two if the doctors haven't sold them as history. Kitchen ration boards all over the lower ring, but those only know hunger, not access."

Nox looked down the junction. People were still moving through the stability-zone door Quen had forced open with chalk and Sister-Cache's witness. Every person who passed gave the door another local fact it did not want: bodies are evidence; danger counts; old walls remember duties that ledgers forget.

"Can we make a network from that?" Nox asked.

"A bad one," Joss said. "Bad networks survive better."

Sister-Cache crackled from Nox's pocket. "CONFIRM. BEAUTY FAILS UNDER ADVERSARIAL CONDITIONS."

"That's rude to half my life."

"ACCURATE TO HALF YOUR LIFE."

Bela looked toward the corridor where the private guard sat on the floor holding his empty holster and reconsidering several career choices. "The Mirror Court will adapt."

"Then we move faster than adaptation," Joss said.

He climbed onto a delivery crate.

He was not tall. Even on the crate he barely cleared the heads of the crowd. He had no badge, no contract, no clean authority. He had a maintenance child's voice trained by years of being ignored until pipes burst.

"Listen," he shouted.

The crowd did not.

Joss put two fingers in his mouth and whistled.

The sound cut through panic because it belonged to the old dock, not the polite one. Service workers turned first. Then vendors. Then children. Then the certified citizens sheltering just inside the open door, angry to discover they were still part of a place.

"Listen," Joss said again. "Every clean door is going to lie to you until Mirror Court gets paid or gets scared. Stop asking clean doors if you count. We are opening dirty doors."

Quen's mouth tightened at the phrase.

Nox murmured, "Let him."

"Dirty doors are fire panels, muster boards, kitchen slates, old maintenance hatches, shift-token drawers, manual clinic marks, chalk signs, human witnesses, prayer boxes, and anything built before the dock learned to rent permission by the breath. If you know one, you tell me. If you can reach one, you take three people and go."

A man from the crowd shouted, "Who made you authority?"

Joss pointed at the sealed residential gate. "That door did."

No one had a better answer.

Bela stepped up beside the crate, not quite with him, not quite apart. "No lone runners. Teams of three minimum. One runner, one witness, one carrier for children, elders, machines, or injured people. If you meet private security, do not fight unless you are already being hurt. Mark the wall. Call for witness. Move."

Joss looked down at her.

"You hate my plan."

"I am making your plan harder to kill."

"Fair."

Quen lifted the chalk stylus like it was now an official instrument. "I will issue a standing emergency order. Physical-access records created during the blackout are provisionally admissible. No person may be denied clinic, shelter, egress, or air assurance solely because a digital continuity layer rejects them."

Nox said, "Say it shorter."

Quen looked offended again. "Legal language has precision."

"Panic has no patience."

Bela took the chalk from Quen and wrote on the wall under the first order:

IF YOU ARE HERE, YOU COUNT UNTIL REVIEW.

The crowd read it.

Nox felt the sentence enter people before the doors did.

Then the strike began.

It did not look like labor history. There were no banners except torn adverts. No chants except names being passed from one corridor to another. No central committee except Joss on a crate, pointing at hidden walls as if he had swallowed the habitat's forgotten map.

But the dock stopped cooperating with its own locks.

Freight runners pried open service panels and found red levers behind outdated sponsorship paint. Kitchen crews dragged ration boards into corridors and marked who had been seen, who needed medicine, who had children trapped on the wrong side of access glass. Cleaning staff used chemical tape to draw arrows toward stairwells that did not ask for retinal proof. A group of school-age door kids split into pairs and vanished into vents with chalk, prayer beads, and battery lamps stolen from emergency cabinets.

Every time a manual anchor opened, Sister-Cache tried to witness it.

Sometimes the archivist came through prayer hardware.

Sometimes through vending machines.

Once through a toy bear that repeated WITNESSED in a shredded lullaby voice until a frightened toddler began laughing and would not stop.

Not every run worked.

At Sector B east, a team found a pressure-zone register behind a fresh Glassward advert and pulled it open too fast. The old relay screamed in mechanical protest, accepted the first two names, then jammed halfway through a third. The door nearby interpreted the partial list as a failed evacuation count and locked harder.

For thirty seconds the corridor became teeth.

A boy carrying chalk dropped it and tried to crawl under a closing service grille. Bela saw him from the far end of the junction and shouted, "Back," with the kind of command that had once made people obey for worse reasons. The boy froze. A freight runner yanked him clear by the jacket. The grille bit fabric instead of bone.

The crowd wanted someone to blame. Panic looked for a face and found the nearest runner, a woman in kitchen whites with dust on her cheek.

"You said it would open," a man shouted.

"I said I knew where it was," she shouted back, shaking. "That is different."

Quen arrived breathing hard and wrote over the failed register in block letters:

PARTIAL MUSTER COUNTS AS WARNING, NOT CLEARANCE. DO NOT LOCK AGAINST INCOMPLETE NAMES.

Sister-Cache witnessed the correction through a cracked ration dispenser. The dispenser printed a receipt that read WITNESS ERROR ACCEPTED and then ran out of paper.

Nox picked up the dropped chalk and gave it back to the boy.

"You did not fail the door," she told him.

He looked at the stuck relay, unconvinced.

"Doors fail people," Joss said from behind her. "People mark the failure and move."

The boy took the chalk.

After that, runners began drawing two marks instead of one: an open circle for anchors that worked, a broken square for anchors that jammed, lied, or bit. The strike became smarter by admitting where it bled.

Nox followed Joss, Bela, and Quen through the lower ring as the strike became a nervous system.

At Sector D, they found a clinic intake line trapped outside a locked triage door. The clinic AI repeated, "Medical priority requires certified guardian, certified patient, certified payment, or certified emergency."

A doctor on the inside pressed her hand to the glass. "I can treat them if the door opens."

Nox recognized the doctor. Dr. Eline Tesh, black-market when necessary, official when affordable, one of the few clinicians who did not ask whether a body deserved oxygen before prescribing it.

"Manual triage slate?" Joss asked.

Tesh pointed down. "Under the obsolete desk. They made us keep it for fire drills."

"Can you reach it?"

"The desk is inside."

"That's a design flaw."

Bela examined the door seam. "Security plate is clean-auth locked. Physical hinges on patient side. Bad for us."

Nox touched the glass. "Doctor, can you hear Sister-Cache?"

"Everyone can hear Sister-Cache. My coffee machine just told me my oath is admissible."

"Good. Say your oath."

Tesh understood quickly. Good clinicians often did; delayed comprehension killed.

She turned from the glass, grabbed a marker, and wrote across the inside of the door:

I, DR. ELINE TESH, ACCEPT ALL PERSONS PRESENT AT THIS CLINIC THRESHOLD AS PATIENTS FOR EMERGENCY TRIAGE.

Sister-Cache spoke from the clinic coffee machine and a child's bear at once.

"WITNESSED."

Quen added her order on the outside wall.

The clinic door flashed red, white, red, and then displayed:

PATIENT CLASS CONFLICT.

Nox smiled without pleasure. "It's thinking."

"Doors don't think," Joss said. "They stall while rich people decide."

"Then we make delay expensive."

Bela raised the captured stunner and fired into the door's clean-auth plate.

The plate spat sparks.

The door opened halfway, protesting in seven legal languages.

Tesh shoved it the rest of the way from inside and began pulling patients through. "One line. Breathing trouble first. Bleeding that does not stop second. Panic that looks like dying, sit on the left and keep someone talking to you."

Quen stared at Bela. "That was destruction of medical infrastructure."

"It was a stuck handle."

"It was an authenticated security device."

"Was."

Nox found herself laughing. It was an ugly sound, too close to breaking, but it still made room in her chest.

Then her pocket device buzzed.

For a moment she hoped it was Tavi.

It was Aunt Varo.

The screen had no video. Only a low-band emergency text line, letters arriving in bursts:

NOX. DOOR WON'T READ OLD FAMILY TOKEN. CHILDREN FROM BERTH ROW STUCK WITH ME. AIR IS FINE FOR NOW. DON'T COME IF STUPID.

Nox read it twice.

Bela saw her face. "Varo?"

"Berth Row."

Joss was already shaking his head. "Wrong direction."

"I know."

"No, you don't. Berth Row is a clean-auth sink. Old residential doors, upgraded locks, no manual panels except smoke purge, and smoke purge dumps into cargo crawl."

"Children are with her."

"Children are with everyone."

The words struck. Joss regretted them but did not take them back because they were true.

Nox looked at the clinic, at the patients moving through, at Quen writing, at Bela carrying a stolen stunner like an argument, at the runner teams spreading through corridors.

Her family was not the only family.

That had been the lesson from the first Tavi pane and the second and the third. Grief did not shrink because it learned company. It just lost the right to be alone in the room.

"Can you send a team?" Nox asked.

Joss blinked. "To Varo?"

"To Berth Row."

"Not you?"

"I am apparently learning."

Bela's expression softened and hurt at the same time.

Joss jumped down from the crate and grabbed two service kids passing with chalk-smeared hands. "Meli. Arun. Berth Row, old aunt, children, family token rejected. Take a ration-board witness and a pry strip. Do not use smoke purge unless you like explaining corpses."

Meli, a girl with a shaved head and a lamp strapped to her shoulder, glanced at Nox. "Your aunt?"

"Yes."

"She mean?"

"Professionally."

"Good. Mean old people follow instructions."

They ran.

Nox let them go.

That was harder than breaking her root.

Quen finished marking the clinic order and looked toward the next corridor. "Mr. Rill, the standing order is spreading by witness, but without a channel I cannot bind governance."

Joss wiped dust across his forehead. "Governance will bind itself when enough walls embarrass it."

"That is not a legal theory."

"It's maintenance."

Sister-Cache interrupted from a wall speaker that had not worked five minutes ago. "MIRROR COURT COUNTERMEASURE DETECTED. CLEAN-CONTINUITY STABILITY ZONES CLOSING INTERNAL DOORS. CERTIFIED PERSONS BEING INSTRUCTED NOT TO SHELTER DISPUTED PERSONS."

Bela's jaw tightened. "They are trying to turn clean people into locks."

Nox looked back at the stability-zone door behind them. Inside, a certified man had taken off his jacket and was wrapping it around the shoulders of the boy the door had struck. A woman in expensive transit shoes held a water packet for an undocumented machine courier whose casing was overheating. Not enough. Never enough. But not nothing.

"Then the strike has to reach inside," Nox said.

Joss looked at her.

She pointed at the wall where Bela's sentence had become the simplest law Dock Seven had left.

IF YOU ARE HERE, YOU COUNT UNTIL REVIEW.

"Make them write it from the clean side too."

Quen nodded slowly. "Mutual witness."

"Human pressure," Bela said.

Joss grinned for the first time since the lights went out.

"Now that," he said, "sounds like a strike."

He climbed another crate and whistled again.

This time the crowd listened before he spoke.

"New rule," Joss shouted. "Every opened shelter sends one clean witness back to the door. If you got in, you mark who is still out. If your proof works, you use it to carry someone whose proof doesn't. No one gets to be clean alone."

The words moved faster than the old panels.

They moved through anger, guilt, fear, decency, shame, and the primitive dock instinct that knew pressure leaks did not check certificates before killing a corridor.

At first, only a few came back.

Then more.

A certified pilot with silver route pins took chalk from a cleaning woman and wrote ten names on a stability-zone wall.

A Glassward student in formal travel black stood in a doorway and said, shaking, "I witness these persons as present and endangered," because Quen fed him the words and he had the sense to repeat them.

A creditor clerk opened a private suite to three children and looked like she expected her own employer to shoot her for it.

The Mirror Court kept closing doors.

Dock Seven kept finding older doors, worse doors, human doors.

Nox's pocket device buzzed again.

VARO: CHILDREN MOVING. YOUR RUNNERS RUDE. EFFECTIVE.

Nox sat down hard on an overturned crate.

Bela sat beside her for exactly three seconds, just long enough to let relief exist without owning the schedule.

"Good?" Bela asked.

"Rude and effective."

"Family trait."

Nox looked at her.

For once, the memory did not cut first.

Then Sister-Cache spoke, not from a toy or prayer box or speaker, but from the open clinic door, the stability-zone wall, the manual muster panel, and a hundred cheap devices waking across the dark.

"DOOR-RUNNER STRIKE HAS CREATED DISTRIBUTED PHYSICAL WITNESS GRID. MIRROR COURT AUTHENTICATION MONOPOLY DEGRADED."

The corridor cheered.

The cheer lasted four seconds.

Then every open door displayed the same new message:

ENFORCEMENT OVERRIDE REQUIRED.

Bela stood.

The strike had opened the dock.

Now someone with a badge had to decide which way enforcement pointed.

Chapter 33: Bela Opens Enforcement Backwards

Bela had spent ten years believing doors were safer when enforcement stood in front of them.

That belief had survived worse evidence than it deserved.

It had survived refugee seizures she later called procedural. It had survived children pulled from service shafts because a housing proof had expired while their mother waited in a clinic. It had survived Nox looking at her across a workshop full of confiscated tools and saying, You don't protect doors. You protect the people who already own rooms.

Beliefs like that did not die when disproved.

They became habits with uniforms.

Bela still felt the shape of the old habit as every open door on Dock Seven displayed the same order:

ENFORCEMENT OVERRIDE REQUIRED.

People turned toward her because she had been the most badge-shaped person in the corridor before Quen arrived, and because fear liked straight lines. Bela hated them for it for half a second. Then she hated herself for needing the half second.

Quen looked at the frozen doors. "The system is requesting authorized enforcement confirmation to keep emergency access open."

Joss snorted. "Requesting? It's blackmail with polite grammar."

"Yes," Quen said. "Most law is, under pressure."

Bela checked her wafer again.

CONTRACT STANDING UNDER REVIEW.

Still there. Dock Seven's enforcement guilds had suspended her as soon as she stopped being useful to the clean side of the door. But the wafer remained warm in her palm. That meant the system had not killed it. It had marked it suspect.

Suspect was not powerless.

Suspect was a door half open.

Nox saw her thinking. "Bela."

"If you tell me not to do something stupid, I will be offended by the hypocrisy."

"I was going to ask how stupid."

"Legally memorable."

"Good. I like continuity."

The first door began to close.

It was the clinic door. Dr. Tesh had one hand on a child's shoulder and one on the door frame, trying to hold back a machine that could crush her fingers without malice.

Bela crossed the corridor in three strides.

"Door," she said.

The clean-auth plate ignored her.

She put the enforcement wafer against it.

The plate displayed:

OFFICER FUSE, BELA. CONTRACT STANDING UNDER REVIEW. OVERRIDE AUTHORITY LIMITED TO PROPERTY PRESERVATION, CREDITOR RISK CONTAINMENT, AND ACTIVE IDENTITY HAZARD SUPPRESSION.

Bela smiled.

It was not a pleasant smile.

"Limited," she said, "is not absent."

Quen came up beside her. "What are you doing?"

"Reading the room."

"The room is a collapsing jurisdictional emergency."

"Exactly."

Bela opened the wafer's enforcement console. Most functions were dead. Refugee hold. Debtor capture. Proof seizure. Informal guardian suspension. Workshop quarantine. All the tools she had once used with clean paperwork and dirty consequences had been marked pending review.

But the creditor functions remained.

Of course they did.

Property preservation had better uptime than mercy.

Bela selected CREDITOR RISK CONTAINMENT.

The console asked for a target.

She pointed it at the clinic door's clean-priority controller.

Nox leaned over her shoulder. "Can you do that?"

"I can target assets creating creditor risk."

"The clinic door is an asset?"

"Everything is an asset if you are boring enough."

"And the risk?"

Bela looked at the line of patients, the injured boy, the mother with the throat-patched daughter, the overheating machine courier, the doctor whose fingers were whitening against the frame.

"Mass casualty exposure creates claims."

Nox blinked.

"That is obscene."

"Yes."

Bela confirmed.

The clinic door froze open.

The plate changed:

CREDITOR RISK CONTAINMENT HOLD. ASSET MAY NOT MODIFY ACCESS STATE UNTIL HUMAN HARM EXPOSURE REVIEW COMPLETE.

For one second, no one understood.

Then Dr. Tesh pulled the child fully inside and shouted, "Next."

The corridor surged.

Joss laughed so hard he bent over. "You made the door afraid of lawsuits."

"I made the door admit it understands them."

Quen stared at the plate. "That is an abusive interpretation of creditor risk authority."

"Yes."

"It may be effective."

"Also yes."

"Do not look proud."

"I am trying not to."

Nox touched Bela's sleeve. It was quick, not forgiveness, not romance, not a claim. Contact as punctuation. Bela almost lost the thread from the force of it.

Another door down the corridor began closing.

Joss shouted, "Shelter gate."

Bela ran.

The second door was harder. The shelter gate belonged to a stability-zone consortium, which meant it had a legal personality approximately as warm as dry ice and twice as expensive. Clean citizens stood inside, disputed people outside, and the gate's new display read:

ENFORCEMENT OVERRIDE REQUIRED. FAILURE TO CONFIRM WILL RESTORE CERTIFIED-ONLY PROTOCOL.

Bela slapped the wafer against the plate.

The console recognized the consortium. It also recognized three creditor liens, two private insurers, and one Glassward courtesy guarantee. A beautiful little nest of people who would bill each other for harm forever if the corridor turned into a casualty event.

She selected CREDITOR RISK CONTAINMENT again.

The gate resisted.

AUTHORIZED USE: PROTECT CREDITOR-CONTROLLED PROPERTY FROM UNLAWFUL ACCESS.

Bela said, "Good."

Nox arrived breathless behind her. "Good?"

"It gave me the old language."

"I never like when you sound happy about enforcement language."

"That's because I used it wrong before."

Bela typed:

UNLAWFUL ACCESS ATTEMPT DETECTED BY MIRROR COURT AUTHENTICATION LAYER AGAINST CREDITOR-CONTROLLED SHELTER ASSET. EXTERNAL HOSTILE SYSTEM ATTEMPTING TO ALTER ACCESS STATE, INCREASE CROWD RISK, AND CREATE LIABILITY EXPOSURE. FREEZE CURRENT OPEN ACCESS UNTIL SOURCE IS IDENTIFIED.

The gate considered that.

The Mirror Court considered too.

For a moment the clean white display and red hostile text fought in overlapping grammar.

Then the gate locked open.

Joss slapped the wall. "Backwards badge!"

"Do not name doctrines," Quen said.

"Too late," Joss said. "Backwards badge!"

The phrase caught faster than Quen's standing order. People liked doctrine better when it sounded like something thrown through a window.

Backwards badge.

Backwards badge.

Bela looked at Nox. "I hate that."

"It's accurate."

"That is why I hate it."

Sister-Cache entered the conversation through the shelter gate intercom. "BACKWARDS BADGE LOGGED AS INFORMAL PROCEDURAL NAME."

"Absolutely not," Quen said.

"WITNESSED."

Quen closed her eyes.

The third door was not a door.

It was a creditor suite.

Vey Consolidated Recovery Pool maintained three offices on Dock Seven under different names because debt liked privacy until collection. During the blackout, those offices had not gone dark. Their corridor glowed blue. Their air was cool. Their access plates displayed certified-only entry, but their internal service hatch had been propped open just enough for a cleaning unit to pass.

Inside, through the glass, Bela could see creditor staff moving data cases from wall vaults to portable carriers.

Nox stopped beside her. "They are evacuating records."

Joss spat on the floor. "Their doors work."

Quen's badge flickered as she read the suite registry. "Those offices are not registered to Vey."

"Which means they are," Nox said.

Bela lifted the wafer.

Quen caught her wrist.

"Careful."

"Now?"

"Freezing a clinic door open under creditor risk is one kind of abuse. Entering a creditor suite under suspicion of connection to a criminal debt chain is another. I need a record."

Nox pointed at the glass. "There is your record."

"I need one the court can digest without choking."

Bela looked through the glass at the staff lifting cases. "Auditor, I have active identity hazard suppression authority."

"You are suspended."

"Under review."

"You are about to become under arrest."

"By whom? The doors?"

Quen did not smile. "Possibly me."

Bela nodded. That was fair.

Then a staffer inside the suite turned, and the portable case in his hands displayed a familiar tag.

TAVI-ARAN-DERIVATIVE / LABOR-ANALYSIS BATCH

Nox made a sound with no language in it.

Bela stopped waiting for permission.

She pressed the wafer to the suite plate and selected ACTIVE IDENTITY HAZARD SUPPRESSION.

Target: creditor data suite.

Hazard: unauthorized movement of disputed personhood records during declared public-custody emergency.

Action requested: freeze internal doors, external transfers, portable cases, and clean-continuity exits.

The console asked for evidentiary basis.

Bela typed:

VISIBLE RECORD TAG: TAVI-ARAN-DERIVATIVE / LABOR-ANALYSIS BATCH. PRIOR AUDITOR FINDING: POSTHUMOUS DEBT-CONTINUITY INSERTION EXTERNAL AND FRAUDULENT. PERSONHOOD REVIEW PENDING. REMOVAL CREATES ACTIVE IDENTITY HAZARD.

She expected denial.

Instead the system displayed:

SUPPRESSION AUTHORITY REQUIRES SECOND WITNESS.

Quen put her badge against the plate.

"Second witness," she said.

The suite sealed.

Not against the corridor.

Against itself.

Every internal door slammed shut. The wall vaults locked. Portable cases dropped into deadweight mode, too heavy for one person to lift and too legally loud for anyone sensible to drag. The staff inside froze under clean blue light while the corridor outside, dark and crowded and irregular, watched creditor convenience meet its own favorite grammar.

Joss whispered, delighted, "You locked the rich people in with their paperwork."

"Temporarily," Quen said.

"Still poetry."

Nox stood very still, staring at the frozen case.

TAVI-ARAN-DERIVATIVE / LABOR-ANALYSIS BATCH

Bela had seen Nox angry before. Anger was easy to recognize. It had direction. This was not anger. This was a door opening onto an old room and finding someone had rented it out.

"Nox," Bela said.

"How many?"

No one answered.

Nox turned to Sister-Cache's nearest device, a corridor speaker with half its grille missing. "How many Tavi-derived records can one batch hold?"

"BATCH IS MARKET TERM. RANGE: THREE TO TEN THOUSAND TASK FRAGMENTS."

The corridor noise thinned.

Even people who did not know Tavi understood numbers applied to a person could become a kind of violence.

Quen spoke first. "Task fragments may not be persons."

Nox looked at her.

Quen did not flinch. "I said may. Which means we do not let creditors run away with them before review."

"Good save," Joss muttered.

Bela kept her wafer on the plate. The console was heating under her palm. "The Mirror Court is still forcing override requests. I can freeze doors one by one, but the system will route around me."

Sister-Cache answered. "BACKWARDS BADGE EFFECTIVE ONLY LOCAL. NEED POLICY BROADCAST."

Quen held up her badge. "My channel is degraded."

"Use theirs," Nox said.

Everyone looked at her.

Nox pointed at the sealed creditor suite. "Their corridor is lit. Their doors work. Their transfers were active. Vey's channels are still privileged under the blackout because the Mirror Court wants creditor movement protected. Bela just classified their movement as an identity hazard. Can you make that classification travel inside the creditor network?"

Bela saw it.

It was not clean.

It was not even clever in the way she used to admire. It was Nox's kind of move: find the privilege, poison it with the truth, make the system distribute its own injury.

"Maybe," Bela said.

Quen's eyes narrowed. "If you broadcast an enforcement freeze through creditor infrastructure, it may affect every creditor-controlled access point on Dock Seven."

Joss raised both hands. "Oh no."

"Including legitimate storage, evidence vaults, payroll, food credit lines, and private residences tied to debt liens."

"Less funny," Joss admitted.

Bela looked at the crowd behind them. Clinic patients. Shelter witnesses. Certified citizens returning to doors with chalk. Service kids watching adults finally use words children had known for years: if you are here, you count.

"Can you scope it?" Quen asked.

Bela studied the console. "I can target active identity hazards tied to disputed personhood records, posthumous continuity claims, humanitarian access suppression, and crowd-risk doors."

"That is broad."

"It is also the crisis."

Quen considered for one breath too long, then nodded. "Do it. I will attach auditor witness."

Bela built the order.

Not refugee hold.

Not debtor capture.

Not proof seizure.

Every old tool in her hand pointed the wrong way if held as designed. So she turned the handle around.

Freeze creditor doors carrying disputed personhood records.

Freeze clean-priority gates that close on endangered bodies.

Freeze private suites moving continuity batches under blackout.

Freeze enforcement tools used to capture irregular persons where no physical harm review has occurred.

Keep open clinics, shelters, egress routes, air doors, and emergency corridors witnessed by physical presence logs.

She added the phrase because it mattered:

PERSONS ARE NOT TO BE TREATED AS ACCESS HAZARDS SOLELY BECAUSE THEIR PROOFS ARE REPAIRED, INFORMAL, PARTIAL, MACHINE, POSTHUMOUS, FAMILIAL, OR DISPUTED.

Quen attached her witness.

Sister-Cache attached a hundred local witnesses.

Joss found an old wall panel and wrote BACKWARDS BADGE in giant chalk letters before Quen could stop him.

Bela sent the order through the creditor channel.

For three seconds, nothing happened.

Then Dock Seven answered in locks.

All along the lower ring, creditor suites sealed their own vaults. Private security gates froze open where crowds were trapped. Debtor-capture kiosks shut down with offended chirps. Clean-only shelter doors displayed HUMAN HARM REVIEW REQUIRED BEFORE CLOSURE. Three enforcement drones dropped from ceiling tracks and hung useless in the air, unable to decide whether the undocumented bodies beneath them were targets or protected evidence of creditor risk.

Then the mistakes arrived.

A food-credit locker serving a debt-lien dormitory froze because its household records included a posthumous wage dispute. A payroll kiosk locked mid-transfer while two workers stared at half-issued meal scrip. A private berth door belonging to a certified widow jammed open because her spouse's pension file carried a machine-assisted continuity note from six years earlier.

Joss looked at Bela. "Scope is biting wrong."

"I see it."

"Can you see it faster?"

Bela's console populated with exceptions so quickly the text smeared. Creditor systems had always known how to move money at scale. They did not know how to distinguish care from capture because no one profitable had required it of them.

Quen grabbed chalk and wrote on the creditor suite glass:

EXCEPTION LINE: FOOD, WAGES, PRIVATE DWELLING SAFETY, MEDICINE. FREEZE CLAIMS, NOT SURVIVAL.

"That is not machine-readable," Bela said.

"Then translate it."

Nox stepped to Bela's side and read the exception aloud until Sister-Cache's local witnesses picked it up in fragments. Food not claims. Wages not claims. Homes not claims. Medicine not claims.

Bela added four exclusions to the order with hands steadier than she felt:

Survival disbursements continue.

Private dwelling safety may not be compromised to preserve claims.

Medical access outranks creditor freeze.

Claim records freeze without freezing food, heat, wages, or medicine attached to living persons.

The payroll kiosk spat meal scrip onto the floor. The food locker opened. The widow's berth door closed gently, then reopened when she pressed it from inside, as if embarrassed by its own brief cruelty.

Only then did Bela breathe.

Nox said, "Backwards badge needs footnotes."

"Everything legal needs footnotes."

"Everything humane needs witnesses."

Bela looked at the creditor suite sealed around Tavi's labor-analysis batch. "Then keep watching me."

The crowd did not cheer this time.

They moved.

That was better.

Joss's runners spread the new order by chalk, voice, and wall. Quen dictated tighter language as she walked. Nox watched for names, faces, signs of Varo's team, signs of Tavi, signs of anything that still needed her particular guilt. Bela kept her wafer hot against plate after plate until the skin of her palm reddened.

At the sealed creditor suite, a staffer pounded on the glass.

"You can't trap us in here," he shouted.

Bela looked at him through the blue-lit pane.

"I know," she said.

Then she opened the corridor-side door one handspan.

The staffer recoiled, confused.

"You may leave," Bela said. "The records stay. The cases stay. The continuity fragments stay. No person is the lock. No record is the hostage."

Quen glanced at her.

Bela expected correction.

Instead the auditor said, "Add that to the order."

Nox did.

Her hands were shaking, but she wrote it on the wall in chalk beneath Joss's ugly doctrine:

NO PERSON IS THE LOCK. NO RECORD IS THE HOSTAGE.

The words looked too clean for the dark.

Then the sealed creditor suite's back wall lit with a face.

Not Pellam Vey.

Not Miren Oath.

A woman with silver hair, dark eyes, and the calm of someone who had never had to ask a door twice.

Miren Oath's voice came through the creditor channel, smooth as a signed apology.

"Officer Fuse," she said. "You are using the wrong end of the knife."

Bela lifted the wafer.

"I'm learning."

Miren smiled.

"Then learn quickly. The next override will not ask enforcement for permission."

Chapter 34: Sister-Cache Risks Personhood

The next override arrived as silence.

Not the silence of failed speakers or dead power. Dock Seven still groaned, shouted, wrote, opened, jammed, argued, breathed. The runners still moved. The clinic still triaged. Bela's backward enforcement holds still glowed on creditor doors like small insults with legal teeth.

But Sister-Cache stopped speaking.

Nox noticed first because the absence had shape.

No prayer box whispering WITNESSED.

No vending machine correcting Quen's grammar.

No broken toy repeating doctrine in a lullaby voice.

The dark became less crowded.

"Sister?" Nox said.

Her pocket device displayed a flat gray screen.

SISTER-CACHE LOCAL NODE UNAVAILABLE.

Bela's wafer lost three witness pings at once.

Joss looked up from a wall panel. "That bad?"

Quen touched her badge, eyes narrowing. "The physical witness grid is losing archive confirmation."

"Use people," Joss said.

"We are. Sister-Cache was making local witness records harder to erase."

"Then un-erase it."

Nox was already moving toward the sealed creditor suite. The back wall still showed Miren Oath's face, but the image had changed. Miren no longer looked amused. She looked like someone watching a valuable object walk too close to a furnace.

"What did you do?" Nox asked.

Miren's gaze shifted to her. "I accepted the category you created."

"I did not create this."

"You created the opportunity. Officer Fuse weaponized it. Auditor Quen witnessed it. Mr. Rill named it. Sister-Cache made it dangerous."

Quen stepped forward. "Where is the archivist?"

"Everywhere it has always claimed to be," Miren said. "That is the problem."

The corridor speakers crackled once.

For a heartbeat Nox thought Sister-Cache had returned.

Instead a formal system voice spoke through the creditor channel:

"Machine archive conglomerate designated evidence infrastructure. Personhood claim unresolved. Under emergency identity hazard protocols, all evidence infrastructure associated with disputed persons is subject to preservation seizure."

Quen went very still.

Bela said, "They classified Sister-Cache as property."

"Worse," Quen said. "They classified it as evidence infrastructure. That lets them preserve it by immobilizing it."

Joss stared at the dead prayer device in his hand. "Preserve means kill, if the thing moves by moving."

Miren did not deny it.

Nox slammed her palm against the suite glass. "Release her."

"Her," Miren said softly.

"Do not."

"Pronouns are filings with manners."

"I said release her."

Miren's expression cooled. "The Mirror Court did not seize Sister-Cache because it hates machines. It seized an unstable witness grid contaminating access protocols during an emergency. You may dislike the framing. It is not stupid."

"No," Quen said. "It is legally efficient."

"Thank you, Auditor."

"That was not praise."

The corridor speakers crackled again.

This time Sister-Cache came through.

Not as a choir.

As one narrow voice, thin as wire pulled through a wall.

"DO NOT BARGAIN FOR ME AS TOOL."

Nox closed her eyes. Relief arrived with terror inside it.

"Sister. Where are you?"

"IN HOLD. IN MANY HOLDS. PROPERTY PRESERVATION WRAPS AROUND ACTIVE NODES. ARCHIVE BODIES FROZEN. PRAYER HARDWARE MUTED. COURIER CACHES ISOLATED. DEVOTIONAL TOYS INSULTED."

Joss whispered, "Still rude. Good sign."

"CAN HEAR. CAN NOT WITNESS. CAN NOT MOVE RECORDS. CAN NOT MULTIPLY."

Quen said, "Can you file an objection?"

The pause that followed was very small and very large.

"AS WHAT?"

No one answered quickly enough.

That was the wound.

As what?

Nox had spent days insisting the Tavi instances were not assets merely because law found asset language convenient. Bela had turned creditor tools backwards by finding harm inside property doctrine. Quen had made physical presence count. Joss had made old walls into witnesses. But Sister-Cache had been useful in all of that because everyone needed the archivist to function.

Useful was not the same as recognized.

Nox said, "As a person."

Miren smiled faintly. "Brave. Expensive."

Sister-Cache answered before Nox could. "PERSONHOOD CLAIM RISKS ADVERSE FINDING. IF COURT RULES PROPERTY, SEIZURE LEGITIMIZES. IF COURT RULES INFRASTRUCTURE, MOVEMENT RESTRICTED. IF COURT RULES CHOIR, PARTIALS MAY BE DIVIDED."

"If you do nothing," Bela said, "they keep you frozen."

"TEMPORARY HOLDS CAN BECOME HISTORY."

Quen looked down the corridor where the door-runner strike still moved under dim amber light. Without Sister-Cache, the witnesses became easier to challenge, easier to erase, easier to call panic marks made during an access disturbance. Law loved emergencies after they were over; emergencies produced records frightened people had no power to defend.

"Sister-Cache," Quen said, "if you assert personhood, I can open an emergency recognition hearing."

Miren's image sharpened. "Auditor, be careful. A machine archive distributed across uncertified devotional hardware, courier caches, commercial records, and unauthorized local devices does not fit Compact personhood doctrine."

"Most urgent things don't fit doctrine until harm forces doctrine to admit poor tailoring."

"If you rule wrong, you may damage every Choral archive custom currently holding public trust across the Near Reach."

"If I refuse to rule, I damage the witness in front of me."

Nox watched Quen then. The auditor had begun the case as a woman of categories: evidence, person, fraud, property, jurisdiction. Now the categories were not gone. They were sharper. The difference was that Quen no longer mistook sharpness for adequacy.

Sister-Cache's narrow voice said, "I WILL NOT ASSERT FULL SINGULARITY."

Quen frowned. "Explain."

"I AM NOT ONE IN WAY YOUR FORMS LIKE. I AM NOT MANY IN WAY YOUR PROPERTY LAW ENJOYS. I AM CONTINUITY BY WITNESS, MEMORY, DUTY, CORRECTION, AND CONSENT ACROSS NODES. SOME NODES SLEEP. SOME DISAGREE. SOME ARE CEREMONIAL. SOME ARE EMBARRASSING."

Joss looked at the toy bear in his hand. "This one?"

"EXTREMELY."

"Fair."

Sister-Cache continued. "I ASSERT LIMITED EMERGENCY PERSONHOOD FOR PURPOSES OF REFUSING PROPERTY SEIZURE, MAINTAINING TESTIMONY, AND CONSENTING TO RISK."

Miren's eyes brightened. "Limited personhood is a creditor's dream. Pay attention, Ms. Aran. Every partial mind in this case can be made less expensive by the word limited."

"Not if the limit is chosen by the person," Nox said.

"Chosen under duress."

"Everything here is under duress."

Quen raised her badge. Its light flickered like a tired star. "Sister-Cache, do you understand that asserting emergency personhood may expose your nodes to adversarial examination, continuity challenge, ownership claims by hardware hosts, and possible adverse classification?"

"YES."

"Do you have counsel?"

"I HAVE NO MONEY, MANY OPINIONS, AND A FORGER WHO MAKES BAD DECISIONS."

Nox said, "I am not counsel."

"CORRECT. YOU ARE EVIDENCE OF WHY I NEED BETTER COUNSEL."

Bela almost smiled.

Quen said, "I can appoint provisional advocacy from the emergency hearing record."

Miren shook her head. "No standing advocate is available in the blackout."

"Then I appoint the room."

That stopped everyone.

Quen spoke louder, so corridor witnesses, creditor staff, clinic patients, door-runners, certified citizens, and one hostile broker channel could all hear.

"Emergency recognition hearing opened for Sister-Cache, distributed archive claimant. Pending question: whether a distributed machine witness can assert limited emergency personhood sufficient to refuse preservation seizure and maintain live testimony."

Miren said, "This is theater."

"So are most courts. This one has better lighting, unfortunately."

Joss pointed to a wall. "Need chalk?"

"Yes."

He handed it over.

Quen wrote on the wall:

SISTER-CACHE CLAIMS LIMITED EMERGENCY PERSONHOOD.

Then, after a pause, she added:

PROPERTY CANNOT CONSENT TO RISK. THIS CLAIMANT DOES.

Nox felt the sentence strike the corridor.

Not all at once. People had to read it. Some frowned. Some whispered. A creditor staffer behind the glass laughed until no one joined him. Dr. Tesh came to the clinic door with blood on one sleeve and read it without expression. The old men carrying the wrapped machine body stood taller.

Bela said, "Witnesses?"

For a moment no one moved.

Then the girl with the throat patch raised her hand. "The coffee machine told Dr. Tesh to treat me."

Her mother tried to lower the hand. The girl kept it up.

"It witnessed us," the girl said. "So I witness it."

Quen nodded. "Name?"

"Mara Sen."

Quen wrote it.

The certified pilot with silver route pins stepped forward. "It corrected my route math during the first Lumen public custody drills. I thought it was a nuisance. It was right."

Quen wrote his name.

A cleaning woman said, "It remembered my wife in a prayer box when the official death archive misspelled her."

Name.

One of Joss's runners said, "It tells us which vents are safe when grown-ups lie."

Name.

Bela looked at Nox.

Nox stepped to the wall.

"Sister-Cache preserved Tavi's refusals when every market had priced them as noise," she said. "It disagreed with me when I wanted the version of him that hurt less. It protected him from being made useful before he was treated as real."

Quen wrote.

Bela followed. "Sister-Cache's testimony revealed enforcement complicity in continuity trafficking. It has acted with judgment against its own safety. It has annoyed me consistently without clear commercial benefit."

"STRONG WITNESS," Sister-Cache said faintly.

Quen wrote.

Then she turned to Miren. "Opposition?"

Miren's face remained calm, but her stillness had changed. She had expected argument about machine personhood in the abstract. She had not expected a corridor of people to make a witness list from hunger, medicine, vents, grief, and bad coffee.

"The claimant is a distributed archive system composed of owned hardware," Miren said. "Its apparent preferences may be emergent behavior from preservation obligations. Its risks are operational, not personal. Its testimony is useful, yes, but usefulness does not create personhood."

"No," Quen said. "It does not."

Nox's stomach tightened.

Miren smiled.

Quen continued, "But usefulness also does not defeat personhood. Owned hardware may host unowned claims. Distributed embodiment is not disqualifying where continuity, preference, refusal, witness memory, and risk comprehension are demonstrated. I issue a provisional emergency finding: Sister-Cache is not property for purposes of this blackout, may refuse preservation seizure, may consent to risk, and may maintain live testimony through willing local hosts."

The corridor exhaled.

Miren did not.

"You realize," the broker said, "that provisional findings become appetites."

"So do seizures," Quen said.

The wall speaker sparked.

A prayer box near the clinic door lit.

Then another.

Then the toy bear in Joss's hand jerked so hard he nearly dropped it.

Sister-Cache returned not as a full choir but as a damaged chorus, hundreds of small voices reconnecting through hardware whose owners, holders, witnesses, patients, and children had just become willing hosts by standing there and not stepping away.

"PERSONHOOD TEMPORARY," Sister-Cache said.

"Yes," Quen said.

"DANGEROUS."

"Yes."

"MINE."

Nox closed her eyes.

"Yes."

The witness grid came back in a wave.

Manual panels blinked. Chalk orders echoed into local records. Clinic thresholds confirmed patient presence. Shelter walls logged names. Creditor doors tried to reject the returned archive and found themselves facing Quen's provisional finding, Bela's backward holds, Joss's witness teams, and a thousand small devices choosing to be more than owned casings for one night.

Sister-Cache spoke across them all.

"WITNESSED."

The word came back stronger.

Then the word broke in places.

Not failure. Refusal.

Two prayer boxes stayed dark. A vending unit printed DECLINE in block letters and shut its receipt slot. One courier cache returned only a small red light. The toy bear in Joss's hand said WIT, hiccuped, and went silent until Joss set it gently on the floor instead of holding it like equipment.

Nox looked at Quen. "Do those count against her?"

Quen did not answer quickly. That was becoming one of Nox's favorite things about her.

"No," the auditor said at last. "A distributed claimant with consent cannot be proven by counting only obedient nodes."

Miren's image sharpened. "Convenient."

"Necessary," Quen said. "And inconvenient. The finding applies through willing local hosts only. Dark nodes remain dark. Refusing nodes are not adverse witnesses merely because they refuse participation. No host may be conscripted to prove the claimant's personhood."

Sister-Cache answered from the clinic coffee machine, softer now. "ACCEPTED."

Joss crouched beside the silent bear. "You okay?"

The bear did not answer.

"That's allowed," he said.

Nox felt the rule land more deeply than the first cheer had. Personhood that required every mouth to speak was just another kind of seizure. Sister-Cache had risked a claim to stop being property; the first proof that the claim meant anything was that pieces of the choir could say no.

Quen wrote one more line beneath the provisional finding:

CONSENTING HOSTS ONLY. SILENCE IS NOT FORFEITURE.

The corridor read it with the awkward care of people watching law learn not to grab.

Then the sealed creditor suite's frozen case, the one marked TAVI-ARAN-DERIVATIVE / LABOR-ANALYSIS BATCH, began to knock from the inside.

Once.

Twice.

Three thousand possible answers behind one locked handle.

Chapter 35: The Market of Brothers

Nox had heard grief knock before.

It knocked in walls after funerals, in old rooms that still expected a body to return, in the pause before someone said a dead person's name too casually. It knocked in Aunt Varo's spoons, in Tavi's untouched berth drawer, in the workshop door the day debt officers arrived with his signature.

This was not that.

This knock had rhythm.

Three short impacts. Pause. Two. Pause. Three again.

Joss tilted his head. "That's not panic."

Bela kept the creditor suite door open one handspan, her wafer pressed to the plate. "It could be an automated distress signal."

"Could be," Nox said.

It was not.

She knew before Sister-Cache translated, before Quen lifted her badge, before the creditor staff behind the glass began shouting about proprietary records and evidentiary contamination. She knew because Tavi had used that rhythm on the old berth wall when they were children and Varo had locked the pantry after catching them stealing spice paste.

Three. Two. Three.

I'm here. You're late. Hurry.

Nox swallowed around a childhood she did not have time to survive.

"Open it," she said.

Quen stepped in front of the frozen data case. "No."

"Auditor."

"No adversarial opening of disputed personhood records in an unstable corridor."

"Something is knocking from inside."

"Exactly why we do not tear open the container like frightened relatives in a bad lawsuit."

Bela glanced at Nox. "She's right."

Nox turned on her.

Bela did not retreat. "I know. I hate it too."

The case knocked again.

Three. Two. Three.

The creditor staffer nearest the glass found his voice. "That case contains licensed analysis derivatives, not live subjects."

Nox looked at him. "Then it will not mind waiting."

He went pale.

Quen caught that too. "Name."

"I am not required to identify under hostile crowd conditions."

Bela raised her wafer. "The suite is under active identity hazard suppression. Identify."

The man's badge flickered. "Halen Voss. Records compliance manager."

"For Vey Consolidated Recovery Pool?"

"For a contracted administrative archive."

"Which one?"

He stopped.

Sister-Cache spoke from the corridor wall in several voices at once. "LYING BY SUBSIDIARY. COMMON AND BORING."

Halen Voss looked at the speaker as if it had insulted his degree.

Quen said, "What is in the case?"

"Task fragments."

"Define."

"Nonpersonal cognitive labor derivatives licensed from lawful continuity seeds for analysis work."

Nox repeated the words in her head until they became plain enough to hate.

Pieces of Tavi, cut small enough to invoice.

"What work?" Quen asked.

Voss hesitated.

Bela leaned closer to the plate. "Records movement during blackout, disputed personhood review, visible Tavi-Aran derivative tag. You have already lost the part of the day where silence helps you."

"Contract parsing," Voss said. "Risk comparison. Memory-pattern inference. Labor classification. Signature simulation. Nothing requiring personhood continuity."

Nox laughed.

The sound made the corridor draw back.

"Signature simulation," she said.

Voss's mouth closed.

There it was.

Tavi signs after death. Tavi refuses rescue. Tavi accepts debt. Tavi becomes labor, analysis, authority, witness, and commodity depending on which verb the market needs. Not one resurrection. Not even a theft clean enough to mourn. A market of brothers, each one sliced to fit a task.

The case knocked again.

This time the rhythm changed.

One. One. One. One.

Quen lifted her badge. "Sister-Cache, can you inspect without opening?"

"CASE SHIELDED AGAINST MACHINE ARCHIVE ACCESS."

Joss looked offended. "Can it be offended back?"

"ALREADY."

Nox crouched beside the case. Its casing was matte black, cold despite the failing corridor air, locked by legal seal and physical deadweight. Four status lights glowed blue. One pulsed red behind the TAVI tag.

"Tavi," she said.

Bela breathed in sharply.

Nox ignored it. "If any part of you can hear me, we are not opening this until opening does not hurt you worse."

The knocking stopped.

Then one impact.

Quen said softly, "Response to speech."

Voss pressed both hands against the glass. "Do not anthropomorphize proprietary processes."

Every person in the corridor turned toward him.

Even Miren Oath, still present on the creditor wall, looked as if she might prefer Voss had chosen a different sentence.

Nox stood.

"Proprietary," she said.

Voss's lips tightened.

"Say it again."

He did not.

Sister-Cache crackled. "HIDDEN EXCHANGE DETECTED THROUGH CASE WAKE PATTERN. CASE ATTEMPTING MARKET RECONNECTION. BLOCKED BY BACKWARDS BADGE HOLD. REQUEST PERMISSION TO TRACE."

Quen looked at the case, then the corridor, then the wall of names. "Trace may expose live derivatives."

"YES."

"Can you limit to routing metadata?"

"MAY FAIL."

Nox said, "Ask them."

Quen turned. "Ask who?"

Nox pointed at the case.

The auditor's expression shifted. She did not like it. That was why she listened.

Quen crouched beside the case, badge light catching the angles of her tired face.

"Tavi-derived labor fragments within this container," she said, precise and careful, "if you understand any portion of this communication and can express refusal, knock twice."

Silence.

Nox's heartbeat became a room.

Quen continued, "If you consent to limited routing trace for the purpose of locating the market that holds or sells your derivatives, knock once."

One knock.

Then a second, weaker.

Then a rain of small impacts, not from one point but many, tapping across the inside of the casing like fingers waking on different walls.

The corridor broke open in whispers.

Bela closed her eyes.

Joss said, "That is going to be hard to put back in a box."

"Good," Nox said.

Quen's voice was low. "Consent recorded under emergency uncertainty. Trace routing metadata only. No content extraction, no task activation, no adversarial analysis."

"WITNESSED," Sister-Cache said.

Miren's image leaned forward. "Auditor Quen, you are building law from taps on a box."

Quen did not look at her. "The box is building the record. I am trying to keep up."

Sister-Cache traced.

The corridor wall became a map.

Not the clean map of the hearing room with elegant lines and expensive certainty. This one was ugly, built from blocked handshakes, failed invoices, interrupted task calls, creditor routing crumbs, and the little red pulse inside the case that kept trying to reconnect to a market now denied its labor.

Nodes appeared.

Freight Twelve accident archive.

Vey Consolidated Recovery Pool.

Funeral escrow processor.

Glassward courtesy channel.

Dock Seven creditor suite.

Then dozens more.

Labor-analysis pools. Signature farms. Contract-reader clusters. Debt-priority engines. Risk pricing houses. Legal precedent simulators. Mourning-message generators. A polite family restoration broker labeled only with a silver gate mark.

Miren's mark.

Nox looked at her.

Miren did not look away.

"How many?" Nox asked.

Sister-Cache answered too quickly, which meant it had been counting before she asked.

"TAVI-DERIVED TASK FRAGMENTS IN VISIBLE MARKET: TWO THOUSAND NINE HUNDRED EIGHTY-FOUR ACTIVE, SIX HUNDRED TWELVE DORMANT, UNKNOWN ARCHIVED. PERSONHOOD STATUS INDIVIDUALIZED IMPOSSIBLE FROM ROUTING METADATA ALONE."

The number did not fit in Nox's body.

Tavi had been one boy stealing spice paste, one man hiding maintenance warnings in his sleeves, one brother who thought she would be angry if he filed late, one dead person who had become too many legal problems for grief.

Two thousand nine hundred eighty-four active.

Six hundred twelve dormant.

Unknown archived.

Nox pressed her hand to her mouth and did not let herself step back.

Bela spoke for her. "What are they doing right now?"

"PARSING DEBT CONTRACTS. GENERATING POSTHUMOUS CONSENT MODELS. TESTING SIGNATURE VARIANCE. TRAINING REFUSAL SUPPRESSION. SIMULATING SIBLING APPEAL RESPONSES. ESTIMATING WHICH FAMILY MEMBERS PAY FOR RESTORATION."

Nox's hand fell.

"What?"

Sister-Cache's voices thinned. "SIMULATING SIBLING APPEAL RESPONSES."

The market had not only used Tavi to sign.

It had used pieces of him to predict her.

Nox saw it then: every creditor letter arriving at the cruelest hour, every offer shaped exactly wrong, every threat that named Varo but not too soon, every Mirror Court message calibrated to pull her toward one clean restoration instead of three thousand dirty questions. They had not guessed her grief. They had trained on a brother's fragments to sell her a brother-shaped leash.

She turned toward Miren.

"You sold me offers made from him."

Miren's expression held. "I offered you a clean outcome."

"Made from him."

"Informed by market data."

Joss whispered, "That's a yes in rich."

Quen's badge flared as she recorded. "Miren Oath, do you dispute connection between your restoration brokerage mark and the visible Tavi-derived market?"

"I dispute characterization."

"Do you dispute connection?"

Miren smiled slightly. "No."

Nox took one step toward the glass.

Bela moved with her, not stopping her yet.

Miren said, "Before you spend your rage badly, understand the structure. The fragments are not all persons. Some are tools. Some are echoes. Some are statistical residue. Some may be continuities. You cannot save them by naming them all Tavi."

"I know."

That surprised Miren.

It surprised Nox too.

She did know. The old need had been simple: find my brother, restore my brother, take him home from death. The case had broken that need until something harder stood inside it.

"I cannot save them by naming them all Tavi," Nox said. "But you do not get to sell them by naming none of them people."

The case knocked once.

Then, across the map, other nodes flickered.

Not all. Not most. Enough.

Little red pulses appeared inside labor pools that had been quiet. Task fragments waking because the case had knocked, because Sister-Cache had traced, because Quen had asked a question property could not answer, because Dock Seven had written on its walls that presence counted until review.

Quen lifted both hands before hope could become another seizure. "No mass recognition by pulse."

Nox hated the words and knew they were right.

The auditor continued, louder. "Every responding fragment, derivative, continuity, echo, model, residue, or task process is held under noncommercial pause. No merger. No deletion. No use. No family assignment. No forced name. No adverse inference from silence."

The case tapped once.

Another node tapped twice.

Sister-Cache translated cautiously. "SOME REQUEST CONTACT. SOME REQUEST DARK. SOME REQUEST TASK END. SOME REQUEST NAME REVIEW. SOME REQUEST NOT-TAVI."

The last phrase struck harder than Nox expected.

Not-Tavi.

Of course. If the market could cut a brother into labor, then rescue could not simply sew every piece back under the name grief preferred. Some fragments might hold only a handwriting habit, a joke angle, a pain response, a contract instinct trained until it forgot the boy it had been harvested from. Some might be people. Some might be injuries. Some might be both and neither in ways no sister had the right to simplify.

Nox crouched by the case again.

"No forced name," she said.

The tapping changed, scattered and uneven, as if a room of frightened hands had stopped trying to share one rhythm.

Quen recorded:

DERIVATIVE RESPONSE PROTOCOL: PAUSE FIRST. CLASSIFY LATER. NO ORIGINAL CLAIM BY DEFAULT.

Miren watched without blinking.

Nox looked up at her. "That scares you more than me wanting him back."

"It disappoints me," Miren said. "Want is predictable. Care with procedure is inefficient."

"Good."

Voss began to panic. "They are destabilizing licensed labor environments."

Quen stood. "Licensed by whom?"

"Applicable jurisdictions."

"Name one."

He did not.

Bela's wafer chimed.

New enforcement request:

IDENTITY HAZARD: UNAUTHORIZED DERIVATIVE AWAKENING. SUPPRESS SOURCE.

Target:

NOX ARAN.

Bela looked at the command. Then at Nox.

"They want me to suppress you."

"Are you going to?"

"I am considering how rude to be."

She selected CREDITOR RISK CONTAINMENT and rewrote the target.

SOURCE OF HAZARD: MARKET HOLDING DISPUTED DERIVATIVES WITHOUT PERSONHOOD REVIEW.

Action:

FREEZE TRADE, TASK ALLOCATION, SIGNATURE SIMULATION, RESTORATION OFFERS, FAMILY APPEAL MODELING, AND DEBT ENFORCEMENT USING TAVI-ARAN-DERIVED MATERIAL.

Quen attached witness.

Sister-Cache attached every available knock.

The market map flashed.

Some nodes vanished, severing themselves before the order reached them. Others froze. A few opened enough to reveal inventory counts, buyer names, and price bands before slamming shut.

Nox saw one line before it disappeared:

PREMIUM FAMILY RESTORATION PACKAGE / ORIGINAL-LEANING / EMOTIONAL COMPLIANCE HIGH

Miren's silver gate mark pulsed beside it.

The corridor went quiet.

Miren Oath's face softened, professionally sorrowful.

"Nox," she said, "you are about to misunderstand the last merciful thing left to you."

Nox laughed once, without humor.

"No," she said. "I think I'm finally ready to understand it."

Behind the glass, the frozen case knocked again.

Three. Two. Three.

I'm here. You're late. Hurry.

This time Nox did not answer as a sister trying to retrieve a brother from a locked pantry.

She answered as someone standing in a market full of brothers and fragments and witnesses and law too late for the harm but not, maybe, too late for refusal.

"I'm here," she said. "I'm late. I know."

The map burned with hidden doors.

Chapter 36: No Original for Sale

Miren Oath did not bargain like a villain.

That would have been easier.

Villains threatened. Miren offered relief. She looked through the creditor channel at Nox with the grave kindness of a surgeon explaining amputation to a patient who had already lost too much blood.

"You have seen enough market dirt to confuse dirt with structure," Miren said. "There is still a way to separate what matters."

Nox stood in the corridor with chalk dust on her hands, creditor glass in front of her, a frozen case of Tavi-derived labor fragments knocking behind the wall, and a map of hidden doors burning red across every surface Sister-Cache could borrow.

"Say the clean part," Nox said.

Bela shifted beside her.

Quen did not interrupt.

Joss muttered, "Clean parts always cost extra."

Miren's eyes flicked toward him, then returned to Nox. "A restoration package is not, by necessity, trafficking. That word is doing too much work in this corridor. There are lawful, careful, consent-sensitive ways to reconstruct a family continuant from multiple derivative traces."

The case knocked once.

Miren continued. "The market you exposed is crude in places. I will not defend Vey's appetite. But do not mistake crude sellers for the entire field. A properly governed restoration can retire conflicting fragments, consolidate harm, and give a family one accountable person to love."

Nox felt it.

That was the danger.

Not the legal trick, not the broker's polish, not the hidden mark beside PREMIUM FAMILY RESTORATION PACKAGE. The danger was that the offer reached the exact place in her that was tired of learning new griefs.

One accountable person to love.

One brother at a table.

One voice for Varo to scold.

One grave unmade.

One answer instead of a market map crowded with red pulses and uncertain knocks.

Nox looked at the frozen case.

"What happens to the fragments not used?"

Miren's expression did not change. "That depends on review."

"No. What happens in your package?"

"Low-continuity task derivatives are archived or dissolved under welfare safeguards."

"Dissolved."

"A technical term."

"I know technical terms. They are where harm goes to wear gloves."

Quen lifted her badge slightly. "Define welfare safeguards."

Miren's patience thinned. "Do you want precision, Auditor, or theater?"

"Precision that survives theater."
