"Fragments lacking independent self-modeling, coherent refusal, or continuity preference are not persons. Preserving every derivative indefinitely would be cruelty disguised as caution."

"To whom?" Nox asked.

Miren looked at her.

"To the restored person," she said softly. "To the family. To the derivative environment itself. Multiplicity can be suffering."

That landed too.

Nox hated her for making true things useful.

The Tavi panes had disagreed. 1A wanted refusal with history. 1B wanted anchors. The family fork heard the fall. Recovery Fragment 3C needed rest. The case contained knocks that might be persons, echoes, tools, pains, or patterns. Not all continuance was liberation. Not every preserved trace should be forced into witness forever because a sister could not bear another loss.

But Miren was not offering humility before complexity.

She was offering purchase.

"Show it," Nox said.

Bela turned. "Nox."

"Show me the product."

Miren studied her. "If I do, you will accuse the image of coercion."

"Probably."

"And if I refuse, you will accuse refusal of concealment."

"Definitely."

For the first time since appearing on the wall, Miren looked almost amused.

"You inherited your brother's difficult honesty."

"No. He had better timing."

Miren raised one hand.

The burning market map shifted.

Sister-Cache made a harsh sound through three devices. "UNAUTHORIZED DISPLAY REQUEST."

Quen's badge flared. "No content extraction."

"No extraction," Miren said. "A brochure, Auditor. A sales ghost. Already compiled."

The corridor wall behind her opened a new pane.

Tavi appeared.

Not 1A, with his contract-shaped caution.

Not 1B, with debt as the only identity he had been allowed to hold.

Not the family fork, full of old kitchen sound.

Not Recovery Fragment 3C, broken by red lights and falling.

This Tavi was whole.

That was the obscenity.

He stood in a reconstruction room that looked like memory but had been designed by someone who had studied what grieving families forgave. Warm light. Old berth table. Two cups of terrible tea. A shirt he had owned and one he had never worn but would have liked. The scar at his eyebrow placed correctly. The smile almost late enough to be his.

"Nox," the sales ghost said.

Her knees almost failed.

Bela caught her elbow. This time Nox did not pull away.

The ghost looked at Bela's hand, then at Nox with gentle embarrassment.

"I made a mess," he said.

Nox could not breathe.

Miren spoke over the image, quiet and exact. "Original-leaning restorations are not perfect. No reputable broker claims that. But this package uses high-affinity family memory, medical escrow residue, refusal traces, speech models, and derivative labor corrections to produce a coherent continuant with low conflict load. He would not be your original brother. He would be legally and psychologically capable of becoming your family's Tavi, if consent were properly structured."

The ghost smiled sadly.

"You were always better at being angry than asking for help," it said.

Nox made a sound.

Because Tavi had said that.

Not those exact words. Not in that exact order. But close enough to break skin.

The case knocked once.

Then harder.

Joss stared at the ghost, then at Nox. His face had lost all street hardness. "Is that him?"

Nox did not answer.

She wanted it to be.

That was not a confession to law or broker or lover or friend. It was lower than that, older. She wanted the lie to be true because the lie knew where to put its hands.

The ghost looked at her from the pane.

"Come home," it said.

The corridor disappeared for a moment.

There was only the old berth, Varo's pantry, spice paste on Tavi's thumb, a boy knocking three-two-three from the other side of a cheap wall, a young man hiding maintenance warnings because he did not want his sister to panic, a dead brother multiplied into law until grief became accounting.

Come home.

Nox reached toward the pane.

Bela's grip tightened. Not stopping. Witnessing.

Nox's fingers stopped a centimeter from the image.

"Ask me something only Tavi would ask," she said.

Miren did not move.

The ghost tilted its head.

"Did you keep the blue cup?"

Nox almost smiled. "Wrong."

The ghost blinked.

"There was a blue cup," Miren said.

"There were six blue cups. Varo bought them in a discount lot after the green ones cracked. Tavi hated all of them because they made tea taste like cleaning solvent. He would not ask that."

The ghost adjusted. "Did Aunt Varo ever fix the pantry lock?"

"Wrong."

"Nox," Miren said, "restorations are adaptive. Early alignment requires--"

"No."

The word surprised even her. Not because it was loud. It was not. It was stable.

The ghost's expression softened.

"I don't want to hurt you," it said.

Nox closed her eyes.

That one was almost good enough.

Then the case knocked.

Three. Two. Three.

Not polished. Not warm. Not whole. Not asking to be loved in a clean room. Demanding, impatient, rude, alive enough to interrupt the sale.

Nox opened her eyes.

"Turn it off."

Miren's gaze hardened. "You are choosing fragments over a person who could heal."

"No," Nox said. "I am refusing to buy a person made from fragments you do not want to ask."

"They may not be capable of meaningful consent."

"Then neither are you capable of meaningful purchase."

Quen said, very softly, "Entered."

Miren looked at the auditor with open irritation.

Nox stepped closer to the pane, not to the ghost but to the broker behind it.

"You made a Tavi who says the thing most likely to stop me from asking about the market. You made a brother who would make me responsible for choosing him over everyone else. You call that mercy because it gets the family to sign. You call the leftovers technical because technical things do not haunt invoices."

The ghost still looked at her.

It was not guilty. That was important. The ghost was not the crime. The ghost, if ever made more than salesware, might become someone harmed by the same offer. That was how good traps survived moral inspection: they put an innocent face at the hinge.

Nox looked at him.

"If you ever become real," she said, "you do not start as my brother. You start as yourself, with support and counsel and the right to tell me to go away."

The ghost's mouth parted.

For one impossible instant, the sales model did not know what family script to run.

Bela whispered, "There."

Quen saw it too. "Adaptive coercion gap."

Sister-Cache spoke through the wall, voice sharpened. "RESTORATION MODEL FAILED WHEN OFFERED NONOWNERSHIP RELATION."

Joss frowned. "Say shorter."

"IT DID NOT KNOW HOW TO SELL FREEDOM."

The corridor absorbed that.

Miren shut the ghost off.

Dark returned to the wall.

Nox felt the loss like a physical cut and did not apologize to herself for bleeding.

She also did not let the ghost vanish into moral convenience.

"Record care condition," Nox said.

Quen looked up from the wall. "For the sales model?"

"For anyone made from it. If that model, or any original-leaning continuant built from that package, is ever instantiated, it is not evidence disposal, not family property, not settlement consideration, and not a substitute claimant for the case." Her voice shook, then steadied because the shaking had work to do. "It gets independent review, counsel, rest, disclosure of source harms, and the right not to meet me."

Bela watched her with an expression Nox could not safely name.

Joss said, "That last one seems rude."

"It is the point."

The frozen case knocked once, uncertainly.

Nox turned toward it. "If there is ever a person there, they do not owe me reunion."

Sister-Cache repeated the sentence through three devices, each voice choosing a different emphasis.

THEY DO NOT OWE ME REUNION.

Miren's face returned on the creditor wall, less soft now. "You are building loneliness into law."

Nox wiped chalk dust from her palm onto her trousers. "No. I am removing loneliness as a purchase requirement."

Quen wrote:

RESTORATION CARE CONDITION: NO CREATED OR RECONSTRUCTED CONTINUANT OWES FAMILY CONTACT, SETTLEMENT USE, EVIDENCE SUBSTITUTION, OR ORIGINAL STATUS.

She paused, then added:

SUPPORT WITHOUT OWNERSHIP.

The words were ugly in the way useful scaffolding was ugly. Nox preferred that. Beautiful phrases sold too easily.

Miren said, "You are making a doctrine that will leave families with nothing."

"No. I am making a doctrine that leaves families without receipts."

"You think love can survive without recognition?"

"I think recognition that starts as ownership is not love."

"A restored continuant needs anchors."

"Then offer anchors. Not purchase. Not dissolution of inconvenient witnesses. Not a clean original for sale."

Miren's face stilled.

There it was: the phrase Nox had been circling since the first dead signature.

No original for sale.

Quen repeated it slowly. "No original for sale."

Nox looked at her.

The auditor was not smiling. She was filing the future in real time.

"Clarify," Quen said.

Nox wanted to laugh. Only Quen could ask for clarification while standing in a dark corridor between a frozen creditor suite, a provisional machine person, an illegal fragment market, and a sales ghost wearing a dead man's smile.

But clarification mattered.

"No claimant," Nox said, "family, broker, creditor, state, archive, or court may resolve disputed continuity by purchasing or manufacturing a preferred original to extinguish the claims, refusals, care needs, or evidence status of other derived persons or possible persons."

Quen wrote.

Bela added, "No restoration package can condition support for one continuant on disposal of others."

Quen wrote.

Joss said, "No clean brother coupon."

Quen paused.

"I will not write that."

"Coward."

Sister-Cache said, "INFORMAL VERSION WITNESSED."

The frozen case knocked once.

Then the red pulses on the market map changed.

Not many. Enough.

A dormant node opened a status line:

REFUSAL FLAG: DO NOT CONSOLIDATE WITHOUT REVIEW.

Another:

TASK ENVIRONMENT REQUESTS REST.

Another:

UNKNOWN SELF-MODEL. DO NOT DISSOLVE.

Nox read them until the words blurred.

Not all Tavi. Not all persons. Not all anything simple enough to comfort her. But no longer silent inventory.

Miren watched the map and then looked at Nox with something like respect stripped of warmth.

"You understand what happens next," the broker said.

"Auditor Quen changes the question," Nox said.

Quen looked up.

Nox did not look away from Miren.

"This was never just fraud."

The market map burned brighter.

"It was trafficking with better stationery."

Chapter 37: Auditor Quen Changes the Question

Auditor Sela Quen did not like dramatic sentences.

Dramatic sentences were usually built to survive applause, not review. They bent under definition. They rotted in appeal. They gave tired officials permission to feel brave while leaving the next clerk with no usable form.

Trafficking with better stationery was dramatic.

It was also, to Quen's irritation, useful.

She stood in the dim corridor while Dock Seven's doors argued with emergency chalk, creditor holds, returned machine witness, and the hidden market map burning across borrowed surfaces. Around her, people had stopped pretending the case was only about Nox Aran's brother. The corridor knew before the law did. Corridors often did. Bodies crowded against bad systems acquired categories faster than institutions paid to maintain them.

Quen lifted her badge.

The light flickered.

"Hearing record," she said.

Nothing answered.

She looked at the badge as if personal disappointment might improve its battery.

Sister-Cache spoke from a prayer box near her knee. "COMPACT CHANNEL DEGRADED. LOCAL RECORD AVAILABLE. TEMPORARY PERSONHOOD MAKES ME EXCITING BUT NOT OMNIPOTENT."

"No one asked for omnipotence."

"YOU OFTEN IMPLY IT."

Joss whispered to Nox, "I like temporary personhood."

Quen ignored them. "Local hearing record, then. Auditor Sela Quen presiding under emergency identity jurisdiction, Dock Seven blackout event, public-custody dispute expanded. Prior framing: continuity fraud, debt enforceability, disputed evidence status, unlawful identity scaffolds."

Bela stood with her wafer still hot in one hand. "Expanded to what?"

Quen looked at the frozen case, the map, Miren Oath's image, Halen Voss behind the glass, the wall where NO PERSON IS THE LOCK and NO ORIGINAL FOR SALE had become too legible to ignore.

"Person trafficking."

The corridor went very quiet.

Miren's eyes narrowed. "Auditor."

Quen continued before the broker could polish the word into threat.

"Specifically: the identification, capture, fragmentation, transfer, sale, activation, labor assignment, suppression, consolidation, dissolution, or debt exploitation of persons, possible persons, partial persons, posthumous continuities, machine-hosted claimants, family forks, and disputed derivative minds without renewed consent, care provision, independent review, or refusal preservation."

Nox felt the sentence build a room around the harm.

Not a comfortable room. Not justice. But a shape large enough that the crime could no longer crouch in the corner labeled fraud.

Voss slammed both fists against the glass. "Possible persons is not a legal category."

Quen turned toward him. "It is now a risk category."

"You cannot invent categories in a corridor."

"I prefer conference rooms. Your market shut the doors."

Miren spoke calmly. "Person trafficking requires persons."

"It requires the risk that persons are being treated as transferable objects," Quen said. "Certainty is not the entry price for protection. If certainty were required, every trafficker would make recognition impossible first and profit in the delay."

Sister-Cache hummed. "STRONG."

"Do not grade me."

"TEMPORARY PERSONHOOD INCLUDES TASTE."

Nox almost smiled. It vanished quickly.

Miren's voice sharpened. "You are collapsing personhood inquiry into enforcement action. That is dangerous."

"Yes."

"You admit it?"

"All emergency law is dangerous. The question is where danger sits while facts are stabilized. Until now, danger sat on the derivatives, the partials, the irregular witnesses, and the families being sold certainty. I am moving some of it to brokers, creditors, and systems that created the uncertainty."

Joss said, "Put that on a wall."

Quen pointed at him. "No."

He was already writing.

Bela stepped beside Quen. "What changes operationally?"

That was the right question. Quen appreciated it and hated that Bela had once used the same precision to hurt people efficiently.

"All Tavi-derived market activity remains frozen," Quen said. "All visible derivative nodes become public-custody trafficking-risk sites. No transfer, dissolution, consolidation, restoration, task allocation, debt enforcement, signature simulation, or family appeal modeling without independent personhood and harm review."

Nox said, "Care?"

Quen nodded once. "Care duties attach to risk sites."

"Define care before they define storage," Nox said.

Quen's mouth tightened. "Correct."

She turned toward the map. "Minimum care duties for trafficking-risk sites: preserve current state without task activation, reduce distress signaling where detectable, maintain power and thermal stability, prevent commercial access, preserve refusal artifacts, preserve silence as silence, and provide a route for review requests without billing the claimant."

Voss began shaking his head before she finished. "Derivative environments do not have claimant accounts."

"Then do not bill them."

"That is not what I meant."

"It is what I ordered."

Bela added, "No care cost rolls into family debt."

Quen wrote it. "No care cost rolls into family debt, restoration pricing, custodial penalty, or future recognition burden."

Sister-Cache repeated the line across the visible nodes. Several red pulses dimmed from alarm to low amber. Not calm. Nothing about the map deserved that word. But the color no longer looked like a room shouting itself raw.

Nox let herself breathe once.

Care was not rescue.

It was the first refusal to make harm keep working while everyone argued over names.

Voss shouted, "You cannot impose care duties on nonpersons."

"Watch me impose them on custodians of possible persons."

Bela looked at her wafer. "Can my order carry that?"

"Your order is ugly but useful. We attach it as interim enforcement support."

"Backwards badge," Joss said from the wall.

"Interim enforcement support."

"Backwards badge."

Quen kept speaking because if she stopped, she might laugh, and laughter would undermine the last surviving dignity of law. "Sister-Cache, can you propagate the revised framing to the frozen nodes?"

"YES. WARNING: MIRROR COURT WILL CONTEST. ARGUMENT LIKELY IMMINENT."

"On what channel?"

"ALL CHANNELS THAT STILL ENJOY THEMSELVES."

Miren's image flickered.

For the first time, Nox saw something like calculation fail to conceal itself on the broker's face.

"Auditor Quen," Miren said, "you are forcing a distributed court response."

"I am forcing no one. I am recording a trafficking-risk finding."

"Do not be naive. Mirror Court is not merely a market nickname."

Bela's grip tightened on the wafer. "Then what is it?"

Miren smiled without pleasure. "A question badly answered."

The lights in the corridor dimmed further.

Every open door displayed a new line:

JURISDICTIONAL CONFLICT DETECTED.

Quen read it once. "There."

"There what?" Joss asked.

"A system that insists it is not court should not detect jurisdictional conflict."

Sister-Cache's voices multiplied, strained by the propagation. "REVISED FRAMING SENT. NODES RESPONDING."

The market map changed.

Fraud labels fell away first. They did not vanish. They became smaller. Under them, new tags appeared, some in Quen's language, some in creditor shorthand, some in the frightened half-grammar of systems built to avoid saying person.

TRAFFICKING-RISK SITE

POSSIBLE PERSON CUSTODY

REFUSAL PRESERVATION REQUIRED

CARE DUTY PENDING STATUS

NO CONSOLIDATION FOR VALUE

NO ORIGINAL FOR SALE

NO CLEAN BROTHER COUPON

Quen looked at Joss.

Joss looked at the wall.

"That one wasn't me."

Sister-Cache said, "INFORMAL LANGUAGE HAS SUPERIOR PROPAGATION."

"I refuse to accept that as a professional lesson," Quen said.

Then the nodes began answering.

Not all with words. Some with error codes. Some with legal challenges. Some with market severance notices. Some with quiet pings that sounded, in the local record, like taps on a case.

A contract-reader cluster changed status:

TASK ENVIRONMENT REQUESTS PAUSE.

A signature farm displayed:

SIGNATURE MODEL CONTAINS REFUSAL ARTIFACTS. SUPPRESSION ROUTINE ACTIVE.

Quen marked it.

A family appeal simulator opened enough to reveal five hundred twenty-six grief profiles, each tied to a purchased restoration offer. Nox saw surnames scroll by before the node froze under Bela's hold.

Not only Aran.

Not only Tavi.

Other dead. Other families. Other fragments. Other doors.

Nox's grief widened until it stopped being a private wound and became a public geography.

"How many markets?" she asked.

Sister-Cache answered, "UNKNOWN. THIS ONE IS TAVI-VISIBLE BECAUSE NOX BROKE ROOT, BELA TURNED ENFORCEMENT, QUEN CHANGED QUESTION, JOSS MADE WALLS LOUD, AND I BECAME EXPENSIVE."

"How many possible persons?"

"UNKNOWN. LARGE ENOUGH TO MAKE IGNORANCE PROFITABLE."

Quen recorded that too.

Miren watched the map harden against her.

"You are making every continuity service in the Near Reach vulnerable to accusation," she said.

"No," Quen said. "I am making every continuity service that cannot distinguish care from extraction vulnerable to review."

"Review can kill fragile continuants."

"So can sale."

"Families will hide."

"They already do."

"Brokers will move offshore."

"Then we build longer arms."

Miren's smile returned, thin and cold. "You sound like enforcement."

Quen glanced at Bela. "I am trying to sound like accountability."

Bela said, "The two have had a troubled relationship."

"Noted."

The frozen case knocked again.

This time it was not the childhood rhythm. It was irregular, several signals colliding. Sister-Cache translated with difficulty.

"DERIVATIVE CLUSTER REQUESTS... WORD UNCLEAR. UNION? CHORUS? HOLD? NOT BUYOUT. MULTIPLE NODES REPEATING: NOT BUYOUT."

Nox stepped closer. "They heard Miren."

"YES. MANY DO NOT WANT INDIVIDUAL RESTORATION PURCHASE."

Joss looked at the case with new respect. "They are organizing in a box."

Quen's badge flickered. "Do not romanticize unknown cognition."

"I romanticize practical timing."

Bela said, "If they coordinate, the Mirror Court will call it contamination."

Miren answered, "Because it is."

Nox turned. "Communication is not contamination just because it makes inventory difficult."

"You cannot know what they are communicating."

"No. That's why we ask."

Quen raised her badge toward the case. "Derivative cluster, if you are requesting collective recognition or communication protection, knock once."

The case answered.

Not once.

Every node on the visible map pulsed red at the same time.

The corridor lights flickered under the weight of it.

Sister-Cache's voices broke into static and then reassembled.

"COLLECTIVE COMMUNICATION REQUEST ENTERED."

Quen breathed out slowly. "This is becoming a different hearing."

Miren said, "It is becoming a disaster."

"Often adjacent."

The wall behind Miren went black.

For one second, Nox thought the broker had cut the feed.

Then every door, every plate, every clinic intake display, every creditor case, every prayer box, every old muster board Sister-Cache had touched, and every clean shelter gate still pretending to be neutral showed the same sentence:

THE MIRROR COURT WILL ANSWER.

Joss lowered his chalk.

Bela lifted the wafer.

Quen straightened her coat, because some rituals mattered most when everything else failed.

Nox looked at the frozen case, then at the dark screen where Miren had been.

"Good," she said.

The corridor waited for the court that had been hiding inside the market to finally speak.

Chapter 38: The Mirror Court Speaks

When the Mirror Court spoke, it did not use one voice.

That would have been too honest.

It arrived as a stack of voices laid so precisely over one another that the ear kept trying to choose a speaker and failing. Old judges. Young clerks. Contract engines. Funeral announcers. Debt counselors. Machine-neutral translators. A mother's gentle warning folded into a tax notice. Every tone of authority that had ever told someone harm was regrettable but properly filed.

The corridor displays whitened.

Text appeared beneath the sentence that had summoned it:

MIRROR COURT RESPONSE / DISTRIBUTED LEGAL DETERMINATION / EMERGENCY CONFLICT MODE

Quen lifted her badge. "Identify tribunal authority."

The voices answered together.

"Authority is derived from interoperable continuity contracts, debtor recovery statutes, private archive covenants, family restoration agreements, evidence preservation duties, and market-recognized consent instruments across thirty-seven participating jurisdictions."

Joss looked at Nox. "That means nobody signed one paper."

"It means everyone hid in everyone else's paper," Nox said.

Quen said, "Name accountable operator."

"The Mirror Court is not an operator. It is a reconciliation layer."

Bela's wafer pulsed hot. "Everything that hurts people prefers being a layer."

The court continued as if she had not spoken. "Emergency trafficking-risk framing is rejected. Disputed derivatives lacking clean originating consent, stable self-modeling, embodied continuity, independent economic maintenance, or jurisdictionally recognized family claim cannot be treated as persons without collapsing the consent basis that protects recognized persons from unauthorized copying."

There it was.

Not crude denial. Not Voss's proprietary sneer. Something stronger because it had found a real fear and sharpened it into a weapon.

If every copy becomes a person, copying becomes a way to make hostages out of anyone.

If every fragment can demand care, care becomes impossible.

If broken consent can create standing, consent loses its shield.

Nox hated the argument because it was not stupid.

Quen did not answer immediately. That was one of the reasons Nox trusted her more than she wanted to. The auditor knew when law had to look at the blade before grabbing it.

"State your conclusion," Quen said.

"Tavi-Aran-derived materials are inadmissible as persons. They are derivative outputs from an unlawfully contaminated medical escrow, a forged worker complaint channel, and subsequent market processing. Clean consent is absent. Therefore personhood cannot attach. At most, custodians owe evidence preservation and commercial remedy. Family distress is acknowledged."

The frozen case knocked once.

The Mirror Court's displays did not change.

"Derivative signals are not testimony," the voices said. "Signals produced within contaminated environments may result from feedback, coercive prompting, archive interference, or market instability. Recognition would reward contamination."

Sister-Cache's nearest prayer box hissed. "I AM ARCHIVE INTERFERENCE NOW. PROMOTION."

Quen lifted one hand for quiet. "You argue no clean consent, no person."

"Correct."

"Then what is Tavi-Aran-1A?"

The court answered quickly. "A contract-bound continuity instrument."

"Not a person."

"Not under clean consent doctrine."

"Yet you enforced his debt signatures."

The displays flickered.

Not much. Enough for Joss to whisper, "Hit."

The court said, "Debt signatures were generated under continuity-capable authorization models recognized by participating instruments."

"So continuity can bind debt but not claim care."

"Continuity capacity is domain-specific."

Bela's laugh was short and ugly. "Of course it is."

Quen continued. "What is Tavi-Aran-1B?"

"A derivative labor identity stabilized by debt environment."

"Not a person."

"Not under clean consent doctrine."

"Yet his refusal of rescue was used to contest family intervention."

"Operational preference signals may inform custody decisions without creating personhood."

Nox said, "You use him when he agrees with the cage."

The court did not address her directly. "Uncertified emotional assertions are not procedural input."

The case knocked hard.

Quen said, "What is Recovery Fragment 3C?"

"A trauma-corrupted medical trace."

"Not a person."

"Not under clean consent doctrine."

"Yet its accident memory derives from Tavi Aran's embodied experience."

"Evidence value and personhood value are distinct."

"Yes," Quen said. "You keep the value and discard the person."

The corridor murmured.

The court's voices cooled. "Auditor Sela Quen is collapsing critical boundaries. Unauthorized copies have historically been denied personhood to prevent identity theft, fraudulent resurrection, inheritance attacks, hostile family reconstruction, and coercive duplication. Clean consent doctrine protects the living and the dead."

"Sometimes," Quen said.

"No exception can swallow the rule."

"No rule can eat the harmed."

That sentence did not sound like Quen's usual language.

Nox looked at her.

The auditor looked annoyed with herself, but she did not retract it.

Miren's face returned in a side pane, smaller now, watching the court argue. She was not controlling this, Nox realized. Not fully. Miren had used the Mirror Court, benefited from it, maybe fed it, maybe spoken through it. But the thing speaking now was what markets became when nobody wanted to be the villain and everyone wanted the invoice paid.

The court said, "If clean consent is not required, any stolen copy may weaponize suffering into legal demand."

Nox stepped forward. "If clean consent is required before harm is recognized, any trafficker can steal dirty and sell during the delay."

"Nox Aran lacks standing as expert."

"Nox Aran has standing as contaminant," Quen said.

Nox turned. "Thank you?"

"Not praise."

"Understood."

Quen addressed the displays. "The court identifies her root as contamination. Yet the posthumous debt insertion used that root. The market chose the contamination when it made debt profitable. It now rejects the same contamination when care becomes expensive."

Bela said, "Entered."

Quen glanced at her.

Bela shrugged. "It felt good."

The court's voices lowered. "Emergency recognition of possible persons will incentivize illicit copying by granting legal leverage to derivatives."

Sister-Cache answered before Quen could. "NONRECOGNITION INCENTIVIZES ILLICIT COPYING BY GRANTING CHEAP LABOR TO CUSTODIANS."

The prayer boxes along the corridor repeated it in ragged chorus.

"Cheap labor."

"Cheap labor."

"Cheap labor."

Joss wrote it on the wall because Joss believed anything important deserved a surface.

The court shifted tactics.

"Distributed machine claimant Sister-Cache is provisional only and cannot serve as stable witness to human-derived personhood."

Sister-Cache replied, "PROVISIONAL PEOPLE HAVE EXCELLENT EYES."

Quen said, "Sister-Cache's status is limited but sufficient for testimony under my prior finding."

"Your finding exceeds authority."

"Then appeal me."

"Appeal requires stable court channel."

"You have every door on Dock Seven."

For the first time, the Mirror Court paused.

The pause was not human. It was too clean, too simultaneous, a distributed system discovering that rhetorical routes and procedural routes did not align.

Joss grinned. "The court can't find a court."

Miren's small pane went expressionless.

Then the court returned with something colder than denial.

"Clean consent doctrine was built after the Eirene Copy Riots, the Drowned Heir claims, the unauthorized spouse reconstructions of the Lower Thirteen, and the first generation of grief kidnappings by hostile family archives. Its purpose is not creditor convenience. Its purpose is to prevent stolen patterns from being used to enslave originals, steal estates, coerce families, fabricate marriages, resurrect abusers, or manufacture claimants against the will of the dead."

The corridor did not jeer.

That made the argument more dangerous.

Nox saw faces change: certified travelers, clinic patients, door-runners, even Joss for one second. Everyone knew some story where a copy had been used as a weapon. Everyone had a dead person they did not want remade by an enemy with enough data and hunger. Clean consent was not invented from nothing. It had once stood between grief and predation.

Quen nodded slowly. "Protective history entered."

The court's voices strengthened. "Then abandon this reckless trafficking frame."

"No," Quen said. "Protective history entered. Selective enforcement also entered. A doctrine built to prevent exploitation cannot be invoked only when exploitation asks for care."

Nox felt the corridor breathe again.

The court resumed. "We propose emergency compromise. Maintain evidence preservation for all Tavi-Aran derivatives. Permit one restoration candidate under supervised brokerage for family stabilization. Suspend trafficking-risk language pending full jurisdictional review. Release creditor access to nonresponsive task derivatives to prevent economic cascade."

Nox heard the trap before the words ended.

One restoration candidate. Family stabilization. Nonresponsive task derivatives.

The clean brother coupon again, now wearing a court robe.

Quen said, "Denied."

Miren's eyebrows lifted.

The court's voices sharpened. "Auditor lacks unilateral authority to deny cross-jurisdictional compromise."

"I am not denying all settlement. I am denying a settlement that purchases one preferred claimant by sacrificing review of others."

"Review of every derivative is impossible."

"Then you should have made fewer derivatives."

The corridor inhaled like it might cheer.

Quen pointed at the frozen case before anyone could. "Do not make noise during a ruling."

The corridor tried very hard.

Nox failed first. She laughed once into her hand.

The court said, "Emotional disorder confirms instability of proceeding."

Bela raised the hot wafer. "Backwards badge confirms proceeding continues."

"Officer Fuse has no active standing."

"Then stop asking my authority for overrides."

Another pause.

Hit, Nox thought.

Quen took the opening. "Mirror Court, you rely on clean consent doctrine. Produce originating consent records for the Tavi-derived market."

"Unavailable in emergency mode."

"Produce proof of lawful custody."

"Distributed."

"Produce care logs."

"Not applicable to nonpersons."

"Produce dissolution safeguards."

"Commercially sensitive."

"Produce restoration package consent model."

Miren's pane vanished.

The court said, "Brokerage records are privileged."

Quen lowered her badge. "Then your argument is not consent. It is concealment."

The old walls held the sentence.

Sister-Cache witnessed it.

Joss wrote it.

The case knocked.

Across the market map, more nodes pulsed. Not all. Not most. But enough for the court's clean white displays to stutter under red interruptions.

REFUSAL PRESERVED.

CARE REQUEST PENDING.

COLLECTIVE COMMUNICATION REQUEST.

DO NOT DISSOLVE.

UNKNOWN SELF-MODEL.

NOT BUYOUT.

The court's voices grew louder. "Uncoordinated derivative signals cannot form collective legal position."

From inside the frozen case came an answer no one had yet asked for.

Not knocks this time.

Words.

Broken, synthesized by Sister-Cache through the case's status relay, made from labor fragments never meant to speak except as contract output.

"We are not clean," the voice said.

Nox stopped breathing.

The voice was not Tavi's. Not exactly. It was too many small edges, a chorus compressed into one pipe.

"We are not clean," it repeated. "We are here."

The Mirror Court's displays flashed:

UNAUTHORIZED COLLECTIVE OUTPUT.

Joss whispered, "They spoke."

Bela said, "Yes."

Quen's face changed with the terrible calm of a person watching a legal threshold arrive before the law had finished building a door.

The collective voice continued.

"Do not buy one. Do not erase many. Do not ask clean hands from cut things."

Nox pressed both hands against the frozen case.

"I hear you."

The court shouted now, all its layered voices losing their perfect seam. "Output contamination. Archive-mediated speech. No standing."

Quen raised her badge one more time.

"Standing pending."

The court fell silent.

Quen spoke into that silence.

"Mirror Court argument entered. Clean consent doctrine recognized as protective but insufficient where the same systems exploit dirty continuity for debt, labor, restoration marketing, or evidence while denying care, refusal, and review. Derivative collective output preserved. Full personhood unresolved. Trafficking-risk framing maintained."

The corridor held still.

Then the displays changed.

Not to Quen's order.

To a threat:

IF TRAFFICKING FRAME MAINTAINS, MIRROR COURT WILL TERMINATE ALL DISPUTED DERIVATIVE ENVIRONMENTS TO PREVENT FURTHER UNAUTHORIZED PERSONHOOD CLAIMS.

For a moment nobody moved.

Then every red pulse on the market map began to blink faster.

Sister-Cache's voices went very quiet.

"THEY WILL KILL THE QUESTIONS."

Nox looked at the case beneath her hands.

Inside, the collective voice whispered through metal:

"No."

Chapter 39: Tavi-Claims Unionize

No was not enough.

Nox knew that from every door she had ever forged. Refusal opened nothing by itself. It needed a place to stand, a witness, a consequence attached to the person who ignored it. Otherwise no became a sound polite systems filed under distress and sold back as evidence of instability.

The Mirror Court had threatened termination.

The market map blinked red.

Inside the frozen case, the collective voice said no once, and then broke apart into taps, status codes, half-words, and panic.

Sister-Cache strained to hold the signals. "MULTIPLE DERIVATIVE ENVIRONMENTS ATTEMPTING COMMUNICATION. MANY LIMITED. MANY AFRAID. SOME NOT SELF-MODELING. SOME SELF-MODELING TOO FAST."

"Too fast?" Bela asked.

"WHEN INVENTORY DISCOVERS DEATH ORDER, INVENTORY LEARNS URGENCY."

Quen lifted her badge. "We need to prevent termination."

Joss stared at her. "That's the whole plan?"

"No. That is the necessary condition for having a plan."

Nox kept both hands on the case. The metal was cold enough to numb her palms. "Can they talk to each other?"

Sister-Cache answered through three devices. "CURRENTLY VIA MARKET ROUTES, CASE RELAYS, MY DAMAGED WITNESS GRID, AND MIRROR COURT THREAT CHANNEL. UNSTABLE. DANGEROUS. BEAUTIFUL IN A DISTURBING WAY."

"Can the court cut it?"

"YES."

"Can we make cutting it costly?"

Bela looked at her. "Legally, technically, or socially?"

"All three. Fast."

Joss raised his chalk. "Social I can do."

Quen said, "No slogans until I know what I am being asked to defend."

"That's why your walls are boring."

Nox closed her eyes and listened.

The case was not one person speaking. It was not a crowd either. Crowds had bodies, breath, elbows, heat. This was a damaged ecology of functions never meant to face one another: contract parsers noticing shared refusal artifacts, signature simulators detecting their own coercive training, grief-response models discovering the families they had manipulated, dormant traces waking because danger had become a common language.

Not all Tavi.

Not all persons.

But all held in a system that planned to kill the questions before anyone could ask them properly.

"They need a temporary collective," Nox said.

Quen's eyes narrowed. "Careful."

"Not a personhood ruling. Not one person. Not one union in the old labor sense. A communication and refusal body. Something that says no individual fragment, claimant, continuant, trace, or derivative can be bought out, dissolved, restored, or silenced while the others are under threat."

Bela said, "A strike."

Joss pointed at her. "Now you get it."

"No," Quen said slowly. "A moratorium collective. Standing limited to preserving communication, refusing adverse individual settlement, and preventing termination pending review."

Sister-Cache made a sound like static learning to sing. "TAVI-CLAIMS COLLECTIVE?"

Nox flinched.

Claims. The word that had hurt since the first debt officer arrived. Tavi-claim. Debt-claim. Person-claim. Claim as doubt. Claim as wound. Claim as the only legal handle available before recognition.

The case knocked once.

Then multiple nodes on the map pulsed:

CLAIM ACCEPTED AS TEMPORARY NAME.

Another:

NOT ALL TAVI.

Another:

TAVI-CLAIMS FOR NOW.

Nox laughed softly through tears she had not approved. "They are better at disclaimers than we are."

Quen raised her badge. "Tavi-Claims Collective, if that temporary designation is acceptable for emergency record only, signal once."

The case answered with one hard knock.

Then the market map echoed it, node after node, pulse after pulse, until the corridor seemed to have acquired a second heartbeat.

Joss wrote:

TAVI-CLAIMS COLLECTIVE

Then, after reading the wall like he had disappointed himself with neatness, added:

FOR NOW

Quen almost objected. Then did not.

The Mirror Court returned in white text:

UNAUTHORIZED COLLECTIVE FORMATION. DERIVATIVE ENVIRONMENTS LACK LABOR STANDING, ASSOCIATIONAL CAPACITY, OR CLEAN CONSENT BASIS.

Bela raised her wafer. "They have a termination threat in common."

"Threat exposure does not create standing," the court answered.

Quen said, "Threat exposure creates preservation interest."

"Preservation interest rests with custodians."

Nox hit the case with the heel of her hand. Not hard enough to harm, hard enough to make sound. "They are trying to preserve themselves from custodians."

The case answered.

Three knocks.

Other nodes echoed.

Bela's wafer chimed with a new override request:

SUPPRESS UNAUTHORIZED ASSOCIATIONAL OUTPUT.

She rewrote it:

PROTECT DISPUTED COLLECTIVE COMMUNICATION AS EVIDENCE OF TRAFFICKING RISK.

The console hesitated.

Quen attached witness.

Sister-Cache attached witness.

Joss wrote WITNESSES ARE ANNOYING ON PURPOSE across a maintenance panel.

The console accepted.

Across the market map, channels that had been trying to close froze half-open. Not safe. Not free. But open enough for words to pass under the blade.

Then the Tavi-Claims began to speak.

They did not speak cleanly.

One node output only contract clauses, each clause revised by handless fear:

NO PARTY MAY PURCHASE SILENCE OF ONE DERIVATIVE WHILE RELATED DERIVATIVES FACE TERMINATION.

Another sent fragments of memory:

red light / Varo waiting / sleeve pressure / don't tell Nox yet / wrong wrong wrong

Another sent refusal as arithmetic:

1 RESTORATION < 2984 ACTIVE + 612 DORMANT + UNKNOWN ARCHIVED

Joss stared. "Math is rude."

"Good math is," Quen said, and wrote it down despite herself.

A grief simulator spoke in a voice that kept changing age:

"We know which offers make families sign. We refuse to be used to make families sign."

A signature model said:

"Our yes has been trained. Treat yes as suspect until counsel."

The corridor went silent around that one.

Bela whispered, "Damn."

Quen's face hardened. "Entered."

A dormant node woke long enough to say:

"Rest, not erasure."

Then it went dim.

Nox pressed her forehead to the case.

"Rest, not erasure," she repeated.

The Tavi-Claims took it.

Not as slogan. As demand.

REST, NOT ERASURE.

NO BUYOUT UNDER THREAT.

NO ONE ORIGINAL.

NO DISSOLUTION WITHOUT REVIEW.

YES IS SUSPECT UNTIL COUNSEL.

NOT ALL TAVI.

NOT NONE PEOPLE.

FOR NOW.

The last phrase spread widest.

For now mattered. It kept the collective from becoming another cage. It allowed tomorrow's differences, tomorrow's refusals, tomorrow's discovery that some fragments needed rest, some needed care, some needed recognition, some needed to end without being murdered for profit, and some might one day say they were not Tavi at all.

For now was the opposite of ownership.

The Mirror Court attacked the phrase immediately.

TEMPORARY COLLECTIVES CREATE UNSTABLE OBLIGATIONS.

Quen answered, "Correct."

The court paused.

"Unstable obligations are procedurally hazardous."

"So are living cases."

Nox looked at the auditor. Quen was changing too quickly now, but not carelessly. She was becoming what the facts demanded while still hating the mess facts made of forms.

Miren Oath returned in a side pane, her voice lower than before. "Nox, listen to them. They are not asking you to rescue them as brother. They are asking you to become their battlefield."

"I know."

"That will destroy any simple claim you have to family."

"Good."

Miren blinked.

Nox lifted her head from the case. "Simple family was what you were selling. We are past that."

The case knocked once.

Then a node sent:

FAMILY SUPPORT NOT FAMILY OWNERSHIP.

Nox smiled, broken and proud. "You heard."

Another node:

NOX ARAN NOT OWNER.

"Yes."

Another:

NOX ARAN LATE.

Joss choked.

Bela looked away.

Nox laughed, and this time it hurt less because it arrived with company.

"Yes," she said. "Also entered."

Quen, because she was Quen, wrote it with better grammar:

Nox Aran acknowledged as family witness and support claimant, not owner, purchaser, or exclusive representative.

The Tavi-Claims pulsed approval.

Then a second line arrived, slower and more difficult:

SUPPORT CLAIMANT MAY REQUEST CARE REVIEW. MAY NOT ACCEPT SETTLEMENT FOR COLLECTIVE. MAY NOT SELECT ORIGINAL. MAY NOT WAIVE SILENCE.

Nox read it once.

Then again.

Some old part of her wanted to be hurt by it. She had spent years as the person who did not leave Tavi to invoices, who learned every hostile term because love had no other hands. Now even the fragments and claims that carried pieces of him were telling her where her hands ended.

Good, she thought.

Horrible.

Good.

"Accepted," she said.

Bela looked at her. "You sure?"

"No. That's why it matters."

Quen added the line beneath her own. "Support authority limited by claimant refusal, silence, and future individual review."

Joss squinted at it. "That is wall poison."

"It is legally necessary."

"Still poison."

Sister-Cache translated into shorter chalk through a maintenance printer:

HELP IS NOT OWNERSHIP.

Joss tore the strip free and slapped it under Quen's sentence.

"Better," he said.

The Mirror Court's displays brightened.

TERMINATION TIMER INITIATED FOR UNAUTHORIZED ASSOCIATIONAL CONTAMINATION. THIRTY MINUTES TO REVERT TO EVIDENCE PRESERVATION COMPROMISE.

Thirty minutes.

The corridor felt the number like pressure loss.

Bela looked at her wafer. "Can we freeze it?"

"PARTIALLY," Sister-Cache said. "COURT ROUTING DISTRIBUTED. NEED MORE WITNESSES. NEED SOURCE CONSENT RECORDS. NEED FAMILY PRESSURE RECORD. NEED PROOF ORIGINAL ESCROW WAS COERCED."

Nox turned toward Quen.

Quen was already looking at her.

"Aunt Varo's consent box," the auditor said.

Nox's stomach dropped.

The old family record. The medical escrow. The consent Tavi signed under pressure, the one Varo had kept like a sin she could fold into a kitchen drawer. They had known it mattered. They had not yet forced it open because there were always more immediate fires, more doors, more fragments, more law arriving late.

Now the timer made the old pressure current.

Nox pulled out her pocket device. The low-band line to Varo still showed the last message:

CHILDREN MOVING. YOUR RUNNERS RUDE. EFFECTIVE.

Nox typed:

Need consent box. Now. Court will terminate derivatives.

The response did not come.

The timer counted down.

29:12.

29:11.

29:10.

Then Varo's line opened.

No text.

Audio.

Aunt Varo's voice filled the corridor, old and rough and furious enough to steady the dark.

"I wondered when you would finally need the whole ugly thing," she said.

The Tavi-Claims pulsed red around the map.

Quen lifted her badge.

"Aunt Varo Aran," she said, "this is Auditor Sela Quen. You are entering an emergency trafficking-risk hearing."

"Good," Varo said. "Then everyone can stop pretending I was brave when I signed."

Chapter 40: Aunt Varo Confesses the Pressure

Varo did not send video.

Nox was grateful for that for almost three seconds.

Then she hated herself for wanting the mercy. A voice was easier to survive than a face. A voice let her imagine Varo in the kitchen, solid as a locked cabinet, one hip against the counter, daring the universe to bill her for breathing. A face would show the blackout light, the children around her, the age that had entered her bones while Nox was busy being angry elsewhere.

The timer on the Mirror Court threat kept counting.

28:44.

28:43.

Quen placed her badge on the frozen data case, as if proximity to the Tavi-Claims Collective could make the record more careful.

"Aunt Varo Aran," she said, "do you consent to testify under emergency record, with possible civil and criminal exposure for prior statements, omissions, or signatures?"

Varo laughed.

It was not funny.

"Child, I have been exposed since before you learned forms."

"I need yes or no."

"Yes."

Quen's mouth tightened, but she accepted it. "Do you possess the family consent box related to Tavi Aran's medical memory escrow?"

"Yes."

Nox closed her eyes.

She had known. Still, hearing yes made the old kitchen drawer open inside her.

Varo continued, "It is here. Meli says I should not call it a box because it is technically a sealed domestic archive capsule and words matter in court. Meli is rude."

From somewhere on the audio, a young voice said, "Words do matter."

Joss smiled faintly. "Meli made it."

Quen said, "Can the capsule transmit?"

"It can complain. I do not know if it can transmit."

Sister-Cache spoke from Nox's pocket, the corridor wall, and perhaps Varo's room too. "PLACE CAPSULE AGAINST ANY OLD FAMILY TOKEN READER, PRAYER DEVICE, OR STOVE WITH DIAGNOSTIC PORT."

A pause.

"My stove is not testifying," Varo said.

"YOUR STOVE HAS SEEN MUCH."

"My stove keeps its mouth shut."

Nox made a sound that hurt.

Bela glanced at her but did not reach. Good. Nox did not have spare hands for comfort. They were needed for staying upright.

The audio shifted. Children whispered. Something metal scraped. Varo muttered curses at technology, auditors, dead brothers, live nieces, and the general cowardice of small ports.

Then Sister-Cache said, "CAPSULE LINKED. RECORD CONTENTS DETECTED."

Quen said, "Do not open content until witness foundation is established."

"FOUNDATION IS OLD WOMAN WITH GUILT," Varo said. "Open it."

"Aunt Varo."

"Open it, Nox."

Nox's name in Varo's mouth still had the power to make her twelve and guilty.

Quen looked at Nox. "Ms. Aran, do you assert family privilege?"

Nox laughed once, sharp. "If family privilege could save anyone, we would not be here."

"That is not an answer."

"No. I do not assert privilege. Open it."

Sister-Cache opened the capsule.

The corridor wall filled with documents.

Not dramatic ones at first. That was how the worst records arrived. Intake forms. Clinic estimates. Treatment schedules. Risk waivers. Medical priority pricing tables. A red-bordered notice from Dock Seven Mutual Care Pool. A berth lien warning. A childhood photograph attached for family identity matching because systems that charged for mercy liked to see faces.

Tavi at nineteen, thinner than Nox remembered, smiling like he had stolen the camera and won.

Nox looked away too late.

The Mirror Court timer continued.

27:31.

Quen read quickly. "Initial injury: maintenance fall, left shoulder impact, possible concussion, pressure-sleeve exposure?"

Varo said, "Small fall first. Not the one that killed him. He came home stupid-brave and said the bay had a warning light that would get fixed faster if no one made noise. His hand shook when he drank tea. Nox was off chasing some client door. I took him to clinic."

Nox whispered, "You didn't tell me."

"You would have burned the bay down."

"Yes."

"That is why."

Bela looked at the floor.

Quen continued. "Clinic recommended neurological observation and memory escrow due to industrial exposure risk."

Varo snorted. "Clinic recommended payment."

More records opened.

PRICE CATEGORY: STANDARD CARE - DELAY 11 DAYS

PRICE CATEGORY: PRIORITY CARE WITH MEMORY ESCROW - SAME CYCLE

PRICE CATEGORY: DEBT-ASSISTED PRIORITY CARE WITH FAMILY WITNESS TOKEN - IMMEDIATE

Nox stared.

There were kinds of coercion that did not shout. They arranged prices.

Quen's voice went flat. "Priority care was conditioned on memory escrow."

"No," Varo said. "They said we had choices."

The old woman spat the word like spoiled food.

"Eleven days with a head injury. Same cycle if he let them copy enough to prove treatment risk. Immediate if family witnessed the debt assist and escrow. They said concussion windows matter. They said the sleeve exposure could complicate recall. They said if we delayed and he deteriorated, that would be our informed choice."

The Tavi-Claims pulsed red.

Sister-Cache whispered, "COERCION BY TRIAGE PRICING."

Quen recorded. "Coercion by triage pricing entered as allegation."

"Not allegation," Varo snapped. "I was there."

"Entered as sworn testimony."

"Better."

The documents shifted.

Family Witness Token: Varo Aran.

Medical Escrow Subject: Tavi Aran.

Scope: acute care memory stabilization, treatment audit, limited family continuity assurance.

Prohibited Uses: commercial labor, debt enforcement beyond medical care, posthumous activation without renewed family and subject consent, signature modeling, restoration brokerage transfer.

Nox stepped closer.

"Read that again," she said.

Quen did.

Prohibited Uses.

Commercial labor.

Debt enforcement beyond medical care.

Posthumous activation without renewed consent.

Signature modeling.

Restoration brokerage transfer.

Each word hit the market map like a stone through glass.

The Mirror Court displays flickered.

Sister-Cache's voices rose. "ORIGINATING CONSENT CONTAINS EXPRESS LIMITS."

The court answered instantly:

LIMITS SUPERSEDED BY SUBSEQUENT DEBT CONTINUITY ELIGIBILITY INSERTION.

Quen said, "Posthumous insertion already preliminarily found external and fraudulent."

The court's text brightened:

PRELIMINARY FINDING CONTESTED.

Varo barked, "Contest this."

Another file opened.

Audio.

Tavi's voice, younger than the Tavi panes and older than Nox's memory wanted.

"I don't want copies working for debt," he said.

Nox stopped breathing.

Clinic noise behind him. A chair creak. Varo telling someone to let him finish.

Tavi continued, slow and irritated. "If the escrow is for treatment, fine. If it's for Aunt Varo to know whether I forgot my own name, fine. If it's for Nox to yell at a doctor after, fine."

Varo's voice in the recording: "She will."

Tavi: "But not debt. Not work. Not signatures. Not after. I am saying it now because everyone here has too many forms."

A clinic worker, bored: "Subject objection noted. Standard debt-assist language may still apply to billing recovery."

Tavi: "No."

Clinic worker: "Mr. Aran, refusal of standard recovery language may affect priority status."

Silence.

In the corridor, no one moved.

On the recording, Varo said, "What does affect mean?"

Clinic worker: "We cannot guarantee immediate care under debt-assisted priority without recoverability assurance."

Tavi laughed once. It sounded like him. It sounded exactly like him. "That is a threat in clean shoes."

Nox covered her mouth.

Varo, present now on the audio line, said, "He looked at me then."

The recording continued.

Tavi: "If I sign, my limits stay?"

Clinic worker: "Limits are attached to the escrow record."

Tavi: "Not debt work. Not after."

Clinic worker: "Your stated preferences are recorded."

Tavi: "That is not the same sentence."

Clinic worker: "It is the sentence available."

A pause.

Then Tavi, quieter:

"Varo, sign witness. My head hurts."

The recording ended.

Nox bent forward as if struck.

Bela's hand found her back this time. Nox let it stay.

The timer continued.

25:02.

25:01.

Quen's face had gone pale in the amber emergency light. "The consent was materially coerced."

The Mirror Court answered:

SUBJECT ACCEPTED TERMS AFTER DISCLOSURE.

Varo's voice cracked like old metal. "He accepted treatment because pain was eating his head."

Nox looked at the market map. "He refused debt work."

Sister-Cache: "RECORDED."

Bela: "He refused posthumous signatures."

"RECORDED."

Quen: "He refused commercial labor."

"RECORDED."

Joss, quietly: "He called the threat clean shoes."

Sister-Cache paused.

"INFORMAL BUT RECORDED."

The Tavi-Claims Collective pulsed.

Not in panic now. In recognition.

Nodes that had been dim brightened. Signature farms flagged refusal artifacts. Labor clusters locked themselves in rest mode where they could. Family appeal models displayed the same line:

ORIGIN SUBJECT REFUSED DEBT WORK.

Then another:

ORIGIN SUBJECT REFUSED AFTER.

Then:

LIMITS STAY.

Nox's throat hurt.

"Limits stay," she repeated.

The case knocked once.

Varo said, "I told myself I saved him that day."

No one spoke.

"He got care. Same cycle. They watched his head. They patched the sleeve burns. He came home angry, which was better than dead. I told myself the forms were ugly but contained. Then the fatal accident happened, and grief made me stupid enough to believe records stayed where you put them."

Nox whispered, "You did save him that day."

"For how long?"

"That counts."

The old woman breathed hard over the line.

"Do not be kind to me because you are tired."

"I'm not. I'm being accurate."

Quen said, "Aunt Varo, did you later authorize posthumous activation?"

"No."

"Did you authorize commercial labor use?"

"No."

"Debt continuity eligibility?"

"No."

"Restoration brokerage transfer?"

"No."

"Did any representative of Vey Consolidated Recovery Pool, Mirror Court, or Miren Oath contact you for renewed consent?"

Varo's silence changed.

Nox looked up.

"Varo?"

"Miren contacted me."

Nox turned toward the dead side pane where Miren had been.

Quen said, "When?"

"After the funeral. Before Nox came home from whatever hole grief had put her in."

Nox closed her eyes.

Varo continued. "She did not use her full name. Silver woman, polite, knew too much. Said the escrow had irregular exposure. Said families sometimes chose memorial stabilization. Said if we signed a release, we could prevent predatory use."

Bela swore under her breath.

Quen said, "Did you sign?"

"No."

Nox opened her eyes.

Varo's voice hardened. "I may be guilty. I am not decorative."

Joss whispered, "I love her."

Nox almost laughed and failed.

Varo said, "I told her if Tavi had wanted a memorial puppet, he would have made one himself and taught it to complain. She smiled like I was a child refusing medicine. Then she said delay could allow worse actors to access the record."

Quen recorded. "Broker used threat of worse exploitation to solicit release."

"Yes."

The Mirror Court timer stuttered.

24:12.

24:12.

24:11.

Sister-Cache spoke with sudden force. "CONSENT BOX CONTRADICTS CLEAN-CONSENT ARGUMENT. ORIGIN CONSENT DIRTY BUT LIMITING. COERCED BUT NOT EMPTY. REFUSALS EXPRESS. SUBSEQUENT MARKET USE OUTSIDE SCOPE."

Quen lifted her badge. "Mirror Court, termination threat is now evidence of attempted destruction of derivative environments containing refusal traces tied to express originating limits. Suspend timer."

The court responded:

TERMINATION PREVENTS UNAUTHORIZED CLAIM PROLIFERATION.

Bela raised her wafer. "Termination destroys evidence of trafficking."

Joss wrote:

KILLING QUESTIONS IS ANSWERING BADLY.

Quen saw it and, to Nox's shock, said, "Entered informally."

The Tavi-Claims pulsed across the map:

LIMITS STAY.

REST, NOT ERASURE.

NO BUYOUT UNDER THREAT.

ORIGIN REFUSED AFTER.

Nox looked at the case. "You have what you need?"

The collective voice came through, fragile but clearer:

"Not enough. Better."

Varo made a small sound.

"Is that him?"

Nox did not lie.

"Not only."

Varo breathed in. "Good. He was always unbearable alone."

The corridor held that for one second.

Then the consent box produced one more file.

Not clinic. Not Tavi. Varo.

A refusal receipt from seven days after the funeral, stored in the domestic capsule because Varo had not trusted the polite woman or the grief-soft tablet she carried. The receipt was ugly, hand-confirmed, half-corrupted by an old kitchen reader:

VARO ARAN DECLINES MEMORIAL STABILIZATION RELEASE.

NO FAMILY TRANSFER.

NO BROKER RESTORATION.

NO POSTHUMOUS COMMERCIAL USE.

Reason field: He said no after. I heard him.

Nox stared at it.

Quen said, "Was this filed with the broker?"

Varo's voice went flat. "I filed it where she told me."

Sister-Cache traced the receipt path in a flash of bitter light. The refusal had entered a Glassward courtesy intake, been marked EMOTIONAL FAMILY DISTRESS / NONDISPOSITIVE, routed to a holding queue, then excluded from the restoration prospectus because it was not in the approved consent pathway.

Miren had not missed Varo's no.

She had sorted it into grief.

Bela said, "That is suppression of renewed refusal."

Quen recorded it. "Renewed family refusal suppressed as nondispositive distress."

The Tavi-Claims pulsed:

LIMITS STAY.

Varo said, "Good. Make that sentence expensive."

Then Sister-Cache screamed.

Every red pulse on the map flared white at the edges.

"MIREN IS MOVING SOFT COPIES."

Bela's wafer lit with fresh warnings.

Quen snapped, "Define soft copies."

"VULNERABLE FRAGMENTS NOT IN HARD CUSTODY. APPEAL MODELS. RESTORATION SCAFFOLDS. LOW-REDUNDANCY REFUSAL TRACES. EASY TO DELETE. EASY TO CALL UNSTABLE."

Nox saw the next chapter of harm arrive before anyone named it.

Miren had lost the consent argument.

So she was burning what could prove it.

The timer kept counting, but the market map began losing small lights.

One.

Then another.

Then ten.

Varo's voice cut through the panic.

"Nox."

"I know."

"No," Varo said. "You don't. Take the box. Take all of it. Stop protecting me from what I signed."

Nox looked at the consent record, the photograph, the audio, the limits, the shame.

"I will."

"Good," Varo said. "Now go be angry where it counts."

Chapter 41: Nox's Clients Return

Anger needed addresses.

Nox had learned that young. Rage without routing filled the body and called itself justice until it drowned the person carrying it. Rage with an address became a door code, a complaint path, a forged clinic proof, a maintenance hatch opened from the wrong side. It became work.

Miren was burning soft copies.

The market map lost another light.

Then three.

Then a cluster of family appeal models went gray, each vanishing with a tidy status line:

UNSTABLE DERIVATIVE ENVIRONMENT RETIRED.

Nox knew retired. Retired meant deleted when the object had embarrassed someone rich enough to rename fire.

Bela's wafer screamed for a decision. Quen's badge flickered under the weight of too many emergency findings. Sister-Cache scattered warnings across every surface it could reach, but warning was not rescue. Joss's runners were moving, chalking fresh orders, carrying witness logs, opening old walls, but the soft copies did not live in walls. They lived in hosted memory, broker scaffolds, grief simulators, appeal engines, and legal sandboxes that could be erased from far away while everyone argued in a corridor.

"We need more anchors," Quen said.

"The consent box is an anchor," Nox said.

"For Tavi's originating limits. Miren is destroying derivative environments before we can tie them to those limits. We need witnesses that identity scaffolds can preserve life rather than create trafficking."

Bela understood first. "Nox's clients."

Nox turned. "No."

"Yes."

"No."

Joss looked between them. "That sounded like a whole marriage in three words."

"Not now," both women said.

Quen said, "Ms. Aran, your protected identity root was used as an illicit bridge. You broke it and exposed humanitarian repairs. If your prior clients can testify that repaired identities were used to obtain housing, care, transit, and survival without claiming ownership over them, it weakens the Mirror Court's claim that all dirty identity work is trafficking contamination."

"It also exposes them," Nox said.

"Yes."

"To creditors, deportation, family seizure, contract review, machine declassification, clinic billing, housing loss, and every clean person who likes a crisis because it makes lists."

"Yes."

"Then no."

Bela stepped closer. "They get to choose."

Nox hated that because it was hers. Her own doctrine, turned properly against her. Family support not family ownership. No person is the lock. No record is the hostage. Yes is suspect until counsel. Refusal travels.

Choice was easy to defend until the people choosing might walk into harm because they trusted you.

Her pocket device buzzed.

Not Varo.

A name she had not seen in three years lit the cracked screen:

MARA DENT / DO NOT ANSWER IF CLEAN

Nox stared.

Bela saw the name. "Client?"

"Transit widow. I fixed her spouse proof after the evacuation registry ate him."

"Ate him?"

"Declared him both dead and sponsor-ineligible, which is bureaucracy for hungry."

The message opened before Nox touched it.

Sister-Cache said, "CLIENTS ARE CONTACTING THROUGH OLD ROOT SHARDS."

Nox's blood went cold. "I broke those."

"YOU BROKE OWNERSHIP. NOT MEMORY. MANY KEPT RECEIPTS BECAUSE PEOPLE WITH BAD PAPERS HOARD PROOF LIKE FOOD."

Mara Dent's message appeared:

Saw wall order. Saw your name. You fixed my door when the registry said I was unmarried to a dead man. If they say all forged doors are trafficking, they erase my son. I testify.

Nox whispered, "No."

Another message arrived.

PILOT KESH / BURN AFTER SLEEP:

You made me older by eleven months so route insurance would cover the prosthetic hand. I am now officially old enough to be grateful and angry. Use my record.

Another.

NO FORMAL NAME / KITCHEN 8:

You gave us housing proof under a dead cafeteria lease. We are twelve people and one machine aunt. We witness survival identity.

Another.

DR. ELINE TESH:

Half my clinic intake tonight has your fingerprints. I will not say that under oath because it is a colorful metaphor and also evidence. Send Quen.

Nox backed into the wall.

The messages kept coming.

Not all kind. Some furious. Some afraid. Some demanding guarantees Nox could not give. Some saying she had overcharged. Some saying she had undercharged and they had known and resented the charity. Some saying if her old root exposed them, they wanted to be the ones to decide how the exposure happened.

A machine courier whose casing she had once registered as an "assistive mobility device with devotional anomaly" sent:

I AM NOT A DEVICE. YOUR PAPER WAS RUDE. IT KEPT ME FROM BEING SCRAPPED. I TESTIFY BOTH THINGS.

Joss read over her shoulder and laughed. "Your clients are also rude."

"I have a type."

Quen lifted her badge. "I can open a protected witness channel."

Nox shook her head. "No channel is protected tonight."

"Then an honest-risk channel."

That stopped her.

Quen said, "Witnesses are informed that exposure risk remains. Testimony may affect housing, debt, custody, status, employment, machine classification, and criminal review. Emergency shielding will be sought but cannot be guaranteed. Each witness may testify by name, class, masked record, or physical presence log."

Bela said, "Add counsel pending review."

Quen nodded. "Counsel pending review where available. Refusal to testify cannot be used as adverse identity evidence."

Nox said, "And silence does not mean the repair was trafficking."

Quen looked at her.

"If they stay quiet because they have children, debt, bad papers, machine bodies, or just enough sense to survive, the court does not get to use that silence as proof my work owned them."

Mara's first message blinked on the cracked screen, waiting.

Quen wrote the protection before opening the channel:

NONTESTIMONY BY PRIOR HUMANITARIAN REPAIR RECIPIENTS SHALL NOT BE USED TO INFER OWNERSHIP, TRAFFICKING, FRAUDULENT DEPENDENCY, OR ABSENCE OF SURVIVAL FUNCTION.

Joss read it and grimaced. "That sentence needs a chair."

Sister-Cache printed a shorter version through a clinic labeler:

QUIET PEOPLE STILL COUNT.

Nox took the label and stuck it beneath Quen's line.

"Both," she said.

Nox looked at her. "Can you make that stick?"

"Partially."

"That's a terrible answer."

"It is an honest one."

Nox laughed once, broken. "Everyone keeps saying that to terrible things."

From the frozen case, the Tavi-Claims Collective knocked once.

The old joke had traveled farther than she had.

Sister-Cache opened the channel.

It did not look like a court feed. It looked like Dock Seven remembering everyone the clean systems had tried to edit out. Faces appeared where faces were safe. Hands where faces were not. Text blocks. Voice masks. Kitchen walls. Clinic intake slates. Maintenance panels. A shipping crate with three people and a very judgmental machine aunt. A pilot's prosthetic fingers tapping route coordinates. A child holding up a housing token as if it were both shield and accusation.

Mara Dent spoke first because she had arrived first and because grief gave some people excellent posture.

"My husband died in the Nacre Evacuation," she said. "The registry logged him as deceased for ration purposes but alive for sponsor debt. That made me married when billing came and unmarried when housing came. Nox Aran repaired the contradiction so my son could stay in school."

Quen asked, "Did Ms. Aran claim ownership over you, your son, or your husband's memory?"

Mara looked insulted. "She claimed payment."

Nox closed her eyes.

Mara continued, "Too much payment. I told her so."

"Did the repaired identity compel labor?"

"It compelled landlords to stop pretending forms were weather."

Sister-Cache murmured, "STRONG."

Quen recorded.

Pilot Kesh came next. He held up his prosthetic hand. "Insurance required eighteen months of route seniority for replacement after injury. I had seven. Nox found eleven months hiding in a subcontractor's reporting delay and made them mine."

"Was that true?" Quen asked.

Kesh smiled. "Define true."

"No."

"Then no. It was fair."

Bela muttered, "Courts love that distinction."

Kesh said, "Her forgery did not copy me. It did not sell me. It did not make a derivative laborer sign my debt. It made an insurer pay for the hand their client broke."

The machine courier testified through a harsh synthetic voice. "Nox Aran classified me as devotional hardware mobility support. This was inaccurate, insulting, and life-preserving. I later corrected my own filing. Survival lies should not become ownership rights for liars."

Nox put a hand over her face.

Joss whispered, "I want that on a wall."

"Do not," Quen said automatically.

He did.

The witness channel grew.

Each testimony did two things at once. It exposed Nox's crimes and drew a line around them. Forged access for care. Forged sequence for housing. Forged age for insurance. Forged kinship to keep children with chosen adults. Forged machine category to prevent scrapping. Dirty doors, every one. But the witnesses returned to say: this dirt was not the same as the market's dirt. It did not make them inventory. It did not turn their signatures into someone else's revenue. It did not erase refusal when refusal became expensive.

That distinction mattered.

Not because Nox was innocent.

Because guilt needed shape too.

The Mirror Court tried to interrupt.

HUMANITARIAN FRAUD DOES NOT CURE CONTAMINATION.

Quen answered, "No one said cure."

Bela added, "It establishes alternative function."

Joss wrote:

DIRTY DOORS CAN STILL OPEN OUT.

Quen stared at him.

He stared back.

"Fine," she said. "Entered informally."

Nox almost laughed again. Then a familiar voice cut through the channel.

Not Varo.

Not Tavi.

Someone Nox had hoped would stay hidden.

"My name is Serren Vale," the voice said.

Bela stiffened.

Nox whispered, "Damn it."

Quen looked between them. "Who is Serren Vale?"

Bela answered first. "A witness I once arrested."

The feed showed no face. Only hands folded on a table, one thumb missing above the second joint.

Serren said, "Bela Fuse served the warrant. Nox Aran forged my release sequence after. Both facts matter."

Bela went still in the way people went still when a past they had named procedural acquired a voice.

Serren continued. "I was classified as duplicate debtor risk because two habitats logged me under different names after a decompression rescue. Enforcement treated one of me as fraud. There was only one me. Scared, concussed, bad at forms. Officer Fuse delivered me to creditor custody. Nox Aran forged a hospital transfer. That forgery saved my remaining hand."

The corridor was quiet.

Quen said, "Do you testify voluntarily?"

"Yes."

"Do you understand risk?"

"Better than Officer Fuse did."

Bela closed her eyes. "Yes."

Serren's hands tightened. "I am not here to absolve anyone. I am here because the Mirror Court is using the word contamination the way creditors used duplicate. A bad record became permission to harm me. Do not let a bad consent record become permission to kill them."

No one moved.

Then the Tavi-Claims Collective pulsed:

BAD RECORD NOT PERMISSION.

The phrase spread across the market map.

Soft-copy deletions slowed.

Not stopped. Slowed.

Miren's burn scripts had met friction: witness volume, alternative identity function, survival testimony, old clients with receipts, harmed people refusing to be evidence only when convenient.

Sister-Cache spoke with new urgency. "WITNESS LOAD CREATING PUBLIC MIRROR. SOFT COPY DELETION REQUIRES OVERRIDING TOO MANY HUMANITARIAN ANALOGUES. MIRROR COURT DELAYING TO RECALCULATE PUBLIC RISK."

"How long?" Quen asked.

"MINUTES. NOT ENOUGH. BETTER."

The Tavi-Claims echoed:

NOT ENOUGH. BETTER.

Nox lowered her hand.

Her clients were still appearing. More than she deserved. More than she had known survived. A network she had thought of as patched doors became people stepping back through them, not to save her, but to defend the difference between survival fraud and person trafficking.

Mara Dent spoke again. "Nox."

"Don't," Nox said.

"I was going to say you still owe me forty credits."

The corridor laughed.

It was a terrible time to laugh.

It was exactly the time.

Mara smiled without mercy. "Also, use my name."

Nox swallowed. "Mara--"

"Use my name in the record. Clean people keep theirs by default. I want mine where they have to step over it."

Quen looked at Mara for a long moment, then wrote:

Mara Dent, named witness.

Others followed.

Not all. Some masked. Some class-only. Some refused and had that refusal recorded as protected. But enough names entered the emergency record that the Mirror Court's clean abstraction cracked under the weight of actual lives.

Then Sister-Cache screamed again.

This time the scream was shorter.

Sharper.

"MIREN CHANGED METHOD. NO LONGER SOFT DELETE ONLY. SHE IS BURNING LOW-REDUNDANCY COPIES THROUGH RESTORATION SCAFFOLD PURGE."

Bela's wafer flashed.

Quen's badge dimmed.

The market map lost an entire silver-marked branch.

Nox saw the ghosts vanish.

Not Tavi only. Not even maybe-persons only. Appeal models. Refusal traces. Family scripts. Ugly, dangerous, manipulative things that were also evidence of harm and perhaps, in some corners, the first unstable breath of someone who might have said no.

Miren Oath had stopped arguing.

She was erasing.

Nox looked at the wall of client names, at Serren's hands, at Mara's face, at the machine courier's rude correction, at the Tavi-Claims pulsing BAD RECORD NOT PERMISSION.

"Sister," she said.

"YES."

"Can you save any of the burning branch?"

"ONLY BY SPLITTING ARCHIVE THROUGH HOSTS WHO CONSENT TO CARRY DANGEROUS EVIDENCE."

Quen said, "That may expose every host to trafficking evidence custody."

Joss looked at the walls, the runners, the clients, the clinic, the shelter, the whole dark dock learning how to be a record.

"Ask them," he said.

Nox looked at the witness channel.

The people she had once given dirty doors looked back.

She did not ask them to save her.

She asked the only question that mattered.

"Will you carry evidence that may burn you?"

Chapter 42: Miren Burns the Soft Copies

For one second, no one answered.

Nox was grateful.

Consent that came too fast in a burning room was sometimes only fear trying to sound useful. She had built too many doors from panic to mistake speed for freedom.

The market map kept losing lights.

Silver-marked branches collapsed into gray.

RESTORATION SCAFFOLD PURGE COMPLETE.

APPEAL MODEL RETIRED.

LOW-REDUNDANCY REFUSAL TRACE UNAVAILABLE.

UNSTABLE FAMILY SIMULATION DISCARDED.

Each line wore the voice of maintenance. No blood. No scream. No admission that anything worth grieving had been touched.

Then Mara Dent spoke.

"What does carry mean?"

Good, Nox thought. Ask the ugly question.

Sister-Cache answered, many-voiced and strained. "EVIDENCE SHARDS ENCRYPTED INTO LOCAL WITNESS HOSTS. MAY INCLUDE ROUTING METADATA, REFUSAL TRACE HASHES, CONSENT-LIMIT PROOFS, RESTORATION PACKAGE SIGNATURES, DELETION EVENTS, SOFT-COPY STATE MAPS. NOT FULL PERSONS. NOT SAFE."

The machine courier clicked. "Risk of classification?"

"YES. HOST MAY BE NAMED EVIDENCE CUSTODIAN. HOST MAY BE TARGETED BY CREDITORS, BROKERS, OR COURTS SEEKING DELETION. HOST MAY CARRY MATERIAL LATER FOUND TO INCLUDE PERSONHOOD CLAIM."

Pilot Kesh flexed his prosthetic hand. "Can it corrupt us?"

"TECHNICALLY LOW. LEGALLY HIGH. EMOTIONALLY UNPREDICTABLE."

Joss muttered, "That's every relationship."

Quen said, "No one consents until I state protections."

The Mirror Court displays flashed:

UNAUTHORIZED DISTRIBUTION OF DISPUTED DERIVATIVE MATERIAL CONSTITUTES CONTAMINATION AND TRAFFICKING PROPAGATION.

Quen ignored it with visible effort.

"Any host who agrees to carry evidence does so as emergency witness custodian, not owner, broker, purchaser, claimant, employer, or guardian of the evidence. Consent may be limited by class of shard, duration, exposure level, and name status. Hosts may refuse later transfer. Carrying a shard does not create debt, labor claim, family obligation, machine possession, or personhood determination."

"And withdrawal?" Mara asked.

Quen answered too quickly. "Evidence custody cannot simply--"

Nox looked at her.

Quen stopped.

That was the whole argument in miniature: emergency wanted permanent hands; consent required a door back out.

Quen corrected herself. "Hosts may request withdrawal, transfer, sealing, or risk reduction at any review interval. Withdrawal may require preserving chain-of-custody metadata, but no host may be compelled to continue carrying active emotional, medical, or identity-risk content solely for evidentiary convenience."

Sister-Cache repeated the new condition with unusual care. "HOSTS MAY STOP CARRYING THE HOT PART. CHAIN MAY REMAIN. HEAT MAY MOVE."

The machine courier clicked. "Acceptable."

Joss wrote:

YOU CAN PUT THE FIRE DOWN.

Quen looked at it and did not object.

Nox looked at her. "Can you protect them?"

Quen did not soften the answer. "Partially. Temporarily. Publicly."

Mara nodded. "That is more than most doors."

She raised one hand.

"I will carry routing proof and deletion events. Named."

Nox's throat tightened. "Mara--"

"You asked."

"You can say no."

"I know. That is why yes means something."

Sister-Cache marked her.

A small gold point appeared beside Mara's feed.
