LEAVE WITHOUT CONFESSION / NO PENALTY

The No Furniture Theology group applauded until the chapel attendant threatened to charge them for acoustics.

At the clinic, the problem was kinder and therefore more dangerous.

The triage desk copied CARE NAME ACCEPTED correctly, then added:

DECLARED CONTINUITY RECOMMENDED FOR BETTER CARE MATCHING.

Dr. Tesh saw it and swore so loudly the pediatric wall changed colors.

"Better care matching is how you turn fear into intake," she said.

The junior triage nurse looked stricken. "We meant medication conflicts."

"Then ask medication conflicts."

The field changed:

CARE NAME ACCEPTED.

MEDICATION / SUBSTRATE / ACCESS CONFLICTS MAY BE DECLARED WITHOUT CONTINUITY DISCLOSURE.

REFUSAL TO DECLARE CONTINUITY SHALL NOT LOWER TRIAGE CATEGORY.

The nurse stared at the new language. "This is longer."

Tesh said, "So is harm."

The public wall wrote:

ASK THE NEED, NOT THE SOUL.

Nox kept that one.

At a cheap sleep-pod row, the vendor saw opportunity before ethics finished tying its shoes.

The pod board changed NO CONTINUITY MATCH WITHOUT REQUEST to:

CONTINUITY PRIVACY SURCHARGE: TWO CREDITS

Joss arrived first because door-runners treated sleep-pod rows as weather stations for exploitation.

"Privacy surcharge?" he said.

The vendor smiled with the smooth confidence of a person who had never been dragged publicly by a wall. "Privacy has costs."

Marn, the boy from the courier locker, stood behind Joss holding his parcel like a shield. "So does sleep."

Joss looked at Nox when she arrived. "Can I break the board?"

"No."

"Can the board experience consequences?"

"Yes."

They made the vendor post the cost basis. It showed no additional cost. The surcharge had been pure appetite.

The wall wrote:

NO FEE FOR NOT BEING READ.

Quen turned that into order: "A service may charge for actual additional privacy-preserving labor or infrastructure only if the same service is available without coercive disclosure, cost basis is public, and emergency shelter or medical need is not involved."

Joss groaned. "Your version has too many doors."

"My version survives lawyers."

"Fine."

The pod vendor removed the surcharge and raised blanket fees instead.

Bela pointed at him. "Retaliatory pricing."

The wall wrote:

DO NOT MOVE THE PUNISHMENT SIDEWAYS.

That one traveled immediately. Dock Seven understood sideways punishment. Longer routes, colder meals, worse bunks, missing cards, delayed repairs, a door that technically opened but only after rain.

By the time the bazaar ring pulsed with evening light, the minimal query had grown a companion label:

ASKING STANDARD:

ASK ONLY WHAT THE SERVICE NEEDS.

NAME THE USE.

PRESERVE REFUSAL.

NO SIDEWAYS PUNISHMENT.

SHOW EDITS.

HUMAN REVIEW MEANS A PERSON, NOT A SALES FUNNEL.

The machine courier requested an amendment.

"Human review excludes machines."

Quen looked pained. "Reviewer review?"

Nox shook her head. "Advocate review."

The wall changed:

ADVOCATE REVIEW MEANS A QUALIFIED PERSON, MACHINE, OR MIXED WITNESS ABLE TO CHALLENGE THE SERVICE.

The machine courier clicked. "Currently less species-ist."

"I'll take it," Quen said.

"DO NOT."

Quen, to her credit, did not.

By evening, the bazaar had split into factions with names ridiculous enough to become permanent.

The Proof Purists wore white tags and claimed civilization would collapse if doors stopped demanding legal continuity from anyone buying soup.

The Ask First League formed accidentally outside the tea stall after the owner discovered that people paid extra to enter a place whose door did not shout their past.

The No Furniture Theology group objected to doors asking any question that could make a person think about personhood before breakfast.

Varo joined that one for twenty minutes, insulted them all, and left after rewriting their sign to say NO FURNITURE CONFESSION.

Lio tested route practice twice and rang no bell.

1B kept kitchen audio muted through a grief warning and then unmuted it for the ordinary sound of Varo cursing a pump seal.

The family fork listened to home-sound for nine minutes, saved none of it, and asked whether a sound could be enough without becoming evidence.

"Today," Nox said, "yes."

Reader 7F did not wake.

1A sent no reply.

Both absences were recorded as protected conditions, not failures.

That mattered too.

Near dark, Nox stood in the central bazaar ring while the new field propagated across the old boards in uneven pulses. Some doors asked well. Some asked badly. Some asked because asking was fashionable and fashion always learned ethics late.

A chapel board changed CONFESS CONTINUITY to REQUEST WITNESS.

A clinic intake changed TRUE NAME REQUIRED to CARE NAME ACCEPTED.

A cheap sleep-pod vendor changed NO REFUNDS FOR FALSE IDENTITY to NO CONTINUITY MATCH WITHOUT REQUEST, then immediately raised prices by twelve percent.

"I hate this place," Bela said.

"You came back."

"I contain contradictions."

"We all do."

Bela glanced at the mirror doors lining the ring. They reflected too many versions of Nox: younger, harsher, cleverer, worse, impossible. For once, none of them looked like a verdict. They looked like doors that might have to ask before opening.

"Miren's people will try to use this," Bela said.

"Miren will try to use this from a supervised room with bad tea."

"People like Miren."

"Yes."

"The Compact will try to own it."

"Yes."

"Families will use it to pretend refusal means they were generous."

"Yes."

Bela looked at her. "That doesn't discourage you?"

Nox watched a market child press REFUSE SCAN on a sweet vendor's door just to see if it would work. The door opened anyway. The child shouted in triumph and bought nothing.

"It terrifies me," Nox said. "But yesterday the bazaar asked for proof and called the answer consent. Today some of it knows those are different crimes."

Sister-Cache, quiet for too long in Nox's inner line, spoke with archive softness.

"Correction," she said. "Those are different categories. Crime remains under review."

Nox laughed, and this time it had a little humor in it.

Above the ring, one last repair notice opened. It did not come from a household, court, debt window, or Compact office.

It came from Nox's own oldest door, the private repair address she had used before Bela, before Tavi's copy, before the Mirror Court had shown her that survival could be forged into a collar.

UNCLAIMED ACCESS POINT / NOX ARAN WORKSHOP

CURRENT SETTING: LOCKED TO ALL MIRROR CLAIMANTS

RECOMMENDED REVIEW: FINAL DOOR STATUS

Bela read it and went still.

Nox did not touch the notice.

Not yet.

The bazaar had learned to ask badly, then better, then badly again.

Now one door remained for her.

Chapter 60: Every Door a Mirror

Nox waited until the rain stopped.

That was not strategy. It was cowardice with good weather sense. Dock Seven after rain looked briefly honest: wires rinsed black, market tarps sagging under clean water, neon reflected in puddles instead of faces. The bazaar smelled of hot metal, old tea, and the electrical sourness of systems pretending they had not spent the day learning manners from a grieving aunt.

Her workshop door stood in a maintenance corridor behind the mirror arcade.

It was a bad door.

Nox had always liked that about it.

Good doors advertised legitimacy. Good doors shone, filed access records with clean timestamps, used polite intake language, and betrayed you in formats a court could quote. Nox's door had three different hinges, a scratched manual latch, a dead creditor seal under the paint, and a small dent where Bela had once kicked it open during an argument about whether love was a conflict of interest.

Bela stood beside her now, hands in her coat pockets.

"You can leave it locked," Bela said.

Nox looked at the access notice hovering above the latch.

UNCLAIMED ACCESS POINT / NOX ARAN WORKSHOP

CURRENT SETTING: LOCKED TO ALL MIRROR CLAIMANTS

RECOMMENDED REVIEW: FINAL DOOR STATUS

"That's the cowardice option," Nox said.

"Sometimes cowardice is a safety policy with better branding."

"You defending caution now?"

"No. I'm annoying you with accuracy."

Nox touched the edge of the notice. It opened without drama. Her old access structure was ugly, clever, and cruel in the places she had once mistaken for necessary. It had been built for clients who needed forged provenance, corrected birth chains, repaired work licenses, quiet exits from families and creditors and governments. It had also been built by a woman who believed she could help people cross thresholds if she controlled every hinge.

The Mirror Court had not invented ownership.

It had industrialized a desire already common in smaller rooms.

Nox read her old rules.

NO CONTINUITY CLAIMANTS WITHOUT PRIOR SPONSOR.

NO PARTIAL PERSONS WITHOUT PAYMENT ESCROW.

NO UNVERIFIED FORKS.

NO DEAD-NAME DISPUTES DURING ACTIVE REPAIR.

NO CLIENTS WHO CANNOT IDENTIFY ORIGINAL SOURCE.

Bela said nothing.

That was worse than accusation.

Nox laughed quietly. "I thought I was protecting myself."

"You were."

"And?"

"And sometimes the thing protecting you is also a blade facing outward."

Nox leaned her forehead against the door. The metal was cool. Behind it were benches scarred by work, old routing maps, memory-cleaning rigs, a kettle that belonged to neither of them because Bela had bought it and Nox had stolen custody, and three crates of illegal empathy buffers no one had ever successfully proven were illegal.

It had been a refuge.

It had been a business.

It had been a locked room where she decided who was damaged in acceptable ways.

Her inner line clicked.

Sister-Cache did not speak immediately. The archive had learned, too, that presence did not require filling every gap with archival fact.

At last she said, "Your old exclusion rules match seven Mirror Court intake assumptions."

"Only seven?"

"I excluded spelling."

Bela's mouth twitched.

Nox opened the rule editor.

The first deletion was easy because it was theatrical. She removed NO PARTIAL PERSONS WITHOUT PAYMENT ESCROW and replaced it with:

NO PERSONHOOD REPAIR SHALL BE CONDITIONED ON OWNERSHIP ESCROW.

The wall accepted it.

The second took longer.

NO UNVERIFIED FORKS.

Nox stared at the phrase until it stopped looking practical and started looking like a small locked box with breathing inside it.

"What is a verified fork?" Bela asked.

"One that made me comfortable."

"Terrible standard."

"Efficient."

"Still terrible."

Nox replaced it.

FORKS, MIRROR CLAIMANTS, LEGAL COPIES, PARTIALS, AND UNCLASSIFIED PERSONS MAY REQUEST REPAIR WITHOUT CERTIFYING ORIGINALITY.

The door hesitated. Its old software did not know whether unclassified persons were clients, risks, or weather.

Nox added:

CLASSIFICATION MAY BE REPAIRED DURING SERVICE. IT SHALL NOT BE REQUIRED BEFORE ENTRY.

The lock gave a tiny mechanical sigh.

That sound hurt.

It sounded relieved.

Lio arrived without ringing because route practice had taught them the last turn and had not told the house.

They stood at the corridor mouth, rain drying in their hair, wearing no name on their sleeve.

"I can leave," Lio said.

"Yes," Nox said.

The answer came fast enough that Lio looked surprised.

Nox made herself continue. "You can leave before speaking, after speaking, while I'm answering, after I annoy you, before I apologize, or because the hinge sound is bad."

"The hinge sound is bad."

"Known issue."

Lio came closer anyway.

They read the new rules. "This door is yours."

"Yes."

"Then if I come through it, am I asking you to fix me?"

"No."

"What am I asking?"

Nox looked at the workshop door, then at the reflected corridor behind them. In every mirror panel, the same scene fractured differently. One Nox looked guilty. One looked brave. One looked like someone who had arrived too late and still wanted credit for bringing tools.

"You are asking whether repair can happen near me without belonging to me," she said.

Lio considered that. "Can it?"

"Not reliably."

Bela looked at her.

Nox did not look away from Lio. "I'm going to fail at it. I will reach for control because I know how to make control sound useful. I will mistake worry for authority. I will probably use the word careful like a weapon at least once."

"Unattractive advertisement," Lio said.

"Accurate one."

"What happens when you do?"

Nox edited the next field.

CLIENTS MAY HALT, REDIRECT, OR EXIT REPAIR WITHOUT ADVERSE LABELING.

REPAIR WORKER CONCERN SHALL NOT CREATE CLAIM AUTHORITY.

ALL ADVICE MUST REMAIN DISTINGUISHABLE FROM ACCESS CONDITIONS.

Bela read the last line and made a sound in her throat.

"What?" Nox asked.

"I am remembering six fights."

"Only six?"

"I am being tender."

Lio touched the last clause. "Advice is not a lock."

"That is the hope."

"Hope is not policy."

Nox smiled despite herself and added:

ADVICE SHALL NOT BE IMPLEMENTED AS DEFAULT POLICY WITHOUT EXPLICIT CLIENT CONSENT.

The door accepted it.

1B came next.

They did ring. The old bell made a flat, ugly buzz that embarrassed the corridor.

"That bell is abusive," 1B said.

"Also known," Nox replied.

1B's expression was guarded, but less armored than before. They had muted the kitchen audio. A small icon near their wrist showed grief warning pending, not opened.

"I don't need repair," they said.

"Good."

"I might need documents that stop other people from deciding I need repair."

"Also good."

"Can this door make documents that say I am not available for redemptive family experiments?"

Varo's voice came through 1B's household channel, faint and immediate. "It had better."

1B flinched, then looked irritated at themselves for flinching.

"Kitchen audio was supposed to be muted," Nox said.

"Emergency aunt privilege," Varo said.

Bela pointed at the air. "No."

Nox added, with deep satisfaction:

HOUSEHOLD SUPPORT CHANNELS SHALL NOT OVERRIDE CLIENT MUTE SETTINGS EXCEPT UNDER CLIENT-DEFINED EMERGENCY CONDITIONS.

There was a long pause from Varo.

"Fine," she said. "But if someone is bleeding, I will become a legal problem."

"That is already your status," Bela said.

1B looked at the new clause. "I want a document."

"What should it say?"

"That resemblance is not appointment."

Nox felt the sentence settle into the room.

She wrote it as a header.

RESEMBLANCE IS NOT APPOINTMENT.

No face, memory, inherited habit, mirrored grief, legal continuity, or family recognition shall appoint a person to satisfy another person's unfinished relationship.

1B swallowed.

"Yes," they said.

The family fork arrived while Nox was printing the first copy.

They did not enter the corridor fully. They stayed where the mirror arcade light touched half their face and left the other half in ordinary shadow.

"I listened to the pump," they said.

Nox nodded.

"It was enough."

"For now?"

"For today."

"That counts."

The fork looked at the open workshop door. "Can a person come here if they don't want to become more complete?"

Nox's first answer was wrong, so she did not say it.

Every repair worker wanted completion. It made before-and-after stories easier to sell. It pleased courts, families, clinics, lovers, historians, and machines. Completion was clean. Completion let everyone close the file.

But the fork had not asked for clean.

"Yes," Nox said. "A person can come here to remain partial with better protections."

She added:

REPAIR MAY MEAN STABILIZING A CHOSEN PARTIAL STATE.

COMPLETION SHALL NOT BE ASSUMED AS THE DESIRED OUTCOME.

The family fork smiled then, barely. It was not Tavi's smile. That was what made it possible to see.

Sela arrived with two Compact observers, one local magistrate, and the expression of a woman who had found a wildfire filing a permit.

"This door is now being cited by three public systems," she said.

"It has been open for nine minutes."

"Dock Seven is efficient in the worst direction."

The magistrate, whose name Nox did not know and did not intend to learn under stress, tapped the access clauses. "You cannot operate a public doctrine from a private illegal workshop."

"It's not a public doctrine," Nox said. "It's a door."

"A door currently setting intake behavior across the bazaar."

"Then perhaps the bazaar was hungry for a better hinge."

Bela closed her eyes. "Nox."

Sela surprised them by raising one hand before the magistrate could speak. "The Compact is not here to seize the door."

"That's new," Bela said.

"The Compact is here to witness that the door cannot be treated as law."

Nox stared at her.

Sela continued, precise as a clean blade. "If treated as law, it will be captured. If treated as contraband, it will be romanticized. If treated as a working prototype, it can be copied, criticized, and improved without making Nox Aran its owner."

Nox did not trust her.

That did not make her wrong.

"Witness accepted," Nox said.

Sela looked at the door. "Add a noncanon clause."

Bela laughed once. "I hate that I agree."

Nox added:

THIS DOOR IS NOT LAW, DOCTRINE, FAMILY, COURT, OR PROOF OF PERSONHOOD.

IT IS A WORKING ACCESS PRACTICE.

COPYING REQUIRES LOCAL REVIEW, CLIENT REFUSAL PROTECTION, AND NO CLAIM OF ORIGINATING AUTHORITY.

The local magistrate read the clause twice, which Nox enjoyed more than was morally useful.

"A private door cannot disclaim public effect after causing public effect," the magistrate said.

Sela nodded. "Correct."

Nox looked at her. "You are agreeing with him?"

"Partly. The door has already influenced the bazaar. That creates responsibility. Not ownership. Responsibility."

Nox hated that distinction because she believed it.

Bela said, "So what does responsibility look like without making her the hinge?"

The question was too precise to be accidental.

Sela opened a blank field under the noncanon clause.

REVIEW PRACTICE / FIRST SEVEN USES

Nox said, "Absolutely not."

Sela did not blink. "Absolutely yes."

Lio looked interested. That was usually bad for Nox.

Sela continued. "If this door becomes a model, its first uses need review. Not client exposure. Practice review. What did the door ask? What did it not ask? Was refusal preserved? Did Nox Aran become gatekeeper by habit? Did advice become condition? Did speed become crown? Did anyone feel obligated to be grateful?"

Bela murmured, "Last one matters."

Nox stared at the door. "I do not want my clients turned into case law."

"Then do not make case law," Sela said. "Make practice notes under privacy masks."

Sister-Cache added, "I CAN HOLD NOTES BADLY."

"No," Nox and Sela said at once.

Sister-Cache sounded offended. "I WAS OFFERING HUMILITY."

Quen opened a smaller, meaner field:

PRACTICE NOTE SHALL NOT CONTAIN CLIENT NAME, FACE, ROUTE ORIGIN, FAMILY LINK, DEBT STATUS, MEDICAL STATUS, OR CONTINUITY CLAIM UNLESS THE CLIENT EXPLICITLY CHOOSES DISCLOSURE AFTER SERVICE.

PRACTICE NOTE SHALL RECORD DOOR BEHAVIOR, WORKER BEHAVIOR, REFUSAL PATH, EXIT FUNCTION, ADVICE/CONDITION SEPARATION, AND ANY REPORTED PRESSURE.

CLIENT MAY DELETE OR SEAL THEIR PRACTICE NOTE AFTER REVIEW UNLESS THE NOTE IS NEEDED TO PREVENT IMMEDIATE HARM TO OTHERS; STRUCTURAL HARM MAY BE PRESERVED WITHOUT CLIENT CONTENT.

Nox read it.

Then read it again.

"This is slow," she said.

Bela answered, "You keep saying that like speed did not just testify against all of us."

The magistrate looked uncomfortable. Good. He was standing inside a repair shop where illegal women and disputed persons were drafting better safeguards than his office had managed.

He cleared his throat. "Who reviews the practice notes?"

Nox said, "Not me."

Sela said, "Correct."

Joss arrived at the corridor mouth with tea nobody had asked for and a face that said he had been listening anyway. "Door-runners."

Dr. Tesh's voice came over a clinic relay. "One clinic reviewer."

The machine courier clicked from somewhere in the bazaar. "One machine witness where classification or host access is involved."

Varo said, "One person old enough to distrust everyone."

"You nominated yourself," Nox said.

"I am qualified."

Sister-Cache said, "ONE DISSENT SLOT."

Sela looked up. "Define."

"A REVIEWER WHOSE TASK IS TO SAY WHAT THE PRACTICE HIDES, EVEN IF THE PRACTICE IS HELPFUL."

Bela said, "Adverse reviewer."

Nox looked at her.

Bela looked back. "Everything useful needs someone it annoys on purpose."

The practice review panel formed there in the corridor, absurd and therefore Dock Seven: a door-runner, a clinic reviewer, a machine witness when needed, an elder or household skeptic when family signals touched the work, a clerk, an adverse reviewer, and the client or chosen advocate first where possible.

The door accepted the field.

Then it asked:

OWNER?

Nox's stomach tightened.

The door had never asked that before. It had assumed. All her old tools had assumed. Root access, repair key, workshop authority, emergency override: Nox, Nox, Nox.

The new field waited.

Lio said, "Answer carefully."

"Thank you for the pressure."

"Pressure disclosed."

Nox almost laughed.

She typed:

STEWARD: NOX ARAN / REVIEWABLE / REMOVABLE FOR MISUSE

CONTROL: SHARED BY PRACTICE REVIEW FOR ACCESS CONDITIONS ONLY

CLIENT MATERIAL: CLIENT-CONTROLLED WHERE POSSIBLE

EMERGENCY OVERRIDE: SPLIT-KEY / CARE RISK ONLY / POST-EVENT EXPLANATION REQUIRED

NO ROOT OWNER.

The door rejected the last line.

INVALID FIELD: NO ROOT OWNER.

Nox felt cold.

Sister-Cache said, "OLD ARCHITECTURE REQUIRES ROOT."

The magistrate said, "All systems require an accountable owner."

Nox said, "That is how they become cages."

Sela did not argue. She looked at the old hardware. "Can root be escrowed?"

Nox shook her head. "Escrow becomes capture."

Lio stepped closer. "Can root be broken?"

Bela said, "That sounds unsafe."

"It is," Nox said.

The old root structure had saved people because Nox could act before systems woke. It had endangered people because everything eventually woke in her hand. Breaking it would make the workshop clumsier. Safer in some ways. Worse in emergencies. Less hers.

She opened the root map.

It showed old paths in colors she had invented before she knew color could become doctrine: mercy blue, debt red, family gold, clinic green, no-law gray. Each path had a master branch leading back to her private key.

Nox copied the key.

Then she cut it into five useless pieces.

Bela inhaled.

Sister-Cache said, "DRAMATIC BUT MATHEMATICALLY INCOMPLETE."

"I know," Nox said.

She assigned the shards to functions, not owners: emergency care, client exit, evidence preservation, access condition update, and misuse shutdown. Each required different combinations of reviewers. None allowed Nox alone to open client material. None allowed the panel alone to force repair. All of them could fail awkwardly.

Especially awkwardly.

The door accepted:

ROOT REPLACED BY FUNCTIONAL SPLIT-KEY.

NO SINGLE PARTY MAY OPEN, CLOSE, EXPORT, MERGE, DELETE, OR RECLASSIFY CLIENT MATERIAL.

EMERGENCY CARE ACCESS SHALL BE MINIMAL, LOGGED, AND REVIEWED.

CLIENT EXIT KEY SHALL REMAIN SIMPLE ENOUGH TO USE UNDER FEAR.

Nox stared at that last line.

Lio said, "Good."

Bela said, "Good."

Varo said, "About time."

Sela said nothing. She only recorded.

The door asked again:

OWNER?

Nox typed:

NO OWNER. ACCOUNTABLE STEWARDSHIP ONLY.

This time the door accepted it.

The latch clicked once, then held.

Sister-Cache hummed in Nox's inner line. "That clause reduces archival elegance."

"Good."

"It increases survival probability."

"Better."

The door unlocked.

No music played. No hidden system blessed them. The latch simply gave up its old certainty.

Inside, the workshop was exactly as Nox had left it and not the same room at all.

The kettle sat on the back bench. Bela's old cup was chipped near the handle. Tavi's illegal game tile, the one Nox had kept after pretending she had thrown out all sentimental evidence, lay under a spool of witness fiber. Dust made every surface look accused.

Lio stood at the threshold and did not enter.

1B entered, took the document titled RESEMBLANCE IS NOT APPOINTMENT, and left.

The family fork entered only far enough to hear the workshop's old ventilation fan, then stepped back as if that, too, had been enough.

Sela did not enter.

Bela did.

She walked to the kettle, picked it up, and inspected the mineral line. "You neglected my property."

"I stole it years ago."

"Theft does not cancel stewardship."

"I'm sorry about the kettle."

Bela looked at her over it.

Nox let the smaller apology hold the larger one without pretending it completed the work.

"I know," Bela said.

That was not forgiveness.

It was an open door with a latch still attached.

Nox turned back to the threshold.

Reader 7F remained asleep behind protected rest.

1A remained absent behind protected refusal.

Miren Oath remained alive, watched, useful, and not clean.

Varo's kitchen remained loud, conditional, and full of ghosts who were not asked to move chairs for the living.

Dock Seven kept arguing with itself in every door language it knew.

The Mirror Court was broken, not erased. There would be fragments. Buyers. Families who wanted miracles cheap. Creditors who learned the new words before the wounds did. Officials who would try to own whatever people needed most.

Nox could not build a door that ended hunger.

She could build one that did not feed on it first.

She changed the final setting.

WORKSHOP ACCESS: UNLOCKED FOR REQUESTED IDENTITY REPAIR

DEFAULT MODE: ASK FIRST

OWNERSHIP CLAIM: NONE

RIGHT TO LEAVE: ALWAYS

RIGHT TO REMAIN PARTIAL: ALWAYS

RIGHT TO BE UNRECOGNIZED: ALWAYS

RIGHT TO RETURN UNDER NEW TERMS: ALWAYS

The mirror panels outside the corridor caught the text and fractured it into a hundred uneven copies. Some would be misread. Some would be used badly. Some would save a person for one hour, which was not enough and still not nothing.

Bela came to stand beside her.

"Every door a mirror," she said.

Nox watched the open workshop reflect the bazaar, the bazaar reflect the damaged court, the court reflect every old family hunger that had ever mistaken love for possession.

"No," Nox said.

Bela waited.

Nox touched the latch, not to close it, but to feel that it still could.

"Every door asks what we think a person owes before we let them through."

Behind them, Lio laughed softly.

"Terrible slogan," they said.

"It's not a slogan."

"Good."

The first stranger came at the change of shift.

They wore three names crossed out on their sleeve and a fourth written in pencil. They stood at the threshold with both hands visible, eyes on the exit behind them, body ready to run.

Nox did not ask who they had been.

She did not ask who owned them.

She did not ask for proof.

She pointed to the door text where the person could read it before deciding whether to enter.

"What are you asking for?" Nox said.

The stranger looked at the open room, the old tools, the visible exit, the people who were not reaching.

"I don't know yet."

Nox nodded.

For once, that was enough to begin.
